recherche

Before the fame, there was the fall, and everything that came with it.

Chapter One

2005 || N E W   Y O R K 

"Kids, come downstairs!" the eager voice of her father had called towards the staircase, "dinner is prepared, we have guests!"

With that, the young man headed back to the massive dining room, knowing well he wouldn't have to call more than once. He takes his seat back at the table respectively, bowing his head as a greeting to the guests sitting with him. "They should be down shortly," he informs with a kind smile. 

One of the guests, a young woman looking in her early thirties, had looked down to a tiny boy that sat beside her. His black fringe would be falling in his eyes if it wasn't styled nicely to the side, and there's a bored expression on his face as he kept picking away at the food the maid's had prepared for the special occasion. It would've been obvious had anyone paid attention that the woman was faking her cheery tone as she leaned over to tap the boy's shoulder, gaining his focus instead of the plate before him, "did you hear that, Sunwoo? You can make some new friends."

The young boy peers up at presumably his mother blankly. No sign of emotion is on his face, and she didn't think he was going to bother to reply until he monotones, "Friends?" Hearing this confused the other couple at the table, did he not know what it meant? He was already around ten years old, surely he had friends.

"Yes, Sunwoo, so be polite, okay? We'll be seeing them a lot now, they're our new partners," she tells him sternly, but yet still keeps the baby tone since she was talking to her child. 

His brown irises only stare back into her soul. Sunwoo didn't seem very interested nor convinced in anything she was saying. Recalling what he heard his parents talking about in the limo on the way here, in fact, he found it quite odd. 

Not being old enough to fully comprehend what they had meant when he overheard them earlier, he was still smart enough to scout out that something was fishy. "Partners? But you and dad were just saying earlier-"

"-Sunwoo, sweetheart, don't spout nonsense," his mother cuts him off with a slight giggle as if nothing in the world was wrong. He could see the burning look of anger in her eyes, however, from what he nearly spilled.

His father, who was on the other side of his mother, was beyond annoyed. After all, he was the mastermind behind it all. Something as foolish as his ten year old son blurting out his plan could still blow the entire operation.

The young couple were unaware of what to make of it. Exchanging glances, they figure it was just Sunwoo being a kid and spouting just what his mother said, nonsense. With that, the sound of frantic, tiny steps slamming against the wooden floors are heard. It causes any conversation to be cut off, and everyone's eyes wander to the entrance of the dining room.

"Here they are now," the young woman boasts, heart eyes taking over her expression as she watches her four beloved children run into the room. Well, all running except for one. Jaehyun was trying to stop his younger siblings from running since they were supposed to be polite and not cause commotion, which meant he was chasing after them. 

"Kids, introduce yourselves," their father had commanded as they all stop before the table. 

This was something they were used to doing. Everyone already knew the drill, they had met their father's business associates many times. He always seemed to be meeting new ones every few weeks, and the young children had been through the process it came naturally at that point.

In unison, they all bow to the new guests at their table. One by one, the siblings introduce themselves before taking their assigned seats. 

Like per usual, the last one had cleared her throat, clapping her hands together as she bows a few times to the man and woman at the table, "hello, nice to meet you, my name is Solar."

They offer some greetings in return as she takes her seat. "Oh, your children are so cute!" the young woman had been gushing, "they're so polite and well-mannered!"

Her parents chuckle as if it was nothing. "That's our kids for you."

The dinner goes by like every other one does. It was full of fake compliments, forced laughs, and occasional business talk. Of course, the children at the table were completely uninterested in anything going on. Their only source of entertainment was kicking each other under the table, since it was the only thing they could do without their parents noticing and getting mad at them. 

Other than that, the four kids remained silent. Solar finishes her small plate of food rather quickly, and she had taken the time to actually look up at the guests. 

Only being nine years old, she wasn't smart enough to jump to conclusions on what kind of people they were. All her mind could think was the fact they didn't seem like the usual associates her father got involved with. She stares at them for a while, but didn't dare to bring up the subject or speak a word at the table at all. If her father invited them into their home, she figured, they had to be good. 

Suddenly, two brown eyes lock onto hers. She hadn't known she was staring until they did, and she nearly jumps from shock of the boy staring holes into her soul. His glare was intimidating, and she clears her throat, averting her eyes to her plate instead.

"May I be excused?" she speaks up, making sure all conversation had been halted first.

Dabbing her face with a napkin before placing it back in her lap, her mother smiles sweetly to her, "of course, Solar."

Nodding, she pushes out of her chair. A maid came by to take her plate to the kitchen, and she thanks them before heading out of the room. Something about that boy had made her uncomfortable, and she had yet to put her finger on what it was. Either way, it made her want to get out of that room as fast as possible. 

Not knowing what to do to distract herself, she wanders into the large living room. The ceiling was high, held up by white pillars decorated in golden lining. Crinkles spread through the air from the burning fire in the fireplace, and it's a peaceful sound to her. Picking up one of her school books, she climbs onto the sofa before opening it on her lap. 

Studying was her favorite activity. Toys weren't her thing, she felt as if she had already grown out of them. School was an exciting thing to Solar, however. Learning new things everyday had become a craving, so much so she even like to constantly study on her own.

Chin in the palm of her hand, her eyes narrow in intense concentration down at the page. Math was her worst subject, which was why she was trying to study it the most. She wanted to get good enough to automatically do it in her head, that'd be so impressive.

"It's 61."

"Huh?!" she shrieks, dropping the book on the floor from how surprised she'd been at the abrupt voice from behind her. It hits the floor with an echoing slap, and Solar holds a hand to her head, spinning around to glare wide-eyed at the culprit who caused such a thing.

The boy, whom she didn't ever learn his name, raises a brow at her. A smirk is on his face, like he was trying not to laugh at what he just saw, before he explains further, "Six times six is thirty six, minus five is thirty one, plus fifteen times two which is thirty, equals sixty one."

Blinking at him a few times, she slowly picks up the math book again. Staring down at the problem he was talking about, she flips to the next page to see the answer. There was no way he was right in such a short amount of time.

61.

Gawking, she can't hide her bewilderment, "how did you do that so fast?"

Shrugging, he grabs onto the back of the sofa, before jumping over it to fall next to her. Solar found herself shocked by pretty much everything he did, and he slowly takes the pencil from her hand. Sitting close enough to her, he slides the book so it was on both of their laps at the same time.

A lump is caught in her throat that she couldn't get rid of. Sure, she'd been around boys before, but not like this. It was scary.

"Like this," he's muttering to her, and she could only watch as he started scribbling away in the book. Before her, this kid solved a math problem like he was a pro, leaving the nine year old Solar in complete awe. It was something she had never seen anyone do before, not even herself since she was so bad at math. "See? Easy," he says triumphantly, dropping the pencil with a plop back onto the book. 

Leaning forward to inspect it, she saw he'd done yet another problem in the span of about ten seconds. Although his writing was messy, she could still see how he worked out each part. 

"You're good," she compliments.

There's still a blank expression on his face, he just shrugs it off, "yeah, I'm good at math."

Infatuated with the new boy's crazy math skill she couldn't seem to believe, the little girl raises a finger, pointing to another question. It was a lot harder to kids as young as them, and she excitedly questions, "can you teach me this one?"

Inside, he chuckles at how happy she sounded at watching someone do a math equation. But on the outside, he remained monotone as he picks up the pencil again, his voice barely above a whisper, "sure."

And he did. He did each and every math equation the girl asked him to do like it was a piece of cake to him. She didn't think he was human for being so good at something that was actually insanely easy, but as young children, it was different. 

When she didn't understand how he got a number, he'd calmly explain it to her step by step. Solar honestly thought he was better than her actual math teacher at school.

It was when she finally deemed she understood it confidently, that she tried to take the pencil from his hand. Staring down at the problem she wanted to do, she hadn't been looking where her hand was going as she reached it out nonchalantly. 

But her tiny hand wasn't met with the feeling of her pencil, it was met with the warmth of skin. Jumping, her cheeks grow rosy from realizing she didn't grab the writing utensil - but his hand. She's left in a state of shock from what she just did, trying to figure out what she was supposed to do to recover from such an awkward event. 

"Here," he clears his throat, holding out the pencil to her when he comprehended that was what she wanted.

She didn't meet his eyes as she takes it. Their skin grazes again, and if she wasn't so young then, she would've felt the sparks. 

Little did she know, his eyes never left her the entire time she leaned forward to try an equation on her own once again. Her pencil skids across the paper like it was nothing. She felt like she could walk on air, all of a sudden math was coming so easily to her. It was all thanks to some kid she didn't even know.

Usually, she never cared for any of her father's business partners, but Solar was beginning to think this one might not be so bad despite her previous thoughts.

"Oh my god!" sudden cries are heard from the doorway to the living room, and Solar was too focused on her equation to look up. Sunwoo, on the other hand, had blankly turned around to see what the commotion was about. Two kids zoom into the room, playful grins on their faces as they look to the two sitting together, "Solar's already close with the new kid!"

A confused look is on the nine year old's face as she gradually looks up from her math equation. At the sight of her face, Chaerin had only started giggling away, "Solar, is this your new boyfriend?!" the six year old was screeching, "I can't believe you're doing math together," she gasps as she points a tiny finger at the book, "think of all the coodies you have now, ew!"

She only narrows her eyes at her younger sister. Juyeon had looked to Chaerin, who was standing beside him, and a devious smirk spreads over his lips as he slowly reaches out to wipe his hand across her arm. "Now you have them, too!"

Upon feeling his touch, she shrivels away with a high-pitched scream. The idiotic children were a lot different than one would imagine from just seeing how they acted at the dinner table. Knowing in reality how immature her siblings were, a deep sigh leaves Solar's lips as she watches Juyeon evilly laugh before chasing his little sister around in circles, yelling about howo he was going to 'kill her with coodies.'

"You have to deal with this everyday?" the boy abruptly speaks up to her in question over their endless yelling.

Finishing the math equation, she sets the pencil down with a dull exhale, "yeah."

"God, I'm glad I don't have any siblings."

"What's wrong with siblings?" a sudden hum is heard, and the two had looked up again to see no one other than the oldest child of the family beaming down at them from behind the sofa. The eleven year old boy lays a hand on Solar's shoulder as he grins from ear to ear, "I think your big brother is pretty cool, bet you're glad to have him, huh?"

Rolling her eyes, she realizes she was never going to be able to do anymore work. Closing the book, she sets it aside on the couch before snapping, "no, I honestly don't, the only thing I think of you is how annoying you are."

An offended look takes over his face, but the boy beside her had only started snickering. He never really found anyone funny, but for some reason, this girl was something else.

"Hey, kid," Jaehyun had suddenly said to him, "watch it, I can see how you're looking at my sister."

He blinks at him in misunderstanding, "what, am I not allowed to make eye contact with someone?"

"What's your name?" the oldest had asked instead.

Not getting why he's asking, he realized he had yet to tell Solar that either. He only knew any of their names since they were forced to introduce themselves at dinner.

"Sunwoo."

Falling onto the couch beside the boy, he punches his shoulder as he hums in the most intimidating voice he could muster, "well, Sunwoo, you better keep your hands off my sister, dude.

Chapter Two


2006 || N E W   Y O R K

"God dammit!" the screams of her father boomed through the entire house, so loudly it felt like the walls were trembling from the sound-waves. A young girl is perched on her window seat, a book in her lap, and she had been enjoying it rather thoroughly, but the yelling was spoiling her peaceful time. As if just that wasn't bad enough, the sounds of crashing is heard downstairs, followed by shattering glass. There was no telling what was going on down there, but she honestly didn't want to know as she sighs, leaning her head against the cold window and shaking her head in disappointment.

No matter how hard she tried to ignore it, it was becoming harder day by day. The young girl didn't have any clue why her father was so on edge recently, but it had only happened within the last couple of weeks. She had been trying to eavesdrop on her parents until she got caught, so now she just figured it was best to sit in her room. 

Her siblings were rather freaked out about it, too. Nobody knew what was going on, and all she had to offer was that she heard her parents talking about a traitor. They all guessed someone had done something bad to their father's business. It seemed so serious, that he hadn't invited only any new business associates in the last few weeks like usual. Although, the break was nice, it sure wasn't pleasant nor worth if they had to listen to the screams constantly.

The only one with any common sense other than her was her older brother. He'd brought it upon her that the other Kim family her father had grown so closely acquainted with hadn't been over in weeks, which was around the time he started throwing the tantrums. She'd thought about this rather hard, and had definitely noticed it, too. Ever since the first dinner, the Kim family had been coming over practically every week. She got to see Sunwoo so often that they were becoming rather close friends, and she might even have a small crush, too.

Never would she tell anyone that, though. Her little siblings already pestered her about it enough, anyway.

But suddenly, the Kim family's visits stopped, and no one knew why. She hadn't seen Sunwoo in weeks now, and with no way to contact him since things like cellphones weren't really relevant yet in time, she was hopeless. He didn't even go to her school, and it wasn't like she could just get up and walk to his house.

Solar heaves out a sigh, missing her newest friend. She was never someone who had a lot of those, after all. Friends were hard to come by when you grew up like her.  You're only allowed to be friends with certain people, and half the time, those same people are just using you. 

Of course, even then, someone like her wasn't friendless. She'd actually found great friendships in the families of her father's other close business partners. 

"I could lose everything!" her father's cries of anger are heard through the walls once again, "everything I fucking built, everything I worked for!"

Exhaling, she closes her book and sets it aside on the cushion she sat on as a knock had sounded on her door. Rising to her feet, she pads across the carpeted floor to unlock her door. 

The eyes of her older brother peer into hers, and she steps aside to let him in the room. "What's up?" the eleven year old is asking her brother as he waltz right in, which she didn't mind since she was used to it. Closing the door behind him, she walks to take a seat on her bed like he just had, or more like catapulted onto the mattress. 

"I think I found out what the Kim family did to father," he informs her, causing her eyes to widen. Leaning over his shoulder, she peers down at what he was holding in his hands. It was a newspaper, and he opens it to fulfill her curiosity. 

There, upon the front page, was the title in big black lettering: Lee Industries going head to head with Kim Industries. He points to the article after it, that was in smaller lettering so she had to squint a bit to see. Head Business Associate at Lee Industries Murdered, Police Investigation in Progress, she read mentally, and gawks down at it once she understands what it was talking about.

"Ji Dongwoo," he reads the name of the man that was killed out loud, before turning to look at his sister, "that's the name of your friend's dad, right? He worked for father."

Blinking at him, she hesitantly nods, "Yeah, Changmin's been really sad about it," she replies, still beyond confused at what he was getting at, "but what does that have to do with the Kim's?"

The two kids were far too young to ever begin to understand the article about the business drama. They didn't know what any of the words meant since they were only eleven and twelve years old, but what they did know, was how to use their massive imaginations. Even though children were known for having crazy minds, little did they know, they weren't crazy at all.

Looking around her room as if there would be someone else there listening to them, Jaehyun leans towards her to whisper in her ear, "I think Sunwoo's dad killed him."

Solar gasps loudly at her brother's words, before slapping his arm. He didn't even flinch at her movement, and she scoffs, "don't say that, he's nice!"

"No he's not, Sol, he tried to steal father's company!" Jaehyun shoots back, shaking his head, "think about it, it happened at the same time!"

"You're crazy," she tells him sternly.

He only sighs as he gets up from her bed, "you never know, Solar. You know what father says, there's evil people out there."

A frown is on her face as she takes in his theory. She could see how it made sense in their young minds, but she still didn't want to believe it. "That doesn't mean Sunwoo's dad is."

The look on her brother's face is impatient as he glares at her. She groans in response, throwing a pillow at him. After a second of thought, she pends, "if his dad is evil, does that make Sunwoo evil, too?"

Such a sudden question from his little sister makes him frown. Walking towards her bed again, he takes a seat next to her on the edge this time. His hand reaches out to lay on her shoulder as he asks, "well, you're the one who's friends with him, what do you think?"

Pursing her lips in a straight line, Solar stares at her feet as she thinks hard about it. Swinging them back and forth off the edge of the bed, she finally confidently concludes, "no, there's no way someone like Sunwoo is evil, he's too nice!"

"But you said Sunwoo's dad was nice, too."

"Shut up!" she whines, grabbing another pillow and hitting him with it. Her brother only laughs as he takes it from her small hands, throwing back to it's original place. "I really like Sunwoo," she mutters a second later, staring at her hands in her lap.

Jaehyun suddenly started making gagging sounds, holding his throat as if there was something stuck in it. "Ew, Solar, I don't want to know!" he wrinkles his nose, "but I'm sure Chaerin and Juyeon sure want to," he smirks a second later, referring to their two younger siblings that love to tease her about having a crush on Sunwoo.

Before she can object, the oldest brother shoots to his feet, running for the door like a lunatic. "No, Jaehyun, don't tell them!" she cries in panic, trying to catch him as he swings the door open, running straight for one of their rooms.

"Guys," her brother ignores her pleads as he kicks open Chaerin's door and then Juyeon's as they were right across from eachother's, "Solar really does like Sunwoo!"

Gripping her hair on her head and trying to resist the urge to pull it all out, she reaches an arm out. Managing to grip Jaehyun's sleeve, he stops him from running around screeching about her liking Sunwoo, but it was too late. By that point, the little siblings had heard all they needed to hear to pester her.

"I don't like him!" she's whining minutes later, trying to find any level of peace in the house besides her father's endless screaming downstairs, and the mess that Jaehyun created that involved her little siblings yelling about her liking Sunwoo outside her door. 

"Liar, liar, pants on fire!" Chaerin had giggled from the other side of Solar's closed door, banging on it again with her small fists, "Solar likes Sunwoo, Solar likes Sunwoo!"

Groaning in absolute pain, she slams her head gently against the door that she was leaning against. She wanted to just delete her little sister from the world sometimes. It was the fact she took pleasure in making her want to die that bothered Solar. She was no doubt going to hurt her older brother when she had the chance for what he caused her.

Feeling lost inside, she closes her eyes and sits for a while. Figuring Chae would go away if she just ignored her for a while, it was exactly what she was going to do. With that, she sighs, preparing to tune out anything she said.

Upon doing this, she finds herself wandering into deep thoughts. It wasn't like she would ever see Sunwoo again, what did it matter? He was just a temporary friend, one she only got to see when he came over with his parents for some sort of business meeting. Since his father had done whatever it was he did to Solar's father, he definitely wouldn't be coming over anytime soon, that was for sure. Since he didn't go to the same school as her, she'd never see him again.

"Just forget about him," she mutters to herself. What did a little school girl crush matter to her anyway? Liking boys was honestly not her thing, she thought, maybe when she was fifty - but poor little Solar was only eleven. 

So she does, Sunwoo didn't cross her mind again. Whenever her little siblings would bother her about it, they'd get snapped at. Her short-term friend Sunwoo was gone, and his family was bad news anyway. She knew that from hearing her father scream about how much he hated them everyday.

Though, she couldn't help but wonder exactly what they did.

///

"Solar, Solar, SOLAR!" 

The girl is nearly tackled to the ground by her twelve year old brother. A cry of pain leaves her lips when she stumbles over and would've fallen face-first if Chanhee hadn't caught her. Annoyed as she pushes him off of her, she turns to him with bewilderment splattered on her expression, "Jaehyun, what is it?!" she cries, dusting herself off.

Breathing heavily from the fact he had ran what felt like a mile to catch up to her, he takes a moment to catch his breathe. Holding the straps on his backpack tightly, he walks with her as he clears his throat, "listen, you're not gonna believe me-"

"-Wait, first," she cuts him off, raising a hand to make him stop, "ask yourself, does Solar care?"

Chanhee is giggling from beside her, before he shakes his head, "Sol, that's not very nice."

"He's annoying!" she shrugs in defense, until her eyes fall on her impatient brother again, who was just gazing at her. Knowing he wasn't going to stop, she groans, "alright, what?"

By now, they had reached the entrance of their elementary school. The three stop outside the large doors, and Jaehyun motions for them to both come closer. Chanhee and Solar exchange looks of wonder, and they both don't look very expectant of whatever they were about to be told. It was common for him to mess with them all the time, after all.

Leaning forward to whisper, Solar taps her converse against the concrete impatiently, "don't freak out but-," he pauses for a second, exhaling deeply as if he was about to say something mind boggling, "Sunwoo is here."

Solar accidentally dropped her backpack that was hanging off one arm out of shock. Her mouth gawks, and she holds a hand over it to try to hide how she was doing exactly what her brother asked not to do. "Tell me you're kidding," she tells him harshly, but his face remained the same. Unable to contain herself, she starts jumping around like some sort of mental patient. "I can't believe this!"

"But you know father's not gonna like it if you're friends with him," her brother adds, but she was too joyful to pay much attention.

"I don't care!" she's crying, turning to her best friend who had nicely picked up her bag and was holding it for her.

Watching her excited session, he raises a brow, looking for confirmation, "is that the guy you like?"

She only nods vigorously, "yeah, yeah it's him!"

The bell had suddenly let out a ring, and her brother had cursed as he rushed inside the building so he wasn't completely late for class. Her and Chanhee are left to gasp, before they do the same. But unlike usual, she couldn't help but continue to be giddy.

She said she'd forget about him, but now she didn't have to. 

Not that she was ever going to.






Chapter Three

2006 || N E W  Y O R K

Solar was too afraid to talk to Sunwoo for weeks. She didn't have any classes with him, but sometimes she would see him in the halls, walking by himself, head hanging low. He looked like a zombie most of the time, like if anyone tried to talk to him he would snarl and eat their brains out. 

It was clear she wasn't the only one who thought that, because no one dared to say a word to Kim Sunwoo. All the other eleven year olds thought he was weird, they were confused and intimidated by someone who seemed so blank and lifeless. The thing about Kim Sunwoo, was that he wasn't lifeless, he just didn't feel like he had much of a life to live. 

"I can't believe that's the guy," Chanhee would lecture her every time they would walk past him. "God, Solar, that's not a very good first crush." The boy would wrinkle his nose every time, as if he was looking at some kind of trash. Solar found it weird, she didn't think Sunwoo was trash. He was cool. 

"I don't have a crush on him," she would slap his arm in annoyance, "he was just my friend, but now it's like he doesn't even see me."

"That's cause he doesn't," her best friend rolled his eyes, shaking his head as they left the school building and entered the outside world to head home, "he always looks like he doesn't know where he is."

Like her friends had said, they thought he was weird, they didn't get why she would want to be friends with someone like him. But she would just tell them that they needed to know him before they could judge. She just had to build up the courage to say something. 

So, eleven year old Solar waited until recess on a Friday. The sun was beaming down on her as she made her way across the tiny rocks that scattered the playground, using her hand as sunglasses as her eyes raked across every piece of playground equipment. She didn't see him anywhere, and was beginning to think that maybe he didn't even go to recess.

That was, until her eyes landed on a tree that was in the corner of the playground, all the way at the very back. And there, sat a boy against it, a hoodie over his head that was hanging down. He looked dead, it was hard to see if he was even breathing. She didn't have to think to know it was him.

It took every ounce of courage in her tiny body to make her walk over there. Here she was, quiet girl Solar going to talk to the "weird and creepy" new kid that everyone was scared of. She would be friends with Sunwoo again, and then show everyone that he was a great person. Then, Sunwoo would get lots of friends, or at least get to be friends with her friends.

It was a great plan to her. 

"Sunwoo," she blurted out, her feet skidding to a stop in the rocks. There she stood, feet away from the boy that had somehow not seen her the entire time. 

Something seemed to have snapped inside of him when the voice entered his ears. He seemed confused at first, like he was in a dream, as he hesitantly lifted his head up. 

Only then did she see the kind of state he was in. There were dark purple eyebags practically around his entire eye, almost making him look like a raccoon. A bruise was on his cheek, and he had a cast around his wrist. If she kept staring, she would probably have found more marks on him, as her eyes bulged out of their sockets, "Whoa, are you okay?!"

He'd opened his mouth to say something, likely to just say hello or that he didn't know she went to school here, but her question caused his mouth to slam shut again. As if he was having a battle with himself, his eyes jerked away to look at his feet again. His brain yelled at him, telling him to think faster. 

Until he had mumbled ever so quietly, "my dad has been teaching me how to box."

Good enough excuse.

"Oh," Solar hummed, still feeling like that seemed off, but she didn't want to push it. After a moment of debate, she sat down across from him on the ground. "I didn't expect you to move to my school, I thought I'd never see you again."

The boy swallowed a lump in his throat, but also a dark secret along with it. He knew too many things, too many things an eleven year old boy shouldn't know. And they haunted and taunted him everyday of his life, every time he looked in the mirror. He was reminded that he wasn't a normal boy, he wasn't a preppy average rich kid like everyone else around him. It made it so horrible to be in such an environment, such a town where everyone seemed to be photo copies of each other.

Though, Solar didn't make him feel that way. 

"Yeah, um..." he paused for a second, looking to his lap in thought, "I guess our parents aren't really friends anymore, something to do with their companies, I guess."

The newspaper that Jaehyun had showed her suddenly entered her mind. She found herself thinking about what her brother said, the thing she had swore sounded crazy. But she almost found herself wanting to ask Sunwoo.

Knowing that would be way out of line, she just nodded, "uh, yeah, it seems that way. I was kinda sad... um... you know, not getting to hang out with you anymore."

The boy everyone called a zombie had smiled as he looked down at his hands in his lap. "I was sad, too, Solar," he agreed after a moment, "I'm glad to know someone at this place."

Solar decided to not ask him anything weird. She didn't want him to be uncomfortable, especially now that this was the first time they were seeing each other after a while. So, she just cheerily piped, "Why don't I introduce you to my friends? They're super nice and will for sure help you feel more comfortable!"

You could tell he wanted to say no. But with the way the girl was beaming at him with raw excitement, he felt like he couldn't.

"Okay," he mumbles, still looking down at the ground. 

The girl had jumped to her feet almost instantly. "Great, wait here!" she tells him, before her feet run off across the playground. 

There was a small smile lingering on Sunwoo's lips, before he seemed to remind himself he shouldn't be doing that. Clearing his throat, his face fell back to being as blank as ever as he hung his head back down, pulling the hood of his hoodie further over his face. His gut was telling him this was a bad idea, to make any friends here, especially friends with Solar, the eldest daughter of the family his father was currently fighting with. He knew it was bad. 

But something about her made him feel a certain way he couldn't understand yet. She made him feel like he fit in. And Sunwoo liked it, it was the only time he had ever felt like he was just like all the other kids. 

"Solar! I don't want to meet him!" a hushed, sassy voice was whining. The sound of footsteps scraping through the rocks of the playground were heard coming towards him again. 

"Hee, just give him a chance, he's so nice!" Solar was insisting in a pleading manner, "You guys will give him a chance, won't you?" 

"Umm... sure, Sol!" another voice had cheerily spoke. 

Solar was standing before Sunwoo again, and he slowly lifted his head to peer at four boys besides her. He didn't know their names, but he knew of them - she had told him about them before. 

"Sunwoo, these are my friends - Chanhee, Jacob, Kevin," she was pointing to each one as she said their names. The boy didn't think much of it until her finger pointed to the very last one, "and Changmin." 

His stomach dropped at the sight of the last kid. His brown hair was in his eyes and he looked depressed. The boy had his hands in his pockets and didn't even manage to smile his way, it was like he was frozen in time. And Sunwoo knew why.

That was the kid who's dad was killed.

And Sunwoo was the only one who knew how and who did such a thing. 

He hung his head back down before he could show any emotion on his face. Never could anyone know how much he knew about that. If he felt any guilt, it would go away soon enough. This was his life. 

He'd get used to it. 

"Nice to meet you, Sunwoo!" Kevin is waving, "We've heard a lot about you-"

"-Shut up!" Solar slapped his arm in frustration, causing the boy to pout as he muttered out an ow.

"Hey," is all the boy deadpans, but he didn't raise his head to even look at them this time. 

Solar seemed to realize he was growing uncomfortable. She knew Sunwoo didn't really like to socialize, so she turned to her friends and clapped her hands together. "Well, I guess that's that - you guys can go back to the swings, I'll meet you over there." 

The boys exchanged looks. Chanhee was rolling his eyes, whispering out, "I told you it was a stupid idea!" as he motioned for the other three boys to follow him back to where they were originally hanging out at.

"And what was on his face? It looked painful," Jacob had been mumbling before they had gone out of ear range. 

Solar frowned - it hadn't gone how she planned. "Sorry," she apologizes with a dull tone, sitting back down on the rocky playground next to him, "they're kind of a lot but they mean well." 

Sunwoo was starting to feel bad that he just wasn't a social person. Solar was clearly trying hard. "I'm just not good at making friends," he admits to her, trying to make her feel better about the situation, "thank you for trying to help me." 

"Oh - sure," she seems to pipe up from his nice words, "no one deserves to be alone - but, I'm happy to always keep you company, Sunwoo." 

He looked back down at his lap, nervously spouting, "I - I'd like that." 

///

So, weeks would pass. And Solar kept her word. She spent endless amounts of time with Sunwoo at school, always hanging out with him on the playground and making him sit with her at lunch. Other kids would give him weird looks and whisper about him, they would say he looked different, that the bruises on his face were creepy. 

Sunwoo was just quiet, he was reserved - and there was nothing wrong with that. Solar liked him, she had a silly school crush on him to the point that she began to think he could do no wrong. She refused to believe his family could ever have anything to do with what happened to Changmin's dad because they couldn't be bad people, they just didn't seem like it. 

The only thing she was worried about was his bruises. As time went on, she noticed he seemed to get more the second one would heal. But every time she would ask, he would just shake it off and say that he was getting really into boxing.

Boxing? She guessed she didn't really know what boxing was like at that age. 

"How do you do this one?" she was asking him, pointing to a question on the worksheet with the end of her pencil. The two were sitting at the lunch table, Solar was working on homework, and Sunwoo was sitting there - slowly eating an apple. 

"Let me see," he mumbles, sliding her paper in front of him with the hand that wasn't holding the apple. Taking another bite, the only sound Solar could hear was his munching as he took her pencil, working out the steps for her on the paper so she could clearly see how to do it.

She was just sitting there, watching him do math like it was the craziest thing she'd ever seen, when someone sits besides her, snapping her out of her daze. 

"Sol, not to bother your weird date time - but God, Kevin is being weird again," Chanhee was sighing out, holding onto the girl's arm like he was terrified. There was a look of horror on his face when she turned to look at her best friend, and she could only blink in confusion. 

"What do you mean?" she's asking, having to think for a moment. "Oh - is he doing that thing where he starts singing-" she cuts herself off when she turns to see what Kevin was doing at the lunch table they usually sat at. Once she saw him, her suspicions were confirmed. Their ridiculous friend was standing on top of one of the lunch room chairs, singing. 

"Oh my God," she starts giggling uncontrollably, "not again." 

"It's not funny!" Chanhee is whining, "Everyone stares at us because he's so weird!"

Kevin was indeed a little weird. But she always thought it was cool because he simply didn't care about how weird he was. He simply embraced the disturbed looks he would get from others. Solar admired him for that. 

"Welp, looks like he has to stop now," she's humming, seeing one of the school teachers get onto Kevin for causing a ruckus during lunch again.

"Thank God, I can finally finish my lunch now without feeling like the entire cafeteria is looking at me," Chanhee exhales in relief, jumping up from the seat, "see you later, Sol, no kissing!"

Then, he rushes off back to the lunch table, before she could even snap at him for his words. 

"Here you go," Sunwoo slid the paper back over to her once she had turned around to face him again. Her eyes widen as she looks down at it, seeing that he did not only one but all of the problems on the page.

"Whoa, you didn't have to do that," she gawks down at it in surprise, "thanks, Sunwoo."

He just shrugs and goes back to eating his apple. 

Meanwhile, Solar could feel her cheeks getting red as she stared at the paper. At the bottom of the page, Sunwoo had wrote a little heart. It was a small thing that made her flush with happiness. Ugh, this school crush was getting annoying.

But did the heart mean something? She could only ponder with excitement, it wasn't like she was going to say something to him out loud. 

"Hey Sunwoo or whatever, are you still trying to mess with my sister?" 

She had just been daydreaming staring down at the heart on the paper when she heard the most annoying voice ever. Her eyes jolt up to stare at Jaehyun, who had taken a seat across from Sunwoo and was leaning forward, narrowing his eyes and making a fist on the table. He was trying way too hard to look cool, and it made her groan. 

"Jae, go away!" she demands in annoyance, praying to God he wouldn't say anything else. 

Sunwoo didn't look phased by anything that was happening. He just lifted his head, hair falling in his eyes, and deadpans, "I'm not trying to do anything - you think boys can't be friends with girls?" 

The older brother just glared. "Nah, I think that I don't trust you, dude." 

"Jae!" the girl snaps again, not wanting him to ruin everything she had going now. This was serious to her, and she swore to God she was going to beat him up when they got home. She didn't understand why Jaehyun was acting like this now. He was the one who even told her Sunwoo moved to their school in the first place. 

Annoying brother.

"I'm just trying to protect you, Sol, he's suspicious!" her brother had insisted. 

But she didn't care for what he had to say. "Go away!" she kept demanding, until he finally got up. 

"Fine, but we will be talking after school!"

And that they would. As soon as the last bell rang, Solar had rushed down the hall to catch her older brother. He was hanging out with some of his friends by the lockers when she walked up to him, grabbing the strap of his backpack and pulling him somewhere they could talk privately. 

"Ow - hey, what the heck!" the boy was cursing out until she finally let him go. 

"What's wrong with you?!" she's crying in a hushed voice.

Her brother blinked at her a few times, his brain slowly processing what she was even talking about. Until a look of realization spread over his face, and he shrugs, "I don't think you should trust him, Sol, remember the talk we had about the news article? I think it's true, seriously - and those bruises he always has, something is fishy about him!"

"None of that is his fault!" she defends with a pout, "And he just says that he's into boxing - but it worries me, too - I think maybe you're right that his dad isn't very nice... but I still like him so can you please give him a chance?"

His expression softens from her pleading words. Despite how protective Jaehyun was over his little sister, he seemed to see where she was coming from. 

"What if father finds out?" was his next question, "Sunwoo's dad's company is father's biggest enemy right now, he'll kill you, Solar."

She just shook her head. "He never will."






Chapter Four

2007 || N E W  Y O R K

Winter had come and gone, and with it, something had quietly shifted.

Solar couldn't put her finger on exactly when it happened. It wasn't like there was a moment she could point to and say there, that's when everything changed. It was more like she had blinked and suddenly things were just — different. Sunwoo was different, or maybe she was. Maybe they both were, and neither of them had the words for it yet.

What she did know was this: she thought about him a lot. More than she probably should've for someone who was just her friend. She thought about him when she was doing her homework, when she was eating dinner, when Chaerin would pester her about something stupid and she would wish she was somewhere else entirely. She thought about the small heart he'd drawn at the bottom of her worksheet, which she had quietly torn off and kept in the back of her desk drawer where no one would ever find it.

She was twelve years old. She didn't fully understand what any of it meant yet. But she knew enough to be embarrassed about it.

"You're doing it again," Chanhee announced from beside her.

Solar blinked, snapping back to the hallway around her. Students were rushing past them, the usual morning chaos of arriving at school, and she had apparently been staring at nothing for an undetermined amount of time. "Doing what," she said flatly.

"The face."

"I don't have a face."

"You have a face," he insisted, pulling his backpack strap higher on his shoulder and giving her a long, knowing look. When she didn't say anything, he tilted his head pointedly in the direction she had, apparently, been staring.

Sunwoo was at the end of the hall, at his locker. His back was to them. He was just getting his things out like a normal person, completely unaware of being observed, and Solar felt her face go warm in a way that deeply annoyed her.

"I wasn't—"

"Solar."

"I was just looking down the hall!"

Chanhee stared at her for another long moment, then sighed the way he always did when he decided she was a lost cause. "Sure you were," he said, and walked ahead of her.

///

Lunch was its own kind of chaos, the way it always was.

Kevin had, at some point in the last few months, decided that Sunwoo was his new favorite person. This was not a sentiment Sunwoo shared, but it didn't seem to discourage Kevin in the slightest. He had taken to sitting directly beside him at the lunch table whenever he got the chance, which Sunwoo tolerated with the same blank patience he seemed to apply to most things that were out of his control.

"Okay but listen," Kevin was saying, gesturing wildly with his fork, "if you could only eat one food for the rest of your life, what would it be?"

Sunwoo stared at him, deadpanning, "why would I only be able to eat one food?"

"It's hypothetical."

"It's a bad hypothetical."

"Just answer it!"

Solar pressed her lips together to keep from laughing. Across the table, she caught Jacob watching the exchange with quiet amusement, chin resting in his hand. He had warmed to Sunwoo gradually, in the easy, uncomplicated way Jacob warmed to most people — not with fanfare, just with a steady, gentle acceptance that Solar had always admired about him. He wasn't someone who needed a reason to be kind to someone.

Changmin was at the end of the table, eating quietly and reading something. At some point his drink had been knocked to the edge of the table by Kevin's elbow — Kevin hadn't noticed — and it was teetering there, about to go. Changmin hadn't noticed either, still absorbed in whatever he was reading.

Sunwoo reached across without a word and moved it back from the edge.

Changmin looked up, surprised. Sunwoo was already looking elsewhere, expression unchanged, like he hadn't done anything at all. After a moment Changmin said, "oh — thanks," quietly, and went back to his book.

Solar watched it happen. She wasn't sure why it stayed with her. It was such a small thing — the kind of thing anyone might do. But there was something in the way Sunwoo had done it, the quickness of it, almost like a reflex, that she couldn't quite file away as nothing.

She told herself it was just because they were both quiet people.

She tried not to think about why else it might be.

"Rice," Sunwoo said finally.

Kevin pointed his fork at him triumphantly. "See, was that so hard?"

"Yes," Sunwoo said, and went back to eating.

Chanhee, who had been pointedly ignoring the conversation in favor of his lunch, glanced sideways at Sunwoo with an expression that Solar knew well — it was the expression he wore when he was revising an opinion he didn't want to admit he was revising. He hadn't fully warmed to Sunwoo yet. He was still circling him the way he circled most things he wasn't sure about, careful and a little suspicious. But she had noticed recently that the circling had slowed. That he laughed sometimes at things Sunwoo said, quietly, like he was trying not to.

She didn't point it out. She knew better.

"Solar," Kevin hums, turning to her now with the same manic energy, "one food, rest of your life, go."

"Pasta," she declared immediately.

"Boring!"

"It's not boring, there are hundreds of types of pasta, Kevin, it's actually the most strategic answer at this table—"

"She's right," Sunwoo said, without looking up from his tray.

Solar felt the back of her neck go warm. Across the table, Jacob was smiling at his food.

"Of course you'd take her side," Kevin groaned, flopping dramatically backward in his seat.

Chanhee glanced between them. He didn't say anything. But Solar saw the small, reluctant twitch at the corner of his mouth before he looked away.

///

It was Sunwoo who said it first, which surprised her. He wasn't someone who said things first. He was someone who sat with things quietly for a long time and then let them out in the smallest possible way, like a heart drawn at the bottom of a math worksheet. So when he said it, Solar genuinely didn't know what to do with herself.

They were on the playground after school. It was early spring, the air still carrying the last stubborn bite of winter, and they were sitting in their usual spot at the back near the tree. Solar had been talking — she was always talking when it was just the two of them, filling the quiet because she never knew if Sunwoo liked the quiet or just tolerated it — when he had cut her off.

Not rudely. He never did anything rudely, with her. He just said, "Solar," in that low, even tone that meant he had something to say and needed her to stop for a second.

She stopped.

He was looking at his hands in his lap. There was a new bruise on his forearm she had noticed earlier and filed away to ask about, yellow-green at the edges which meant it was a few days old at least. His jaw was set in that particular way it got when he was making himself say something he'd been sitting on for a while.

"I like you," he said. Very simply. Like it was a fact he was reporting.

Solar stared at him.

He finally looked up at her, and something in his expression was almost defensive, like he was bracing himself. "Like — not just as a friend," he clarified, which was completely unnecessary since she had understood him perfectly, she just hadn't figured out how to respond yet.

"Oh," she said.

He looked back down at his hands.

"I like you too," she said, before she could talk herself out of it. "Not just as a friend."

The silence that followed was the kind that felt full rather than empty. Sunwoo didn't say anything else, and neither did she, and they just sat there with the cold air and the sound of other kids playing somewhere across the yard. Eventually, Solar looked back down at the book she had open on her knee, though she wasn't reading a single word of it.

She could feel him looking at her. He did that a lot — looked at her when he thought she wasn't paying attention. She had learned not to call attention to it because he would stop, and she didn't want him to stop.

At the bottom of the page, without a word, Sunwoo reached over and drew a small heart in the corner of her book.

She bit the inside of her cheek to keep from smiling.

///

She made the mistake of coming home looking happy.

She didn't think she was being obvious about it. She had walked in the front door at the perfectly normal time she always did, said hello to her mother in the kitchen, and was halfway up the stairs when she heard the sound of small feet thundering down the hall.

"Solar's home!" Chaerin shrieked, appearing at the top of the stairs with the energy of someone who had been waiting for exactly this. Juyeon materialized half a second behind her, which meant they had been in the same room, which meant they had been scheming.

Solar's eyes narrowed. "What?"

"You look weird," Chaerin announced, studying her with the intense critical focus of a nine-year-old who had nothing better to do. "Why do you look weird?"

"I don't look weird."

"You're smiling."

"I'm allowed to smile, Chaerin, I have a face—"

"You never smile when you come home from school," Juyeon said, crossing his arms. He was trying to look serious and almost managing it. "You usually look like this." He arranged his expression into an exaggerated scowl.

"I do not look like that!" She snaps, aggravated by her siblings persistence.

"You kind of do," Chaerin agreed, nodding gravely. Then her eyes went wide and she grabbed Juyeon's arm, whispering aggressively but yet so blatantly trying to make it where Solar could hear, "Juyeon, Juyeon, it's the Sunwoo thing."

"It's not—"

"IT'S THE SUNWOO THING!" Chaerin screamed, dissolving into giggles and grabbing the banister for support. Juyeon started laughing too, the traitor, and Solar grabbed her backpack strap and pushed past both of them with as much dignity as she could manage.

"Both of you are so annoying," she informed them, walking down the hall toward her room.

"Solar has a boyfriend, Solar has a boyfriend—"

She shut her bedroom door, unable to take anymore of their teasing. Nonetheless, the giggling continued for approximately four more minutes on the other side of it before they got bored and went away.

Solar sat on the edge of her bed and looked at the ceiling and tried very hard to stop smiling. She failed.

A soft knock came at her door a few minutes later — different from Chaerin's usual assault. She already knew who it was from the sound of it.

"Come in," she sighed.

Jaehyun opened the door and leaned against the frame, arms crossed, looking at her with that expression she had come to think of as his I already know but I'm going to make you say it face. He was thirteen now, which apparently meant he had decided it was his job to be even more insufferable than before.

She looked back at him. Neither of them said anything for a moment.

"I'm not going to say anything stupid," he said finally.

"Good."

"I'm just going to say—" He stopped. Exhaled. "Be careful, Sol. Okay? That's all."

She looked at him for a moment, at the genuine worry sitting quietly underneath the older brother act. Despite everything, she felt a small rush of affection for him that she would absolutely never admit to.

"I know," she said.

He held her gaze a second longer, then nodded, and pulled the door shut behind him.

Solar looked back at the ceiling.

Be careful. Like it was that simple.

///

The bruises were getting worse.

She had been telling herself for months that it wasn't her business, that she believed him about the boxing, that there was a perfectly reasonable explanation for why a twelve-year-old boy had marks on him that didn't quite match what a twelve-year-old boy learning to box would reasonably have. She had been telling herself this so consistently and so firmly that she had almost started to believe it.

But it was getting harder.

It was a Tuesday when she finally said something. They were at the library after school, sitting across from each other at one of the low tables near the window. Sunwoo had his sleeves pushed up because the library was warm, and Solar had looked up from her book and seen the marks on his forearms and felt something cold move through her chest that had nothing to do with boxing.

"Sunwoo," she said quietly.

He looked up.

She nodded toward his arm. He followed her gaze, then looked back at her. His expression didn't change exactly, but something behind his eyes shifted in that way it did when he was deciding how much to say.

"I know you said boxing," she said carefully, keeping her voice low. "But those look—" She stopped. She didn't know how to finish it without sounding like she was accusing him of something, and she didn't want to do that. She just wanted him to know she was looking. That she saw.

Sunwoo was quiet for a moment. He pulled his sleeve back down.

"My dad," he started, then seemed to reorganize whatever he was about to say, "my dad is really serious about it. The training. He says if I'm going to be good at it, I have to actually practice." A beat. "I'm fine, Solar."

She looked at him. He met her eyes, and his were steady and patient and giving nothing away, and she wanted so badly to push further, to ask him the thing she had been circling around for almost a year now. But she didn't know how, and she didn't want to make him shut down, and she told herself she believed him.

"Okay," she said.

She picked her book back up. He picked his back up.

But she didn't read a word for the rest of the hour.



Chapter Five

2007 || NEW YORK

There was a version of Solar Lee that existed at the dinner table.

She knew how to be her. It wasn't difficult — it had never been difficult, she had been doing it her whole life. You sat up straight. You answered questions when they were asked of you. You didn't bring anything to the table that would disrupt the careful, pleasant equilibrium her mother worked so hard to maintain. You were Solar Lee, eldest daughter, top of her class, future of the family. You ate your dinner and you smiled when it was appropriate and you did not, under any circumstances, think about Kim Sunwoo.

She was thinking about Kim Sunwoo.

"Solar, how was school?" her mother asked from across the table. Solji Lee was a beautiful woman — composed, graceful, the kind of person who always seemed to be exactly where she was supposed to be. She was looking at Solar now with that warm, attentive expression she wore at dinner, the one that meant she was paying attention in the way that good mothers paid attention. The candles were lit, the table was set, everything in its right place.

"Fine," Solar said. "We got our math results back."

"And?"

"Hundred."

Her mother smiled, pleased. "Good girl."

Across the table, Jaehyun was cutting his food with the focused energy of someone who was very deliberately not looking at his sister. She could feel it from across the table. He had a particular talent for being loudly uninvolved.

"I got a good grade too!" Chaerin announced from beside Solar, unprompted.

"What grade?" their father asked, not looking up from his own plate.

A brief silence. "...seventy two."

"That's not a good grade, Chaerin," Jaesang said, in the tone he used when he was stating a fact rather than scolding. It landed the same way regardless. Chaerin shrank slightly in her seat, and Solar watched her mother reach over to pat her hand once, briefly, before returning to her own food.

That was how it went. A brief softness, and then back to the surface.

Juyeon, who had been suspiciously quiet for the entire meal, chose this moment to knock his glass of water directly into his own lap. The sound of it hitting the floor sent the table into the kind of chaos that only a nine-year-old could generate — Juyeon yelping, Chaerin shrieking with laughter, their mother calling for the maid, their father exhaling slowly through his nose in the manner of a man exercising profound patience.

Solar used the commotion to look back down at her plate.

She was thinking about the way Sunwoo had looked at her that afternoon, when they were walking home from school in the cold and she had said something that made him laugh — actually laugh, not just the small almost-smile he usually allowed himself, but a real one, brief and a little startled, like it had escaped before he could stop it. She had never heard him laugh before. She hadn't even known he could.

She was going to be thinking about that for a very long time.

"Solar."

She looked up. Her father was watching her from the head of the table, the chaos of Juyeon's spilled water already being handled around him. His expression was its usual unreadable composed thing, but his eyes were on her with a focus that made her sit slightly straighter.

"Yes, father?"

"Your teacher called the office today," he said. "She says you've been distracted in class recently."

The table had gone quiet again. Even Chaerin had stopped giggling. The tension seemed to rise out of nowhere, just like that. And the siblings obviously knew why, they knew what she was hiding, and they were fearful that their father may have finally figured it out.

Solar kept her face very still. "I'll do better," she said.

He held her gaze for a moment longer, then looked back at his plate. "See that you do." And that was it, nothing about her deep secret she swore he would never know.

Her mother offered her a small smile from across the table — encouraging, gentle, meaning nothing in particular. Solar smiled back.

The version of Solar Lee that existed at the dinner table smiled back.

///

The plan had been Chanhee's idea, technically — or at least, she had let him believe it was.

What had actually happened was that she had told him, very casually, that she needed to be somewhere on Saturday mornings sometimes and that she needed her parents to think she was at his house. Chanhee had stared at her for a long moment with the expression he reserved for when she was doing something he found genuinely concerning, and then he had sighed deeply and said, "how long is sometimes going to be?"

"I don't know," she had said honestly.

Another long sigh. "Fine," he'd said. "But Solar, if your father ever—"

"He won't."

"You keep saying that like it makes it true."

She had smiled at him then, and he had rolled his eyes, which was as close to okay, I'll cover for you as Chanhee ever got.

So on Saturday mornings, Solar Lee was at Chanhee's. That was the story, clean and simple, and her mother accepted it without question because Chanhee was a good boy from a good family and there was nothing suspicious about it. Solar had been going to Chanhee's since they were small. It was the most natural thing in the world.

What was slightly less natural was the fact that she was actually sitting on a bench in the Castle's east park with Sunwoo Kim, who she was, technically, not supposed to know.

The east park was the quieter one — more removed from the main path that the adults used for their morning walks, tucked behind a row of tall hedges that made it feel slightly separate from the rest of the grounds. In the warmer months it would have been full of the Lee family's gardeners tending to things, but in the grey chill of late autumn it was mostly empty. The occasional resident walked a dog past the far gate, well-dressed and unhurried, but no one paid attention to two kids on a bench.

It had taken them a few weeks to find the right spot. Sunwoo had been particular about it in the way he was particular about everything that involved not being caught — methodical, unhurried, thinking three steps ahead without appearing to. He had walked the perimeter of the east park twice before selecting the bench, which Solar had found both impressive and slightly unnerving for a twelve-year-old.

She hadn't said that out loud.

"Kevin told Jacob that he thinks we're going out," she said now, watching a single leaf drift down from the tree above them and land on the path.

Sunwoo was quiet for a moment. "What did Jacob say?"

"He said he already knew."

"What did you say?"

"I wasn't there, Kevin told me after." She glanced at him sideways. "What would you have said?"

He considered this with the same gravity he brought to most things. The park was still around them, the kind of quiet that felt expensive. "I would've said it wasn't any of Kevin's business," he said finally.

"That's not an answer."

"It's an answer."

"It's not an answer to the question I was asking," she said patiently.

He looked at her then, and the look on his face was the one she had come to recognize as his version of being caught — not embarrassed exactly, just momentarily without his usual defenses. He held it for a second before looking away again.

"Yeah," he said quietly. "We're going out."

Solar looked back at the path ahead of them. She could feel herself smiling and was doing very little to stop it.

"Okay," she said.

"Okay," he echoed.

They sat there for a while after that, neither of them saying much, the Saturday morning moving quietly around them. At some point his shoulder had ended up pressed against hers, and neither of them moved away. Solar thought about the dinner table last night — her father's eyes on her, her mother's small meaningless smile, the version of herself she knew how to be in that house — and then she thought about this, the park and the cold air and Sunwoo's shoulder warm against hers, and the distance between those two things felt enormous.

She didn't mind the distance, not right now. Right now it just felt like a secret worth keeping.

"You laughed," she said, about twenty minutes later, out of nowhere.

He turned to look at her. "What?"

"Yesterday, when I said the thing about the pigeons." She said it like she was presenting evidence. "I didn't know you did that."

The look on his face was difficult to read, which was normal. But underneath the blankness there was something that might have been embarrassment. "I laugh," he said.

"I've never heard it before."

"I laugh," he repeated, more firmly.

"Okay," she said, smiling now and not hiding it. "Prove it."

He stared at her. "I'm not going to perform laughing for you, Solar."

"I'm not asking you to perform it, I'm just saying I've never—"

"I know what you're saying."

"So?"

"So, I'm not doing it."

She laughed then, at the absolute certainty in his voice, and he watched her do it with that expression she had come to think of as his most unguarded one — like he wasn't sure what to do with the fact that she existed. After a moment, she caught him. "What?"

"Nothing," he said, looking away.

"You were doing the thing."

"I wasn't doing anything."

"The staring thing," she clarified, undeterred. "You do it and then you look away when I catch you and pretend you weren't."

A pause. "I don't do that."

"Sunwoo."

Another pause, longer this time. Then, very quietly, as if admitting to a minor crime: "you're funny sometimes."

She blinked. It wasn't what she was expecting. "...thank you?"

"It's an observation."

"Right." She was smiling again, wider this time, and she didn't bother hiding it at all. "What else don't I know about you?"

It was a light question, the kind she asked all the time, and she expected a light answer — or no answer, which was also very Sunwoo. But something in him shifted slightly at it, something she caught at the edges of his expression before it was gone. He looked back at the path.

"A lot," he said, after a moment. The word was quiet and carried more weight than she knew what to do with at twelve years old.

She looked at the side of his face. He was watching nothing in particular now, jaw set, something moving behind his eyes that he wasn't going to let out. There was a bruise along his jaw she had noticed when she sat down, a few days old at least, the kind of yellow-green that meant it had been worse before. She had filed it away the way she always filed them away, neat and quiet in the back of her mind where she kept the things she didn't know how to ask about yet.

She didn't push. She had learned not to push.

But she reached over and tucked her hand into the crook of his arm the way she had seen older girls do with boys they liked, and after a second he didn't pull away. His posture stayed exactly the same — still, composed, giving nothing away — but she felt the tension in his arm ease the smallest amount, like something had been set down.

A lot, he had said. She already knew. She just didn't know yet how much.

///

She was home by noon, which was the arrangement. In through the side entrance, up the stairs, change out of her coat before anyone saw how cold she'd gotten sitting outside for two hours. She had the routine down by now.

What she didn't have down was Chaerin, who was sitting cross-legged at the top of the stairs eating an apple with the air of someone who had been there for a while.

Solar stopped on the third step, trying to hold her sigh. "Why are you sitting there?"

"I live here," Chaerin said simply, taking another bite.

"Go sit somewhere else."

"I don't want to." Her younger sister's green eyes tracked over her with that nine-year-old sharpness that Solar found deeply unfair. The mischief was evident in her irises, as she finally lets it out, "you went to Chanhee's."

"Sure did."

"You always go to Chanhee's on Saturdays now."

"He's my best friend, Chaerin."

"Mmhm." Another slow, considered bite of the apple. "Your cheeks are really red."

"It's cold outside."

"Chanhee's house is inside, so how are you cold?"

Solar stared at her. Chaerin stared back, serene and unbothered, the apple held aloft. She was nine years old and already the most aggravating person Solar had ever encountered in her life.

"Move," Solar said.

"I'm just sitting here!"

"Chaerin," she was stern, trying to intimidate her in her older sister way, but she already knew nothing like that would ever work on Chaerin Lee.

And that was confirmed by the young girl's smirk, as she repeated back mockingly, "Solar."

A standoff. Solar was older and taller and theoretically in a position of authority here, and none of that mattered even slightly because Chaerin had never once in her life been intimidated by anything Solar did. She just sat there eating her apple with the patience of someone who had nowhere to be and all day to get there.

Solar stepped around her and walked down the hall to her room.

"Say hi to Sunwoo for me!" Chaerin called after her, bright and cheerful and knowing perfectly well what she was doing.

Solar shut her door.

She stood there for a second with her back against it, looking at the ceiling. From somewhere down the hall she could hear Juyeon and Chaerin dissolving into laughter together, which meant Juyeon had been listening the whole time and they had planned the entire thing. She should have been annoyed.

She crossed to her desk, pulled open the back of the bottom drawer, and looked at the small torn corner of worksheet paper she kept there. The little pencil heart on it was smudged at one edge from being handled too many times.

She put it back and closed the drawer and went to do her homework.

The smile on her face stayed there for the rest of the afternoon.




Chapter Six

2008 || NEW YORK

Her father's office was the most serious room in the house.

It wasn't just the dark mahogany paneling that lined every wall from floor to ceiling, or the towering shelves of books that no one ever seemed to actually read, their spines too perfect and uncracked to suggest otherwise. It wasn't even the heavy curtains that swallowed most of the natural light and left the room in a perpetual state of dim, amber-tinted gravity regardless of the hour. It was the feeling of the room — the way it pressed down on you the moment you crossed the threshold, the way it made you acutely aware of your own posture, your own breathing, the precise amount of space your body was taking up. It was a room that had been designed, whether intentionally or not, to make whoever sat across from Jaesang Lee feel small.

Solar sat across from her father and tried not to feel small.

She had been called in without explanation, which was always the part that got to her — the walk down the hall, the closed door, the few seconds of standing outside it before she knocked. She had replayed the last several weeks in her mind the entire way there, searching for something she had done wrong, something that had slipped. Her grades were perfect. She had been careful, meticulous, about Sunwoo. She was almost certain nothing had gotten back to him.

Almost.

"Sit," her father instructed, without looking up from whatever he was reviewing on his desk. She sat, folding her hands in her lap the way she always did in this room, and waited while he finished. Jaesang Lee was not a man who rushed, and everyone around him had learned to adjust their pace accordingly. The only sounds were the faint scratch of his pen against paper and the distant ticking of the clock on the mantle, and Solar kept her eyes forward and her expression neutral and breathed slowly.

When he finally set down his pen and looked at her, his expression was unreadable in the way it usually was — composed and deliberate, giving away nothing until he decided to give it. He studied her for a moment with that particular inventory-taking look she had grown up under, and then he leaned back in his chair.

"What do you know about the company?" he asked.

She blinked, not having expected that. "Lee Industries?"

"Is there another one?" He was sarcastic, all without the humor. There wasn't an ounce of humor in his tone, only snark. A lump formed in her throat, one she couldn't acknowledge.

"No, sir," she answered quickly, straightening. "I know that you built it. That it's one of the most successful businesses in the country. That it employs thousands of people and that — that everything we have comes from it."

He was quiet for a moment, considering her answer with the patience of someone who already knew what he thought and was simply deciding whether she had earned the next part of the conversation. "And what do you think happens to it when I'm no longer running it?"

The question landed strangely. She turned it over carefully, uncertain of the right answer, uncertain of where exactly this was going. "I suppose it would be passed down," she offered, cautiously. "To whoever was most capable of running it."

"Exactly," her father said. "Whoever was most capable." He let the phrase sit between them for a moment before he continued, his voice measured and unhurried. "I've been watching all of you. Your brothers, your sister, and you." His eyes didn't leave her face. "Do you know what I see when I look at you, Solar, that I don't see in them?"

She shook her head slowly, because she genuinely didn't know what he was going to say and she didn't want to guess wrong.

"I see someone who understands what it means to work," he told her plainly. "Someone who doesn't have to be told twice. Someone who walks into a room and pays attention to what's actually happening in it." He paused, letting each sentence land before adding the next. "Your siblings are good children. But you are something different. And I think you already know that."

She did know that. She had always known it, in the quiet way you knew things about yourself that you were never supposed to say out loud — not out of arrogance, just out of fact, the way she knew she was left-handed or that she couldn't sleep without the window cracked. She was different. She had always moved through things differently. She had just never heard her father say it, had never had it handed to her plainly like this, and the feeling it produced in her chest was enormous and warm and a little frightening in how much space it took up.

"I want you to take over Lee Industries," he finishes, not a proposal, but a declaration, a demand for what was to come. "When the time comes, I want it to be you."

For a moment she didn't say anything at all. She looked at her father across the wide expanse of his desk, and understood distantly that she should probably think carefully about her response, that she should weigh it and consider it the way she considered everything. But the warmth in her chest was making it difficult to think in straight lines.

"I — I don't know," she admitted, because it was the truth and she had never been able to lie to him the way she could to everyone else. "I want to. I think I do. I just don't know if I'm ready for something like that."

"You're thirteen," he said, with the faintest trace of something that might have been patience. "You're not supposed to be ready. That's why I'm telling you now — so we can begin." He laced his fingers together on the desk, settled and decided in the way he always was. "I'm arranging a private tutor. You'll begin advanced coursework — economics, business law, financial management. Far beyond what your school currently offers. It will be difficult, and it will require more of your time and focus than you're used to giving."

"I can do it," she said, and meant it, and felt the last of her hesitation dissolve under the simple fact of being chosen — something different, someone who understands — because her father was not a man who said things he didn't mean, and she had spent her whole life learning to survive on whatever small measures of his approval came her way. This was not a small measure. This was everything.

She would do it. She would do it because he believed she could, and because she wanted, more than she had words for yet, to be worthy of that belief.

"Good," he said simply, and reached for his pen.

She was dismissed. She rose from the chair, smoothed the front of her skirt, and walked back to the door. The click of the latch behind her sounded very loud in the quiet of the hallway, and she stood there for a moment alone, her back almost against the door, and let herself exhale. The feeling in her chest was still enormous, still warm, and she pressed her hand briefly against her sternum like she could hold it there and keep it from spilling out before she was ready for it.

She had made it approximately four steps down the hall when Jaehyun's door swung open.

He leaned against the frame with his arms crossed, eyebrows raised, wearing the expression of someone who had been waiting with what he felt was considerable patience. "Well?" he prompted, in a low voice that wouldn't carry downstairs.

"Nothing," she said automatically.

He gave her a look. "Solar, I watched you go in there looking like you were about to be executed and you came out looking like—" he paused, tilting his head, "— I don't actually know what you look like right now, which means it was something."

She glanced toward the staircase to make sure neither of her parents had followed, then looked back at her brother. Before she could say a single word, a door further down the hall flew open and Chaerin appeared in it, already suspicious, her green eyes darting between the two of them with the energy of someone who had been listening through the wall and only just decided to stop pretending she hadn't.

"What happened?" she demanded, in a whisper that was not really a whisper.

"Nothing happened," Solar groaned again, already moving toward her room.

"You're walking weird," Chaerin informed her, falling immediately into step beside her. "You walk like that when something happened."

"I don't walk weird—"

"You do," Jaehyun confirmed from behind them, now also following her down the hall, because apparently she wasn't going to be allowed a single private moment in this house. Juyeon's door opened as they passed it, and he stuck his head out with the bleary expression of someone who had just been woken up from a nap, taking in the procession moving past his room with mild confusion.

"What's going on?" he mumbled.

"Nothing," Solar whines, for the third time.

"Something," Chaerin corrected cheerfully, and grabbed Juyeon by the sleeve to drag him along.

By the time Solar pushed open the door to her own room, all three of her siblings had followed her into it without being invited, which was so entirely normal that she didn't even bother to object. Jaehyun dropped into the chair at her desk, Chaerin threw herself across the foot of the bed, and Juyeon sat on the floor against the wall with his knees up, still blinking the sleep out of his eyes. Solar stood in the middle of all of them and looked around at their expectant faces and sighed.

"Father wants me to take over the company," she blurts, tired of walking around it and knowing they wouldn't let it go. "When I'm older. He's getting me a tutor."

The room was quiet for a moment.

"The company," Jaehyun repeated, carefully.

"Lee Industries," she confirmed.

Another beat of quiet. Then Chaerin sat up slowly, her expression cycling through several things in quick succession before landing on something that was equal parts impressed and indignant. "He just — asked you? Just like that?"

"More or less."

"And you said yes?"

"I said I'd do it."

Chaerin flopped back down dramatically, staring at the ceiling. "Of course you did," she muttered, though without any real edge to it.
