She said it the way she said most things about Solar — with the particular exasperated fondness of a younger sibling who had accepted, more or less, that her older sister was simply going to be like this forever.

Juyeon was frowning at the carpet, turning it over. "Does that mean you have to do more school stuff?"

"A lot more, probably."

He winced sympathetically. "That sounds terrible."

"It doesn't, actually," Solar admitted, and felt the warmth move through her chest again. "I think I want to do it."

Jaehyun was watching her from the desk chair with his arms crossed and his expression doing the quiet, layered thing it sometimes did — like he was thinking several things simultaneously and had decided to say none of them. He held her gaze for a moment, and then something in his face shifted into something softer, something that was just her brother being glad for her even with everything else underneath it.

"Then you'll be good at it," he said simply.

It was such a Jaehyun thing to say — not effusive, not performed, just plain and certain — and Solar felt it settle into her somewhere deep. She looked at him for a second longer than necessary, and then she threw a pillow at Chaerin because she was still lying across the foot of her bed without permission, and Chaerin shrieked and threw it back, and Juyeon immediately declared himself on Chaerin's side because he always did, and just like that the moment folded itself back into the ordinary noise of the four of them.

///

The announcement happened three evenings later, after dinner, when Solji asked them all to come to the sitting room.

This was unusual enough that even Chaerin behaved on the walk there, which was always the clearest possible signal that something significant was happening. They arranged themselves in their natural formation — Jaehyun in the armchair by the door, Solar on the smaller sofa, Chaerin and Juyeon claiming the larger one with the particular sprawling disregard for personal space that seemed to be the exclusive domain of ten-year-olds. Juyeon had his legs dangling over the arm. Chaerin had already stolen one of the decorative cushions and was hugging it to her chest. Solar sat with her ankles crossed and her hands in her lap, watching her parents near the fireplace.

Her mother was containing something. Solar could see it in the brightness of her eyes, in the way she kept glancing at Jaesang beside her with the suppressed energy of someone who had been holding a secret for longer than was comfortable. Her father stood with his hands clasped behind his back, composed as ever, giving nothing away — but he was standing, which meant it mattered.

"We wanted to tell you all together," Solji began, her voice warmer than its usual register, animated in a way Solar didn't often see from her. "You're going to have a new sibling."

For one perfect second, the sitting room was completely silent.

Then Chaerin's cushion hit the floor.

"WHAT," she exclaimed, sitting bolt upright, her green eyes going wide with an outrage so immediate and thorough it was almost impressive in its completeness. "Another one?! There are already four of us, where are they even supposed to go?"

"Chaerin—" her mother began, startled into laughing.

"I'm serious!" Chaerin insisted, gesturing broadly at herself and Juyeon as if presenting evidence. "We are already a lot of people! This house is already very loud!"

"You are why this house is very loud," Jaehyun remarked from his armchair, in the exact tone of someone making a neutral observation.

"That is so—" Chaerin pointed at him furiously, "— that is so unfair, Jae, you're always—"

"A little sister," their mother interjected warmly, over the noise of Chaerin's outrage, "we think."

"A SISTER," Chaerin repeated, in the voice of someone receiving genuinely catastrophic news. "There are already two of us, why does there need to be—"

Beside her, Juyeon had gone very quiet in the way he went quiet when something had genuinely surprised him, his brow furrowed and his mouth slightly open, working through the information in real time. "So," he murmured, mostly to himself, staring at the middle distance, "there's going to be another one." He said it again, slowly, as if repetition might help. "Another one."

"Yes, Juyeon," Solar said, pressing her lips together to keep from laughing at the look on his face.

"But there's already — we're already—" He counted something on his fingers, apparently, and then looked up with the expression of a person who had arrived at a number he found concerning. "Five," he announced. "There are going to be five of us."

"Groundbreaking math," Jaehyun murmured.

"I'm just saying," Juyeon said, with great dignity.

Solar was laughing now, properly, because she couldn't help it — the absolute chaos of her two youngest siblings processing this in their entirely different ways was genuinely one of the funniest things she had witnessed in recent memory, and even her mother was still laughing softly near the fireplace, her composure having slipped entirely. Chaerin had progressed from outrage into a kind of theatrical despair, leaning sideways against Juyeon's shoulder with the air of someone who had accepted their fate but wanted it noted that they were not happy about it.

"I just think," Chaerin announced to the room at large, in the mournful tone of someone delivering a eulogy, "that nobody asked us."

"We're asking you now," Solar pointed out.

"That's not the same."

"Chaerin, sweetheart," their mother said, in the gently amused voice she used when she was trying to be firm but finding it difficult, "this is happy news."

Chaerin considered this. "For who," she muttered, but quietly enough that only Solar heard it, which meant she had made her peace with it, more or less, and was now simply registering her dissatisfaction for the record.

Solar looked across the room at Jaehyun. He was smiling — the real one, warm and genuine, and she believed it entirely — but it was smaller than she might have expected, and it didn't quite reach all the way. His gaze had moved to their father, who was standing beside Solji with an expression of composed satisfaction, surveying the room with the quiet pleasure of a man watching something he had arranged unfold correctly. Jaehyun watched him for just a moment too long, something careful and knowing moving through his expression before he seemed to decide something and let it go.

He looked at Solar then, and his face smoothed back into something easier, his eyebrows lifting slightly in the direction of Chaerin and Juyeon as if to say, well. Here we are.

She smiled back. She believed his happiness — she did — she just also believed the other thing underneath it, the thing he wasn't going to say.

"Can we name it?" Juyeon asked suddenly, having apparently moved through his processing and arrived somewhere more practical.

"No," their father said.

"What if it's actually a girl and we just—"

"No."

"What if we each suggested one and then you picked from—"

"Juyeon."

Solar reached over and pulled her ridiculous little brother sideways by the arm, and he crashed into her with a groan of protest, trying immediately to escape, and Chaerin used the disruption as an opportunity to launch herself dramatically across both of them, still sighing about the injustice of a fifth sibling to anyone who would listen. Solar sat there in the warm, loud, overcrowded sitting room with an elbow in her ribs and Chaerin's hair in her face and Juyeon trying to pry himself free, and laughed until her sides ached.

Across the room, her father watched his family with the quiet satisfaction of a man surveying something he had built, and her mother beamed at all of them with that particular warmth of hers that never went very deep but was genuine in its way, and Solar thought, not for the first time and not for the last, that from the outside they must look exactly like what they were supposed to be.

///

The days had gotten longer, the last of the winter cold finally releasing its grip on the city, and at recess the east yard of the school smelled like wet grass and something almost like spring.

Kevin wanted to play.

This was not a surprise — Kevin always wanted to play, had never once in his life looked at a stretch of open yard and thought anything other than yes, immediately — but the enthusiasm with which he was currently trying to recruit the rest of them was notable even by his standards. He had already gotten two reluctant agreements out of Jacob, who would agree to almost anything if asked nicely enough, and was now standing in front of the rest of them with his arms spread wide, looking genuinely baffled by the resistance he was encountering.

"It's a nice day," he implored, gesturing at the sky as evidence. "Look at it. It's a great day. Why are we just standing here?"

"Because we're tired," Chanhee informed him, from where he was leaning against the wall with his arms crossed and his eyes half-closed.

"We're thirteen," Kevin said. "We're not tired, we're thirteen."

"I'm tired," Changmin said quietly, from beside Chanhee, not looking up from the book he had open on his knee.

"Sunwoo," Kevin tried, turning hopefully.

Sunwoo looked at him with an expression that communicated, without a single word, that this was not going to happen.

Kevin turned to Solar as his last viable option. She gave him an apologetic look, because she genuinely did feel bad about it, and he threw his hands up and went to go bother Jacob into actually following through on his agreement, muttering something about how nobody appreciated a nice day anymore.

"He's not wrong," Solar conceded, watching him go.

"He's a little wrong," Chanhee said.

She smiled, and then drifted, the way she had been drifting all week, back to the thing she had been turning over since her father's office. She had told her siblings. She hadn't told anyone else yet, not even Chanhee, because it still felt tender in a way she couldn't entirely explain — too new, too large, like something she needed to carry carefully for a while before she let anyone else look at it.

But she was standing next to Sunwoo, and his shoulder was close to hers in the way it always ended up being when they were standing next to each other, and she had been wanting to tell him since it happened.

"My father called me into his office," she started, and felt him go slightly more attentive beside her, the particular quality of his stillness shifting. "He wants me to take over the company. Lee Industries. He says he's going to get me a tutor, start me on advanced coursework — business, economics, all of it."

Sunwoo was quiet for a moment. "What did you say?"

"I said I'd do it." She paused. "I think I want to. I think it's — I mean, it's a lot. But he said—" She stopped, feeling faintly self-conscious about repeating it, about how much it had meant to her. "He said he sees something in me that he doesn't see in the others. That I'm different."

Sunwoo didn't say anything immediately. She glanced at him sideways and found him looking at the yard, his expression doing the neutral, unreadable thing, but she had learned enough of his face by now to see the something moving underneath it.

"Does your father ever — I mean, your parents own a company too," she ventured, carefully. "Is there ever any pressure from them? About what you're going to do?"

Something shifted in his jaw, almost imperceptibly. "Yeah," he said, after a moment. "My dad has been — he's been training me. Getting me ready for things."

"Training," she repeated, her eyes dropping briefly to his arms, where his sleeves were pushed up in the warmth of the afternoon, a faded bruise visible at his wrist that she had already noticed and already filed away. "Like the boxing."

"Like the boxing," he agreed, evenly, meeting her eyes for just a second before looking away again.

She held the word in her mouth for a moment, tasting the shape of it, the way it didn't quite fit the bruises and never had. She looked at him — at the composed set of his shoulders, the deliberate calm of his face, the way he answered her without answering her — and felt the familiar pull of wanting to push further, wanting to ask the thing she had been circling around for two years.

She didn't.

"I guess we're both being prepared for something," she said instead, quietly.

He looked at her then, and something in his expression went briefly and entirely unguarded — something that looked almost like relief, or maybe gratitude, or maybe just the particular feeling of being understood by someone without having to explain yourself. It was gone in a second, folded back behind the blankness, but she had seen it.

"Yeah," he said. "I guess we are."

Across the yard, Kevin had successfully gotten a game going and was now yelling for Chanhee to join, which Chanhee was refusing with the particular energy of someone who had made a decision and intended to stick to it. Changmin had closed his book and was watching them with the small, quiet smile he sometimes got when the people around him were being ridiculous. The sun was out properly now, warm on her face, and Solar stood beside Sunwoo in the easy comfortable silence they had built between them over years of practice, and let herself just be there for a moment.

She didn't know yet what he was being prepared for. She didn't know yet what it would cost either of them.

Right now it was just a nice day, and they were just two kids standing in the sun, and that was enough.




Chapter Seven

2008 || NEW YORK

The Kim mansion was not a home in the way the Lee mansion was a home.

It was large — larger, in some technical sense of square footage, than most of the residences in the Castle — and it was expensive in the way that everything Sihyuk Kim owned was expensive, which was to say deliberately and without warmth. There were no family photographs on the walls. There was no fire burning in the sitting room. The furniture was dark and angular and arranged with the precision of a showroom rather than a place where people actually lived, and the staff — of which there were several, all of whom had learned early that the correct posture in this house was invisibility — moved through it quietly and without expression, like figures in the background of someone else's painting.

Sunwoo had grown up inside these walls and had never once thought of them as home. He wasn't sure he had a word for what they were. Just the place where he slept. Just the place he returned to, every time, because there was nowhere else to go.

He was in the east corridor when one of the staff found him — a woman whose name he had never been asked to learn, who communicated exclusively through slight variations in posture — and told him, with a small incline of her head, that his father was waiting for him in the study.

He had gone without being told twice. That was one of the first things his father had taught him.

///

The study was darker than the rest of the house, if such a thing was possible. Sihyuk Kim was seated behind his desk when Sunwoo entered — not the posture of a man who had been waiting, but the posture of a man who had simply been occupied with other things and had now chosen to redirect his attention. He did not look up immediately. He let his son stand there in the middle of the room for a measured moment before he set down whatever he had been reading and folded his hands on the desk in front of him.

Sihyuk Kim was not a large man, but he occupied space in the way that dangerous things occupied space — not through size, but through the absolute certainty that nothing in the room would move without his permission. He had sharp, dark eyes that assessed everything they landed on with the same flat efficiency, and he had never, in Sunwoo's entire life, raised his voice. He had never needed to.

"Sit down," he commanded.

Sunwoo sat.

His father looked at him for a moment, the way he sometimes did — not with warmth, never with warmth, but with the particular attentiveness of someone taking inventory of an asset and deciding whether it was performing to specification. Then he said, simply, without preamble:

"Your mother is gone. She won't be coming back."

The words entered the room and settled there. Sunwoo looked at his father's face and found nothing in it — no grief, no discomfort, not even the performance of either. Just the flat delivery of a fact, the same tone he used for everything, as though he were reporting a shift in weather.

"She decided," his father continued, "that this life was not for her. Some people," he added, with a slight pause that managed to contain a world's worth of contempt, "are not built for what is required of them."

Sunwoo said nothing. He was thirteen years old and sitting in a chair in his father's study and his mother was gone and his father was looking at him the way he looked at spreadsheets, and somewhere very deep and very quiet inside him something was happening that he had no language for yet. It wasn't grief exactly, or maybe it was, but it was moving through him so slowly and so quietly that it almost didn't feel like anything at all. Like a door closing somewhere in a room far away.

He thought about her hands. He wasn't sure why — just her hands, the way they had looked at the dinner table, the faint tension she could never quite get out of them even when she was smiling. He thought about the way she had smiled that night at the Lee house, when she was talking to Solar's parents and the smile was very careful and very bright and never reached her eyes.

He let himself think about it for exactly as long as he could afford to, and then he put it somewhere he couldn't easily reach, and he looked back at his father and kept his face very still.

"Okay," he said.

Something shifted almost imperceptibly in his father's expression — satisfaction, perhaps, or confirmation. The inventory check, passed.

"Good," Sihyuk said. "Then we can focus on more important things."

///

He was called back to the study three days later.

The house had been the same in the interim — the staff moving quietly through it, the rooms as cold and precisely arranged as always, no evidence that anything had changed. And maybe nothing had, Sunwoo thought. Maybe this was always what the house had been, and the difference was just that he could see it now more clearly than he had before.

He sat in the same chair. His father stood this time, at the window, looking out at the grounds below with his hands clasped behind his back.

"You're thirteen," his father began, without turning around. "Older than I was when I started understanding what this family requires." A pause. "I've been preparing you for a long time. Your training, your discipline — these weren't accidents. They were investments." He turned then, and looked at his son with those flat, assessing eyes. "It's time to tell you what they were investments in."

Sunwoo waited.

"I need you to build something," his father explains. "A unit. A group of people who work under you, who answer to you, who are capable of doing what needs to be done when the company requires it. Enforcement. Discretion. Loyalty." He moved away from the window and settled into the chair across from Sunwoo rather than behind the desk, which was unusual enough to register. "You understand what I'm telling you?"

"You want me to run a gang," Sunwoo monotones, flatly.

His father's expression didn't change. "I want you to build a team," he said, with a slight emphasis that suggested the distinction mattered to him, at least in terms of how things were framed. "People you find yourself. People who have something to prove, who have nowhere better to be, who will be loyal because you gave them something worth being loyal to." He leaned forward slightly. "You're not going to find them in this school, among those children. You're going to find them the way I found my people — by looking for the ones who already know what hard looks like."

Sunwoo thought about this. "How am I supposed to manage something like that," he asked, "at thirteen?"

"You're not supposed to manage it at thirteen," his father said, without any particular patience. "You're supposed to start building it at thirteen. There's a difference." He rose from the chair, signaling that the conversation was concluding. "Your training intensifies from this point. The physical work you already know. I'm adding other things — surveillance, tactics, how to move without being seen. You'll need to know all of it before the unit is operational." He moved back toward the desk. "Find the first one. Bring them here. We'll assess from there."

Sunwoo looked at his father's back for a moment.

"What happened to her?" he deadpans the question, though it didn't sound like much of a question. He already knew, he just wanted to hear him say it himself, to confirm the reality his gut was already informing him of. 

The pause that followed was very short, barely a pause at all. His father didn't turn around.

"I already told you," he hums, as if this was nothing to him, as if it was a nuisance that he would ask such a thing. "She decided this wasn't for her."

He picked up the document he had been reading before, which meant the conversation was over. Sunwoo rose from the chair, smoothed the front of his jacket out of habit, and walked back to the door. He closed it quietly behind him, the way he had been taught.

He stood in the corridor for a moment in the expensive, silent house, with the staff moving invisibly around him, and looked at the wall across from him, and understood something he had probably known for a long time but hadn't yet been willing to name.

He filed it away. He was good at filing things away.

Then he went upstairs to train.

///

He heard about the boy at school, which was how he heard about most things — not through anyone talking to him, since very few people did, but through the particular quality of sound that a hushed conversation produced in a hallway, the way it carried differently from normal noise. He had learned, early on, to pick those frequencies out of the ambient chaos of a school day the way you might pick a single instrument out of an orchestra.

Two boys near the lockers. A name he didn't recognize — Sangyeon — and enough context to fill in the rest. The grade above. The parents. The kind of thing that people whispered about with the guilty excitement of those who found other people's suffering interesting.

Sunwoo didn't find it interesting. He found it useful, which was a different thing entirely.

He found the boy by the end of the school day without much difficulty — he had a name and a general description and a habit of paying attention that most people underestimated, which was one of his more practical assets. Sangyeon was easy enough to locate: older, broad through the shoulders already in the way that some kids grew before the rest of them, moving through the end-of-day crowds with his head down and the specific posture of someone who had learned that being noticed was rarely beneficial.

Sunwoo followed him at a distance that was close enough to track and far enough not to register. This was something his father had drilled into him until it was reflex — how to follow, how to watch, how to know when the moment was and when it wasn't. He was, he supposed, reasonably good at it by now.

The neighborhood changed around them as they walked, the way neighborhoods did when you moved far enough from the Castle — the buildings getting quieter, older, less maintained, the streets narrowing. The house, when Sangyeon finally reached it, was the kind of house that looked like it had been giving up for a long time and had simply never quite finished the process. One of the upper windows was boarded over with plywood. The front step had a crack running diagonally across it that no one had ever gotten around to filling.

Sunwoo stopped at the corner across the street and waited.

He was good at waiting, too.

Sangyeon went inside. The door closed. From somewhere within the house, muffled and indistinct, there was the sound of a voice — a single voice, raised — and then quiet again. Sunwoo stood at the corner with his hands in his jacket pockets and watched the front of the house and kept very still, the way he had learned to keep still in circumstances that required patience.

Forty minutes passed.

The door opened again.

Sangyeon came out slowly, the way someone came out of a place when they weren't sure what was waiting for them on the other side of it. He lowered himself onto the front step and sat with his forearms resting on his knees, and Sunwoo could see, even across the street in the fading afternoon light, the cuts on his arms — not one or two, but the accumulated evidence of something ongoing, glass by the look of it, the kind of marks that came from proximity to things that were being broken in anger.

He sat there for a moment with his head bowed, and his shoulders were doing something that, on anyone else, Sunwoo might have called crying. On Sangyeon it was smaller than that, more controlled, like someone who had learned to keep even their grief quiet.

Sunwoo crossed the street.

He didn't try to be subtle about his approach — subtle approaches, he had found, tended to alarm people more than direct ones, and the last thing he needed was for the boy to bolt. He walked at a normal pace, crossed the cracked front path, and stopped a few feet from the step. Sangyeon had heard him coming and had already gone still and watchful by the time Sunwoo came to a stop, his eyes tracking upward with the particular wariness of someone who had learned that strangers appearing unannounced was not usually a good development.

They looked at each other for a moment.

Sunwoo had thought, on the walk over, about how to open this. He had considered several approaches and discarded all of them, because none of them were honest, and dishonesty at this stage seemed both unnecessary and counterproductive. His father had told him to find people who were loyal. Loyalty, in Sunwoo's limited but carefully observed experience, did not typically begin with a lie.

"I know somewhere better than this," he said.

Sangyeon's eyes narrowed slightly. His voice, when it came, was rougher than Sunwoo expected, careful and flat. "You don't know me."

"Sure don't," Sunwoo agreed.

A silence settled between them. Down the street, a car moved slowly past, its headlights already on in the early dark. Sangyeon looked at him with the expression of someone running a calculation they had learned, through experience, to run on anything that looked too much like an offer.

"What's the catch?" he muttered, his eyes not leaving Sunwoo's face.

Sunwoo considered lying. It would have been simpler. He was good enough at it by now that Sangyeon probably wouldn't have known. But he had looked at this boy on that step and recognized something that he didn't have a word for yet, something that had to do with the way they had both arrived at thirteen years old already carrying things that were too heavy, and he found he didn't want to start it with a lie.

"There is one," he admits, truthfully. "But it's better than this."

The silence this time was longer. Sangyeon looked down at his own hands, then back up at the boy standing in front of him — this quiet, blank-faced kid who had appeared from nowhere and was offering him something in the most straightforward and terrifying way possible. And Sunwoo watched him weigh the known thing against the unknown one, watched the calculation work its way across his face, and waited.

Sangyeon stood up from the step. "Okay," he decides suddenly, "I'll follow you, then." He motioned for Sunwoo to go wherever this 'place' was that he was talking about. Because he was right, anything was better than this. 

Sunwoo nodded. He turned, and heard the footsteps fall in behind him, and they walked back toward the Castle without speaking. He didn't look back at the house. He had learned not to look back at things, and he had found, lately, that there were more and more things worth not looking back at.

///

It took three weeks to understand what Sangyeon was actually capable of.

He brought him to the Castle — to a part of the property that the staff didn't frequent and that his father, occupied with other things, didn't pay close attention to — and he started with the basics, the same things that had been drilled into his own body since he was young enough that it was simply the texture of his childhood. How to stand. How to fall. How to take a hit without losing your feet.

Sangyeon was a quick learner. More than that — he was the kind of person who took instruction seriously, who paid attention with his whole body, and who, once he had been shown something, did not need to be shown it twice. The broad shoulders that had seemed purely physical turned out to be the outer sign of something more fundamental, a kind of physical intelligence, a body that understood weight and balance and leverage in a way that most people's didn't.

He also didn't ask unnecessary questions. Sunwoo appreciated this more than he would have expected to.

In the evenings, in the small room that served as something close to an office in the Castle property's secondary building — far enough from the main house that Sihyuk was unlikely to wander through — Sunwoo opened a laptop and enrolled Sangyeon in the first of the online courses. Coding, to start. Then systems architecture. Then, once the foundation was there, the more specific things that would eventually be necessary.

Sangyeon looked at the screen with the expression of someone who had been handed something they didn't expect and weren't sure they deserved. He didn't say thank you in a way that was effusive or uncomfortable. He just looked at it for a moment, and then he looked at Sunwoo, and whatever was in his expression was something Sunwoo recognized as genuine without being able to name it further.

"I'll be good at this," Sangyeon assures, quietly. Not a boast. A statement of intent. "It's easy enough."

"I know," Sunwoo said, because he already did.

He closed the laptop and looked at the wall across from him in the dim room and thought about what his father had said — find the ones who already know what hard looks like. He thought about the front step and the cuts on the arms and the controlled, quiet grief that Sangyeon had been sitting with alone in the fading afternoon light.

He thought about his mother's hands and then he stopped thinking about them, the way he always did, the way he was getting better and better at.

Outside, the Castle was quiet and expensive and sealed behind its walls, and somewhere across the grounds the Lee mansion burned with warm light in every window.

He looked at it for a moment before he turned away.




Chapter Eight

2009 || NEW YORK

The problem with math competitions was that they required you to be good at math.

Solar had been staring at the same problem for forty minutes. She knew this because she had looked at the clock when she sat down and she had looked at it again just now and the numbers had not improved in the interim, which was more than she could say for her understanding of the problem in front of her. The worksheet was for a regional mathematics competition that her father had entered her into without asking, which was how most things in her life were entered into at this point, and it was sitting on her desk looking back at her with the smug confidence of something that knew it had won.

She put her pencil down. She picked it back up. She looked at the problem again.

The problem did not become clearer.

"You're going to break that pencil," Jaehyun said from her doorway.

She looked up. He was leaning against the frame with his arms crossed, watching her with the particular expression he wore when he had been observing a situation for longer than he was going to admit. She had not heard him come down the hall, which meant she had been more absorbed in her own frustration than she realized.

"I'm fine," she said.

"You've been in here for two hours."

"I'm aware."

He pushed off the doorframe and wandered in, uninvited, which was standard. Dropping onto the edge of her bed, he leaned over to look at the worksheet from a distance and his expression shifted into something that was equal parts sympathy and the faintest trace of relief that it wasn't him. "How bad is it?"

"It's for high schoolers, Jae," she muttered, dropping her head into her hands. "I'm fourteen. I don't know why father thinks I can just — " she gestured at the paper, "— do this."

"Because you've never not been able to do something he asked you to do," Jaehyun said, simply, which was both kind and not kind at the same time, in the way that true things sometimes were.

She groaned into her palms.

"Go find Sunwoo," he said, after a moment.

She lifted her head and looked at him. He was already getting up from the bed, examining the ceiling with the elaborate casualness of someone who had definitely not just said what he said.

"I don't know what you're talking about," she told him.

"Sure," he said, and walked back out into the hall, pulling her door halfway shut behind him.

She looked at the worksheet for another ten seconds. Then she texted Sunwoo.

///

He was already at the park when she got there, sitting on their bench with his hands in his jacket pockets, watching nothing in particular with the patient blankness he brought to waiting. She had always found it slightly unnerving, how still he could be — most people fidgeted, checked their phones, manufactured some activity to fill the time. Sunwoo simply existed in it, like waiting was just another state of being rather than something to be endured.

He glanced at her as she dropped onto the bench beside him, and she could tell from the quality of that glance that he had already assessed she was frustrated before she said a word.

She held up the worksheet.

He took it from her without comment, looked it over, and handed it back.

"It's not that bad," he said.

"It's for high schoolers."

"So?"

"So I'm in eighth grade."

He looked at her with the expression he wore when he found her reasoning unpersuasive. Then he reached over and took the worksheet back, along with the pencil she was holding, and set it on his knee. "Come here," he said, which was as close as Sunwoo got to let me show you.

She shifted closer so the worksheet was between them, their shoulders pressing together in the familiar way they had settled into over months of sitting on this bench. He tapped the pencil against the first problem.

"You're trying to solve the whole thing at once," he said. "Break it down. What's the first operation?"

She looked at it. "Order of operations — parentheses first."

"So do that part."

She did. He waited, not impatiently, just present, the pencil loose in his fingers.

"Okay," she muttered, "that gives me — " she worked through it, "forty-eight."

"Right. Now what?"

She kept going, slower than she wanted to, feeling his attention beside her like something warm and steady. He didn't take the pencil back or correct her, just watched and let her find her way through it, only speaking when she got stuck and then only to ask a question rather than give an answer. By the third problem she had stopped feeling like the worksheet was something happening to her and started feeling like something she was doing, which was a different thing entirely.

"How do you know this," she said, at the end of the fourth problem, looking at him sideways. "This is high school level."

He shrugged. "I'm good at math."

"Sunwoo, this is beyond being good at math. This is " — she tapped the paper — "this is things people study for years."

He looked at the worksheet rather than at her, and something in his expression went briefly elsewhere, the way it sometimes did when a conversation touched something he wasn't going to explain. "My father had me tutored," he said, after a moment. "Started young."

She held his gaze for a second. Another piece of the puzzle that she was slowly, quietly assembling, the one she had no name for yet. She let it go, the way she had learned to let things go with him.

"Well," she said, looking back at the worksheet, "you're better at it than my actual tutor, so."

Something at the corner of his mouth moved. Not quite a smile. Close enough.

He reached over and drew a small heart in the margin of the worksheet, right next to the last problem she had solved. She looked at it for a moment.

"That's on my competition sheet," she informed him.

"I know," he said, and handed her the pencil back.

///

Spring arrived in the way it always did in New York — abruptly, as if the city had simply decided it was done with winter and moved on without consulting anyone. The trees along the Castle's east path went from bare to tentatively green in what felt like a matter of days, and the light changed, and Solar found herself spending more time at the park bench and less time staring at worksheets, which her tutor would have had opinions about if she had told him.

She didn't tell him.

The problem was the time. Or rather, the lack of it — the way her calendar had started to look less like a calendar and more like a document of everything she was supposed to be doing at every hour of every day. Math competitions, yes, but also a young executives program her father had enrolled her in on Saturday mornings, and an economics lecture series at Columbia that was technically optional and was not optional, and the increasing expectation that she would appear at company events with her father on certain evenings, standing beside him in something appropriate and being introduced to people she would forget by morning.

She was fourteen years old. She was tired in a way she didn't have language for yet.

Sunwoo was the one place where the calendar didn't follow her. But even that was getting harder.

He would cancel on her sometimes — not often, but enough that she noticed — with the same brief explanation every time. My father needs me. He never elaborated. She had stopped asking him to because she could tell, from the particular way he said it, that asking wouldn't produce an answer, and she didn't want to spend their limited time pushing at walls.

What she had started to notice instead were the small things he did when they were together, the things he seemed to think were invisible. The way he would arrive at the bench with a drink already in hand for her — the specific brand of iced tea she had mentioned once, weeks ago, in passing, in the context of something else entirely. The way he'd shown up one afternoon with a small bag of the pretzels she'd offhandedly called her favorite during a conversation she was almost certain had been about something completely different. He never said anything about it. He just had them, and he'd hand them over, and he'd look somewhere else while she figured out what to say.

She had figured out, over time, that saying nothing was the correct response. Acknowledging it directly seemed to make him uncomfortable in the way that being caught at anything made him uncomfortable, like he'd rather she just accepted the thing without making it a moment. So she did. She accepted the thing, and she stored it, and she understood it for what it was.

"You remembered," she'd said once, when she genuinely couldn't help it, looking down at the iced tea.

"You mentioned it," he'd said, which was technically a non-answer and also, she had come to understand, the closest he got to of course I remembered, I remember everything you say.

She thought about that a lot.

///

Yein came home on a Wednesday.

Solar had been in the middle of the economics lecture series when her mother's name appeared on her phone screen, and she had slipped out of the auditorium before she'd even registered what she was doing, standing in the corridor outside with her heart doing something complicated in her chest. The car came for her forty minutes later, and by the time she walked through the front door of the Lee mansion it was full in a way it hadn't been that morning — fuller than she'd expected, noisier, the particular quality of chaos that a new arrival generates in a house that has been waiting for it.

Yein was very small. This was Solar's first coherent thought upon seeing her — not a complex or sophisticated thought, but an overwhelming one. She was small and pink and making a face of profound displeasure at the general situation she had found herself in, and Solar looked at her in her mother's arms and felt something enormous and uncomplicated move through her chest.

"She looks angry," Chaerin announced, peering over their mother's shoulder.

"She just got here," Jaehyun said.

"I'm just saying, that's an angry face for a baby."

"Chaerin," their mother murmured, warmly, exhausted.

Juyeon had been uncharacteristically quiet since Solar arrived, standing slightly back from the group with his arms crossed and his brow furrowed in the concentrated way he got when he was genuinely trying to figure something out. Solar glanced at him. He noticed.

"She's really small," he said, quietly, as if this were a concern he was registering for the record.

"She's a newborn, Juyeon," Solar said.

"I know, but — " he frowned at Yein, who continued to be very small and very pink and entirely indifferent to the opinions of her siblings, "she just seems — I don't know. Fragile."

Something about the word caught Solar in the chest. She looked back at Yein — at the small fist curled against her mother's shoulder, the face slowly moving from displeasure toward something more uncertain — and thought, in a way she couldn't have fully articulated, that Juyeon wasn't wrong.

"Come here," her mother said, looking at Solar, and she moved forward without thinking, and her mother transferred Yein into her arms with the practiced ease of someone who had done this before, and Solar stood there holding her youngest sister for the first time and felt the fragile, specific weight of her and forgot entirely, for a few minutes, about the math competitions and the lecture series and the company events and every other thing that had been pressing down on her since the morning.

Yein blinked up at her. Her eyes were unfocused in the way of brand new things.

"Hi," Solar said, quietly, to her. "I'm Solar. I'm the one who's going to let you get away with things."

Jaehyun made a noise of protest from behind her. Chaerin immediately said she was also going to let her get away with things. Juyeon, after a moment of consideration, announced that he was going to teach her things, which provoked a separate and sustained argument about what kinds of things, and Solar stood in the middle of all of it with Yein in her arms and laughed.

///

The Grand Meridian's private ballroom had a particular quality in the evening — the chandeliers turned down to something warm and amber, the tables set with the precision of people who had been doing this for decades, the whole room arranged to suggest that wealth was effortless, which was itself one of the most expensive illusions money could buy. Solar had been coming to these events her whole life and she still felt it every time she walked in — the way the room pressed a particular version of you into existence the moment you crossed the threshold, the way it expected things of you before you had opened your mouth.

She was being introduced differently this year.

Her father had her beside him from the moment they arrived — not trailing at the family's periphery the way she had in previous years, not simply present as one of Jaesang Lee's children, but positioned. Deliberate. Introduced to the men in dark suits and the women in structured dresses as Solar, my eldest, she'll be the one running things one day with the casual certainty of someone laying groundwork that he expected to hold for decades. She smiled and shook hands and said the right things, and she felt the weight of it settle across her shoulders with the specific, growing heaviness of something that was becoming real rather than hypothetical.

The men her father introduced her to all responded the same way — warmly, appraisingly, with the particular interest of people who were recalibrating their understanding of something they thought they already knew. She was fourteen years old and they were looking at her like she was already someone. It was both thrilling and faintly terrifying, and she kept her face at the precise midpoint between the two.

Her mother was luminous beside her father, warm and polished and in her element in a way Solar hadn't quite seen from her before. The Foundation announcement was recent enough that several people sought Solji out specifically to congratulate her on it, and she received them with gracious, unhurried pleasure — the first dinner is in the spring, I do hope you'll come, it's going to be something special — and Solar watched her and understood, perhaps for the first time, that her mother was not simply her father's complement in this room. She was building something of her own, staking out a corner of the Castle's social world that belonged entirely to her, and the room was responding to it. Solji Lee, it turned out, was quietly formidable in ways Solar had never fully seen from inside the house.

She filed that away, too.

Jaehyun was nearby in the way he was always nearby at these events — present, watchful, saying very little, somehow managing to be both approachable and subtly unavailable at the same time. Chaerin and Juyeon had been deposited near their mother's orbit early in the evening, which was where they tended to stay until they found an excuse not to. Yein was home with a sitter, too new for events like this, and Solar found herself thinking of her occasionally in the way you thought about something warm when you were standing somewhere cold.

She found her friends in the second half of the first hour, which was when her father's attention had been sufficiently claimed by other conversations that she could breathe normally again. Chanhee appeared at her elbow with the expression of a person enduring a prolonged and unnecessary inconvenience — arms half-crossed, jacket impeccable, gaze moving around the room with the resigned assessment of someone cataloguing exactly how long they would have to be here before it was acceptable to stop performing.

"I've spoken to eleven adults tonight," he informed her, by way of greeting. "Eleven. Every single one of them asked me about my grades."

"Your grades are excellent," Solar said.

"That's not the point." He reached for a glass from a passing tray with the practiced ease of someone who had been doing this since childhood. "Jacob is over there being charming at his father again, which, good for him, I genuinely don't know how he does it."

Solar looked. Jacob was indeed beside Henry Bae, listening to something with his full, patient attention and responding with the quiet ease that was simply how Jacob moved through the world — present without being performative, warm without being excessive. He caught Solar's eye across the room and gave her a small wave, which Henry Bae didn't notice, and she waved back.

"Where's Kevin," she said.

Chanhee's expression shifted into something pained. He nodded toward the far end of the room.

Kevin was in animated conversation with an elderly woman in a silver dress who appeared to be genuinely delighted by whatever he was saying, her hand pressed to her pearls, laughing in a way that suggested she had not laughed like that in some time. Kevin was leaning slightly forward with his hands moving as he talked, completely absorbed in whatever the conversation was, entirely unbothered by the fact that he was fourteen years old at a formal Castle event and had apparently decided that the most important thing he could be doing was entertaining someone's grandmother.

"He found her ten minutes ago," Chanhee said. "He's been over there ever since. I don't know what they're talking about. I don't want to know."

Solar pressed her lips together to keep from smiling too widely. "He's doing great."

"He's a liability."

She was still smiling when she turned to look for Changmin, and found him near the back of the room with his mother, Eunbi Ji, who was a quiet and elegant woman that the Castle treated with the careful, slightly guilty deference of people who knew the circumstances and had never quite known what to do with them. Changmin was beside her with the composed stillness he had developed over the past few years — not Sunwoo's blankness, which was armor, but something softer, a kind of deliberate peacefulness, like he had made a decision about how to carry the things he was carrying and had committed to it. He caught Solar's eye and gave her the small, familiar smile that meant I'm alright, you don't have to check.

She held his gaze for a moment longer than the smile strictly required, and he understood it, and his smile shifted slightly in acknowledgment before they both looked elsewhere.

She believed him, mostly.

It was midway through the second hour, when the room had settled into the looser, more conversational rhythm that followed the formal introductions, that she felt the shift in the room's atmosphere before she identified the cause of it.

Sihyuk Kim entered the ballroom the way he entered every room — without announcement, without warmth, his presence registering like a change in air pressure rather than anything visible. He was in a dark suit that fit him with the severe precision of something tailored to project rather than impress, and he moved through the room with the unhurried efficiency of a man who was attending to an obligation rather than an evening. The other Castle principals — her father, Henry Bae, Robert Moon, David Choi — all registered his arrival in their various ways, the micro-adjustments of posture and expression that happened when someone entered a room that changed the room's balance. Nobody acknowledged it. Everybody felt it.

Sunwoo was beside him.

Solar kept her face exactly where it was. She was good at this — she had been practicing it since she was eleven years old and she had gotten very good at it — but she was also suddenly and acutely aware of every inch of space between herself and the door through which they had just entered, and of the precise location of Jaehyun's presence approximately four feet to her left, and of the fact that she had not looked at Chanhee and therefore could not confirm whether Chanhee was looking at her.

She did not look at Chanhee. She looked at the room in a general and entirely normal way.

Sihyuk moved toward the cluster of senior figures near the center of the room, and the cluster received him with the careful, measured civility of people who had learned, over many years, exactly how much warmth was appropriate and were not going to exceed it. Her father, she noted from the corner of her eye, greeted him with a handshake that was formal and brief and contained nothing beyond what was strictly necessary. Sihyuk returned it with the same economy. Two men who had once stood together in this very room and announced an alliance that was supposed to define a generation, reduced now to a handshake they could not avoid.

Sunwoo stayed a half-step behind and to the side of his father, which was where he was apparently supposed to be — present enough to be introduced, removed enough to be unthreatening. He was in a dark jacket that made him look older than fourteen, which Solar noticed and immediately decided she was not going to think about. His expression was set to the particular neutral she knew well, the one that looked like nothing and contained everything, his eyes moving through the room with the measured patience of someone taking inventory.

He didn't look at her. He looked at the room.

She looked at the room too.

Then, for approximately one second, across the full width of the ballroom, his eyes found hers.

It was nothing. It was the briefest possible contact, the kind of thing that would register as accidental to anyone watching. But she knew his face — she knew every variation of it, had been studying it for years — and she saw the thing that moved through it in that single second before he looked away. Acknowledgment, and something warmer than that, and a kind of exhaustion that she recognized because she felt it too, the particular tiredness of having to be someone else all evening in a room full of people who thought they knew exactly who you were.

Then it was gone and he was looking elsewhere and she was looking elsewhere and the Grand Meridian continued its expensive, amber-lit proceedings around them as though nothing had happened.

She waited another twenty minutes. She had learned patience from him, among other things.

When her father was deep in conversation with Henry Bae and Robert Moon, she set her glass down on the nearest table, said something to Chanhee about needing some air, and slipped out through the side door near the bar. The corridor beyond it was dim and quiet, the sounds of the ballroom reduced to a low, warm murmur through the heavy door. She found the framed photographs on the wall and pretended to look at them and counted silently, and at fifty seconds — longer than she expected, which probably meant he'd had to extricate himself from something — the door opened behind her.

"Took you long enough," she said, without turning around.

"My father was introducing me to someone," Sunwoo said, from just behind her left shoulder. His voice was quieter than usual, adjusted for the corridor. "Their son runs some kind of acquisition firm."

"What did you say to him?"

"Nothing interesting." He came to stand beside her, close enough that she could feel the warmth of him against her arm in the cool air of the corridor. "You had the face tonight."

She turned to look at him. "I don't have a face."

"You have a very specific face," he said, with the complete evenness he brought to statements of fact, "that you make when you're being introduced to someone you don't care about and you're pretending otherwise."

She stared at him. "How do you even — " she started, and then stopped, because the answer was obvious, and because the fact that he had catalogued her tells well enough to identify them across a crowded ballroom was doing something to her composure that she needed to address. She pressed her hand over her mouth to keep from laughing out loud in the corridor of the Grand Meridian, and Sunwoo watched her do this with the expression of someone who was not going to smile but was making no promises.

"You have the face right now," he observed.

"That's a different face," she managed.

"Is it."

"Yes, this one means I think you're — " she stopped herself, pressing her lips together, collecting herself. He was watching her with those dark eyes that missed nothing, and she was fourteen years old in a dim corridor outside a formal ballroom, and she had been performing all evening and she was so tired of it, and he was the only person she had seen all night who she didn't have to perform for.

She looked up at him properly. He was already looking at her, which he would deny if she called attention to it.

"This year was a lot," she said, quietly.

Something in his expression shifted — just slightly, just enough. "Yeah," he said.

She thought about the math competitions and the lecture series and the company events and Yein small and new in her arms on that Wednesday and all the plans that had gotten cut short because his father needed him and all the times she had wanted to ask why and hadn't. She thought about the iced tea and the pretzels and the heart in the margin of her competition sheet. She thought about being fourteen years old and already so tired.

"I'm glad you're here," she said, and she meant the corridor and also more than the corridor.

He looked at her for a moment. Then he reached out and tucked a strand of hair behind her ear, carefully, like she was something that required care, which was so unlike anything she expected from him that she went completely still. His hand stayed at the side of her face for a second, and she tipped her face up toward him slightly — not thinking about it, just doing it — and he leaned down and kissed her, brief and soft and entirely without ceremony, and then pulled back and looked at her.

She looked back at him.

"Okay," she said, which was possibly the least adequate response available to her, and he made a sound that was almost a laugh, and she felt herself smiling in a way she couldn't do anything about.

She reached down and found his hand in the dim corridor and held it, and he let her, folding his fingers around hers with the quiet certainty of someone who had made a decision and wasn't going back on it. They stood there in the cool quiet for another minute that neither of them would get back and both of them would keep, the sounds of the ballroom a low murmur through the door, and Solar thought that this was the only real moment she had experienced all evening.

Then the door at the far end of the corridor opened and a member of the catering staff appeared with a tray, and they stepped apart with the practiced ease of people who had been doing this for years, and Sunwoo went back through one door and Solar went back through another, and the evening resumed around them as though nothing had shifted.

Jaehyun was exactly where she had left him. He didn't say anything when she reappeared at his side — no raised eyebrow, no pointed look, no comment of any kind. He simply handed her the glass of water she had left on the table before she slipped out, without ceremony, with the expression of someone who had definitely noticed and had arrived at the correct conclusion and had decided, for now, that it was not his turn to say anything about it.

She took the glass. Their eyes met briefly.

"Thank you," she said.

"Mm," said Jaehyun.



Chapter Nine

2009 || NEW YORK

The deliveries happened at night, mostly.

This was practical rather than dramatic — fewer people on the streets, fewer people who might recognize him, fewer complications. His father had explained the logistics with the same clipped efficiency he brought to everything, and Sunwoo had listened and filed it all away, and the first time he did it he had been surprised by how straightforward it was. Not easy, exactly, but straightforward. You knew where you were going. You knew what you were carrying. You knew what to do if something went wrong, because his father had walked him through that too, and because his body had been trained for exactly these kinds of contingencies since before he was old enough to understand what they were contingencies for.

He was good at it. This did not surprise him. He was good at most things his father had prepared him for, which was either a testament to the preparation or to something in him that he had stopped examining.

The motorcycle helped. It was faster than a car in the city, easier to move through traffic, easier to disappear down side streets when the situation called for it. He had been riding since he was twelve, which had also been his father's idea, and he had gotten good at that too.

He kept his phone on him during deliveries but never checked it until he was done. This was a rule he had made for himself rather than one his father had given him — distraction was the kind of thing that got people killed, and he had decided early on that whatever was waiting on his phone could wait until the job was finished.

Solar's texts could always wait until the job was finished.

This did not mean he didn't think about them.

///

where are you

sunwoo

okay i'm just going to assume you're busy

are you coming or not, i've been here for like 20 minutes

okay you're clearly not coming, that's fine

just let me know you're okay

She put her phone face-down on the park bench and looked at the east path and told herself she wasn't annoyed. She wasn't, really. She had learned not to be, or at least she had learned to talk herself out of it by the time he eventually responded, because his explanation was always the same and she had no reason to disbelieve it and being annoyed at something she couldn't change was a waste of energy she didn't have.

my father needed me, sorry. are you still there

She looked at the message for a moment. It had come through forty minutes after the last one she sent, which meant she had been sitting on a bench in the Castle's east park alone for the better part of an hour. The evening had gotten cold in the way evenings did in late autumn, the kind of cold that crept in gradually until you noticed it all at once, and she pulled her jacket tighter and typed back.

no i went home

i'll see you tomorrow

She hesitated for a second, then added:

i'm glad you're okay

She put her phone in her pocket and walked back toward the Lee mansion with her hands curled inside her sleeves, and she thought about the corridor at the Grand Meridian and the way he had kissed her and the quiet weight of his hand folded around hers, and she told herself that whatever his father needed him for was important, because it always was, and that she understood, because she did.

She just wished, sometimes, that she understood a little more specifically.

///

Sangyeon had asked to come along tonight. He did that sometimes — not out of boredom, Sunwoo didn't think Sangyeon was capable of boredom in the conventional sense, but because he understood without being told that the point was to learn everything, and that watching was its own form of learning. Sunwoo had told him no. Not yet. He wasn't ready to bring another person into the operational side of things until he was confident, and confidence with Sangyeon was still being established one session at a time in the secondary building.

The drop-off was in Red Hook, which meant the ride back cut through the edge of Brooklyn — through streets that got quieter and narrower the further he went from the water, the kind of streets that existed entirely outside the city's expensive imagination, that the Castle didn't think about and didn't need to.

He checked his phone at the completion of the job, read Solar's messages in order, felt something move through him briefly before he tucked it away. He would fix it tomorrow. He was good at fixing things with her — not through words, but through showing up, through the small accumulated evidence of paying attention. She would be okay by tomorrow.

He turned the motorcycle down a side street and cut toward the main road, the engine running low and steady beneath him in the dark.

He heard it before he saw it.

He slowed without thinking, years of trained awareness redirecting his attention before he had consciously decided to pay it. The alley was halfway down the block on his left — three men, all significantly older, arranged in the specific geometry of people who had someone cornered. The someone was a boy, roughly his age or a year older, backed up against the brick wall with his chin lifted and his expression locked into the particular stubbornness of someone who knew they were in trouble and had decided, against all better judgment, to meet it head-on.

"— think you can pass off garbage like that and walk away," one of the men was saying, his voice low and deliberate in the way that was worse than shouting, that carried more weight than any raised volume could, "you have any idea what happens to people who waste our time?"

"I gave you exactly what we agreed on," the boy fired back, and his voice was steadier than the situation had any right to produce. "You don't like it, that's a you problem."

Sunwoo had already stopped the motorcycle. He climbed off, unhurried, walked to the mouth of the alley, and took his hand out of his jacket pocket.

The gun didn't need to be raised. It just needed to exist.

Three heads turned. He let them look — let them run their calculation, let them arrive at their own conclusion — and watched the specific moment when they decided the math didn't work in their favor. The man who had been speaking made a sound of contemptuous disgust, low and compressed, and they left. Not running, because running would have cost them something they weren't willing to give, but walking fast, and without looking back, and the alley went quiet.

The boy against the wall hadn't moved. He was staring at Sunwoo now with dark, sharp eyes that were doing rapid and complicated work — processing, assessing, cycling through several responses and discarding each of them in turn. He had a fresh cut along his jaw and he was very pointedly trying to look like none of what had just occurred was particularly remarkable.

"I didn't ask for that," he muttered, his jaw tight.

It wasn't gratitude and it wasn't hostility. It landed somewhere between the two — the specific discomfort of someone who had been surviving on their own terms for long enough that being helped felt like a debt they hadn't agreed to take on. Sunwoo recognized it. He put the gun away.

"I know," he returned, evenly.

The boy pushed off the wall, rolling one shoulder where it had clearly connected with the brick at some point. He cut a glance toward the end of the alley, jaw working, then looked back. "Those guys are going to come back with more people," he muttered, not panicked — just practical, already running the problem forward. "And next time they're going to be a lot less interested in talking."

"Probably," Sunwoo agreed.

"So." The boy spread his hands slightly, a gesture that managed to convey both you started this and what exactly are you planning to do about it in the same motion. He was watching Sunwoo with the eyes of someone who had developed, through extensive and unpleasant practice, a finely calibrated instrument for detecting what was wrong with a situation before the situation finished revealing itself.

Sunwoo looked at him steadily. He thought about Sangyeon on that front step in the fading afternoon light, the cuts on his arms, the controlled and quiet grief of him — and he thought about how different this was, this boy in front of him radiating a kind of bristling, defensive energy, all sharp edges, nothing soft about any of it.

"My name is Sunwoo," he offered, finally.

The boy blinked. "That's not what I asked," he clipped back.

"You didn't ask anything."

A silence fell over the alley. The boy's eyes narrowed slightly — recalibrating, trying to figure out what category of person he was dealing with. "How old are you?" he demanded finally, because apparently this had become the relevant question.

"Fourteen."

Something moved across the boy's face — genuine surprise, quickly suppressed, and underneath it the reluctant, incredulous beginning of something that might have been amusement if he'd let it travel any further. He didn't let it travel any further. "You're fourteen," he repeated flatly, like he needed to confirm he'd heard correctly.

Sunwoo waited.

The boy looked at him for a long moment — at the jacket, at the stillness, at the specific and undeniable fact of what had just transpired in this alley at the hands of a fourteen-year-old — and then he exhaled sharply through his nose and crossed his arms and looked briefly at the ground, like he was having a private argument with himself and losing it.

"My father drinks," he announced abruptly, still looking at the ground, his voice stripped of anything that might be mistaken for vulnerability. "I've been trying to get out for two years. Running small jobs, saving what I can. Those guys —" he jerked his chin toward the end of the alley "— they hired me for a delivery last month and then turned around and said I shorted them, which I didn't, and they've been on me about it ever since." He looked back up, his jaw set hard. "I wasn't trying to cheat anyone. I was just trying to make enough money to leave Brooklyn."

He said all of it the way someone filed a report — quick and clipped and factual, stripped of anything decorative, as if he had decided that Sunwoo had purchased this information with what he did in the alley and he might as well hand it over and be done with it.

Sunwoo said nothing. He let the silence do its work.

"So," the boy pressed again, sharper this time, the edge of someone who didn't like waiting and wasn't going to pretend otherwise. "What do you want?"

"Someone who can handle themselves," Sunwoo answered, quietly. "Someone who shows up when they're supposed to and does what they say they'll do." A beat. "Someone I don't have to explain things to twice."

The boy's expression was doing something almost funny in how transparently it was trying not to be interested. "In exchange for what?" he pushed, his eyes not leaving Sunwoo's face.

"Somewhere better than Brooklyn. Work that pays. People at your back."

"And the thing you're not telling me."

"There's a catch," Sunwoo confirmed, meeting his gaze directly. "I'm not going to pretend otherwise. You'd find out anyway."

The boy's eyes moved over him again — slow and thorough, the instrument running its full assessment. Whatever it was searching for, it appeared to find something, or perhaps not find something, which was the more relevant result. He was quiet for a long stretch.

"What's your name?" Sunwoo asked.

A beat. "Hyunjae," the boy answered, clipped, like he was handing something over under protest.

Sunwoo nodded once and turned toward the mouth of the alley. He heard Hyunjae follow after a moment — quicker than Sangyeon's moment had been, and somehow simultaneously more reluctant, the two things not being mutually exclusive in the slightest.

///

Sangyeon was awake when they got back, which Sunwoo had expected.

He was at the table in the secondary building's main room with a laptop open in front of him, the glow of the screen the only light in the space, and he glanced up when the door opened with the particular alertness of someone who had been waiting without making it obvious that he had been. His eyes moved from Sunwoo to Hyunjae and settled there, running their own quiet, unhurried assessment.

Hyunjae looked back at him with the combative attention of someone stepping into an unfamiliar environment and immediately cataloguing every variable in it.

"Sangyeon," Sunwoo said simply. "Hyunjae." He didn't elaborate. He moved to the far side of the room and sat down, leaving the two of them to work out whatever they were going to work out between themselves.

A silence stretched across the room — not hostile exactly, but taut with the particular tension of two people who had arrived here by different roads and were now in the slow process of deciding what to make of the fact that the roads had led to the same place.

Sangyeon closed the laptop. "You hungry?" he questioned Hyunjae, in the direct, unhurried way he said most things, like it was just a simple question and not an offering of any kind.

Hyunjae blinked. Clearly this was not what he had been bracing for. "What?"

"There's food," Sangyeon clarified, already rising to his feet. "I made too much anyway."

Another beat. Hyunjae glanced at Sunwoo as if checking whether this was a normal thing that happened here, and Sunwoo looked back at him without providing any particular information. Hyunjae looked back at Sangyeon, something shifting in the set of his shoulders — not relaxing exactly, but settling slightly, some of the weight becoming less load-bearing than it had been a minute ago.

"Sure," he relented, after a moment. Grudgingly. Like agreeing to eat someone's food was a significant concession he was making under some form of duress.

Sunwoo looked at the wall and said nothing, and thought about Solar's text still sitting in his phone — i'm glad you're okay — and the way those four words carried more than their weight, the way they said something she probably didn't even realize she was saying.

He would fix it tomorrow.

Across the room, Sangyeon set a bowl in front of Hyunjae and sat back down without ceremony, and the secondary building settled into a quiet that was occupied — two people who had not yet decided whether they trusted each other, sharing a space, beginning the slow and unannounced work of figuring it out. Which was, Sunwoo thought, exactly how all of this had started.

He filed it away the way he filed everything.

Outside, the Castle gates glowed steadily in the dark, and somewhere across the grounds the Lee mansion burned with warm light in every window, the way it always did, the way it always would.




Chapter Ten

2010 || NEW YORK

Upper East Side High was, by any objective measure, the most expensive building Solar had ever attended school in.

This was saying something, given that she had spent the last eight years in the Upper East Side's equally prestigious middle school, which itself occupied a building that most universities would have envied. But the high school was something else — a landmark structure of pale stone and arched windows that sat on the corner of its block like it had always been there and always would be, like it had been installed by the same people who installed the Manhattan skyline and simply hadn't been moved since. The kind of building that communicated, before you even walked through the door, that the people who came out of it were expected to be someone.

Solar had been expected to be someone for as long as she could remember. She was fifteen years old and she was used to it. That didn't mean it got lighter.

She was walking with Chanhee, the way she always walked with Chanhee, because some things about high school were going to be different and some things weren't. The morning was sharp and clear in the way New York mornings could be in September, the kind of light that made everything look more deliberate than it was, and the front steps of Upper East Side High were already full of students — some of whom Solar recognized, Castle kids and the children of their parents' associates, all of them performing the particular calibrated ease of people who had been taught never to look like they were trying.

Chanhee was not performing ease. Chanhee was walking with his chin up and his expression set to the precise temperature of someone who was completely aware that people were looking at him and had made peace with this before leaving the house that morning, which was — in Solar's opinion — the most effortlessly cool thing he had ever done, and he had done a lot of effortlessly cool things.

The pink was very pink.

It caught the September light in a way that made it impossible to ignore, which was presumably at least partly the point. She had done it herself, over the summer, in the bathroom of his house on a Saturday afternoon with a mixing bowl and a pair of gloves and Chanhee sitting on the edge of the sink telling her exactly how to apply it because he had watched seventeen videos in preparation. It had taken three hours and turned the bowl a spectacular shade of magenta and the result had been, in Solar's professional opinion as the person who had applied it, absolutely perfect.

She had been there when he looked in the mirror for the first time. She had watched his face do something she didn't have words for — not surprise, because he had chosen it, but something more like recognition, like he had been waiting to see this version of himself and was relieved it had arrived.

She hadn't said anything. She had just smiled at him in the mirror, and he had smiled back, and that had been enough.

Now, walking up the front steps of Upper East Side High on the first day of their freshman year, she watched people notice him and felt something fierce and uncomplicated move through her chest on his behalf.

Most of the looks were simply surprised — recalibrating, adjusting their understanding of a person they thought they already knew. A few were openly admiring. A handful were the particular kind of look that certain Upper East Side teenagers gave to anything that deviated from the expected image, the look that tried to communicate how embarrassing while actually communicating how threatened. Solar returned those specific looks with the flat, direct gaze she had inherited from years of sitting across from her father in his office, and watched them find somewhere else to be.

"Stop that," Chanhee murmured beside her, without looking at her.

"Stop what?" she returned, with complete innocence.

"You know what." He pulled open the heavy front door, holding it for her. "I don't need you to defend my hair, Sol."

"I wasn't defending your hair."

"You were doing the face at people."

"I have a lot of faces."

"You have one face," he corrected, which was the same thing Sunwoo had said in the Grand Meridian corridor ten months ago, and the echo of it made her want to laugh. She pressed her lips together and went inside.

The interior of Upper East Side High was exactly what the exterior promised — vaulted ceilings, polished floors, the faint institutional smell of something expensive trying to be neutral. Students were moving through the main hall in clusters, lockers opening and closing, the building filling up with the specific noise of a first day that was trying to decide what kind of year it was going to be. Solar had her schedule in hand and had already memorized it, because of course she had, and she was in the process of cross-referencing it with the building map when a hand came down on her shoulder from behind and she nearly dropped both.

"FIRST DAY!" Kevin announced, appearing beside her with the energy of someone who had been awake since before dawn specifically in anticipation of this moment, his grin wide enough to be its own source of light. He was wearing a blazer that was technically within dress code and technically doing something unusual to the dress code simultaneously, which was a Kevin achievement she had come to expect. "Are we excited? I'm excited. Jacob, are you excited?"

"I'm present," Jacob offered, appearing on Solar's other side with the quiet, warm steadiness he brought to every situation, his blazer entirely conventional, his expression genuinely pleased in the way Jacob's expressions always were — uncomplicated, sincere, not performing anything. He looked at Chanhee. "You look great, by the way."

Chanhee blinked, apparently not having prepared for this to be the first thing Jacob said. "Thank you," he managed, with slightly less composure than usual.

"Really great," Jacob confirmed, simply, and then looked back at the hallway ahead of them like this was a settled matter.

Solar watched Chanhee recover his composure and felt the fierce warmth in her chest again, directed this time at Jacob Bae and his complete, uncomplicated kindness, which she had been grateful for since they were ten years old and she hoped she would be grateful for for the rest of her life.

"Where's Changmin?" she asked.

"Library," Kevin reported. "Already. On the first day. Before first period. I love him."

"He was looking for a specific book," Jacob clarified.

"On the first day," Kevin repeated, with the affectionate bewilderment he reserved exclusively for Changmin's habits. "Anyway — Solar, what's your first period? Tell me we have something together."

She looked at her schedule. "AP English."

Kevin's face fell with theatrical devastation. "I have regular English."

"You could have taken AP English," she pointed out.
