"I could have," he agreed, without any particular remorse. "I chose not to. Different priorities." He brightened immediately. "Lunch, though — we all have the same lunch, I checked."

"You checked the lunch schedule," Chanhee said.

"I checked everyone's lunch schedule," Kevin confirmed, entirely unashamed. "It was important information. You're welcome."

Solar smiled and looked down the hall, and found Sunwoo without having to look for long.

He was at a locker near the far end of the corridor, which she had not known was his locker until this moment and which she had now filed away as information she would definitely be using. He was alone, which was typical, and he was doing nothing in particular, which was also typical, and he had not looked in her direction yet, which meant she could look at him for approximately three seconds before he noticed.

He noticed in two.

His eyes found hers across the length of the hall with the same precision they always did — direct and immediate, like her location was something he was simply always aware of. Something moved through his expression, brief and private, and then it was gone and he was closing his locker and looking elsewhere, and she was looking at her schedule, and the hall moved around them like nothing had happened.

Beside her, Kevin was still talking about the lunch schedule.

She let herself smile at her schedule, just for a second, before she looked back up.

///

The thing about Upper East Side High was that it had expectations, and the thing about having expectations was that it had ways of communicating them.

By the end of the first week, Solar had been approached by three separate teachers about extracurricular opportunities — the academic decathlon, the economics club, and a business mentorship program that partnered students with Castle-affiliated companies, which her AP Economics teacher mentioned while making very deliberate eye contact with her in a way that suggested it was not entirely a coincidence that she was being told about it. She signed up for all three, because the version of herself that existed in this building didn't know how to say no to things her father would approve of, and because honestly — she was good at them, and being good at things still felt like it meant something, even when it was exhausting.

Sunwoo, by the end of the first week, had attended approximately half his classes.

She noticed. Of course she noticed — she noticed everything about him, had been noticing everything about him for five years, had developed an involuntary and highly specific awareness of his patterns that she could not have turned off if she tried. The empty seat in the classes they shared. The way he would appear and then not appear, present one day and absent the next, with no pattern she could identify beyond the fact that it was happening more than it wasn't.

She brought it up on Saturday at the bench.

"You missed three classes this week," she opened, setting her bag down beside her and looking at him directly in the way she had learned was the only approach with Sunwoo that produced any result.

He looked back at her with the expression of someone who had anticipated this conversation and had not decided yet what to do with it. "I had things to do," he returned, evenly.

"What things?"

"Things for my father."

She held his gaze. He held hers. This was a familiar standoff — she pushing, he holding, both of them knowing she would eventually stop pushing and both of them knowing she didn't love stopping. "Sunwoo," she exhaled, trying a different angle, "you're one of the smartest people I know. You're smarter than half the teachers in that building. If you fail out of high school—"

"I'm not going to fail," he cut across her, not unkindly. "I'll pass the exams. I know the material."

"That's not the point."

"Then what is the point?"

She looked at him for a moment. The honest answer was that the point was she worried about him, that the point was she could see him disappearing in increments into something she couldn't name, that the point was she was fifteen years old and she loved him and she didn't know what to do with the feeling that she was watching him become someone she wouldn't be able to reach. But she knew better than to say all of that at once, because Sunwoo received large emotional statements the way he received most things — by going still and waiting for them to be over.

"You're too smart to waste it," she settled on, quietly.

Something moved through his expression at that — not dismissal, something more complicated than that, something that looked almost like it hurt before he put it away. He looked at the path ahead of them for a moment, and then he reached over without comment and took the pencil that was sitting on top of her notebook and drew a small heart on the corner of the page.

She looked at it.

"I'll go to more classes," he muttered, still looking at the path.

It wasn't a promise, exactly. But from Sunwoo, it was close enough.

///

It was Kevin who found her, on a Thursday afternoon in October, sitting on the low wall outside the east entrance of the building waiting for Chanhee to finish a meeting with his college counselor.

She had her notes out, because she always had her notes out, but she wasn't looking at them. She was thinking about something Sunwoo had said that morning — something small, a passing comment about being tired, phrased in a way that was so ordinary it had almost slipped past her — and she was turning it over, trying to decide if it meant something or if she was looking for things that weren't there, which was a habit she had developed and wasn't sure was healthy.

Kevin sat down beside her without preamble, which was standard Kevin, and she looked up expecting one of his usual opening salvos — a question about the lunch schedule or an observation about something he had witnessed in the hallway — and found instead that he was quiet.

Kevin was not often quiet.

She put her notes down. "Hey," she said, carefully. "You okay?"

He was looking at his hands in his lap, his expression doing something she hadn't seen from him before — not distress exactly, but a kind of concentrated seriousness, like he was choosing his words with more care than usual. Kevin Moon chose most of his words at high speed and with tremendous confidence. This was different.

"Can I tell you something?" he asked, and his voice was lower than normal, stripped of the usual brightness.

"Always," she answered, without hesitation.

He was quiet for another moment. Then he exhaled slowly, and when he looked at her there was something in his eyes that she recognized — the specific vulnerability of someone who already knew who they were and was asking, carefully, to be seen.

"I think I like boys," he told her. Simply. Directly. In the same way Kevin did most things, even when those things were hard — head-on, without decoration, trusting that the person in front of him would receive it for what it was.

Solar looked at him. She didn't say anything immediately, not because she didn't have words, but because she wanted the first thing she said to be exactly right. Kevin was looking at her with the careful attention of someone who had decided to trust and was waiting to find out if the decision was good.

"Okay," she said, warmly and without ceremony, which was the closest she could get to of course, obviously, this changes nothing, I love you exactly as you are in a single word. "Thank you for telling me."

Something in his shoulders released, just slightly. "I've known for a while," he continued, a little easier now, "I just — I wanted to tell someone. Someone who would — " he paused, searching for the right phrasing.

"Take you seriously," she offered.

"Yeah," he confirmed, and the relief in his voice was so quiet and so genuine that it made her chest ache a little. "Yeah, exactly."

She reached over and knocked her shoulder against his, the way she had been doing since they were ten years old. "For the record," she told him, "I have always taken you seriously. Even when you were standing on a lunch chair singing."

He laughed at that — a real one, surprised out of him, and the brightness came back into his expression in a rush, warm and uncomplicated and entirely Kevin. "That was a great performance," he informed her, with complete sincerity.

"It really was," she agreed, and meant it.

They sat there for a while in the October afternoon, the sounds of the school day winding down around them, and Solar thought about the specific and irreplaceable thing it was to be someone's first safe place. To be the person they came to when they needed to say something true out loud for the first time. She thought about how Kevin had walked toward her with something real and fragile and had handed it over without armor, because he trusted her enough to do that, and she thought that there was nothing she had ever been given that mattered more than that kind of trust.

"Does anyone else know?" she asked, after a while.

"Not yet," he answered. "Just you." A beat. "I'll tell the others when it feels right."

She nodded. "I'll be here," she told him, "for all of it. Whatever you need."

He looked at her for a moment, and his expression was so simply grateful that it didn't need any words, so he didn't use any. He just nodded, and they sat there together on the low wall outside Upper East Side High as the afternoon light moved across the pale stone of the building, and it was quiet in the good way, the way that meant something had been said that needed saying and had landed exactly where it was supposed to.

The door behind them opened and Chanhee appeared, already talking before he had fully exited, and Kevin stood up and the afternoon resumed, and Solar picked her notes back up and smiled to herself and said nothing to anyone.

Some things you kept.




Chapter Eleven

2010 || NEW YORK

His father's voice had a particular quality when he was outlining something that was not a request.

It didn't get louder. It didn't acquire any edge or heat, because Sihyuk Kim did not use heat as a tool — heat was inefficient, heat was imprecise, heat was what people used when they had run out of better options. What his voice did instead was get quieter, more deliberate, each word placed with the specific care of someone who was making sure every syllable landed where it was intended to land.

"You're fifteen," his father had told him, standing at the window of the study the way he sometimes did when he wanted to speak without looking at you. "Fifteen is old enough. I was fourteen when I ran my first operation. I've given you longer than I was given, and you have more than I had. There is no reason," he had continued, turning from the window then, his eyes flat and assessing as always, "that the unit isn't ready."

Sunwoo had sat in the chair across from the desk and said, "It will be."

"When."

"Before the end of the year."

His father had looked at him for a long moment — the inventory look, the one that measured and weighed and arrived at conclusions Sunwoo was never told. Then he had turned back to the window, which meant the conversation was over.

Sunwoo had walked back to the east corridor and stood there for a moment in the expensive silence of the Kim mansion and understood, in the specific and practical way he understood most things, that the timeline had just become real.

///

The training sessions at the secondary building were longer now.

He pushed Sangyeon and Hyunjae harder than he had been pushing them, not because he was angry — he was rarely angry, anger was a waste of resources — but because the margin for unpreparedness had narrowed, and he needed to know exactly where each of them was. What they could do. What they did when things went wrong. Whether they made good decisions when their bodies were exhausted and their thinking was compromised and there was no clean option in front of them.

Sangyeon was solid. He had always been solid — the physical intelligence that had been apparent from the first week had only deepened with months of training, and he absorbed correction with the same focused attention he brought to everything. He didn't complain. He didn't ask why. He just did the work and did it again until it was right, and then he asked what was next.

Hyunjae was different — sharper, more instinctive, the kind of fighter who operated on reflex rather than discipline and was learning, gradually and with some friction, that reflex alone wasn't enough. He pushed back sometimes, not in the way of someone being defiant but in the way of someone who had spent years trusting only himself and was still in the process of learning that trusting someone else didn't mean losing anything. He was getting there. Sunwoo could see it.

After the sessions, in the evenings, the three of them would often end up in the same room without particularly intending to — Sangyeon at the laptop, Hyunjae sprawled somewhere with the loose, post-training bonelessness of someone whose body had nothing left, Sunwoo doing whatever needed doing next. They didn't talk much. They didn't need to. There was a specific kind of ease that came from being around people who had seen you at your worst capacity and hadn't left, and it didn't require words to maintain.

It was on one of these evenings, after a session that had run longer than planned and left all three of them quieter than usual, that Sunwoo first tried it.

He had been in his room — not the spartan space in the secondary building but his actual room in the main house, which he used less and less as time went on because the main house felt increasingly like his father's space rather than any kind of his own. He had been sitting on the edge of the bed with the specific, loaded tiredness of someone whose body was exhausted and whose mind was not going to let them rest, turning over the conversation with his father and the timeline and everything that the timeline implied, when he had opened the drawer of his nightstand and looked at what was inside it.

His father's operation produced and distributed a wide variety of things. Sunwoo had access to all of them, because knowledge of the inventory was part of his job, and because no one in the Kim mansion thought to monitor what the boy who ran the operation was or wasn't taking from it. He had never taken anything before. He had watched other people use and had noted what it did to them — the loosening, the quieting, the way it seemed to create a temporary distance between a person and whatever was pressing down on them — and had filed it away as information without particular relevance to himself.

He picked up the small orange pill bottle. He looked at it for a moment.

He thought about his father's voice. There is no reason that the unit isn't ready.

He shook one out onto his palm. Small, unmarked, the kind of thing that looked entirely ordinary.

He swallowed it and lay back on the bed and waited, and when the edges of everything softened, as promised, he understood immediately and with complete clarity why people did this.

He also understood, in the same moment and with the same clarity, that understanding it was the dangerous part.

He filed it away. He went to sleep.

///

He went to his father two weeks later.

Not to the study — his father was in the main sitting room, reviewing something, which was unusual enough that Sunwoo registered it. He stood in the doorway until Sihyuk looked up, and then he said, evenly, "I'm going to be managing my own finances from here."

His father looked at him. "Is that right," he returned, in a tone that was not quite a question.

"Yes," Sunwoo confirmed. He didn't elaborate. He didn't justify. He had learned, from a lifetime of observation, that the worst thing you could do when making a statement to Sihyuk Kim was to undermine it with explanation.

A silence settled between them — not the comfortable kind, but the kind that had weight, that was being used to measure something. His father set down what he was reading. He looked at his son for a long moment with those flat, assessing eyes, running the inventory the way he always did, arriving at whatever conclusion he arrived at.

"You've been performing well," his father said finally, which was as close to yes as Sihyuk Kim ever got.

Sunwoo nodded once and walked back out of the room.

He found the gym three days later — or rather, he had found it weeks ago and had been returning to it in his mind ever since, testing the idea, deciding whether it was the right decision. It was in Brooklyn, which was far enough from the Castle to feel like a different world entirely, which was the point. A squat brick building on a quiet block, the painted letters above the door faded to near-illegibility, the interior dark and abandoned and smelling of old rubber and disuse. The ring in the center of the main floor was still standing, barely, the ropes slack and the canvas stained. The equipment that remained was old enough to be largely useless but present enough to suggest what the place had once been.

He stood in the middle of it and turned slowly, taking inventory the way he always did. The dimensions. The exits. The sight lines from the windows. The structural condition of the ceiling. What was here and what would need to be brought.

He bought it the following week, which was not a complicated process when you had your own money and knew the right people to call. The transaction was completed quietly, as most things in his life were completed, and the deed was in his name before the month was out.

He didn't tell his father.

///

The delivery was in Bushwick, which took him through a part of Brooklyn he didn't often pass through — wide blocks of industrial buildings, some occupied and some not, the kind of neighborhood that existed in the city's periphery without being fully claimed by it. He was on his way back, the motorcycle moving easy through the late afternoon quiet, when he saw the building.

Or rather, he saw the kid outside the building.

He was leaning against the wall of what had clearly once been some kind of commercial space, now empty, with the comfortable ease of someone who had decided this was where he was going to be and was fine with it. He was around Sunwoo's age, maybe the same, with a quality about him that was hard to name immediately — something loose and unguarded that was unusual in the contexts Sunwoo generally operated in, where everyone was either performing something or protecting something.

The kid looked at the motorcycle as Sunwoo slowed, and then looked at Sunwoo, and said, with the directness of someone who had already decided they had nothing to lose by asking, "You a dealer?"

Sunwoo stopped the motorcycle. He looked at the kid.

"Why?" he returned.

"Cause I want to do what you do," the kid answered, simply, like this was a reasonable and entirely ordinary thing to tell a stranger who had just pulled up on a motorcycle. "I've been watching this block. I know who moves through here and when. I just need a connect."

Sunwoo studied him. He had the specific quality of attention he brought to things he was still deciding about — not hostile, just thorough, taking in the details and letting them accumulate. The kid held his gaze without flinching, which was its own kind of information. Most people flinched.

"What else?" Sunwoo said.

The kid raised an eyebrow. "What else what?"

"You said you've been watching the block. You know timing and patterns. What else can you do?"

Something shifted in the kid's expression — recalibration, the recognition that this conversation had turned into something slightly different than he had opened it as. He reached into the back of his waistband and produced, with a practiced motion that suggested it lived there regularly, a knife. It was a good one — the blade slim and balanced, the handle wrapped in dark cord, the kind of thing that had been chosen rather than simply acquired.

"I can use this," he announced confidently, holding it out for inspection.

Sunwoo looked at the knife, then back at the kid. "Show me."

A beat. "Show you what, exactly?"

"Show me you can use it."

The kid looked at him for a second. Then he looked around at the empty block, apparently identified something suitable, and sent the knife spinning through the air in a clean, confident arc that embedded it in a wooden post across the lot with a sound that echoed off the brick walls around them.

He looked back at Sunwoo with the expression of someone who had made their point and was aware of it.

"I don't actually know any technique," he admitted, with a cheerfulness that suggested this did not particularly concern him. "I just know that part."

Despite everything, despite himself, Sunwoo felt something that was almost amusement. He kept it entirely off his face. "You have a cigarette?" he asked instead.

The kid's face broke into a grin — wide and immediate, the kind of smile that rearranged a person's whole face, and Sunwoo noted distantly that this was going to be either extremely useful or extremely complicated, possibly both. He produced a pack from his jacket pocket and held one out.

"What's your name?" Sunwoo asked, taking it.

"Hyunjoon," the kid answered. Then, with the easy confidence of someone extending a pleasantry: "Yours?"

"Sunwoo."

Hyunjoon nodded slowly, looking at him with those loose, unguarded eyes, reassessing in a way that was entirely different from the way Sangyeon or Hyunjae had reassessed — lighter, more curious, less weighted. "So," he said, exhaling smoke, "you gonna tell me what you actually do? Because I'm getting the feeling it's more interesting than deliveries."

Sunwoo looked at him for a moment. Then he said, "There's a catch."

"There always is," Hyunjoon returned, pleasantly, like this was just a feature of the world he had accepted long ago. "What is it?"

"You'd be working for me."

Hyunjoon looked at him — the full assessment, quick and cheerful and surprisingly thorough. Then he shrugged one shoulder with the ease of someone for whom most decisions were made on instinct and had generally worked out fine. "Alright," he agreed, like it was that simple.

Because for Hyunjoon, Sunwoo would come to understand, most things generally were.

///

He found Eric twenty minutes later.

Hyunjoon was on the back of the motorcycle — occupying the space with the unself-conscious comfort of someone who had been doing it for years rather than minutes — when Sunwoo slowed at the bridge. The boy was underneath it, partially obscured by the shadow, in the process of arranging something that might generously be described as shelter. A piece of tarpaulin. A bag. The specific, practiced economy of someone who had been doing this long enough to have a system.

"Keep going," Hyunjoon said quietly, from behind him.

Sunwoo didn't keep going. He stopped the motorcycle.

He could feel Hyunjoon's curiosity behind him as he climbed off and walked toward the bridge, and he heard him follow after a moment — footsteps quiet on the pavement, the instinct to stay close already beginning to operate, which Sunwoo noted.

The boy under the bridge heard them coming. He was on his feet before they were close, the tarpaulin forgotten, his body arranged in the taut, ready posture of someone who had learned that people approaching usually meant a problem. He was thin in the way of people who hadn't been eating regularly, and young — maybe their age, maybe younger, it was hard to tell — with dark eyes that tracked between Sunwoo and Hyunjoon with rapid, wary attention.

"I don't want trouble," he said flatly, which was probably the most common lie in the city, and both of them knew it, and the boy probably knew it too.

Sunwoo stopped a few feet away. He looked at the makeshift shelter — at the tarpaulin and the single bag and the careful, joyless organization of it — and said nothing for a moment. He had learned that silence was often more effective than speaking, and that what it communicated depended entirely on how you held it.

"What happened to the place you were before?" he asked, eventually.

The boy's eyes narrowed. "How do you know there was a place before?"

"Because you have a system," Sunwoo said. "People who've always been here don't have a system. They just have a spot."

A silence. The boy looked at him with those dark, wary eyes, running his own assessment, deciding something. "Foster family," he said finally, clipped, each word measured out like he was deciding exactly how much to give. "Ran about six months ago."

"From them, or from the system?"

"Both."

Hyunjoon had gone quiet behind him, which was its own notable thing.

Sunwoo looked at the boy for a long moment. He thought about what his father had told him, years ago, when this had all been theoretical — find people who know what hard looks like — and he thought about how at fifteen years old he had found four of them now, and he thought about how each of them had arrived at the same general place through entirely different roads, which meant something, even if he couldn't have said exactly what.

"I can offer you somewhere better than this," he said.

The boy's expression shuttered immediately — the specific closing-down of someone who had been offered things before and had learned what offers usually cost. "By who?" he challenged, his voice flat. "You're what, fifteen?"

"Yes," Sunwoo confirmed.

"So what are you offering that you can actually deliver?"

"A place to live," Sunwoo answered. "Work. People. A reason to stay." He paused. "There's a catch."

"Of course there is." The boy's jaw was tight, his eyes not moving from Sunwoo's face. "What do you want from me?"

"To show up," Sunwoo said simply. "To learn. To do what you say you'll do."

Another silence, longer this time. The boy looked at him — really looked, with the slow, thorough attention of someone who had nothing left to lose and was therefore not going to rush a decision that might cost them even that. He looked at Hyunjoon briefly, who gave him a small, easy nod that communicated approximately nothing useful but somehow landed as reassuring anyway, which was a Hyunjoon achievement Sunwoo was already beginning to recognize.

The boy looked back at Sunwoo.

"What's your name?" he muttered.

"Sunwoo. Yours?"

A beat — the longest one yet, the one that contained everything he was weighing and would not say out loud. Then, quietly, with the specific exhaustion of someone arriving at a decision they have no better alternative to:

"Eric."

Sunwoo nodded. He turned back toward the motorcycle, and behind him he heard two sets of footsteps follow — Hyunjoon's easy and unhurried, Eric's slower, more careful, the footsteps of someone who hadn't decided yet whether he trusted the direction but was moving in it anyway.

It was enough. It was always enough, to start.

///

He brought them to Greenwood that evening.

All four of them — Sangyeon and Hyunjae had come over from the Castle on the motorcycle's second run, which had required two trips and had taken longer than intended, and by the time they were all standing in the main room of the gym the Brooklyn dark had fully arrived outside the high windows.

He watched them take it in. Sangyeon moved through the space methodically, checking the room's dimensions the way he checked most things — quietly, thoroughly, without commentary. Hyunjae went straight to the ring, stepped up onto the apron, tested the ropes, dropped back down with the air of someone forming an opinion. Hyunjoon looked around with his hands in his jacket pockets and a slow, approving expression, like he was pricing something he intended to buy. Eric stood near the door for longer than the others, taking it in from a distance first, which Sunwoo noted and said nothing about.

The motorcycle was in the back room — the space he had already been using as a workshop, the tools arranged on a makeshift bench along the wall, the bike itself up on a stand in the middle of the floor. It was in better shape than it had been when he acquired it, which was the ongoing project, the thing he came back to when he needed to do something with his hands that wasn't training or operations or any of the other things that filled the hours. There was something about the work of it — the specificity of it, the way each problem had a correct solution that you could find if you looked carefully enough — that his mind settled into in a way it didn't settle into much else.

He had not shown anyone the bike yet. He would. Not tonight.

"It needs work," Sangyeon observed, having completed his circuit of the main room and arrived at a verdict.

"A lot of it," Sunwoo agreed.

"But it's good," Sangyeon added, which from Sangyeon was the same as enthusiasm.

Hyunjoon dropped onto the apron of the ring and sat there with his legs hanging over the edge, looking at the four of them in the way he already had of looking at things — assessing, but warmly, like he was already deciding he liked what he saw. "So this is us," he announced, to no one in particular, with the easy confidence of someone naming something that had already been decided.

Nobody responded to this immediately. Hyunjae was looking at the ceiling. Eric was still near the door, but he had moved slightly further into the room, which Sunwoo registered without drawing attention to it. Sangyeon was looking at Hyunjoon with the particular expression he wore when he was deciding whether he trusted someone, which took longer with Sangyeon than with most people and was therefore worth more when it arrived.

"There's going to be more of us," Sunwoo said, into the quiet.

Hyunjoon nodded like this was expected information. "How many more?"

"I don't know yet."

Hyunjoon considered this. "Fair enough," he decided, and lay back on the ring apron with his hands behind his head, looking up at the water-stained ceiling with the serene acceptance of someone who had committed to a direction and was done deliberating about it.

Hyunjae finally looked down from the ceiling and at Eric, who was looking back at him with the guarded attention he brought to everything so far. The two of them existed in a brief, sizing-up silence — two people who had both arrived here through roads that nobody would call easy, recognizing something in each other without being able to name it yet.

"You know how to fight?" Hyunjae asked him, bluntly.

"No," Eric answered, equally blunt.

"I'll teach you," Hyunjae said, with the matter-of-fact delivery of someone making a scheduling announcement rather than an offer, and looked away again before Eric could respond.

Eric looked at the side of Hyunjae's face for a moment. Something in his expression shifted — very slightly, barely perceptible, the specific movement of someone whose guard has been breached by something too small and too genuine to defend against.

He moved away from the door. He found a piece of wall to lean against. He stayed.

Sunwoo looked at all of them — at the four boys in the abandoned gym in Brooklyn, the ring and the high windows and the dark outside, the long work ahead of them and everything it was going to require — and he said nothing, because there was nothing that needed saying.

He went to the back room and picked up a wrench and got to work on the motorcycle, and through the open door he could hear the low sounds of them settling in behind him, and for the first time in a long time the silence had a different quality to it.

Not empty. Not his father's.

Just theirs.









Chapter Twelve

 2010 || NEW YORK

The Foundation Dinner was smaller than the Gala — intentionally so, which was itself a kind of statement.

Where the Annual Gala was the Castle at full volume, all of its families and their weight assembled in one room, the Foundation Dinner was curated. Forty guests, perhaps fifty, selected with the specific care of someone who understood that exclusivity was its own form of power. Solji Lee had built something in the past year that was beginning to acquire its own gravity, and the dinner — held at Carnegie Hall's private dining space, because Solji had an instinct for venues that communicated exactly what she wanted them to communicate — was the clearest evidence of it yet.

Solar was seated two chairs down from her mother, which was close enough to observe and far enough to breathe, and she spent the first portion of the evening doing both.

She had been to enough of these events to know how they worked — the specific choreography of a room full of people who all understood the social mathematics of who sat where and spoke to whom and for how long. But watching her mother navigate it was different from watching her father. Jaesang commanded rooms through presence and silence, through the gravitational pull of someone whose approval everyone wanted and few received. Solji moved through a room differently — warmly, deliberately, making each person feel like the conversation they were having with her was the one she had been looking forward to all evening. It was its own kind of power, and it was working.

"She's extraordinary," Jacob murmured from beside her, watching Solji with the quiet appreciation he brought to things he genuinely admired.

Solar glanced at him. Jacob was here with his father Henry, who was one of the Foundation's early donors, and he had been placed at Solar's table in the way that Castle events tended to place the children of allied families together — partly for practicality and partly because their parents had decided, years ago, that these were the relationships worth cultivating. Solar had never minded. Jacob was easy company in the way that genuinely good people were easy company.

"She really is," Solar agreed, and meant it in a way that was complicated, the way most things about her mother were complicated.

Across the room, Solji was speaking to a woman Solar recognized as one of New York's more prominent arts philanthropists, her hands folded, her expression attentive and warm, and she looked entirely in her element in a way that Solar wasn't sure she had ever seen her look inside their own house. Which was something worth thinking about, probably. She filed it away.

Her father was at the head of the room — not the evening's focus, which was unusual for him, but present in the specific way Jaesang Lee was always present, which was to say completely and without appearing to try. He had said very little all evening, which meant he was watching, which was when he was most attentive and most dangerous in a business sense, and Solar had inherited enough of him to recognize the posture.

He caught her eye across the room and gave her a small, almost imperceptible nod. She returned it.

Solar, my eldest. She'll be the one running things one day.

She picked up her water glass and looked at the table setting and breathed.

///

Yein was asleep by the time Solar got home, which she had expected — it was well past ten, and Yein at almost two years old maintained a schedule that did not accommodate Foundation Dinners. But Solar checked on her anyway, the way she always did when she came home late, pushing open the nursery door just enough to see the small shape in the crib, the rise and fall of her breathing visible even in the dim light from the hallway.

She stood there for a moment in the doorway.

Yein had started doing something recently that Solar found completely unreasonable in how much it affected her — she had started reaching for things. Specifically, she had started reaching for Solar whenever Solar was near, her small arms extending upward with the absolute confidence of someone who had no concept of the possibility of not being caught. It was such a simple thing. It made Solar feel something she didn't have precise language for, something that had to do with being needed in a way that was entirely uncomplicated, that asked nothing of her except to be there and catch her.

She pulled the nursery door closed gently and went down the hall toward her room.

Chaerin's door was open, light spilling into the hallway, and Solar slowed without intending to. Her younger sister was at her desk — twelve years old and already with the particular energy of someone who had opinions about everything and the confidence to air all of them — and she looked up when Solar appeared in the doorway with the expression of someone who had been waiting.

"How was the boring dinner?" Chaerin inquired.

"It wasn't boring," Solar corrected.

"It was at Carnegie Hall with a bunch of adults talking about art grants," Chaerin returned, with the authority of someone who had been fully briefed and had formed a verdict. "That's the definition of boring."

"Mother didn't think so."

Chaerin's expression shifted slightly at that — something more genuine underneath the performance of twelve-year-old worldliness. "Was she good?" she asked, and the question was smaller and more real than anything that had preceded it.

Solar leaned against the doorframe. "She was really good, Chae," she said, quietly.

Her sister absorbed this. She turned back to her desk, ostensibly returning to whatever she had been doing, but Solar could see from the set of her shoulders that she was thinking about it, filing it somewhere. Chaerin and their mother had a different relationship than Solar and their mother did — more friction, more spark, the specific tension of two people whose personalities overlapped in ways that produced both recognition and irritation. But underneath it, Solar knew, was the same thing.

"Good," Chaerin said, to her desk.

Solar pushed off the doorframe. "Go to sleep at a reasonable hour."

"I always do."

"You never do."

"Goodnight, Solar," Chaerin said, with great dignity.

Solar went to her room smiling.

///

She was at her desk when it happened.

The Foundation Dinner had left her with approximately two hours of homework she had been putting off, which was unlike her and a sign of how full the weeks had gotten, and she was working through it with the focused efficiency she brought to everything academic when she heard the sound at her window.

She looked up.

There was a figure on the other side of the glass.

The sound that left her was not her most dignified moment — a sharp, involuntary yelp that she immediately muffled with both hands, spinning around in her chair to face the window fully, her heart slamming against her ribs with the enthusiasm of someone who had not been warned. The figure outside raised both hands in the universal gesture of I am not a threat, and even through the glass and the dark she recognized the outline of him — the jacket, the stillness, the complete and almost offensive calm of someone who had just appeared at a second-floor window and found the reaction he was producing mildly interesting.

She crossed the room in three steps and pushed the window open.

"What," she whispered, in the loudest whisper she could manage, "are you doing?"

"I needed to get out of the house," Sunwoo returned, at a volume that suggested he was not particularly concerned about being heard, which was somehow more alarming than if he had been. He was crouched on the narrow ledge outside her window with the easy balance of someone for whom precarious surfaces were entirely unremarkable. "The cameras are looped. No one's going to know."

She stared at him. "You glitched the security cameras."

"They're just playing back footage from last week," he confirmed. "Same time of night, same darkness. Nothing looks different."

She opened her mouth, closed it, looked at the window ledge he was balancing on, then back at his face, then at the drop to the ground below him, then back at his face. "Get in here before you fall," she hissed finally, stepping back to make room.

He climbed through the window with the specific economy of someone who had already worked out the logistics in advance — one hand on the frame, one leg over the sill, the rest of him following without incident — and landed on her bedroom floor with barely a sound. He straightened, and for a moment he just stood there, taking in the room around him the way he took in every new space — methodically, thoroughly, cataloguing it without appearing to.

Her room told a story she had never thought about from the outside. The bookshelves organized by subject, then alphabetically within each subject. The competition trophies lined up on the shelf above her desk with the self-consciousness of someone who was proud of them and slightly embarrassed to be proud of them. The corkboard above her desk covered in notes and schedules and color-coded tabs. The single photograph pinned to the corner of it — her and Chaerin and Juyeon on the steps of the mansion when Yein came home, all four of them looking down at the small bundle in Solar's arms.

Sunwoo looked at all of it without comment. Then he looked at her.

She looked back at him. Her heart was still doing something irregular.

"Why did you need to get out of the house?" she pressed, more quietly now.

"There's an event," he answered. "I didn't want to be there." He said it without elaboration, which was standard, and without apparent interest in her follow-up questions, which was also standard, and she looked at him for a moment and decided — as she often decided with Sunwoo — that there was a version of this conversation that would produce information and a version that would produce walls, and she was not in the mood for walls tonight.

She sat back down at her desk. He moved to sit on the edge of her bed, close enough that she could see him from her desk chair, and he looked at what she was working on.

"Math," he observed.

"Competition prep," she confirmed, with the specific tone of someone who had accepted their fate. "My tutor left me eight problems. I've done two."

He held out his hand.

She passed him the worksheet. He looked it over with that unhurried attention he gave to numbers, the kind of attention that made her feel slightly defensive about how long she had been staring at the same problems before he arrived, and then he set it on the bed beside him and picked up the spare pencil from her desk without asking.

He worked through the third problem in approximately forty-five seconds.

She watched him and said nothing and tried very hard to look less impressed than she was.

When he slid the worksheet back across to her, there was a small heart drawn in the margin beside the completed problem. She looked at it. She looked at him. He was already looking elsewhere, with the expression of someone who had definitely not just done that.

She turned back to the fourth problem and bit the inside of her cheek.

"Why didn't you want to be at the event?" she asked, after a moment.

"It's loud," he murmured, which was technically an answer.

She kept her eyes on the worksheet. "Is it a party?"

A pause, slightly longer this time. "Something like that."

She thought about that. She thought about the boys she hadn't met yet — the ones she knew existed in his life now but only vaguely and in outline — and she thought about what a party at Sunwoo's house might look like, and decided she probably wasn't ready to imagine it in detail.

"You could've just texted me," she pointed out.

"This was faster," he returned.

"You climbed up the side of my house."

"Yes."

"That's not faster."

"It's more direct," he amended, which was so entirely Sunwoo that she gave up trying to argue with it and went back to her worksheet.

They settled into a rhythm after that — Solar working through the problems one by one, Sunwoo on her bed, close enough to help when she got stuck and quiet enough not to crowd her when she didn't. He had pulled one knee up, rested his arm on it, and was looking at her with that quality of attention he brought to her specifically, the one she had never seen him bring to anything else — not inventory, not assessment, just a kind of focused and unhurried interest in whatever she happened to be doing at any given moment.

She had gotten used to it, mostly. There were still moments when she caught it and felt something warm move through her chest that she didn't have a name for.

"You're going to get it wrong," he said, from behind her.

She looked at problem six. "I'm not."

"Third step," he pointed out, without getting up.

She looked at the third step. She had, in fact, gotten it wrong. She erased it without comment and started again, and from the bed she heard the smallest possible sound that was not quite a laugh but was adjacent to one.

"Don't," she warned him.

"I didn't say anything," he replied, in the tone of someone who was very aware of exactly what they had done.

She finished the problem correctly and moved on without acknowledging him further, which was its own form of retaliation, and she could feel him not reacting to it in a way that was somehow louder than if he had.

By the time she finished the last problem, it was past midnight. The house was completely quiet around them — the specific, expensive quiet of the Lee mansion after hours, when the staff had long since gone to their quarters and her parents were asleep and the only sounds were the building settling into itself. She set her pencil down and turned in her chair.

He was still on her bed. At some point he had shifted — leaning back against her headboard now, his long legs stretched out, his phone in his hand but the screen dark, his eyes on her. The quality of his silence had changed over the course of the evening, she realized — it had moved from the careful, slightly performative calm he brought to new situations into something looser, something that wasn't performing anything at all. He looked, in the dim light of her desk lamp, like someone who had stopped trying to be somewhere else and had just arrived where he was.

She crossed to the bed and sat beside him, tucking her feet under her, close enough that their shoulders were almost touching. He didn't move away, which was its own answer.

"Do you like it?" she asked, quietly. "Being here."

He was quiet for a moment. His eyes moved around the room once more — the bookshelves, the trophies, the corkboard, the photograph — and then came back to her. "Yes," he said, simply, which from Sunwoo was the same as a great deal more.

She looked at her hands in her lap. "You can, you know," she murmured. "Come here. Whenever you need to get out." She paused, then added, because she wanted to be precise about it: "Not just when things are loud. Just — whenever."

He didn't respond immediately. She could feel him looking at her from the side, the weight of his attention that she had learned to recognize as the closest he got to saying something important.

"Sol," he said, finally.

She looked at him.

His expression was doing the thing it did — outwardly nothing, inwardly everything, the specific tension of someone for whom feelings were a language they had never been taught and were having to translate in real time. He held her gaze for a long moment, and then he reached out and tucked a strand of hair behind her ear with the same careful, deliberate attention he brought to things that mattered to him, and she went very still.

"Thank you," he said, quietly. "For letting me in."

She held his gaze and understood that he meant more than the window.

"Always," she told him.

The word landed between them and stayed there, warm and unqualified, and something in his expression shifted — brief and private, the way things moved through him when he felt them too much to contain — before he looked away and the moment folded itself closed, the way those moments always did with him, gently and without announcement.

Neither of them moved for a while after that. The desk lamp threw a small circle of light across the bed, and outside the window the Castle was dark and quiet, and Solar sat beside him in the particular peace of two people who have stopped needing to say anything and can simply be in a room together, and she thought this might be her favorite thing — this exact thing, this specific quiet with this specific person — in a way she wouldn't have had the words for even if someone had asked her to explain it.

Eventually he pushed off the headboard. He stood, and she stood too, and they faced each other in the small space beside her bed with the desk lamp between them, and she reached up on impulse and straightened the collar of his jacket, which didn't need straightening, and her hands stayed there a second too long.

He looked down at them. Then up at her.

She dropped her hands.

He crossed to the window without hurrying, and she followed and leaned against the frame while he climbed back out, reversing the careful economy of his entrance. When he was on the ledge he paused, and looked at her, and she looked at him, and neither of them said anything because everything that needed saying had already been said in a language that didn't require words.

Then he descended, and was gone, and she pushed the window closed and stood there with her forehead almost against the glass, looking at the dark below where he had been.

She went back to her desk. She picked up the worksheet — the eight completed problems, the small heart in the margin of the third — and folded it in half and put it in the back of her desk drawer, where she kept the others.

Then she turned off the desk lamp, and got into bed, and lay in the dark listening to the Castle's quiet, and thought about the word always and what it felt like to mean it completely.

She was asleep before she finished thinking.





Chapter Thirteen


2010 || NEW YORK

It became a thing, the way things between them always became things — gradually, and then completely, without either of them making a formal decision about it.

By October he was coming three or four times a week. By November it was more than that. The camera loop had become something he did automatically now, a reflex before he left the Kim property, and Solar had stopped being startled by the sound of him at the window the way she had been the first time. She still got up to let him in, but it was different now — less what are you doing here and more you're late, which was a shift that said everything about how quickly something could go from unprecedented to expected when it was the right thing.

She had told herself, at the beginning, that she should probably say something about it. That she should establish some kind of boundary, some limit, some acknowledgment that this was not a normal or particularly sensible arrangement. She had these conversations with herself regularly and they produced nothing, because the truth underneath all of them was that she didn't want to establish a limit. She wanted him there. It was that simple and that complicated simultaneously, and she had stopped pretending otherwise somewhere around the third week.

He would appear at her window and she would let him in, and they would exist in her room together for hours — Solar studying or reading or working on something, Sunwoo on her bed or in the chair by the window or occasionally on the floor against the wall when he was in one of his more contained moods, the quiet between them the comfortable kind that didn't need filling. He helped with her math when she needed it. He listened when she talked, in the specific, complete way he listened to her that she had never experienced from anyone else — not waiting for his turn to speak, just actually there, actually absorbing every word. And sometimes they didn't do anything at all except be in the same room, and that was enough, and the fact that it was enough was something she had stopped questioning.

The relationship had shifted in other ways, too. At school, they weren't hiding exactly — they had always been too careful for anything public — but something in the register had changed. His hand found hers in hallways, brief and certain, gone before anyone could make something of it. He stood closer than he needed to when they talked between classes. He had a particular way of positioning himself near her in group settings that wasn't possessive in any overt sense but communicated, very clearly, something about the arrangement of the world as he understood it. Their friends had long since stopped raising eyebrows. Even Chanhee, who had opinions about everything and expressed most of them, had settled into a resigned acceptance that expressed itself primarily through sighing.

It was the right word for it — electric. That was the only word that fit. The specific charge of two people who had decided on each other with the kind of completeness that left no room for uncertainty, that had stopped asking questions about the future because the future was simply assumed to contain the other person, and everything else was details.

///

She noticed the bruise on a Tuesday.

He had his jacket off — he ran warm, always, which she had noted years ago and had since catalogued as one of those small specific facts she kept about him — and his sleeves were pushed up, and when he reached across her desk to get the pencil she saw it. High on his forearm, deep purple shading into green at the edges, the kind that came from something substantial rather than something incidental. She had seen bruises on him before. This one was different in a way she couldn't immediately name.

She caught his wrist before he could pull his arm back.

He went still. Not tense — Sunwoo didn't go tense the way other people did — but still in the way he went still when he was deciding something.

"What happened?" she questioned, her thumb hovering over the bruise without touching it.

"Training," he answered, evenly.

"Sunwoo." She said it the way she said his full name, which was only when she meant it, only when she needed him to know she wasn't going to be managed. "This isn't from boxing."

He looked at his arm, then at her. His expression was doing the thing — neutral on the surface, something else underneath. "The training has gotten more intensive," he said, which was true in the technical sense that most things he said to her were true in a technical sense.

"More intensive how?"

"Just more." He gently extracted his wrist from her hand, not roughly, just concluded the moment. "I'm fine, Sol."

She looked at him. He looked back at her with those dark, patient eyes and she thought about how good he was at this — at being honest in the specific ways he chose and closed doors on everything else — and she thought about how much she hated it even as she understood it, and she thought about how the bruises were getting worse in a way that the boxing story was becoming increasingly insufficient to explain.

"You'd tell me," she said, carefully, "if something was actually wrong."

Something moved through his expression — very brief, very private. "Yeah," he murmured, which she filed away as neither a yes nor a no but something in between that she would return to later.

He picked up the pencil and went back to what he was doing, and she let him, because she had learned that there were moments to push and moments to wait, and this was a waiting moment. But she kept thinking about it — the bruise, the more intensive, the specific quality of the almost-answer he had given her — and she stored it with the other things she was storing, the accumulating evidence of a picture she couldn't quite see the whole of yet.

///

"The one at the gym is different," he mentioned, about a week later.

They were on her bed — properly on it now, the way they had migrated over the weeks from him on the edge and her at the desk to both of them against the headboard with her legs across his and his hand on her ankle, the evolution of proximity that happened between people who kept choosing to be close. She was reading. He was doing the thing where he looked like he was doing nothing but was actually thinking about several things simultaneously.

She lowered her book. "The one at the gym?"

"The motorcycle," he clarified, unhurriedly. "I have two. The one I use for work, and the one at the gym that I've been working on."

She looked at him with the particular attention she reserved for when he volunteered information, which was rare enough to warrant it. "What gym?"

A pause. "A place I have in Brooklyn."

"You have a place in Brooklyn," she repeated.

"Yes."

"You're fifteen."

"I'm aware," he returned, in the tone of someone who found the observation irrelevant.

She looked at him for a moment, at the complete equanimity with which he dropped information like I have a place in Brooklyn into a conversation and then waited to see what she did with it, and she thought about how she had been finding out things about Sunwoo in layers for five years and had still not found the bottom of it.

"What are you doing to it?" she asked. "The motorcycle."

Something changed almost imperceptibly in his expression — a slight softening around the eyes, the look he got when something mattered to him and he wasn't going to announce that it mattered. "Rebuilding it," he answered. "The engine. The frame. Everything."

"From scratch?"

"More or less."

She looked at him. "I want to see it," she told him, simply. "When it's done."

He looked back at her. "Okay," he said, after a moment, and the word had the quality of a promise, which from Sunwoo was the same as a certainty.

She went back to her book. His hand on her ankle was warm and present and she thought about a rebuilt motorcycle in a gym in Brooklyn and about all the things she was still learning about him and about how she thought she could spend the rest of her life doing exactly this and not run out.

///

Chaerin wanted her phone charger.

This was not, in itself, an unusual request — Chaerin had a habit of losing her charger on a rotating basis and operating on the assumption that Solar's was communal property, which Solar had been objecting to for years without any measurable effect. What was slightly unusual was that it was 10:15 on a Wednesday night, which meant Chaerin should have been asleep, or at least performing being asleep, and instead she was pushing Solar's door open without knocking — also not unusual, Chaerin had never knocked in her life — and walking in with the specific energy of someone who had decided what they needed and intended to collect it.

"Can I have your—"

She stopped.

Solar, who had heard the door and had approximately one second to process the situation, watched her twelve-year-old sister's eyes move from her, to the boy sitting against her headboard, and back to her, and had a brief and vivid experience of wishing the floor would open and consume her entirely.

Sunwoo looked at Chaerin. Chaerin looked at Sunwoo. The silence lasted exactly long enough to be absolutely devastating.

Then Chaerin's face broke into the widest, most unhinged smile Solar had ever seen on another human being, and she screamed.

Not loudly — she had enough presence of mind to clap both hands over her own mouth and reduce it to a muffled, sustained squeal — but it was a scream nonetheless, and her eyes above her hands were enormous with the delight of someone who had just received the best possible news. She turned around and turned back and turned around again, apparently unable to process the information while stationary, and Solar buried her face in her hands.

"Chaerin," she hissed.

"Oh my GOD," Chaerin whispered back, which was not an improvement.

"Keep your voice down," Solar begged, at the same volume. She looked at Sunwoo, who was watching this unfold with the patient, unsurprised expression of someone observing weather. "I'm so sorry," she mouthed at him.

He lifted one shoulder slightly, which meant it's fine, which was either genuine or deeply concerning, she couldn't tell.

"He's IN your ROOM," Chaerin was whispering, her hands still over her mouth, bouncing slightly on her heels. "In your room, on your bed, at ten o'clock at night, how long has this been happening?!"

"Chaerin—"

"Does Jaehyun know?"

"No—"

"Oh, he is going to LOSE IT—"

"He is not going to find out because you are going to go back to your room and forget you saw anything," Solar whispered, with as much authority as she could muster, which was undermined significantly by the fact that she was also approximately one second away from laughing.

Chaerin lowered her hands from her mouth. She looked at Sunwoo with the frank, assessing gaze of a twelve-year-old who had decided she was owed more information. "Hi," she announced, in a normal voice, because she had apparently decided whispering was over.

"Hey," Sunwoo returned, at the same volume, with the same equanimity he brought to everything.

"I'm Chaerin," she informed him, as though this required clarification. "Her younger sister. I've known about you for years, obviously." She paused. "You're taller than I thought."

"Chaerin," Solar said.

"What? He is." She looked back at Solar with an expression that managed to convey both I am going to be extracting information about this for the rest of our lives and I am very happy for you actually simultaneously, which was a Chaerin achievement. "Fine. I'll go." She held up a hand toward Solar. "Charger."

Solar pulled it out of the wall and handed it over without a word. Chaerin took it, gave Sunwoo one more long and entirely unsubtle look, and turned to leave.

She walked directly into Juyeon.

He was in the hallway, clearly having been woken up or curious about the noise, and he and Chaerin collided in the doorway with a muffled collision of arms and a suppressed yelp from both of them. Juyeon looked past Chaerin into the room, saw Solar, then saw Sunwoo, and his expression went through several phases in rapid succession — confusion, comprehension, and then the specific face of a fifteen-year-old boy who was recalibrating his understanding of a situation and wasn't sure yet how he felt about it.

"Is that—" he started.

"Yes," Chaerin whispered, grabbing his arm, "come on," and physically hauled him back from the doorway.

"Why is he—"

"I'll explain, come on—"

Their hushed, urgent argument retreated down the hallway, and Solar and Sunwoo were left in the relative quiet of her room, listening to the muffled back-and-forth of her siblings becoming incrementally less muffled rather than more as they moved away from her door, which suggested they were very bad at whispering.

Solar looked at Sunwoo. Sunwoo looked at Solar.

"Well," she said.

"Mm," he agreed.

She covered her face with both hands and laughed into them, silently and completely, her shoulders shaking, and when she looked up again there was the thing at the corner of his mouth that was close enough to a smile to count.

They had approximately forty-five seconds of peace before the door opened again.

Jaehyun.

He was in a t-shirt and sweatpants, which meant he had genuinely been asleep or close to it, and his expression when he took in the room was the expression of a sixteen-year-old who had been woken up by the sounds of his two younger siblings failing to be subtle and had arrived at several correct conclusions on the walk down the hall. He looked at Solar. He looked at Sunwoo. He looked at Solar again.

A long silence.

"How long," Jaehyun said, and it was not a question, it was the opening of an accounting.

"Jae—"

"How long has he been coming here."

Solar held her brother's gaze. "A while," she admitted, because lying to Jaehyun had always been more effort than it was worth and never worked anyway.

Jaehyun's jaw tightened. He looked at Sunwoo — really looked at him, with the specific, measuring attention of someone deciding something — and Sunwoo looked back with the composure he brought to everything, not challenging, not deferring, just present and steady and entirely unbothered by being examined.

"You glitch the cameras," Jaehyun said to him, flatly.

"Yes," Sunwoo confirmed.

"Every time."

"Yes."

Another silence. Jaehyun's expression was doing the complicated, layered thing it did when he was holding several emotions at once and sorting through which ones were relevant. He looked at his sister, then at the boy against her headboard, then at the window, then at the floor, then back at Solar.

"I don't love this," he told her, quietly.

"I know," she said, equally quietly.

"I'm also not an idiot," he continued, "so I know there's nothing I'm going to say tonight that changes anything."

She held his gaze. "Probably not," she agreed.

He exhaled slowly through his nose, the exhale of someone making peace with a situation they have not chosen and cannot change. Then he looked at Sunwoo one more time — not with hostility, exactly, but with the particular weight of an older brother communicating something that did not require words.

Sunwoo held his gaze without flinching. After a moment, he gave a small, barely perceptible nod — an acknowledgment rather than a concession, the gesture of someone who understood what was being communicated and was taking it seriously.

Something in Jaehyun's expression shifted. Not warmth, not yet, but something that was further from wariness than it had been thirty seconds ago.

"Don't stay too late," he said finally, to the room in general, and turned and walked back down the hall, pulling the door almost closed behind him.

Solar let out a breath she had been holding for approximately three minutes. She looked at Sunwoo.

He was looking at the door Jaehyun had just walked through, and there was something in his expression she didn't see very often — something evaluative and quiet, like he was filing Jaehyun away specifically, not as a threat, but as something that warranted attention.

"He's not going to say anything," Solar told him.

"I know," Sunwoo murmured. A pause. "He's good," he added, which from Sunwoo was the highest possible compliment and meant more than it sounded like.

She looked at him for a moment. "Yeah," she agreed. "He is."

From down the hall, very faintly, she could hear the ongoing whispered argument of Chaerin attempting to fully brief Juyeon on everything she had witnessed and Juyeon reacting to each piece of information in turn. It sounded like it was going to take a while.

Sunwoo looked at her. The corner of his mouth moved.

"Your family," he observed, which was not quite a complete sentence and communicated everything.

"I know," she sighed, which was also not quite a complete sentence and communicated everything back.

She settled against him — properly, her head against his shoulder, his arm finding its way around her with the easy naturalness of something they had been doing for a long time, which they more or less had — and outside her window the Castle was dark and quiet and expensive, and down the hall her siblings were terrible at whispering, and everything felt, in the specific and precarious way of fifteen, like exactly what it was supposed to be.







Chapter Fourteen

2010 || NEW YORK

They arrived on a Thursday.

Solar found out the way she found out most things that happened at Upper East Side High — through Chanhee, who found out through the specific and highly efficient information network of someone who paid close attention to everything while appearing to pay attention to nothing.

"Four new students," he announced, falling into step beside her in the main corridor before first period, his expression carrying the particular quality it got when he was delivering information he hadn't fully decided how to feel about yet. "All boys. All enrolling at the same time, which never happens. Nobody seems to know where they came from."

Solar kept walking. "I might know where they came from," she said, carefully.

Chanhee looked at her. "Sunwoo's brothers," he said, not a question, because Chanhee was very rarely asking questions when he appeared to be asking questions.

"I think so," she confirmed.

He was quiet for a moment, which from Chanhee meant he was composing something. "How many brothers does he have?" he asked finally.

"Four, apparently."

Another pause. "Solar," he said, in the tone he reserved for when he was choosing his words with more care than usual, "how does someone end up with four adopted brothers all the same age?"

She didn't have an answer for that. She had been not-asking herself the same question for a while now, in the specific way she had developed for questions about Sunwoo that she wasn't ready to receive the answers to. She kept walking.

"I don't know," she said, which was true and also not the whole truth, and Chanhee knew her well enough to know the difference, and was also, on this particular morning, kind enough not to press it.

///

She saw them before she was formally introduced.

It was impossible not to — four new faces at Upper East Side High was an event in itself, in the way that anything unusual was an event in an environment this controlled and this consistent, and the school had absorbed their arrival with the specific, performative casualness of a place that prided itself on being unimpressed by things while actually being very impressed by things. People noticed. People talked. By second period the basic facts had circulated through most of the junior class, and by lunch the speculation had gotten considerably more creative.

Solar had been watching them, when she could do it without being obvious about it, and what she noticed was this: they moved through the school like people who were used to being in unfamiliar environments and had developed, through practice, a very specific way of handling it. Not nervous. Not performing ease the way Castle kids performed ease. Just — attentive. Watchful in the way of people for whom watching was a skill rather than a habit.

All four of them, in different ways.

The broad-shouldered one moved through crowds with quiet efficiency, finding lines of travel that didn't require him to interact unnecessarily. The sharp-featured one with the dark eyes tracked the room constantly, clocking exits and faces with the rapid attention of someone conducting surveillance. The smaller, more careful one stayed near the edges of things, watching more than participating, present but contained.

And then there was the fourth one.

The fourth one was talking to a girl by the lockers, having apparently decided within his first two hours at Upper East Side High that the school was a fundamentally welcoming place and that most of its inhabitants were people he was interested in knowing. He had a quality about him that Solar recognized immediately and filed under this is going to be a problem for Sunwoo — a loose, warm, unself-conscious charm that operated without apparent effort, the kind that certain people were simply born with and had never needed to learn.

She watched the girl by the lockers laugh at something he said and thought, not for the first time that morning, that Sunwoo had made a very specific set of choices in who he had chosen to bring into his life.

///

The introduction happened at lunch, which was where most things at Upper East Side High happened.

Sunwoo appeared at her table — he did this sometimes, migrating from wherever he had been sitting to wherever she was without making it a thing, which had stopped being remarkable to their friend group sometime around March — and behind him came all four of them, carrying trays with the varying degrees of comfort of people who had decided, or been instructed, that this was what was happening now.

Solar's table received this development with its characteristic range of reactions. Kevin immediately made room and introduced himself to everyone within arm's reach with the enthusiasm of someone who had been personally waiting for this. Jacob offered a warm, quiet greeting that made each of them feel individually seen, which was a Jacob skill she had always marveled at. Chanhee assessed the new arrivals with the careful, unhurried attention of someone conducting an interview he hadn't agreed to participate in. Changmin looked up from his book, nodded at each of them in turn with the gentle composure he brought to everything, and went back to his book.

"These are my brothers," Sunwoo said, which was the most words he typically offered in a group introduction, and then he sat down beside Solar and appeared to consider the matter concluded.

She looked at the four of them. They looked back at her with varying degrees of attention — the broad-shouldered one measured and still, the sharp-featured one quick and assessing, the careful one taking her in from a slight distance.

And the fourth one was already grinning.

"You're Solar," he announced, with the confidence of someone who had done research, settling into the seat across from her without waiting to be invited. "He talks about you."

Sunwoo, who had just picked up his fork, said nothing. Something in his jaw, however, suggested he had noted the comment and was filing it for later.

"He doesn't talk about much," the fourth one continued, pleasantly, as if Sunwoo's silence was simply part of the texture of the conversation, "but he talks about you. I'm Hyunjoon." He stuck out his hand across the table with the easy confidence of someone for whom social conventions were a game he had decided to win. "It's really nice to finally meet you."

She shook his hand, and she was smiling despite herself, because Hyunjoon had that specific and slightly maddening quality of making you smile before you had decided to. "Solar," she confirmed, unnecessarily. "These are my friends — Chanhee, Jacob, Changmin, Kevin."

Hyunjoon turned the full force of his attention on each of them in turn, which Kevin received with absolute delight, Chanhee received with the expression of someone being assessed by a mirror and finding it unsettling, and Jacob received with a quiet, interested smile.

"Sangyeon," the broad-shouldered one said, from further down the table, which was apparently his entire contribution to the introduction, and it had a settled, complete quality to it that suggested he felt no need to add more.

"Hyunjae," the sharp-featured one offered, briefly, looking at her with those dark assessing eyes before his attention moved elsewhere.

The careful one at the end of the table looked at her for a moment. "Eric," he said, quietly. He had the specific quality of someone who was deciding how much to give in real time, and what he gave — just the name, offered plainly — had a weight to it that the brevity didn't diminish.

"It's really good to meet all of you," she told them, and meant it, and watched something small and almost imperceptible pass through each of them at that — the specific response of people who were not often told things like that by people who meant them.

She looked at Sunwoo. He was eating, apparently unbothered, but his shoulder was pressed against hers in the particular way that communicated something she had learned to read a long time ago.

See, it said. This is mine, and you're part of it, and I wanted you to know.

She pressed her shoulder back against his, barely perceptibly, and reached for her water glass.

///

"So," Hyunjoon was saying, approximately fifteen minutes later, having migrated to the seat directly beside her while she wasn't paying full attention, "are you always this focused, or is it a school thing?"

She looked up from her notes, which she had pulled out because she had a quiz next period and the lunch period was not going to waste itself. "What?"

"The notes," he clarified, nodding at them with what appeared to be genuine curiosity. "Like, on your lunch break. Very dedicated."

"I have a quiz," she explained.

"Ah." He leaned slightly, apparently reading the top of the page. "Economics?"

"AP Economics."

He made a sound that was almost impressed. "He said you were smart," he remarked, conversationally, and Solar had just enough time to register the he before Hyunjoon had apparently decided that the notes were interesting and had leaned further, his arm coming to rest on the back of her chair, his head tilting toward hers to get a better look at the page.

"I mean, I don't know anything about this, but it looks—"

The back of his shirt moved.

It was one motion — swift, practiced, completely without drama. Sunwoo's hand had closed around the fabric and pulled, and Hyunjoon was suddenly six inches further from Solar than he had been a moment ago, his arm no longer on the back of her chair, his body repositioned with the efficiency of something being redirected rather than confronted.

Hyunjoon blinked. Looked at Sunwoo.

Sunwoo had already let go. He was looking at his tray. When he spoke, his voice had the same flat, unhurried quality it always had — no edge, no heat, just a statement of fact delivered with complete composure.

"She's not for you," he said, and picked up his fork again.

A silence fell over that particular corner of the table. Solar felt Chanhee's eyes on her from across it and did not look at him, because she already knew the expression he was wearing and she was trying not to laugh.

Hyunjoon looked at Sunwoo. Then at Solar. Then back at Sunwoo. Something moved across his face — not chastened, exactly, but recalibrating, the rapid reassessment of someone who had just received clear information and was deciding what to do with it. Then, slowly, a grin spread over his face — not embarrassed, not defensive, just genuinely, helplessly amused.

"Noted," he announced, to no one in particular, and slid back down the table to a more appropriate distance, still grinning, apparently finding the entire situation excellent.

Sangyeon, from his end of the table, looked at Hyunjoon with the expression of someone who had been expecting exactly this and was not surprised to have been right. Eric was looking at his tray with the careful neutrality of someone who had definitely noticed and had decided that neutrality was the correct posture. Hyunjae's expression suggested he was filing this interaction away for future reference.

Solar looked at Sunwoo. He was eating with the composure of someone for whom nothing of note had just occurred.

She looked back at her notes. The corner of her mouth was doing something she couldn't entirely control.

"You okay?" Sunwoo asked, without looking at her.

"Completely fine," she confirmed, which was not entirely accurate, but in the specific direction of being too fine rather than not fine enough, so she let it stand.

///

That evening, when he came through her window and they had settled into their usual configuration — her at the desk, him on the bed, the room quiet and lamplit around them — she found herself thinking about the lunch table. About four boys who had appeared out of nowhere and somehow made complete sense, and about the version of Sunwoo's life she was still only seeing the edges of.

"Your father took them all in," she said, not quite a question, turning slightly in her chair to look at him. "Four of them. All around our age."

"Yes," he confirmed, from the bed.

"That's — a lot," she observed, carefully. "How is he managing it?"

Something shifted almost imperceptibly in Sunwoo's expression — a brief internal movement she would have missed if she hadn't been watching. "Fine," he said. "It's not — it works."

She nodded slowly, turning this over. Then, because it had been sitting at the back of her mind all day and the lunch table had dislodged it: "How's your mom with all of it?" she asked, gently. It wasn't the first time she had asked about Dahee Kim over the years — she had asked a handful of times, always getting some variation of nothing, always noticing the way the question landed differently than other questions did. "I realized I never hear you talk about her. Not for a long time now."
