The pause this time was fractionally longer than his pauses usually were.

"She's fine," he said, flatly.

She looked at him. He was looking at the ceiling — that particular upward gaze he used when he was somewhere she couldn't follow — and his expression was the neutral one, sealed and still. She thought about pressing. She thought about the specific quality of she's fine, how quickly it had come, how little it contained, and she thought about all the other times she had asked and received some version of the same answer, and she thought about the fact that she had not seen or heard anything about Dahee Kim in years, and that Sunwoo's father was apparently now the legal guardian of four additional boys, and that none of it quite —

She turned back to her desk.

"Okay," she said, quietly.

She did not see the thing that moved across his face in the second after she looked away. It lasted only a moment — something surfacing and being pressed back down with the practiced efficiency of someone who had been doing it long enough that the motion was nearly automatic — and then his expression was the neutral one again, and he was looking at the ceiling, and the room was quiet, and he was pretending she was fine the way he always pretended, quickly and quietly and with just enough conviction to make it hold for a little while longer.

It was the only kindness he knew how to give himself.

She went back to her homework. He stayed on her bed. The room held them both in its small lamplit quiet, and outside the Castle was dark and still, and everything that was coming was still coming, and for now they were just here, and that was enough, and he let it be enough, and tried not to think about the rest.

///

She started noticing not long after.

Not all at once — it was incremental, the way most things about Sunwoo were incremental. A pattern she tracked in small data points, the way she tracked everything about him, until the data points accumulated into something she couldn't look away from.

He would excuse himself from class. A raised hand, a quiet word to the teacher, and then he would be gone — fifteen minutes, sometimes twenty — before returning with the specific quality of someone who had been reset. Calmer. More settled. Like something wound too tight had been released just enough to be manageable again. She knew that quality. She had been watching his tension levels for years.

Hyunjoon did it too. The first time she clocked it was a Tuesday, third period, when he raised his hand in the middle of AP History and slipped out with the easy, unbothered confidence he brought to everything. He was back in eighteen minutes, and when he dropped back into his seat he rolled his neck once, slowly, and then sat forward and looked at the board with the particular settled quality she had already learned to recognize from somewhere else.

Eric was subtler about it. He waited until class transitions, or found reasons to be in the hallway, and he was gone for shorter stretches — ten minutes, never more. But the quality when he came back was the same. The same reset. The same release of something that had been held too tightly.

Three boys. The same pattern. The same specific calm afterward that had nothing to do with getting air or using the bathroom or any of the other things a person might excuse themselves for.

The third time she watched Sunwoo slip out, she turned back to the front of the class and stared at the board without reading a single word on it.

///

The bathroom at the end of the east corridor was the one nobody used.

Sunwoo knew this because he had made it his business to know things like that — the empty spaces, the unwatched corners, the places in any environment where a person could exist for twenty minutes without being accounted for. He locked the door and stood at the sink and looked at himself in the mirror for a moment, which he did less and less as time went on because there was less and less in the mirror that he found useful to look at.

He reached into his pocket.

It had started with the pills — something mild, something that took the edge off the pressure without taking anything else with it. That had lasted maybe two months before the edge had gotten sharper than the pills could reach. Then something stronger. Then stronger than that. He moved through things quickly, he had noticed, which he supposed made sense given how quickly his body moved through everything else — how fast he healed, how fast he built endurance, how fast he adapted. The same quality that made him an exceptional fighter made him an exceptionally efficient user, which was not a thought he spent time on.

He wasn't using to feel good. He wanted to be precise about that, at least in his own understanding of it. He was using to function — to create enough distance between himself and the accumulation of things pressing on him that he could do what needed to be done without the weight of it compromising his performance. That was all it was. A tool. A practical decision, made with the same practical logic he applied to everything.

He told himself this with the same conviction he told himself his mother was fine.

The thing about conviction, he was finding, was that it got easier to maintain the less you examined it.

He unlocked the bathroom door and walked back to class, and when he slid into his seat Solar glanced at him from two rows over with those green eyes that missed nothing, and he met her gaze with the neutral expression he had spent fifteen years perfecting, and she looked at him for one second longer than she normally did before looking back at the board.

He looked at the board too.

He thought about the first operation, which his father had told him was happening before the end of the year. He thought about the training that evening, which was going to be brutal, which they all were now. He thought about Solar's room and the lamplit quiet of it, which was the one place in his life where the pressure had a different quality — not gone, but held at a distance, like it was waiting respectfully outside the door.

He thought about her face when she asked about his mother.

He didn't think about that for long.

He looked at the board and waited for the class to end and thought about nothing at all, which was getting easier, which he supposed was either a good sign or a bad one, and he had stopped being able to tell the difference.



Chapter Fifteen

2010 || NEW YORK

The man his father sent was named Choi.

No first name offered, none requested — he arrived at Greenwood on the first Tuesday of every other week in a car that didn't belong in Brooklyn and a jacket that suggested he had spent a significant portion of his life in environments where jackets like that were standard issue. He was somewhere in his late forties, built like someone who had once been exceptional and had maintained most of it out of habit, and he communicated almost exclusively through demonstration and correction rather than explanation, which Sunwoo respected.

He also hit harder than anyone Sunwoo had trained with before, which Sunwoo found useful.

The sessions with Choi were different from everything else — different from the work Sunwoo did with the members, different from the solo training he maintained at hours when the gym was empty and the Brooklyn streets outside were quiet. Choi trained him the way you trained someone you intended to deploy, with the specific, unsentimental thoroughness of a person preparing a tool for a function. Scenario work. Stress positions. The kind of endurance training that was designed not to test how long you lasted but to find the exact point where you stopped being able to think clearly, and then make you think clearly anyway.

The members watched these sessions when their own training was done, from the edges of the gym, with the varying expressions of people witnessing something they found both impressive and slightly alarming.

"He doesn't make any noise," Eric observed once, quietly, to no one in particular, watching Sunwoo take a hit that had made Hyunjoon wince from across the room.

"He never does," Sangyeon replied, with the matter-of-fact tone of someone stating an established fact.

"Doesn't it hurt?" Eric pressed, which was the genuine question, the one underneath the observation.

Sangyeon considered this for a moment. "I think it hurts," he said finally. "I just don't think he lets it matter."

Eric looked at Sunwoo, who was already back in position, expression unchanged, waiting for Choi to come again. He was quiet for a moment. "That's terrifying," he concluded.

"Yeah," Sangyeon agreed, and went back to what he was doing.

///

The regular sessions — the ones Sunwoo ran himself, without Choi — had changed in the months since the gang had assembled into something approaching its final shape.

The physical work was the foundation and always would be, but it had expanded outward into things that required a different kind of attention. Tactical reasoning. How to move through a space without being seen. How to assess a situation faster than the situation was developing. How to make a decision when all the options were bad and the clock was already running.

Sunwoo taught the way Choi taught — through demonstration and correction, with minimal explanation of the reasoning unless the reasoning was specifically asked for, which it sometimes was. Hyunjae asked the most questions, sharp and specific ones that went straight to the operational logic of whatever they were working on. Sangyeon asked the fewest, preferring to observe and repeat until he had it rather than asking to have it explained. Hyunjoon asked questions primarily to be asking them, with the specific energy of someone who found silence uncomfortable and had decided that curiosity was a more charming alternative to admitting it. Eric asked careful questions, the kind that revealed he had been thinking about something for a while before he brought it forward.

They were all getting better. Sunwoo tracked this with the same practical attention he brought to everything — noting what each of them had improved, where the gaps were, what still needed work. Sangyeon's physical capacity was exceptional and his tactical instincts were developing faster than Sunwoo had expected. Hyunjae's reflexes were extraordinary, the best in the group after Sunwoo, though his impulse control under pressure was still something they were working on. Eric was methodical and quietly relentless, the kind of fighter who didn't have Hyunjae's speed or Sangyeon's power but would simply not stop, which was its own formidable quality. Hyunjoon was the wildcard — less naturally disciplined than the others, but possessed of an instinctive read on people and situations that was genuinely difficult to teach and that Sunwoo had already begun thinking about how to use.

None of them were where they needed to be yet.

His father had made clear that before the end of the year was not a suggestion.

///

The motorcycle was in pieces when he needed it to be and assembled when he didn't, which was the nature of the work — you couldn't do everything at once, so you did what you could in the time you had, and you came back to it.

He had bought the frame from a salvage yard in Bushwick for almost nothing, which was partly why he had chosen it. The engine he had sourced separately, and the engine was the part that had taken the most time, the most thought, the most precise and patient attention, because the engine was the thing that mattered most and he was not going to do it carelessly. Everything else — the bodywork, the wiring, the components that made it what it was going to be rather than what it had been — was further behind, but moving.

He worked on it in the evenings, mostly, when the members had finished their sessions and the gym had settled into its after-hours quiet. The work required a specific kind of focus that he had come to value — not the combat focus, not the operational focus, but something quieter and more patient, something that asked only that he pay attention to the thing directly in front of him and nothing else. It was, he had realized some time ago, the closest thing he had to rest.

Hyunjoon had taken to sitting on a crate nearby while he worked, not helping — Sunwoo hadn't asked him to and he hadn't offered — but present, filling the quiet with a low, steady stream of commentary about whatever was on his mind, which was always several things simultaneously. Sunwoo had initially expected to find this irritating and had been mildly surprised to discover that he didn't. Hyunjoon's voice had a quality that didn't demand response, that moved through the air without requiring anything of the space it occupied, and after a while Sunwoo had stopped registering it as noise and started registering it as something closer to ambient sound. Like weather. Like the city outside.

"You know what I've been thinking about," Hyunjoon announced one evening, from his crate.

"No," Sunwoo said, because he didn't, and because with Hyunjoon this was always the correct response to wait for.

"Food," Hyunjoon decided. "Specifically, what we're going to eat after training tomorrow because Sangyeon said he'd cook but he always says he'll cook and then something comes up and Eric ends up making ramen which, I have nothing against ramen, I want to be clear about that, but it's the fourth time this week—"

"Sangyeon will cook," Sunwoo said.

"You don't know that."

"I'll tell him to."

A pause. "Oh," Hyunjoon said, and then: "Yeah, that'll work." He was quiet for approximately twelve seconds, which was a record. "What are you doing to that part right now?"

"Timing chain."

"Is that important?"

"Everything is important."

"Right, but like — scale of one to ten."

Sunwoo looked at the timing chain. "Nine," he said.

Hyunjoon made a sound of genuine interest, leaning forward on his crate to get a better look, and Sunwoo found himself, not for the first time, thinking that Hyunjoon's curiosity was one of his most useful qualities — not the performed curiosity he deployed socially, which was its own tool, but this version of it, the genuine one, the one that came out in the evenings in the quiet of the gym when there was no one to perform for.

He handed him the flashlight without being asked, because he needed more light and Hyunjoon was already leaning forward anyway.

Hyunjoon held it without comment, angling it where it was needed, and the work continued, and neither of them said anything for a while, and that was fine too.

///

The supply was their own, which meant the supply was available, which meant the line between managing it and using it had become thinner than Sunwoo had intended it to become.

He had noticed this in himself before he noticed it in the others, which was how he noticed most things — first in himself, with the dispassionate attention he brought to his own performance, the way a mechanic noticed a problem in an engine before it became something larger. He was using more than he had been. The intervals were shorter. The things that had worked before were working less efficiently, which meant he was compensating, which meant the compensation was becoming the new baseline, which was a pattern he recognized from watching other people and had not expected to have to recognize from watching himself.

He had not expected a lot of things about this particular situation.

The members had started, too — carefully, at first, the way people started things they weren't sure they were allowed to want. Hyunjoon had asked first, with the easy directness he brought to most requests, framing it as curiosity rather than need. Eric had said nothing and simply been present one evening when Sunwoo was using, and the look on his face had not required interpretation. Sangyeon had waited the longest, and when he finally asked it had been in the same quiet, direct way he asked everything, without apology or performance.

Hyunjae had not asked. He had simply helped himself one afternoon when he thought Sunwoo wasn't paying attention, and Sunwoo had paid attention and said nothing, which was its own kind of answer.

He told himself it was manageable. He told himself he would address it before the operation, because going into something like that compromised was not an option and he knew it. He told himself the line was still visible, that he could still see it, that knowing where it was meant he hadn't crossed it.

He was getting better at telling himself things.

///

It was a Thursday evening in the gym, after training, when it happened.

Not anything dramatic. Not a confrontation or a revelation or a moment anyone would have been able to point to afterward and name. It was smaller than that, and more real for being smaller.

Sangyeon had cooked — Sunwoo had told him to, as promised — and the five of them were scattered around the main room of Greenwood in the loose, post-training configuration they had developed over months of existing in the same space. Hyunjoon was on the ring apron, where he spent a significant portion of his non-training time, eating with the contentment of someone whose primary concerns had been addressed. Eric was on the floor against the wall, which was still where he went when he was in a lower-energy state, though he had migrated progressively further from the door over time, which Sunwoo had noted and said nothing about. Hyunjae was lying on his back on one of the mats, looking at the ceiling, which was Hyunjae's version of decompressing. Sangyeon was at the makeshift table near the back, eating with the focused satisfaction of someone who had made the food and was therefore allowed to enjoy it most.

Sunwoo was near the motorcycle, not working on it, just present beside it, eating in the standing position he defaulted to when he wasn't thinking about it.

Nobody was talking. The gym was quiet except for the sounds of five people existing in it, and the distant low sounds of Brooklyn outside the high windows, and the occasional creak of the building settling.

Then Eric said, out of nowhere, in the flat and slightly bewildered tone of someone reporting something they were still processing: "I've never had a home-cooked meal before tonight."

The room absorbed this.

Sangyeon looked at him from the table. Not with pity — Sangyeon didn't do pity — but with the direct, quiet attention he gave to things that deserved it. "You hadn't?" he said.

"No," Eric confirmed, with the matter-of-factness of someone stating a fact about the weather. "Orphanage food. Foster family food." A pause. "This is different."

Another silence. Hyunjoon, who had been in the middle of taking another bite, stopped and looked at Eric with an expression that was entirely genuine — no performance, no charm deployed, just Hyunjoon without his armor, which was something that didn't appear very often.

"It is different," he said, simply.

Hyunjae had stopped looking at the ceiling. He was looking at Eric now, with the dark, assessing eyes that were usually running calculations, and whatever calculation he was running landed somewhere that made something in his expression go briefly and completely unguarded.

"Sangyeon's good at it," Hyunjae offered, after a moment, which from Hyunjae — who gave out compliments approximately never — was a significant statement.

Sangyeon received this with the same equanimity he received everything. "I had to learn," he said, which was not an explanation exactly but contained one.

Sunwoo was looking at the motorcycle. He was listening to all of it — he always listened, even when he appeared not to — and he was thinking about a cold, precise house where meals appeared because staff produced them, where nothing was made for you specifically, where food was fuel and occasion and performance and nothing that resembled what was happening in this room right now.

He didn't say anything. He didn't need to.

But he ate the rest of his food standing next to his motorcycle in the quiet Brooklyn gym, and he thought that his father had told him to find people who knew what hard looked like, and he thought that his father had been right about that and wrong about everything else, and he thought that this — this specific room, these specific people, this particular Thursday evening — was something he hadn't been prepared for and didn't have a name for yet.

He thought he might not need one.



Chapter Sixteen

2010 || NEW YORK

She had been trying to find the right moment for weeks.

That was the thing about Solar Lee — she was not someone who acted without thinking, not about things that mattered. She turned them over, examined them from every angle, considered the timing and the approach and the most likely outcomes, and then she acted. It was the same quality that made her exceptional at academics and at the particular social mathematics of the Castle, and it was the quality that had been failing her completely where Sunwoo was concerned, because the problem with turning something over long enough was that you could start to see every reason not to do it.

She knew what she was watching. She had known for a while now, in the specific way you could know something without having confirmed it — the way you could carry a suspicion like a stone in your pocket and feel the weight of it every time you moved. The pattern was undeniable. The reset quality when he came back from wherever he went. The shorter intervals between times he needed to leave. The tremor she had caught in his hand once, just briefly, gone before she could name it.

She had told herself she would find the right moment.

She found it, and it wasn't the right moment at all.

///

He had been irritable all morning in the way he was only rarely irritable — not the cool, composed blankness he maintained as a default, but something underneath it that was pushing through despite his best efforts. She noticed it the way she noticed everything about him, immediately and without trying, and she spent the first two periods of the school day watching it and deciding what to do with it.

By third period, she had decided.

She saw him slip out — the raised hand, the quiet word, the door closing behind him — and she waited approximately forty-five seconds before she gathered her things and followed, earning a look from the teacher that she responded to with an expression of such complete and earnest apology that it produced, as it almost always did, immediate forgiveness.

The hallway was mostly empty. She moved through it quickly, pushing through the students that remained, scanning ahead, and she heard her own voice calling his name before she had consciously decided to call it.

"Sunwoo!"

Heads turned. She was aware of this and did not care about it, because there were things that mattered more than being the talk of Upper East Side High, and this was one of them. She pushed through the back doors of the building and the cool air hit her, and there he was — sitting on the concrete steps, his elbows on his knees, his eyes fixed on nothing in particular.

She crossed to him and sat down on the step beside him, close enough that their shoulders nearly touched.

"Hey," she said carefully, reaching out to touch his shoulder. "What's up with you? Are you okay?"

He didn't say anything. Didn't look at her. His dark eyes were fixed ahead, flat and distant in the way they only got when something was pressing on him harder than he could manage cleanly. His foot was tapping against the concrete — a rhythm she had never seen from him before, involuntary and impatient, like his body was doing something he hadn't told it to do.

The second her fingers grazed his jacket, his eyes snapped to her.

The abruptness of it made her flinch slightly — not afraid, just startled — and she held his gaze and asked again, more quietly: "What's going on, Sunwoo?"

Something moved through his expression. The same thing she had watched happen a hundred times — him arriving at the edge of something and deciding how far over it he was willing to go. He looked away from her, and she watched him bury his face in his hands, exhaling hard through his palms before they dropped back into his lap.

A silence fell. Then he reached into his jacket pocket and pulled out a cigarette and a lighter, lit it, took a long slow drag, and blew the smoke out into the air in front of them in a thin, steady stream. When he finally spoke, his voice was flat and unhurried.

"I'm just having some problems, Sol," he muttered, eyes forward. "Nothing you need to worry about."

"What problems?" she pressed, evenly. "You can talk to me. That's what I'm here for."

He took another drag. Silence. And then, with the specific resignation of someone who has realized the door they've been keeping closed has already been seen:

"I'm having some rough withdrawal right now," he said. "It happens sometimes, when I haven't had anything in a while." He paused, took another slow hit of the cigarette, watching the smoke dissipate. "The guys and I — we use sometimes. To get through things. It helps with the stress." He stopped there, not elaborating on what things meant, not opening any further than that.

Solar stared at him. She was processing — she could feel herself processing — trying to find where to put what he had just told her.

"Use what?" she asked.

He looked at her then. There was something in his expression — not quite pity, but the complicated look of someone who has spent a long time protecting someone from a truth and has just run out of the ability to continue. He shook his head slightly, and despite the weight of the moment, something in it was almost fond.

"Drugs, Sol," he said, quietly. "Hard drugs. Heroin — the kind that goes straight to the vein." He dropped the cigarette onto the step below and pressed his foot over it, extinguishing it. "It started small. I tried to stay away from it at first. But once you start—" he stopped, turned the words over, "it gets difficult. The withdrawal is nothing I can't manage. I'm fine."

The word landed in her chest and stayed there.

Heroin.

She turned it over in her mind, slow and careful, trying to understand it the way she understood things she had no framework for. She knew the word from school, from the brief and alarming units that covered it in broad strokes. She knew it was injected. She couldn't imagine — genuinely couldn't construct the image — of Sunwoo doing that to himself, and yet she was sitting next to him and he had just told her that he had been, and the evidence of it was all around her in ways she now understood she had been watching for weeks without knowing what she was looking at.

"Heroin," she echoed, under her breath.

"Sol—"

"What's it—" she had started to ask something and then her eyes dropped to his hand and the question dissolved entirely. "Sunwoo," she said, and her voice came out sharper than she intended — more alarmed, "you're shaking."

She had reached out to touch his hand and found it trembling beneath her fingers, not violently but unmistakably, a fine and steady tremor running through him every few seconds like a current.

He pulled his hand away. "I'm fine," he said immediately. "Just a little cold."

She looked at him. At the flat, practiced evenness of his expression. At the foot that had been tapping against the concrete and had gone still the moment she noticed it. At the way he was sitting — contained, composed, working very hard to appear as though none of this cost him anything.

"What's it like?" she asked, quietly. The question surprised even her, coming out before she had decided to ask it.

His brown eyes moved to her slowly. He looked at her for a long moment — at her face, at whatever she was showing him — and his expression did something she couldn't fully name.

"I hope you never find out, Sol," he said.

She held his gaze. The air moved around them, cool and indifferent, and the sounds of the school day continued on the other side of the building's back doors, and she sat beside him on the concrete steps and thought about all the things she had been watching and waiting and filing away, and she thought about how the stone she had been carrying in her pocket had just gotten considerably heavier.

She didn't say anything else for a while. Neither did he.

She just stayed.

///

She was in the library when Hyunjoon found her.

Most of the students were gone — it was well past the end of the school day, the building settling into its after-hours quiet. She had stayed to study, which was a thing she genuinely did rather than a cover, and she had been staring at the same page of her economics textbook for a length of time she wasn't prepared to admit when she heard the chair across from her being dragged out with absolutely no regard for the library's ambiance.

She looked up.

Hyunjoon dropped into the chair with the energy of someone arriving at a party he had thrown himself, leaning back and planting both hands flat on the table like he was calling a meeting to order. He was wearing that expression of his — the warm, slightly dangerous one that meant he had decided something and was going to say it whether she was ready or not.

"You look terrible, hon," he announced, at a volume that was entirely inappropriate for a library and that he appeared completely unbothered by.

"Thank you," she said flatly.

"I mean it in the nicest possible way," he clarified, which was not a clarification. He looked at the textbook, then at her, and tilted his head. "You haven't turned that page since I got here."

"I was thinking."

"About him," Hyunjoon said, not a question, with the easy certainty of someone stating a fact about the weather.

She closed the textbook. She looked at him properly — at the specific quality of his expression, which had dropped the performance and gone somewhere more genuine underneath it. Hyunjoon without his armor was a different register entirely, and it didn't appear often enough that she could be casual about it when it did.

"How much do you know?" he asked, leaning forward now, his voice dropping to something that was actually appropriate for the space. "About what's going on with him?"

"I found out about the drugs today," she said, carefully. "He told me."

Hyunjoon's expression did something complicated — not surprise exactly, more like the confirmation of something he had been waiting for. He exhaled through his nose and looked at the table for a second, his fingers drumming once against the surface, the rings he wore catching the library light.

"Okay," he murmured. Then he looked back up at her, and his eyes were doing the genuine thing, the real Hyunjoon behind all the charm. "Sweetie, that's — that's one piece of it."

She held his gaze. "One piece."

"Yeah." He spread his hands slightly, a gesture that managed to convey both I want to tell you and I really cannot tell you simultaneously. "Look — I'm not going to tell you the rest, because it's not mine to say and he would actually kill me. I want to be clear about that." He said it with the lightness of someone making a joke and the steadiness of someone who meant it. "But you should know that it's there. The rest of it. There's a lot more than what he told you today, Solar."

She stared at him. All the things she had been filing — the gym in Brooklyn, the two motorcycles, the four brothers who had appeared from nowhere, the training that left bruises that boxing had never been able to explain, the things Hyunjoon himself did when he slipped out of class and came back reset — all of it rearranged itself in her mind into a shape she had been refusing to look at directly.

"It's a lot, isn't it," she said. Not a question.

Hyunjoon looked at her steadily, and for once he didn't soften it. "Yeah, babe," he confirmed, quietly. "It's a lot."

She sat with that for a moment. Outside the library windows, the afternoon light was going grey, and she thought she had seen a glimpse of Sunwoo's jacket in the hallway earlier — he was still somewhere in the building, she was almost certain of it — and she thought about what Hyunjoon had just handed her and what she was going to do with it.

"Why are you telling me this?" she asked him.

He leaned back in the chair again, tilting it onto two legs, and the performance came back slightly — that particular Hyunjoon ease, the one he wore like a second jacket. "Because you're going to figure it out eventually anyway, and I'd rather you go get it from him than spend six more months carrying around half a picture," he told her plainly. "And because—" he stopped, tipped the chair back down, and looked at her with that genuine expression again, "— he talks about you, you know. Like constantly. It's honestly a little embarrassing for him." The corner of his mouth pulled. "Whatever the rest of it is — he's not someone who lets people in, Solar. Like, ever. You should know that."

She held his gaze for a long moment. Then, quietly: "Thank you, Hyunjoon."

He pointed a finger at her across the table, the gesture loose and easy. "You did not hear any of this from me," he informed her, with complete solemnity.

"Obviously," she agreed.

He grinned — wide and immediate, the one that rearranged his whole face — and pushed back from the table. He stood, stretched with the total physical abandon of someone who had never once been self-conscious about taking up space, and looked at her one more time.

"Go talk to him, hon," he said simply. "Tonight."

She watched him leave, hands in his jacket pockets, unhurried, the library settling back into its quiet around her.

She sat there for a moment longer, looking at the closed textbook on the table, thinking about a stone in her pocket that had become something she couldn't carry anymore, thinking about a boy at a window and a heart in the margin and I hope you never find out, Sol.

She picked up her bag.

Tonight.










Chapter Seventeen

2010 || NEW YORK

She had been sitting at her desk for two hours without doing anything useful.

The homework was open in front of her. The lamp was on. Everything looked, from the outside, like Solar Lee on a normal evening — composed, focused, working through whatever needed working through with the efficient attention she brought to everything. But the page in front of her was the same page it had been when she sat down, and the pencil in her hand had not moved once, and she had been staring at the wall above her desk thinking about the same things she had been thinking about since she left the library.

There's a lot more than what he told you today. Hyunjoon's words repeated in her head, that weird side smirk sitting on his face, the way he used pet names like he'd known her their entire life, when it had only been what felt like mere moments.

She thought about the gym in Brooklyn Sunwoo mentioned, so briefly. The motorcycle he talked about fixing up, rebuilding, showing her. These four boys who had appeared from nowhere and now sat at her lunch table every day, as if they were old friends and had been there the whole time. The bruises that boxing had never once adequately explained, that he already had when she saw him on the playground that day. And recently, the way he disappeared, and the way he came back, and the specific quality of the reset that she now understood she had been watching without knowing what she was looking at.

She thought about his mother, too. About the last time she had actually seen Dahee Kim — at one of the early galas, years ago, the careful smile and the eyes that never quite matched it. About all the times she had asked about her since and received some variation of nothing. About the way the Castle moved around Sihyuk Kim at events with the specific, careful deference of people who were not entirely sure what he was capable of, and the way Dahee Kim had simply stopped being present at any of those events, and the way nobody said anything about it because in the Castle nobody said anything about anything that mattered.

She had been telling herself, for a long time, that there was a reasonable explanation.

She wasn't sure she believed that anymore. She set the pencil down. She looked at the window, and waited.

///

He came later than usual.

She heard him before she saw him — the faint, familiar sound of him finding the ledge, the quiet economy of his movement — and she was already turned in her chair by the time the window opened. He climbed through in the usual way, landed without sound, straightened. He looked at her.

She looked back at him.

Something moved through his expression almost immediately — the specific quality of his attention shifting, recalibrating, the way it did when he walked into a situation and found it different from what he expected. She had said nothing. She hadn't moved toward him. She was just sitting in her desk chair with her hands in her lap and looking at him, and that was apparently enough for him to understand that tonight was not a normal night.

He didn't say anything. He waited, which was what Sunwoo did when he sensed that someone else needed to go first.

"I talked to Hyunjoon today," she hums, like it was casual.

A pause. Brief, controlled, giving nothing away. "I know," he returned, which meant he had seen them in the library and had been waiting for this, which meant he had come tonight knowing what it was going to be and had come anyway.

She held his gaze. "He didn't tell me anything specific," she continues, carefully, slowly. "He told me there was more. That it wasn't his to say." She paused. "He was right about that."

Sunwoo looked at her for a long moment. Then he moved — not toward the bed, not to his usual place — but to the chair by her window, the one that faced her desk, and he sat in it and looked at her across the small space between them.

"What do you want to know?" he asked, and his voice was even, and his face was the neutral one, and she understood that this was him offering her the door. Not opening it yet. But offering it.

"Everything," she declared.

He held her gaze for a moment that felt longer than it was. Then he exhaled, slow and quiet, and leaned forward with his elbows on his knees — not looking at her, looking at the floor between them — and he began.

///

"My father runs a company, you know this," he started, his voice low and unhurried, the same tone he used to explain math problems, to comment on the weather, to say things that cost him nothing. Except this one cost him everything, and the only sign of it was the slight deliberateness of each word, placed carefully, like he was choosing which ones to set down and which to hold back. "Kim Company. You know it as a business. It's not — it's not only that."

She said nothing. She watched him.

"It operates on the surface the way all the Castle companies operate," he goes on, still looking at the floor. "Real estate. Finance. Acquisitions. But underneath that there's another layer. A different kind of work." He paused, turning something over. "Corporate espionage. Eliminating competition. Stealing contracts. Having people — removed — when they become problems." He said the last part the way you said something you had made peace with a long time ago. Flat. Final. "That's the real business. That's what the money actually comes from."

Solar absorbed this. The word removed sat in her chest and she let it sit there.

"So, like a gang," she maintains, deliberately. "You and the others."

"We're the operation," he confirmed, glancing up at her briefly before looking back at the floor. "We do the work that can't be traced back to him. Jobs. Deliveries. If someone needs to be — dealt with — we deal with them." A pause. "My father started building me for this when I was five. The training, the combat, the tactics — all of it was preparation. When I was old enough, he told me to find people. Build a crew. People who could fight, who had nothing to lose, who would be loyal." Something moved through his jaw. "So I did."

She thought about Sangyeon on a front step in the fading light. Eric under a bridge. Hyunjae in an alley in Brooklyn. Hyunjoon leaning against an abandoned building with a cigarette and a knife he didn't know how to use. Four boys who had nothing and had been given something, and the cost of that something was this.

"The deliveries," she puzzles. "When you have to go because your father needs you—"

"Drug runs, mostly," he stated plainly. "The company runs a distribution operation. It's what funds everything else. When I said the guys and I were in withdrawal — that's where it comes from. We're the ones who move it, and we were using more than we were supposed to." He said it without apology and without drama, just the bare fact of it. "My father cut off the supply temporarily. Hence—" he gestured at nothing in particular, a gesture that meant everything you saw on the steps today.

Solar stared at him. She was thinking about the bench in the east park. About every Saturday morning, the Castle's east park, the excuse of being at Chanhee's. She was thinking about how she had been sitting beside him — at lunch tables, on park benches, in this room — while all of this was happening around him and she had accepted the boxing story because she had wanted to accept it and he had let her because he had wanted her to.

"Sunwoo," she mumbles, quietly. "How long?"

He looked up at her. "Since I was five," he answered, and the simplicity of it — five years old, small and new and reaching for things — landed somewhere in her that didn't have a name.

She held his gaze and didn't let herself look away from it, because she understood that he was watching her face for the thing he had been afraid of — the recoil, the recalibration, the version of her that would look at him differently now. She made sure he didn't find it there. She made sure her expression said exactly what she meant it to say.

He looked at her for a long moment. Something in his face shifted — very slightly, almost imperceptible — and he looked back at the floor.

The room was quiet for a while.

Then Solar stood from her desk chair. She crossed the small space between them and sat on the edge of the windowsill beside his chair, close enough that their knees were almost touching, and she looked at him — at the side of his face, at the composed, still profile of him — and she waited.

She could feel the something that was still in the room. The thing that hadn't been said yet. The door still open.

"There's more," she challenged. Not a question.

He didn't answer immediately. His jaw worked once, slightly, the only visible evidence of the thing he was deciding. She reached out and put her hand over his where it rested on his knee, and she felt him go still under her touch in that particular way of his — not pulling back, just registering it, letting himself feel it for a second before deciding what to do with it.

He turned his hand over beneath hers and held it. His fingers folded around hers slowly, like he was being careful about it, like something that required care.

"My mother didn't leave," he attests, though there remained no emotion in his tone of voice, like he felt nothing about the topic, like he had suddenly made peace with it, or perhaps had a long time ago.

Solar stilled completely.

"My father told me she decided this life wasn't for her." His voice hadn't changed. The same flat, unhurried register, each word placed like a stone. "That she wasn't built for what was required. That was what he said to me when I was thirteen." A pause. He was looking at their hands. "She didn't leave. He had her killed. And then he sat me down in the study and told me she was gone and we moved on."

The room was completely silent.

Solar looked at him — at the composed, still face, at the boy who had been carrying this since he was thirteen years old and had told her every single time she asked that his mother was fine, had told himself the same thing, had made it into a fact rather than a feeling because it was the only way to make it survivable — and she felt something in her chest that she didn't have adequate words for. Not pity. Something bigger and more specific than pity. Something that had to do with the years of it, with the specific cruelty of a man who sat his thirteen-year-old son down in a study and said she wasn't built for what was required and then went back to his work.

She thought about every Gala where Dahee Kim's absence had been quietly absorbed. She thought about a ten-year-old boy picking at his food at a dinner table, his mother smiling that careful, practiced smile beside him. She had suspected. She had known, in the way she always knew things before she was ready for them.

But hearing him say it — in that flat, unchanged voice, the one that made it a fact rather than a wound — was something different from suspecting it. Something she couldn't unfeel.

"Sunwoo," she mutters, her voice barely above a whisper.

"I'm fine," he said, immediately, automatically, the words arriving before he had decided to say them. The reflex of years. Five years old, and every year since, learning to be fine.

She looked at him for a long moment. Then she slid off the windowsill and onto the arm of his chair, which was not designed for two people and didn't care, and she put both arms around him the way she would have done for anyone she loved who had just said something they had never said to anyone before. Her chin came to rest against the top of his head. She held him — properly, completely, the way you held someone when words were the wrong instrument.

He went still. Not the tense, guarded still of someone resisting — but the bewildered still of someone who had never been held like this before and didn't know what to do with it. Like he was waiting for the catch. Like kindness this uncomplicated had to have terms.

It didn't have terms. She just held him.

After a moment — a long moment, the kind that had weight — she felt the quality of his stillness change. Something released in the set of his shoulders, slight and almost imperceptible, the specific easing of something that had been held braced for a very long time and had found, tentatively, that the thing it was braced against wasn't coming.

His arms came up around her. Slowly, carefully, the way he did everything that mattered to him. His face turned slightly into her shoulder.

She held him tighter.

They stayed like that for a long time, in the lamplit quiet of her room, the Castle dark and still outside the window. She thought about everything he had told her tonight — the weight of all of it, the years of it, the child it had been done to — and she thought about how he had come through her window knowing what she was going to ask and had come anyway, and she thought about the specific and irreversible thing it was to be trusted with someone's entire truth.

Eventually he shifted, pulling back slightly without fully pulling away — rearranging rather than retreating, so that they were sitting properly in the chair together, her sideways across it and him beneath her, his arm around her waist and her head against his shoulder. It was a small logistical negotiation that they managed without discussion, the easy physical fluency of two people who had been finding ways to be close to each other for years.

She could feel his heartbeat from where her hand rested against his chest. Steady. Slower than it had been.

"I told you," she said, into the quiet between them. "It doesn't change anything."

He didn't answer immediately. His thumb moved in a slow circle against her side, barely perceptibly, the way he touched things he wanted to remember.

"I know," he murmured, finally. His voice was the same as always — flat, low, unhurried.

But his arm didn't move from around her.

And she had learned, a long time ago, that this was how Sunwoo said everything that mattered.





Chapter Eighteen

2010 || NEW YORK

She gave herself three days.

This was not a conscious decision exactly — it was just what happened, the amount of time it took for the information to move from the surface of her mind to somewhere deeper, somewhere she could actually hold it rather than just carry it. She went to school. She ate dinner with her family. She sat at her desk in the evenings and actually did her homework for once, because doing her homework was something she could control and control felt important right now. She was normal Solar to everyone who looked at her, and underneath normal Solar she was turning everything over, examining it from every angle, deciding what she thought and how she felt and what she was going to do with both.

She had known Sunwoo was keeping things from her. She had known for years, in the accumulating-evidence way she knew most things. She had accepted it because she trusted him and because she understood, without needing to be told, that whatever he was keeping from her was kept for her sake rather than his own. She still believed that. She believed it more now, not less, because she had seen his face when he told her and she knew what it had cost him.

But there was a difference between knowing something existed and standing inside it.

She texted him on the third evening, while Chaerin was doing something noisy down the hall and the smell of whatever the cook had made for dinner was still drifting through the house and everything around her was exactly as it had always been.

sol: I want to come see where you live. The secondary building. Not the main house.

A pause. Longer than his pauses usually were.

sw: why

sol: because I want to.

Another pause.

sw: I'll loop the footage. Saturday.

She put her phone down and looked at the ceiling and thought about the specific feeling of a door opening that you had been standing in front of for a long time.

///

Saturday arrived quietly, the Castle unhurried in the particular way it was unhurried when it wasn't performing anything for anyone. Her mother was at a Foundation meeting. Her father was at the company. Jaehyun was somewhere being Jaehyun. Chaerin and Juyeon were staging some kind of elaborate ongoing argument that had started the night before and showed no sign of resolution.

She told them she was going to Chanhee's.

Chanhee, when she texted him, sent back a string of messages that she read in full, acknowledged internally as fair, and then put her phone away.

The Kim property was on the other side of the Castle — not far, nothing in the Castle was far, but far enough that the walk had a quality of crossing something, of moving between one world and another. She had walked past the main mansion before, always at a distance, always without stopping. It was a different kind of impressive from the Lee mansion — larger in some technical sense, but colder, more deliberate in its severity, the kind of building that communicated power without warmth because warmth had never been the point.

The secondary building sat back from the main mansion, partially obscured by a hedge line that had been allowed to grow denser than anything the Castle's groundskeepers would normally permit. It had the look of something that had once been a guest house and had been quietly repurposed — the bones of it still Castle-appropriate on the outside, but carrying a quality the main mansion didn't have. It felt occupied in a way that had nothing to do with appearances.

She found the side entrance and knocked.

The door opened almost immediately.

Hyunjoon looked at her. She looked at Hyunjoon. He was in a loose shirt and sweatpants, a piece of toast in one hand, and his expression had already cycled through surprise and landed squarely on delight before she had finished taking a breath.

"Sol," he announced, turning his head toward the interior of the building like he was calling out a sports result, "is at our door. By herself. Just — standing here."

"Hi, Hyunjoon," she greeted him pleasantly.

"Hi, hon." He stepped aside and swung the door open with the magnanimous energy of someone granting a royal audience. "Come in, come in. He's been weirdly tense all morning and now I understand completely."

"Hyunjoon," came Sangyeon's voice from somewhere inside — flat, unremarkable, the tone of someone issuing a minor correction without looking up from whatever they were doing.

"I'm welcoming a guest," Hyunjoon replied, unbothered, and gestured her inside.

///

The secondary building was not what she had expected, which was its own kind of information — she realized, stepping into it, that she hadn't known what she was expecting, only that this wasn't it.

It was a guest house that had been made into something else entirely. The structure was still recognizably Castle — high ceilings, good bones, the kind of proportions that came from money — but what was inside it had nothing to do with impressing anyone. The main room was large and functional, the furniture mismatched and arranged for purpose rather than aesthetics. A long table ran along one wall, its surface covered in the organized clutter of people who worked here — laptops, papers, equipment she couldn't immediately categorize. Things that had been pushed aside to make room for other things.

And there were guns on the table.

Not many. Three, arranged with the casual matter-of-factness of objects that simply lived there, as ordinary in this room as the mugs on the counter and the folded jackets draped over chair backs. She had known, abstractly, that Sunwoo carried one — she had registered the shape of it under his jacket more than once and filed it away without looking directly at it. But seeing them here — on a table, in morning light, beside a laptop and someone's half-finished coffee — was a different order of thing entirely.

She kept her expression where she wanted it and kept walking.

Sangyeon was at the table, eyes on the laptop screen, and he glanced up when she entered. He gave her one of his nods — direct, steady, the kind that communicated you're welcome here without making anything larger than it needed to be — and she returned it, grateful for the simplicity of it.

Hyunjae was on the couch, feet up, eyes closed, in that too-alert stillness of his that never quite passed for actual sleep. He didn't open his eyes, but one corner of his mouth pulled upward — faint, brief, gone almost instantly. "Sunwoo owes me twenty dollars," he remarked, to the ceiling.

"For what?" she asked, before she could stop herself.

"Said you'd show up within the week." His eyes stayed closed. "Sunwoo said you wouldn't."

"He was wrong," she pointed out.

"He was," Hyunjae agreed, with the satisfaction of someone who had been right about something and intended to collect.

She was still processing this when Eric looked up from the corner of the room, quiet as always, and gave her the small nod that was his version of a greeting. His expression was careful and measured in the way it always was, but there was something underneath it — not quite warmth, but the acknowledgment of warmth, the way someone looked at you when they were glad you were there and didn't know how to say it simply.

"He's in the back," Hyunjoon confirmed from behind her, appearing at her elbow. "Second door. He cleaned up back there, by the way. Took him a while." He paused for effect. "We timed it."

"We didn't time it," Sangyeon corrected, without looking up.

Solar pressed her lips together, turned away from Hyunjoon before her expression could do anything revealing, and walked toward the hallway.

///

She found him in the back room.

It was clearly his — spare and organized with the precision she recognized entirely as Sunwoo, every object in a specific place, nothing present that didn't need to be there. He was standing when she came in, arms crossed, and he looked at her with that expression she had spent years learning — the neutral one that wasn't really neutral, reading her before she had said a word.

She looked back at him. She felt, standing in his space for the first time, something she hadn't anticipated — a kind of completion, like a part of the picture she had been assembling for years had finally slid into place.

"Hi," she breathed.

"Hi, Sol." Something in his face moved — very slightly, very briefly. He watched her look around the room, taking it in, and he let her look without interrupting it. When her eyes came back to his, he held her gaze for a moment. "You came," he observed, which was not a statement of surprise exactly, but something more private than that.

"I told you I would," she reminded him.

"You said you wanted to," he corrected, mildly. "That's different."

She held his gaze. "Not for me," she told him.

He looked at her for a long moment. Then he uncrossed his arms, crossed the room to her, and stood close enough that she had to tilt her face up to look at him properly — which she did, without looking away, because she had come here specifically to look at things directly. His hand came up and tucked her hair behind her ear, unhurried, the way it always was when he touched her, and his thumb stayed at her jaw for just a moment.

"You didn't have to do this," he murmured.

"I know," she returned. "But... I wanted to show you that I'm thankful," she clears her throat, green eyes daring to wander across the atmosphere of the room again, "that I'm thankful for you letting me into your world." 

He didn't say anything for a moment. Though his expression remained unchanged, there was a slight flash of something in those dark eyes, like he for once couldn't instantly put together the proper words to say. Solar purses her lips for a second, before she quietly adds, "I accept it." She wanted to show him that no matter what, she was going to stick by his side. And that was what she was trying to make herself believe.

And you'd see it then, the faintest twinkle in those dark eyes staring back down at her. Sunwoo hung his head for a moment, only to chuckle to himself in disbelief, before those irises landed back on her. His hand reached out to gently take hers, bringing it up to lay on his chest. 

Sunwoo Kim had been born into the world for one sole purpose, he had never known anything other than that. He had never known what the word love could entail nor mean, and he never planned to. 

There were not many times he could say he could feel his heart beating out of his chest. And Solar Lee could not say she had ever felt it before, the confirmation that he was a real, living being underneath the bruises, scars, leather jacket, and smoke. But she felt it then, the pump of his chest beating underneath the palm of her hand. 

His hand stayed atop hers, and he squeezed it, in his own way of saying, I love you, before he could seem to form the words yet.

A comfortable silence fell over them, until he looked at her for another beat, that dark, reading gaze, and then the corner of his mouth moved — the closest thing to a smile he produced in front of anyone — and he stepped back. "Come on," he said, and led her back toward the main room.

///

They had been in the main room for perhaps ten minutes — Hyunjoon had produced tea from somewhere and placed it in front of her with the flourish of someone presenting a gift, and Sangyeon had briefly shown her something on the laptop that she had followed approximately forty percent of, and Hyunjae had opened his eyes long enough to confirm, without preamble, that she seemed less terrible than he had expected — when she noticed that Sunwoo wasn't in the room.

She had assumed he was getting something. She waited another minute, glancing at the hallway.

Hyunjoon materialized at her elbow with the specific energy of someone executing a plan.

"So," he opened, settling into the chair beside her with the comfortable ease of someone who had decided this was where he lived now, "you and school, how's that going, I feel like we never really talk about your academic life, and I'm genuinely curious, like what are you actually studying right now—"

"Hyunjoon," she interrupted, pleasantly.

"—because I've been thinking about economics actually, like whether it's actually useful or whether it's just—"

"Where is he?"

A pause. Hyunjoon's expression did something brief and complicated that resolved itself back into complete innocence. "He'll be right back," he offered.

"Where," she repeated.

He looked at her for a moment, appearing to weigh something, and then appeared to conclude that she was going to find out regardless and that it was better for everyone if he just — gestured vaguely down the hallway.

She followed the gesture.

The door at the end of the hall was not fully closed. She could hear it before she could see it — the sharp, meaty impact of hits being absorbed, movement that was too fast and too precise to be anything casual. She walked toward it without deciding to, her feet carrying her forward while the sounds got louder, and when she reached the gap in the door she stopped.

Through it, she could see the far end of a training space — mats on the floor, equipment along the walls, and in the center of it, Sunwoo and a man she didn't recognize.

The man was older — somewhere in his late forties, she guessed, with the build of someone who had been exceptional at something physical and had never stopped. He moved with an economy that was almost eerily similar to Sunwoo's, and he was not pulling anything. She could see that immediately. Whatever this was, it was not a demonstration.

And Sunwoo was taking hits.

Not losing — that wasn't what she was watching. He was moving, responding, the footwork and the reflexes operating at a level that made it genuinely difficult to track. But the man hit him, and hit him hard, and Sunwoo absorbed it every time with the same expression she had seen him wear while doing math homework at her desk. The same flat, absent composure. Like the pain was information he was receiving and processing and setting aside, like it had no more claim on him than the weather.

One hit caught him across the jaw — the kind that would have put most people on the floor — and Sunwoo's head snapped to the side and he was already moving before it had fully landed, already responding, his face still perfectly blank.

Solar stood in the hallway and watched him and felt something in her chest go very quiet.

She had known he was trained. She had known it the way she knew most things about him — gradually, incompletely, filling in details over years. She had known about Choi, in the abstract, since Chapter Fifteen. She had accepted the boxing story for years because she wanted to, and she had let go of the boxing story because she had to, and she had nodded and held his gaze when he told her about the training and made sure he didn't find the recoil in her face.

But there was a difference between knowing and watching.

He took another hit. He didn't make a sound.

My father started building me for this when I was five.

She took a step back from the door. Then another. She turned, slowly, and walked back down the hallway into the main room, where Hyunjoon looked at her from the chair he had not moved from and said nothing for once, which was its own kind of answer.

She sat back down. She picked up her tea. She held it in both hands and looked at the table and breathed, slow and even, and she was Solar Lee and she knew how to hold herself together and she did it.

But the sounds continued faintly down the hall, and the guns were still on the table, and all of it was pressing against something inside her that she hadn't been fully prepared for — not the violence, not the guns, but him. The specific, terrible competence of him. The way he didn't flinch. The way someone had put that in him when he was five years old and called it preparation.

She held herself together for another ten minutes. When Sunwoo came back into the room — slightly flushed, jacket back on, the jaw that had been hit showing nothing — he looked at her immediately, the way he always looked at her first.

She assembled her expression carefully and looked back.

"I should probably get back," she mentioned, lightly. "My mother wanted me home for lunch — I forgot to tell you."

He looked at her. He looked at her for exactly long enough that she understood he knew — that he had read her the way he always read her, found what she was trying to hide, and was deciding what to do with it. He didn't call it. He just held her gaze for that beat, and then pushed off the doorframe.

"I'll walk you out," he said.

Hyunjoon, from across the room, gave her a look that was far too knowing for someone who had been apparently focused on his own business. She did not meet it.

///

At the side entrance, she stopped and turned to face him. The Castle stretched out behind her in the morning light — quiet, expensive, entirely unaware.

"I'm glad I came," she told him, and meant it, despite everything.

He studied her face for a moment, unhurried. His hand came up and found her jaw, tilting her face up slightly, and he looked at her with those dark eyes that missed nothing and never had.

"Sol," he murmured.

"I'm okay," she insisted, which was mostly true in the ways that mattered.

He held her gaze for another beat. Then he leaned down and pressed a kiss to her forehead, slow and deliberate, and straightened. "Go," he said, quietly.

She went.

She crossed the Castle in the morning light with her chin level and her pace steady and nothing showing on her face, because the Castle had eyes and she had grown up knowing it. But inside, underneath the composure, everything she had sat inside for the past hour was pressing against something that needed to be said out loud, that needed to exist somewhere outside of her own chest before it became too heavy to carry alone.

She had her phone out before she reached the halfway point.

sol: are you home

Chanhee's response came back in under thirty seconds.

heehee: yes. why do you sound like that over text

sol: can I come over

heehee: solar

sol: please

A pause. Then:

heehee: obviously. come now.

She put her phone away and kept walking, and she thought about the way Hyunjae and Eric had laughed through the door — that easy, unguarded sound — and she thought about all five of them in that building and what had brought each of them there, and she thought about Sunwoo at five years old being handed a future he had no say in.

She had known, in the abstract, that his life was what it was. She had accepted it, she had meant to accept it, and she still did — that hadn't changed and wasn't going to change and she was certain of it in the specific, irreversible way she was certain of very few things.

But there was a difference between accepting something and feeling the full weight of it press against you from the inside. Between knowing the person you loved had never been given a choice and sitting in the room where that choice had been made for him, drinking tea, listening to it happen in the next room over.

She loved him. She had known that for a long time, had been knowing it gradually and then all at once in the way you came to know something that had always been true. She loved him, and he had been five years old, and his father had looked at him and seen a function rather than a child, and she had been sitting beside him on park benches and in classrooms and in her own room for years while all of it was just — happening. Just his life. Just the air he breathed.

That was the thing pressing against her chest. Not fear, not disgust, not the recoil he had been watching her face for. Just the grief of it. The specific, quiet grief of loving someone and understanding, fully and for the first time, what it had cost them to become who they were.

And before her, the Castle was bright and quiet, entirely unchanged, and she walked through it with her chin level and nothing showing on her face, and she thought that she was going to be okay — she was already okay, in the ways that mattered — but she needed to sit across from someone who had known her since she was ten years old and say it out loud, because some things needed to be said out loud before you could properly carry them.

She'd be back. That was the truth of it, and Solar Lee knew it better than anyone.



Chapter Nineteen

2010 || NEW YORK

The text came in at 11:43 on a Saturday morning, while Chanhee was at his desk doing nothing particularly important.

He read it twice.

sol: are you home

He looked at it the way he looked at most things Solar sent him — which was to say, with the specific attention of someone who had learned, over the course of what was essentially his entire conscious life, that the words she used were rarely the whole message. They had been in each other's orbit since before either of them had a coherent memory of a time without the other — since the Choi and Lee families were neighbors in the Castle and their mothers had introduced them in the way Castle mothers introduced their children, which was to say as a formality, because children that small did not need formal introductions to find each other. Solar had found him in approximately three minutes and had been an inconvenience ever since, which was, in his most honest assessment, the best thing that had ever happened to him. He would never say that aloud.

Are you home was not a question about his location. It was a question about whether he was available in the particular way she needed someone to be available, which was different from being available in the ordinary sense and they both knew it.

He typed back: yes. why do you sound like that over text.

Her reply: can I come over

He stared at it. Then he typed solar which she was familiar enough with to understand as a complete sentence — meaning I know something is happening and I am deciding how alarmed to be.

please, she sent.

He sat with it for exactly one second. Then he closed the textbook he had not been reading anyway and got up.

He stood at his window while he waited, looking out at the Castle — the manicured walkways, the old trees, the high walls that enclosed all of it, everything exactly where it always was. He thought about Solar, which he did frequently and with the particular brand of helpless exasperation that was simply the condition of loving her. She had been different lately. Not worse, not better — just different, occupied by something she was carrying in a new way, something that was changing the quality of her silences in a manner he couldn't entirely name. He had noticed it the way he noticed most things about her — quietly, without drawing attention to the noticing, filing it away.

He thought about Sunwoo Kim. He thought about him the way he had been thinking about him all year — carefully, reluctantly, with the mental posture of someone circling something they were not certain they wanted to get closer to. He was not an unreasonable person. He was not someone who decided things before he had adequate information. But there were things you knew without being told — things that accumulated from years of proximity, from growing up on this particular patch of real estate alongside the same handful of families — and one of those things was that the Kims had always been the wrong note in the chord. Present and accounted for and always, somehow, slightly off. Sihyuk Kim moved through Castle events the way a cold front moved through a room. Everyone felt it. Nobody named it.

He heard the front door.

He was already at the top of the stairs when he heard his mother get there first, which he should have anticipated. Miyoung Choi had a particular talent for materializing the moment anyone arrived, as though the house itself alerted her, and she had a specific relationship with Solar that operated on its own frequency entirely — the frequency of a woman who had one child and had quietly, over the course of many years, expanded the category to include his best friend without making a production of it.

"Solar-ah!" He could hear the warmth of it from the landing, the way his mother's voice pitched upward with the genuine delight she brought to Solar's arrivals specifically. There was the sound of movement, the particular shuffling that meant his mother was pulling her into a hug, and then: "Look at you, you walked all the way over, are you cold? Of course you're cold, it's chilly out there! Come in, come in—"

"I'm okay, Mrs. Choi, really—"

"Okay," she pouts, so slightly, before the urgency in her tone returns, "come in here, I made too much this morning, you know I always make too much—"

"Mother," Chanhee called down, before his mother could commit Solar to a full second breakfast, "I've got her."

A pause. Then, from below, his mother's voice took on the particular quality it got when she was performing offense, "I'm the one she came to see, Chanhee, go back to your room—"

"She came to see me."

"I'm the fun one—"

"You are not the fun one."

Solar's laugh drifted up the stairs — small, real, the specific laugh that happened when she wasn't prepared for it — and Chanhee descended to meet her, arriving at the bottom to find his mother still with one arm loosely around Solar's shoulders, looking at him with the knowing expression she wore when she had decided she was winning an argument by virtue of being more charming than him, which was a tactic he had spent fifteen years failing to counter.

He gave Solar a look. She was still smiling, which he noted and said nothing about.

"Upstairs," he told her, and turned to go back up, and heard his mother behind him say softly to Solar, "eat something before you leave, sweetheart," and Solar's quiet I will, I promise, and something in his chest did the thing it occasionally did when he was reminded that this was what his house felt like to her — warm, and open, and entirely without conditions — and he filed it away with all the other things he didn't say out loud.

He led her to his room and she came in the way she always had, without ceremony, without looking around because she knew where everything was, and she sat on the edge of his bed and looked at the floor, and he took the desk chair and turned it toward her and waited. Because that was what you did. You gave Solar Lee the space to get to it, because trying to pull it out before she was ready only made her retreat, and retreating was the last thing he wanted from her right now.

The room was quiet. Outside the window, the Castle was pale and still in the late morning light. She looked up, and she began.

She talked for a long time.

He listened the way he always listened — completely still, taking everything in, giving nothing away. She started in the middle and worked outward the way she always told stories when she was trying to work out what she actually thought about them. She told him about the training — not just today, but everything she had understood and not understood over the past year, the bruises and the almost-answers and the way she had put down the boxing explanation without quite believing it. She told him about Choi, about what Sunwoo had told her, about my father started building me for this when I was five. She told him about the members, about the secondary building, about sitting in the main room with tea and Hyunjoon's easy warmth and guns on the table and all of it being both completely foreign and somehow completely real.

And then she told him about the door.

He heard her voice change when she got to it — just slightly, the composure thinning. She described what she had seen through the gap and he listened and did not look away from her face, because there was nothing to say yet that would have been adequate.

"He didn't flinch," she conveyed, toward the end of it. Her voice was careful. "Not once. The man hit him across the jaw — the kind of hit that would put most people on the floor — and Sunwoo just—" She stopped. Pressed her lips together. "It was like receiving information. Like he was just processing it and setting it aside, like it had no more claim on him than anything else." She exhaled slowly. "And all I could think about was that someone put that in him. Someone looked at him when he was five years old and decided that was what he was going to be."

The room held that for a moment.

Chanhee looked at her. She was assembled the way she assembled herself when she was deciding to be — the deliberate stillness, the steady hands, the level chin. He had watched her do it at galas and at dinner tables and in school hallways and now here, on his bed, after sitting in a room in a building on the Kim property and watching the boy she loved take a hit to the jaw without making a sound.

"You love him," he said. Not a question. Not an accusation. Just the fact of it, placed plainly on the table between them.

Solar looked at him, and for a moment she didn't say anything. Something moved through her expression — not evasion, but the specific difficulty of fifteen, of trying to put language to something that kept slipping out of the shape words could make. "I don't know," she began, slowly, "maybe — I don't—" She stopped. Tried again. "Is that what this is?" Her brow had drawn in slightly, genuinely, like she was asking herself as much as him. "I just can't — I can't wrap my head around the fact that that's his life, Hee. That it's just — that's just what it is for him. Every day." She shook her head, small, more to herself than to him. "I don't know what to do with that."

Chanhee held her gaze and said nothing for a moment, because that was an answer, whether she had realized it yet or not. He had known Solar since before they were old enough to walk to the park without a parent, and he knew what it looked like when she didn't care about something — she didn't lose words over things she didn't care about.

He looked at the window. He thought about 2006. He thought about being eleven years old at a Castle dinner, watching the adults do the particular thing they did when something had happened that required managing — not ignoring, but containing, distributing it between themselves in measured portions as though the thing would be less real if no one said it at full volume. He thought about the deal between the Lee and Kim families ending that same year, the chill that had spread through every subsequent room Sihyuk Kim and Jaesang Lee were both in. Two men who had once shaken hands and now exchanged them like an obligation they were both performing under duress.

And then he thought about Changmin's father.

The Vice President of Lee Industries, who had lived in one of the six Castle residences because that was the kind of trust that warranted proximity, who had died in 2006 and left a quiet, elegant woman and a boy who had been ten years old and had spent the last four years learning to carry it. He thought about the timing of it. The deal breaking. The death. The specific year.

He thought about everything Solar had just told him about Sihyuk Kim. About what he was. About what he had built his son to be.

"Solar," he said carefully, "how much do you know about the deal? Between your father and Sihyuk Kim. When it broke."

Something shifted in her face — brief, almost imperceptible. "Jaehyun told me about it. In 2006. He had a newspaper article." A pause. "He had a theory."

"What theory."

She was very still. "That Sihyuk was connected to what happened to Changmin's dad."

The room went quiet in a different way.

"And you didn't believe him," Chanhee said.

"I was eleven," she returned. "And I—" She stopped. Started again, more carefully. "I didn't want to believe it."

"What do you believe now?"
