She looked at him, and in her expression he saw the thing she had been not-looking-at for a long time — not ignorance, not denial, but the specific deliberate avoidance of someone who already knew the answer and had been choosing, for their own survival, not to make it official.

"I think," she said, slowly, "that I've known for a while. I just—" The sentence didn't finish. It didn't need to.

He held her gaze and felt the thing in the back of his mind complete its assembly — quietly, irreversibly, the way a picture looked different once you saw what it actually was. "He used Changmin's dad as a message," he said quietly. Not a question. "He couldn't get the company, so he took the next most important thing." He paused. "And then he took his own son and—" He stopped. The two facts arrived beside each other, and he sat with it just for a moment — Sihyuk Kim and what he did to Dongwoo Ji and what he had been doing to Sunwoo since he was five years old, and the specific, ugly shape of the kind of man who could do both things without hesitation. "He's been doing it to Sunwoo the whole time."

Solar's expression did something very small and very specific. The look of someone who had been holding a heavy thing alone and had just had someone reach out and take hold of the other side. She didn't say anything. She didn't need to.

"I want to tell you to stay away from him," Chanhee uttered.

"I know," she replied.

"I want to tell you that his father is—" He stopped. Tried again. "That what Sihyuk Kim is doesn't stop at the people who work for him. You're in this now, Solar. You went to that building today. You know things—"

"I know," she said again, quieter.

"But you're not going to," he concluded. It wasn't quite resignation — it was something more complicated, the arriving-at-a-conclusion of a person who had looked at the evidence and found it insufficient to override the fundamental truth, which was that Solar Lee had already decided and had decided before he said a word.

She looked at him steadily. "I don't think I can," she answered, and it was the most honest thing she had said, because it wasn't I won't — it was something further in than that, something that lived below decision entirely.

He looked at her. He thought about how she had sat with that question — is that what this is — and how she had not been able to answer it, and how that was its own kind of answer. He thought about the secondary building and about Sunwoo at five years old and about all of it, and he thought about the fact that Solar was the most stubborn person he had ever known and also one of the most feeling, and that those two things together had always been what made loving her both exhausting and entirely non-negotiable.

He exhaled slowly through his nose. "Fine," he said, at last. Not a blessing — not quite, not yet — but the door not fully closed either. "Fine. But Solar." He met her eyes, the sarcasm stripped away and just him underneath it. "You tell me. Whatever happens. You tell me."

She looked at him for a moment, and something in her face softened — not in a dramatic way, just in the particular way it did when he said exactly the right thing and she didn't want to make a production of it. "I always do," she said quietly.

He scoffed. Looked away. The corner of his mouth moved very slightly. "You are an enormous inconvenience," he informed her, "and I want you to know that I am not happy about any of this."

She let out a small breath of a laugh, which he also chose not to acknowledge, and reached over to take the throw pillow from the corner of his bed and hold it in her lap the way she had been doing since she was small when she needed something to do with her hands. The room settled into a quiet that was occupied differently than it had been an hour ago — heavier in some ways, lighter in others, the particular quality that exists between two people who have just said something real and have nowhere else to put it.

He almost didn't hear it.

The house was so quiet in that specific Saturday way — his mother somewhere below, the low murmur of her radio, no other sound — that the creak registered immediately. Third floorboard from the landing. The one that had always been slightly loose, that announced the presence of anyone coming down the hall before they arrived. He had been aware of it his whole life and had stopped consciously noting it years ago, but it registered now, in the silence, with the specific clarity of something heard at exactly the wrong moment.

He went still.

Solar looked at him. She read his expression and she went still too, the way she always caught up to him a half-beat behind.

The footsteps had stopped.

He looked at the door. It wasn't quite closed — it hadn't been, it never was when Solar was here, just the two of them and no reason for it. There was a gap of perhaps four inches, and through it the hallway was visible in a narrow slice — the edge of the wall, the corner of the framed photograph his mother had hung when he was eight, and the figure standing just outside.

Changmin.

He was very still, facing slightly away from them — the particular body language of someone who had stopped because something had stopped them, caught mid-step by a word or a name arriving before he announced himself. He had his jacket on, which meant he had come from outside, which meant he had let himself in the way they all let themselves in here, had come upstairs, and had gotten to the hallway and heard something.

Chanhee did not know how much.

For one stretched and suspended moment, nobody moved — the three of them in the strange arrested stillness of people who had each arrived at the same point by entirely different roads and had no script for what happened next.

Then Changmin turned. Slowly. And he was in the doorway gap now, visible, and his face was doing something Chanhee had almost never seen it do. Changmin Ji's face was constitutionally oriented toward warmth — the soft, gentle expressions that made people feel immediately at ease, that were simply the natural language of who he was. It was not doing that now. It was doing something quieter and deeper and altogether more private, the expression of someone who had just been handed something heavy without being asked whether they were ready for it. Not collapsed under it. Just — holding it. The way he had always held the things he was given to carry.

His eyes found Solar's first.

She looked back at him and didn't say anything. He could see it happening in her face — the calculation, the grief, the specific awful arithmetic of not knowing how much he had heard and dreading the answer.

Then Changmin looked at Chanhee.

It was brief. A few seconds at most. But in those seconds Chanhee read him the way he read people when it mattered — completely, without pretense — and what he found was not anger. Not accusation. Nothing that required immediate intervention. It was just the look of someone deciding, quietly and in real time, where to put what he was holding.

He nodded once. Small. Directed at both of them, or at neither — it wasn't entirely clear. Then he stepped back, and the gap in the doorway was empty, and his footsteps moved back down the hall, slow and even, and then down the stairs, and then the front door opened and closed with a soft, deliberate click.

The room was very quiet.

Solar had both hands folded over the throw pillow in her lap and she was looking at the empty doorway with an expression Chanhee could not fully read, which was unusual. He could almost always read her.

"Chanhee," she said, after a moment.

"I know," he returned.

Neither of them said anything else for a while. Outside the window, the Castle was bright and unhurried, August light falling clean and pale across the grounds, the old trees standing where they had always stood. Inside, the throw pillow sat in Solar's lap and the room smelled of his mother's rose air freshener and everything was exactly as it had always been, except for the fact that it wasn't, and both of them knew it, and there was a boy walking somewhere across the Castle grounds right now carrying something he had not gone looking for.

After a while, his mother's voice rose up from below — warm, easy, entirely unaware — calling that the food was still out if anyone wanted it.

Solar looked at him.

He looked at her.

"Come on," he said at last, and stood, and reached out a hand. She took it, and he pulled her up and held on a beat longer than necessary before letting go, which was as close as he ever got to saying the thing he was feeling out loud, and she knew that, which was precisely why he didn't have to.

They went downstairs.






Chapter Twenty

2010 || NEW YORK

They didn't discuss it.

That was the thing about having known someone since before you had words for knowing — you didn't always need to negotiate. Chanhee was already pulling on his jacket when she reached the bottom of the stairs, and she was already looking toward the front door, and the look they exchanged lasted approximately one second before he tilted his head slightly in one direction and she understood, and they went separate ways without saying a word about it.

Outside, the Castle was cold.

November had arrived in the particular way it arrived in New York — not gradually, but decisively, the warmth simply gone one morning as though it had somewhere else to be. Solar pulled her jacket tighter and crossed the main path and turned toward the east end of the grounds, her footsteps quiet on the stone. The Castle was still at this hour on a Saturday, the kind of still that happened when all the people inside it were occupied with private things and not yet performing anything for each other. The trees had mostly finished losing their leaves and stood bare and certain against the pale grey sky, and somewhere across the grounds a door closed, and then it was quiet again.

She knew, before she saw him, that he was going to be there.

She couldn't have said how. It was the specific, wordless knowledge that came from fifteen years of proximity — from growing up alongside someone, from knowing the particular geography of a person's grief the way you knew the layout of a house you had always lived in. She knew Changmin Ji the way she knew the Castle, which was to say: completely, and without having to think about it. She knew that when he needed to be somewhere he went outside. She knew that outside meant the east park. She knew the east park meant the bench behind the hedgerow, the one tucked back from the main path, the one that caught the last of the afternoon light and was hidden enough that the adults who walked the grounds in the mornings never bothered with it.

She had sat on that bench with Sunwoo more times than she could count.

She registered that fact when she came around the hedgerow and saw Changmin there — sitting with his hands folded in his lap, his jacket on, looking at the middle distance the way he looked at things when he was somewhere else entirely — and the specific, layered weight of it moved through her and settled and she did not let it show on her face. She just walked toward him, and he looked up when he heard her footsteps, and he didn't look surprised, which she had known he wouldn't.

She sat down beside him.

The bench was cold through her jacket. Above them the sky was flat and pale and the bare branches of the elm tree that had always stood at the edge of the park made their still, precise pattern against it. Neither of them said anything.

The silence was not uncomfortable. It had never been uncomfortable between them, not since they were small — since the age of four or five when they had first been placed in the same orbit by virtue of their families sharing a piece of real estate, since long before either of them could have articulated what a friend was. The Castle had given them each other in that specific, accidental way it gave you most things — not by intention, but by proximity, and you took what it offered and you held onto it because you understood, even young, that not everything it offered was worth holding.

She sat beside him and let the quiet be what it was.

After a while — long enough that the cold had properly settled into her fingers, long enough that a single bird had landed on the path and then departed again and they had both watched it without comment — Changmin said, "I wasn't trying to hear it."

His voice was even. Measured. The specific, careful evenness of someone who had decided, before they opened their mouth, exactly how much they were going to let out.

"I know," Solar told him.

"I just came to see Hee." A pause. "I do that. You know I do that."

"I know," she said again, quieter.

He nodded, once, like this confirmed something he had already understood. He looked back at the middle distance, his hands still folded, and she looked at the side of his face — at the jaw that was very slightly too tight, at the eyes that were steady in the particular way eyes were steady when they were being held steady by effort rather than ease — and she thought about the ten-year-old boy who had sat at Castle dinners with his mother and learned, gradually, to carry the shape of an absence without letting it knock him over. She thought about the way he had moved through the last four years — not fine, not pretending to be fine, but managing, which was its own kind of strength, the kind that didn't announce itself.

"How much did you hear?" she asked, carefully.

He considered this. Not performing consideration — actually taking the time to answer accurately, because that was how he did things. "Enough," he said, at last.

The word sat between them, and Solar felt the full weight of it. Enough. Not everything, maybe. But enough to have the picture. Enough to have the shape of it assembled in front of him for the first time, the thing that had always been there in outline suddenly filled in with something solid and specific and terrible.

"Min," she started.

"I knew it was them," he said, before she could continue. Still even. Still careful. "I've known for a long time that it was connected to the Kims. My mom — she never said it directly, but there were things. The way she got at the mention of his name. The way everyone in the Castle moved around the subject whenever it came near it." He paused. "I just didn't know how."

Solar was quiet, because there was nothing to say to that that wouldn't be insufficient.

"Now I do," Changmin finished, simply.

The elm tree moved slightly in the cold wind and then stilled again. Somewhere in the direction of the main path, footsteps passed and faded. She sat with him in the quiet and felt the full, specific grief of it — not her grief, which was its own complicated thing, but his, which was older and deeper and had been living in him longer than she had known what to do with it.

"I'm sorry," she told him, which she knew was not enough and said it anyway, because it was true, because she had been sorry for four years in the private and wordless way she had been sorry for things she couldn't change, and she had never said it to him directly, and it felt important to say it directly now.

He looked at her. His expression did something brief and complicated — a small shift around the eyes, the careful composure momentarily thinner — and he nodded. "I know you are," he said. "I've always known that."

She looked back at him, and she could see it — the thing he was holding together by the specific and practiced effort of someone who had been holding himself together for years, the thing that was very close to the surface right now in a way it usually wasn't, the thing pressing against the inside of the calm he was wearing. She watched his jaw tighten slightly. She watched him look at the middle distance again and breathe, slow and deliberate.

He didn't cry.

He came to the very edge of it — she could see exactly how close it was, the moment his breath changed just slightly, the moment something in his face went very still in the way faces went very still before they broke — and then he pulled back. Not with force. Just the quiet, practiced movement of someone who had done this many times and knew exactly how much effort it took. The calm resettled. He blinked once. He looked at his hands.

She wanted to reach for him. She didn't, immediately, because she was reading him the way she had always read him — looking for the signal that said now, this way rather than assuming — and after a moment she felt it, the slight shift in his posture that was Changmin's specific version of okay, and she reached over and put her hand over both of his where they were folded in his lap.

He looked down at it.

He didn't say anything. He turned one hand over and held hers, and they sat like that, and the Castle was cold and quiet around them and the elm tree stood bare against the pale sky and neither of them moved.

After a while, he said, "It wasn't him."

She knew what he meant. She looked at him, and he was looking at the tree, and his expression was the one she recognized as Changmin having arrived at something after a long and private deliberation — settled, not easy, but settled.

"Sunwoo," he clarified, needlessly. "In 2006. He was the same age as us." A pause. "It wasn't him."

"No," she agreed, softly. "It wasn't."

"I know you — I know what you—" He stopped. Reorganized. "I'm not angry at you, Sol. I need you to know that. Whatever you feel about him, whatever this is." He glanced at her briefly, and in the glance she saw everything he wasn't going to make her explain — the secondary building, the bench they were both sitting on right now, the years of it. He saw all of it and he was offering her something with his eyes that she didn't quite have the words for. "I've never been angry at you. Not once."

Solar looked at him, and the thing that moved through her then was not relief — it was something more complicated than relief, something that had weight to it, the specific sensation of being known completely and found acceptable anyway. She had not realized, until this moment, how much she had been carrying that in particular. How long she had been carrying it.

"Min," she managed.

"Don't," he said, gently. Not unkindly — just firmly, the way he said things when he had made a decision and didn't want it argued with. "It's okay. I mean it."

She looked at the tree. He looked at the tree. Their hands were still folded together between them and the cold was thorough now, the kind that had settled into everything, and she thought about Changmin Ji choosing, every single day, to be who he was. Not because it was easy. Not because the things he was carrying were light. But because it was a choice he had made, somewhere along the way, about what kind of person he was going to be, and he had committed to it with the same quiet, total certainty he committed to everything.

She had always known he was good. She thought, perhaps for the first time, about how much it cost him.

The footsteps on the path behind them were familiar before she turned to look — the specific, unhurried cadence of someone who had located the thing he was looking for and was approaching it without urgency. She turned her head and Chanhee came around the hedgerow with his jacket collar up against the cold, his expression doing the thing it did when he had been worried and was now performing the opposite of worry as a matter of principle.

He looked at the two of them on the bench.

He didn't say anything. He crossed to the bench and sat down on Changmin's other side, and there was a beat — brief, and full — and then he put his hand on the back of Changmin's neck the way he had done since they were small, the specific, unannounced gesture that was Chanhee's most honest language, and Changmin let out a long and very quiet breath and leaned slightly into it and closed his eyes.

The three of them sat on the bench in the November cold, the Castle around them bare and pale and still, and nobody said anything, because there was nothing to say that was bigger than the three of them on this bench on this day, having known each other their whole lives, holding something together that none of them could have held alone.

After a while, Chanhee said, with the tone of someone re-entering the world from somewhere private, "My mother is going to be furious that we left without eating anything."

Changmin made a sound that was not quite a laugh, but was close enough.

Solar looked up at the pale sky through the bare elm branches and thought that she was going to remember this — this exact thing, this specific cold, these two specific people — for a very long time.

She already knew she was right.






Chapter Twenty One

2010 || NEW YORK

September arrived the way it always did — with the particular, unannounced authority of a season that had decided it was done waiting. The air changed first, that specific shift from the heavy, close warmth of late summer into something cooler and cleaner, and then the light changed, and then the Castle changed with it, the way it changed with everything — quietly, completely, as though it had always been this way and always would be.

Solar noticed it from her window on the first morning back at school and thought, without meaning to, that she had not seen Sunwoo in eight days.

She counted it the way she counted most things she was trying not to think about — precisely, involuntarily, the number arriving before she could stop it. Eight days since the east park bench. Eight days since Changmin's nod in the doorway, since Chanhee's hand a beat too long, since she had walked back across the Castle with everything rearranged inside her, since she walked into the secondary Kim building and saw the boy she loved get beat like it was any other casual day. Eight days in which school had started and her father had added two new events to her calendar and she had sat through a four-hour Lee Industries board introduction dinner on a Tuesday evening and smiled at people she didn't know and answered questions about her academic record and her future plans and the direction of the company with the composure of someone who had been performing this particular version of herself since she was old enough to hold a fork at a formal table.

She was very good at it. That had never been the problem.

The problem was what it cost, and what there was to come home to afterward — the house at night, the homework, the ceiling above her bed in the dark, all the things she was carrying pressing against her ribs with nowhere particular to go. She had told Chanhee most of it. She had told Changmin some of it. She had told Sunwoo none of it, because Sunwoo hadn't been at her window in weeks now, and she had not texted him to ask why because she was Solar Lee and she knew how to wait, and also because some part of her understood, without being told, that whatever was keeping him busy was not a choice he was making about her.

She understood it. That didn't make it lighter.

School helped, in the way that structure always helped — filling the hours with something that required her attention and gave her performance outlet. Upper East Side High in September had a particular energy, the renewed ambition of students who had spent the summer either entirely forgetting everything or convincing themselves this would be the year they got ahead of it. She was, as always, somewhere in the former category in terms of the summer and firmly in the latter in terms of the ambition. AP coursework had started immediately and without apology, her schedule was already more loaded than it had been in spring, and her father had made clear at the board dinner — over the salad course, in the specific tone he used when he was not asking a question — that her grades this year were the foundation of everything that came after.

Everything that comes after was a phrase Jaesang Lee used often, and it meant Lee Industries. It always meant Lee Industries.

She was fifteen years old. She was going to inherit one of the most powerful companies in New York. She had known this since she was thirteen and had, over the two years since, reached a kind of complicated peace with it — not resignation, not enthusiasm, but the specific, determined acceptance of someone who had decided that if this was what she was going to do, she was going to do it well. The tutoring sessions had started again on Monday mornings: economics, corporate law, financial literacy, all of it delivered by a quiet, efficient man named Mr. Park who looked at her across the conference table in her father's study and treated her exactly like the future executive she was expected to be, which she appreciated and also found, occasionally, exhausting.

On Wednesday morning of the second week of school, she sat through the tutoring session and the school day and the late-afternoon meeting her father had scheduled with a Lee Industries department head who apparently needed to be introduced to her formally, and she came home and went upstairs and sat at her desk and looked at her homework and thought about the fact that it was now eight days and then thought very deliberately about something else instead.

Chaerin appeared in her doorway at approximately eight o'clock.

She leaned against the frame with her arms crossed and the particular expression she wore when she had decided to be generous with information she was not technically required to share. "I saw him today," she announced. "At school. He was at the lockers near the east wing."

Solar looked up from her textbook. She kept her expression carefully neutral. "Okay," she said.

Chaerin's eyes narrowed by approximately one millimeter, which was her version of a raised eyebrow. "He looked fine," she continued, with the tone of someone providing a service. "In case you were wondering. Which you weren't, obviously."

"Obviously," Solar agreed.

"Just thought you should know." Chaerin pushed off the doorframe. She was already turning to leave when she glanced back, the mischief sitting openly in her face now, the warmth underneath it equally open in the way it always was with Chaerin — never hidden, just competing with the impulse to be irritating. "Nine days is a long time," she observed lightly, and disappeared down the hall before Solar could respond.

Solar looked at the empty doorway for a moment.

Then she looked back at her textbook, turned the page, and read the same paragraph three times without retaining a single word of it.

It was after eleven when she finally gave up on the homework and turned off the desk lamp and sat in the dark for a moment, the Castle quiet around her. Downstairs, her parents' voices had faded an hour ago. Jaehyun's light was still on under his door — she had seen it on her way to the bathroom, the thin gold line of it in the hallway dark, meaning he was awake and working the way he was always awake and working. Chaerin and Juyeon had gone quiet. The house was in its late-night state, settled and still, and Solar sat in the particular silence of it and thought about not much at all.

The sound at the window was so familiar by now that she was already out of her chair before she had consciously registered it.

She crossed to the window in the dark and pushed it open, and there he was — crouched on the ledge with the easy, unhurried balance he always had there, his jacket dark against the night behind him, his expression doing the thing it did when he had arrived somewhere and was waiting to see what the arrival produced.

She looked at him.

He looked at her.

"Nine days," she informed him, quietly, stepping back to let him in.

Something at the corner of his mouth moved. "I know," he said, and climbed through.

He landed on her floor and straightened in the dark, and she pulled the window closed behind him, and for a moment they just stood there in the near-darkness with the faint light of the Castle grounds coming through the glass, close enough that she could see his face properly. He looked — tired was the wrong word for it. It was more than tired. It was the specific, accumulated look of someone who had been carrying a full operational load for days without adequate rest, who was running on a particular brand of focused determination that Sunwoo had in considerable supply but that was not, she was learning, inexhaustible.

She didn't ask about it. She had learned when to ask and when to let a thing settle first.

They moved to the bed the way they always did — her against the headboard, him beside her, the familiar geometry of the space they had made between them over months of exactly this. The lamp stayed off. The Castle was quiet outside the window, and inside her room everything was dark and warm, and she could feel the specific quality of what he had come to talk about in the way you could feel things before they were said, a certain weight in the air that wasn't quite silence.

She let it sit for a moment longer.

Then she said, carefully, into the dark: "I know what Sihyuk did."

He didn't move. She felt the stillness settle into him — not flinching, not retreating, but a particular quality of being very still that was different from his ordinary stillness, the way a held breath was different from an exhale. She kept going, because she had decided, somewhere in the past eight days, that she was not going to make him pull it out of her.

"Changmin's father," she uttered. "Dongwoo Ji. I know that your father had him killed. In 2006." She looked at the dark in front of her rather than at him, giving him the space to receive it. "I know it was connected to the deal breaking. I know it was meant as a message to my father." She paused. "Chanhee figured it out, when I told him everything about your life. He put the pieces together." A beat. "And then I put the rest."

The silence lasted long enough that she turned to look at him.

He was looking at her already. His expression was the neutral one — sealed, careful — but underneath it something was moving, something she had learned to look for in the micro-adjustments of his face, the small and private workings that happened under the composure's surface. He was reading her. She could feel it, that specific attention he brought to her specifically, taking in everything she had said and everything she hadn't.

He looked, she thought, almost — not surprised. Something more complicated. The specific expression of someone who had calculated that a person might arrive at a conclusion and had then watched that person actually arrive, and found that the watching was different from the calculating in a way he hadn't quite prepared for.

She held his gaze and waited.

"How long," he said, finally.

"Have I known?" She thought about it. "The bones of it, a while. Saying it out loud — a couple weeks."

He nodded, once. A long exhale through his nose, slow and deliberate. He looked at the window, and then back at her, and then he said, with the flatness of someone stating a fact that had been living in him for four years: "If I was older, I would have stopped it."

She looked at him. She felt the full weight of it — not the words, exactly, but what was underneath them, the specific thing it cost him to say it. He was not someone who made declarations. He was not someone who offered up the contents of his interior without cause, without necessity. He said this because he meant it in a way that required saying, because somewhere under everything he was still the person who had moved Changmin's drink back from the edge of the table without being asked, who had answered her text at midnight for years, who had drawn a small heart in the margin of a math worksheet when he was twelve years old and then looked at it like it had surprised even him.

"I know," she told him softly.

He held her gaze for a moment. Then he looked away, and the quiet resettled around them, heavier now and also somehow cleaner — the specific quality of air after something has been said that needed saying, the way a room felt different after a window had been opened in it.

She thought about Changmin on the bench, the almost-cry that hadn't come, it wasn't him. She thought about what it meant that both of them had arrived at the same place by entirely different roads.

"He doesn't blame you," she offered, after a moment. "Changmin. I want you to know that."

Something moved in Sunwoo's expression — very brief, very private, the kind of movement she would have missed if she hadn't been watching for it. He didn't say anything. She hadn't expected him to. She had said it because it was true and because he deserved to have it, not because she expected a response.

She settled against his shoulder, and his arm moved around her the way it always did — easy, unhurried, certain — and the desk lamp stayed off and the Castle was quiet outside and neither of them said anything for a long time. She thought about nine days and about the weight she had been carrying alone and about the way it had just, somehow, distributed itself slightly better. About how that was the specific function of him, in her life — not to take things away from her, not to fix them, but to be the person she did not have to perform anything for.

Down the hall, a floorboard creaked.

They both heard it. Solar closed her eyes briefly, already knowing.

"Sol," came a whispered voice from directly outside her door, at approximately the volume of someone who had decided that a closed door was a sufficient substitute for distance. "I know he's in there."

"Chaerin," Solar whispered back, in the loudest whisper she could manage, "go to bed."

A pause. "I just wanted to say hi."

"You can say hi in the morning."

"But—"

"Chaerin Lee," Solar warned.

Another pause — longer this time, weighted with the particular calculation of a twelve-year-old deciding whether the potential reward was worth the certain consequence. Then, from the floor, came a short quiet knock, three times, in the specific pattern that was the Lee sibling code for fine, but this isn't over. Solar pressed her lips together to keep from laughing.

"She's going to be insufferable about this," she murmured.

"She already is," Sunwoo returned, in the tone of someone who had accepted this as an immutable fact of the universe, and she felt the warmth of it in her chest — him knowing her sister, her world, the specific texture of her life in this house, the way he had folded himself into it so gradually and so completely that Chaerin knocked on her door in code now.

She thought about her father's voice at the board dinner. Everything that comes after. She thought about Mr. Park across the conference table and the four-hour evenings and the weight of the Lee name settling heavier every month. She thought about all the things she was expected to be and all the things she already was, and about the fact that this — the dark room, the window, the arm around her shoulders, the twelve-year-old in the hallway who was absolutely still awake with her ear pressed to the door — was not something Lee Industries or her father's calendar or anyone's plans for her future had any claim on whatsoever.

She was fifteen years old.

She let herself have it, completely, without putting it somewhere smaller than it was.

Outside the window, the Castle was dark and quiet. Somewhere down the hall, a floorboard creaked again as Chaerin retreated, badly, toward her room. Sunwoo's hand was warm at her shoulder and his breathing had slowed into the specific rhythm it took on when he was somewhere he was willing to be, and Solar sat in the particular peace of it — this exact thing, this specific quiet, this specific person — and let the rest of the world wait until morning.

It would all still be there.

It always was.



Chapter Twenty Two

2010 || NEW YORK

The Grand Meridian's private ballroom had a particular quality in the evening — the chandeliers turned down to something warm and amber, the tables set with the precision of people who had been doing this for decades, the whole room arranged to suggest that wealth was effortless, which was itself one of the most expensive illusions money could buy. Solar had been coming to these events her whole life and she still felt it every time she walked in — the way the room pressed a particular version of her into existence the moment she crossed the threshold, the way it expected things before she had opened her mouth.

She was being introduced differently this year.

Her father had her beside him from the moment they arrived — not trailing at the family's periphery the way she had in previous years, not simply present as one of Jaesang Lee's children, but positioned. Deliberate. Introduced to the men in dark suits and the women in structured dresses as Solar, my eldest, she'll be running things one day with the casual certainty of someone laying groundwork he expected to hold for decades. She smiled and shook hands and said the right things in the right register — warm but not informal, confident but not presumptuous, every syllable calibrated to the room — and she felt the weight of it settle across her shoulders in the specific, growing way of something becoming real rather than hypothetical.

She was fifteen years old and this room was already beginning to belong to her, and some part of her understood that belonging to her and belonging to it were the same thing, and that she had not yet fully decided how she felt about that.

By ten o'clock she had shaken forty hands and eaten approximately none of her dinner and had a conversation with a man named Charles Whitmore about supply chain optimization that she had somehow managed to make both intelligent and gracious despite the fact that she had done the relevant reading three days ago and had not slept enough since. Her mother, moving through the room on the other side of it, caught her eye once and gave her the small, private smile that meant you're doing well, which Solar received and felt warmed by and then felt the complicated thing underneath the warmth, the thing she always felt when her mother was proud of her at events like this — the gratitude and the loneliness of it existing here, specifically, rather than anywhere else.

Her father didn't say you did well on the car ride home. He said, looking out the window with the composed satisfaction of someone reviewing a favorable report: "People are starting to see it."

She looked at him. He didn't look back. She understood that this was, for Jaesang Lee, the equivalent of a standing ovation, and she filed it away in the place she kept the things he gave her that required translation.

The house was quiet by midnight. Her parents' voices faded behind the closed door of their wing, the far end of the mansion settling into its late-night stillness, and Solar sat at her desk in the dark and did not do any homework because there was no version of her that had anything left to give to AP Economics at this particular hour. She turned her desk chair toward the window instead and pulled her knees up and looked out at the Castle dark and thought about nothing in particular, which was sometimes the only rest available.

She heard the window.

She was already smiling by the time she crossed the room to open it.

He was on the ledge with his jacket collar up against the early-September chill, his dark eyes finding hers immediately, and something in her chest did the thing it always did — that warm, certain thing she still didn't have a name for and had stopped pretending she needed one. She stepped back and he came through, landing on her floor in one fluid motion, and straightened, and just looked at her for a moment with that specific quality of attention he reserved for her — not assessing, not performing, just present, completely and without reservation.

"You went to something tonight," he observed, taking in the state of her — the dress she hadn't yet changed out of, the slight tiredness around her eyes, the thing she was still carrying from the room.

"My father's event," she confirmed. "Forty handshakes."

Something moved briefly in his expression — not quite sympathy, but the acknowledgment of it, the specific recognition of someone who understood what it was to exist inside a world that had plans for you. He reached out and tucked a piece of hair behind her ear, unhurried, and his hand stayed at the side of her face for a moment with his thumb at her cheekbone, and she leaned into it slightly the way she had stopped pretending she wasn't going to do.

"You're tired," he stated.

"I'm fine," she returned, which was true enough, and also not the point. The point was that he was here, and the room felt different with him in it, and she had been performing Solar Lee for approximately six hours and this was the part of the night where she didn't have to do that anymore.

She stepped closer, and he let her, his hand moving from her face to the back of her neck, easy and certain, and she tipped her head back to look at him properly. "Hi," she said, quietly.

"Hi, Sol," he returned, just as quietly, and the corner of his mouth moved in the way it did when something was actually landing in him and he wasn't going to announce it.

She reached up on her toes and kissed him, briefly and without preamble, because they were past the point of making a production of it and this was just what they did now, and she felt the specific warmth of his response — the slight tightening of his hand at her neck, the way he was always steady and always present and never hurried about anything — and when she pulled back she was smiling and he was looking at her with that expression that was very close to the one she had spent years trying to name.

She went to change while he settled on her bed, and she came back in something more comfortable and climbed in beside him, and they found their usual arrangement without discussion — her against his shoulder, his arm around her, the lamp off and the Castle quiet outside — and she exhaled, really exhaled, for what felt like the first time since the car ride home.

They talked for a while, unhurried, the way they talked when there was no particular agenda — she told him about Charles Whitmore and supply chain optimization and the look on her father's face in the car, and he listened the way he always listened, completely, without filling the spaces she didn't need filled. She asked him how the training was going, and he told her, briefly and without detail, that it was progressing. She heard the exhaustion underneath the brevity and didn't press.

"Are you still—" she started, carefully, sometime after the conversation had moved through several other things and arrived at a natural pause. She found the words. "Are you still using? From the supply."

He looked at her. She met his eyes steadily, because she had learned that looking away from difficult things with Sunwoo only made them harder.

"No," he said, simply. No elaboration, no defensiveness.

"When did you stop?"

A pause. "A while ago." He held her gaze. "It compromised things."

She took that in. She understood what things meant — his precision, his control, the specific and total operational capability he held himself to. She also understood, in the quieter place underneath the practical reason, that he had looked at it and decided it was not worth what it cost, and that this was a thing he had done for himself, by himself, which was not a small thing for someone whose entire life had been shaped by his father's decisions about him.

"Okay," she said, which was not all she felt but was the right amount of it to say, and he received it as such, which was one of the things she had always loved about talking to him — the way he understood proportion. The way he never needed more than she was giving.

She settled back against his shoulder, and his hand found hers, and the room was quiet.

She wasn't sure how much time had passed when she became aware of the sound down the hall. Small, faint, the specific kind of faint that two-year-olds produced when they were awake in the night and not yet certain whether they wanted to announce it. Solar had developed an ear for it over the past year — Yein's room was closer to hers than to her parents' wing, and it was not the first time she had been the one to hear it.

She tilted her head. Sunwoo's hand in hers went still, which meant he had heard it too.

"I'll be right back," she told him.

Yein's door was open the small amount it was always left open, the nightlight casting a low, warm glow across the room, and Solar pushed it wider and found her youngest sister sitting upright in the toddler bed with the expression of someone who had recently made a decision and was now facing its consequences. She wasn't crying. She was just awake, fully and with intent, her green eyes bright in the nightlight, her dark hair in the particular chaos of a child who had been sleeping hard before abruptly not sleeping at all.

"Sissy," Yein announced, in the voice of someone identifying a highly relevant development.

"Hi, sweetheart," Solar whispered, crossing to her. "It's late. Why are you awake?"

Yein considered this question. "Don't know," she concluded, which was honest.

Solar looked at her — at those green eyes, at the small face regarding her with the complete trust of a child who had decided Solar's presence solved the problem even before the problem was identified — and she thought for a moment, and then she picked her up. Yein went willingly and completely, tucking herself against Solar's shoulder with the boneless, total comfort of a two-year-old who was confident in the arms holding her.

"Come here," Solar murmured, and carried her back down the hall.

She pushed her own door open with her foot and stepped inside, and Yein lifted her head from Solar's shoulder and her green eyes found Sunwoo immediately — sitting up on the bed now, having heard them coming, his expression the composed neutral one. He and Yein regarded each other in the low light of the Castle outside the window.

Solar watched it happen.

Yein had no framework for him. She had no category, no prior information, no reason for caution. She just looked at him with the pure, unmediated attention of a child encountering something new and forming an opinion about it in real time. She looked at him for a long moment — at the jacket, at the stillness, at whatever it was about Sunwoo Kim that every room registered before he said anything — and then she made her decision.

She reached out from Solar's arms, both hands extended toward him, in the universal two-year-old gesture that meant you.

Solar bit the inside of her cheek.

Sunwoo looked at the small, extended hands. Something very subtle happened in his face — not warmth, not softness, nothing he would have named or acknowledged, but a slight and private shift, a fraction of something that didn't fit neatly into any of the categories he usually operated in. He was, Solar thought, genuinely at a loss for the correct response, which was one of the rarer things she had ever seen.

He leaned forward slightly, and Yein, interpreting this as an invitation — which, in Yein's assessment, it was — reached out and poked him directly on the nose.

Sunwoo went very still.

Yein found this deeply satisfying. She looked back at Solar with an expression that said I have assessed this and I approve and then looked back at Sunwoo and poked his nose again for confirmation.

Solar pressed her lips together so hard they went white. She was absolutely not going to laugh. She was going to be composed and responsible about this.

"Boop," Yein observed, to nobody in particular, with the gravity of a scholar documenting an important finding.

Solar laughed. She couldn't help it — it came out of her before she could stop it, one of those laughs that arrived without warning, and she pressed her hand over her mouth and shook with it, and from the bed Sunwoo looked at her with the expression he had when he had decided something was her fault, which only made it worse.

He looked back at Yein, who was watching him with those luminous green eyes, entirely unbothered by his stillness, entirely at ease in a way that Solar thought probably had something to do with the fact that Yein was constitutionally incapable of being intimidated by anything she didn't have the context to be intimidated by. She just saw a person. She had decided she liked him. That was the whole of it.

Sunwoo, very slowly, held out one finger.

Yein grabbed it with her entire small fist and held on.

The room was quiet. Solar watched him look at her sister's hand around his finger — the small and complete grip of it, the absolute uncalculated trust — and she watched the thing move through his face again, brief and private, there and then carefully put away. He didn't look at Solar. She was glad, because she wasn't certain she could have held her expression if he had.

She eventually settled Yein back in her own bed — it took three attempts, two glasses of water, and a negotiation about whether Yein's stuffed rabbit needed to be on the left or right side, during which Yein was immovable and Solar conceded entirely — and came back to find Sunwoo exactly where she'd left him, except that he was leaning against the headboard now with one arm across his knee, looking at the window in the specific way he looked at things when he was somewhere else.

She climbed back in beside him. He pulled her in without ceremony, and she settled under his arm and felt the solid, certain warmth of him and thought about a two-year-old with green eyes grabbing his finger like it was the most natural thing in the world.

"She likes you," Solar said, into his shoulder.

A pause. "She doesn't know me," he returned.

"She doesn't need to," Solar told him, simply, and she felt the slight tightening of his arm around her that was Sunwoo receiving something he wasn't going to respond to out loud, and she left it there, in the quiet between them, and let it be what it was.

They stayed like that for a while — the room dark, the Castle still outside, the particular peace of very late at night when the world had gone somewhere else and there was nothing left but this. Solar was almost asleep when she felt him shift slightly, and she heard the quality of the shift — not settling in, but the opposite of settling in, the beginning of a decision.

She tilted her head to look at him. He was looking at the window.

"I have something to show you," he said.

She blinked. "What?"

He looked at her. His expression was the same as always — composed, reading her — but underneath it there was something she recognized as the particular energy he had when he had already decided something and was waiting for her to catch up to it. He moved, untangling from her, and got to his feet, and she watched him cross to the window with the unhurried, certain gait he brought to everything, and push it open.

She sat up. "Sunwoo."

He turned back to look at her, one hand on the window frame, the September night behind him.

"It's a school night," she informed him.

He held her gaze. He did not, notably, say anything.

"It's almost one in the morning," she added.

Still nothing. Just that look — patient, calm, entirely certain that she was going to come with him.

She stared at him. She thought about her alarm, about first period, about the AP English essay that was due Thursday. She thought about her father's voice: everything that comes after. She thought about forty handshakes and supply chain optimization and the weight of the room that pressed a particular version of her into existence the moment she walked through its doors.

Then she thought about the window, and the night outside it, and the boy standing beside it looking at her like he already knew what she was going to do.

"My parents," she said, making one last reasonable attempt. "What if they—"

"The footage is looped," he told her, simply. "How often do they come into your bedroom at night?"

She opened her mouth. Closed it. He was, infuriatingly, completely right. Her parents' wing was on the other side of the mansion. In the six months of window visits, there had not been a single close call that wasn't Chaerin-related, and Chaerin already knew.

Solar Lee looked at Sunwoo Kim standing at her open window in the middle of a school night, the Castle dark behind him, looking back at her with that expression that said I have thought about this and I am not worried, and she made the only decision she had ever really been going to make.

She got up.

He reached out when she was close enough, his hand finding hers in the dark — warm and certain, the way he did everything — and she felt the pull of it, the electric and specific pull that was simply the condition of being near him, and she thought that she would follow him out of this window and across the Castle grounds and wherever it was he was taking her without needing a reason beyond the fact that it was him asking.

Not that she would tell him that.

"If I fail English on Thursday," she informed him, as he helped her onto the ledge, "this is your fault."

"Solar Lee would never fail English," he deadpans, with the tone of someone who had reviewed the available evidence and found the concern statistically unsound.

She looked at him, perched on the ledge in the September night with his hand in hers and the Castle dark and open below them, and something bright and warm and entirely uncomplicated moved through her. And she looked at the ground beneath them, the Castle grounds with unlimited security cameras that he somehow managed to bug out and she was far too afraid to question further.

"That's a long fall..." she mumbles, not really speaking directly to him, but needing to get that fact off her chest before she even made such an attempt in the first place.

"The last thing I'd ever do is let you fall," Sunwoo monotoned, but he meant every word and that much was clear. 

She pursed her lips, eyes glancing from his serious, blank expression, to the ground beneath them. Her mind wandered, trying to think of whatever Sunwoo could possibly have planned, before deciding whatever it was must be worth it.

"Okay," she exhales, "lead the way, then."

And he did.




Chapter Twenty Three

2010 || NEW YORK

The Castle at night had a particular quality that the Castle in daylight didn't — something that had to do with the absence of performance. In the daytime it was always on, always doing the thing it was built to do: suggesting money, suggesting permanence, suggesting that the people who lived inside its walls were exactly who they presented themselves to be. At night, with the lights down and the walkways empty and the big houses glowing warmly behind their windows, it was something else. Just a place. Just a collection of old buildings and careful gardens and the particular, deep quiet that came from being set apart from the rest of the city, which was never truly quiet no matter the hour.

Solar had grown up here and loved it and chafed against it and taken it for granted, often all three at once. Tonight she was seeing it slightly differently — through the gap of something about to change, the specific heightened clarity of a moment that was announcing itself as one she would remember.

Sunwoo moved through the dark beside her, unhurried, his hand still in hers from the window, the light weight of it both casual and entirely deliberate. She had stopped asking where they were going after the second turn — partly because he clearly had no intention of telling her and partly because she had decided somewhere on the descent from the ledge that she trusted whatever this was, which was a decision she made about very few things and had made about him so many times now that it barely registered as a decision anymore.

The east park opened up ahead of them through the line of hedgerows. She recognized it immediately — the familiar path, the old elm tree, the bench tucked back from the main walkway. She had been here in daylight with Sunwoo and without him, in childhood and recently, and it held more than one kind of weight by now.

And then she saw what was parked beside the elm tree, and she stopped walking.

The motorcycle was — there were no other words for it — beautiful. Not in the precious, ornamental way of something meant to be looked at, but in the specific beauty of a thing that had been made to function exactly as it should and had been made with the kind of attention that left its mark. Even in the low light of the Castle grounds she could see the lines of it — the frame rebuilt from the ground up, the engine rebuilt with the patient and precise care she had listened to him describe over months of late-night conversations. Every surface said this was made by someone who cared about it. The chrome caught the distant glow of the mansion lights. The body was dark, clean, deliberate.

Two helmets rested on the seat.

One of them she recognized — dark, worn, the helmet she had seen him with before. The other was new. She could see the difference immediately, the careful condition of it, and something about the way it was sitting there beside his, like it had always been there, like it had been placed — she looked at it and felt something move through her that she wasn't immediately prepared for.

She crossed to it, slowly, and picked it up.

On the left side, small and precise and done with the kind of care that said it had been thought about, was a solar eclipse. Dark circle, the corona fanning out around it in fine, clean lines — unmistakable, unhurried, entirely specific.

She stood there holding it and didn't say anything for a moment, because there was a version of herself that would have made this into something verbal and she chose not to, because some things were more true if you just let them be true without narrating them. She turned to look at him instead.

He was watching her, leaning against the motorcycle with his arms loosely crossed, his expression doing the specific neutral thing that wasn't actually neutral — the one she had spent years learning to read. He looked back at her steadily.

"You had this made," she said, which was not a question.

"Yes," he confirmed.

"Before tonight."

"Yes."

She looked at the helmet in her hands. She thought about the promise of when it's done made months ago in her bedroom, about the specific quality of a Sunwoo Kim certainty, about the fact that he had commissioned a custom helmet for her before he had told her the motorcycle was finished, before he had said I have something to show you, before she had climbed out of her window into the September dark. He had been carrying this for a while. He had decided she was going to be here for this, and he had made it true before she knew it was happening.

"Sunwoo," she said, in the voice she used when she didn't have a better word.

"Get on the bike, Sol," he returned, in the tone of someone redirecting an emotion before it could land somewhere that would require him to do something with it.

She looked at him, then at the motorcycle, then at the helmet in her hands, and felt the nerves arrive properly for the first time — the specific, stomach-tipping nerves of something entirely new, something she had no prior experience to draw from. She had never been on a motorcycle. She had listened to him talk about this one for months and had imagined it in the abstract way you imagined things you hadn't done, and now it was in front of her, real and present and considerably more physical than any version she'd constructed in her head.

"Does this thing go fast?" she asked, which was not the question she meant to ask.

"Yes," he said, with complete honesty, which was not reassuring.

"Is it safe?" she tried.

"Yes."

"Do you even have a license?"

The corner of his mouth moved. "Licenses," he observed, with the mildness of someone sharing a philosophical position, "are for people who get caught, Sol. Get on the bike."

She pressed her lips together. She looked at the motorcycle — at the rebuilt frame and the careful chrome and the dark body and the seat that had room for two people, which he had known before she knew it — and she breathed in and put the helmet on and fastened it, and the solar eclipse sat at her left temple, and she got on the bike.

It was larger than she expected, the way physical things were always larger or heavier or more real than their imagined versions. She settled onto the seat behind him and wasn't sure where to put her hands for approximately one full second before Sunwoo reached back and placed them at his waist, matter-of-fact, like the answer to that particular question was obvious, which she supposed it was. She felt the warmth of him through his jacket and tightened her grip slightly, and tried very hard to look like someone who was not moderately terrified.

He knew anyway. She could tell from the way he didn't move immediately — the slight pause, him waiting a beat longer than he needed to, giving her the moment without making it a moment.

"Ready?" he asked.

"Yes," she said, which was mostly true.

He started the motorcycle.

The engine came alive under them with a sound that was lower and more deliberate than she had expected — not the aggressive roar of something uncontrolled, but the specific, confident sound of a thing running exactly as it had been designed to run, which she supposed tracked with everything she knew about how it had been built. She felt it through the whole seat, through her hands at his waist, through the air around them that was suddenly doing something different.

She had approximately one second to process this before Sunwoo rolled the motorcycle forward about three feet, very slowly, and then stopped.

She jerked slightly from the movement — pure instinct, her hands tightening at his waist — and he looked back at her over his shoulder with an expression she had almost never seen on him. Not quite a smile, but the adjacent thing — that specific pull at the corner of his mouth when something had amused him and he was allowing it to, the particular quality of Sunwoo Kim privately enjoying himself.

"Sunwoo," she hissed, because she knew. She absolutely knew that had been on purpose.

"You were tense," he informed her, with tremendous composure.

"I was not—"

"You were. You're less tense now."

She stared at the back of his head. He was, infuriatingly, correct — the jolt of surprise had done something to the tightness in her chest, replaced it with something closer to indignation, which was functionally less debilitating. He had known it would. She was, she realized, holding onto him slightly less like someone prepared to be launched into the stratosphere and slightly more like a normal person.

"That was mean," she informed him.

"That was practical," he corrected, and she felt more than heard the tone of it — warm in the way he was warm, which was not the way most people were warm, which was quiet and certain and underneath everything rather than on the surface of it.

She settled against his back, properly now, and he pulled out of the east park and they moved through the Castle grounds, and then they were through the gate, and then they were in New York.

She had lived in this city her whole life and she had never seen it like this.

It was past one in the morning and the streets were not empty — New York streets were never empty — but they were different at this hour, the daytime urgency replaced by something looser and more honest, the city in its after-hours state where it stopped performing for tourists and commuters and just existed. The lights were the same lights she had looked at from car windows her whole life and they were entirely different from here, from the open air of the bike, the September night against her face and Sunwoo's jacket solid under her hands and the engine beneath them running smooth and certain.

She understood, for the first time, what he had been working toward. Not just the motorcycle — the feeling of it. The specific and irreplaceable quality of being this exposed to something this large, with nothing between you and the road and the city and the night but your own hands holding on. It was the most unprotected she had felt in months, possibly years, and somehow it was the safest she had felt in just as long, which said something she wasn't ready to say out loud yet.

She held on and let the city move around her and thought about nothing at all, which was, she was realizing, the point.

Greenwood was exactly as she had imagined it — and not at all. The building was squat and old and entirely unremarkable from the outside, the kind of structure that existed in a thousand Brooklyn blocks without distinction, and she would have walked past it a hundred times without looking twice. The sign above the door was barely legible, the lettering faded to a soft suggestion of itself. Sunwoo pulled up alongside the building and cut the engine, and the city noise reasserted itself around them — distant and present, the permanent ambient sound of New York doing what New York did.

She climbed off and pulled the helmet off and looked at the building and then at him.

"This is yours," she said, not quite disbelieving, just recalibrating.

"Yes," he confirmed, already moving to the door.

Inside, the smell hit her first — old rubber and something slightly chemical and underneath it, faintly, the smell of metal and oil that she recognized as the smell of him at work, the smell that sometimes came in with him through the window after a late evening at the gym. She stood in the entrance and let her eyes adjust.

The main room was large — larger than the outside had suggested, the way old buildings sometimes were. A boxing ring stood in the center, the canvas worn and the ropes slightly slack, but structurally sound. Equipment along the walls, some of it old and some of it clearly newer, added after the original inventory. High windows that in daylight would let in significant light, currently dark, the only illumination coming from the work lights mounted to the ceiling.

It was functional. Practical. His.

She looked at all of it and then looked at him and found him watching her the way he watched her in her room — the full attention, the specific quality of it, the version of himself he didn't give to anyone or anything else. He wanted to see what she made of it. She could feel that plainly.

"It's real," she told him, which was the truest thing she could say.

Something moved in his face. He crossed to the far wall and hit a second set of lights, and the back end of the gym came into relief — the workshop space, the tools arranged on a makeshift bench along the wall. She followed him in and stood in the middle of it and looked around, and then she looked at him, and he had taken off his jacket.

She had seen him with his jacket off before. In her room, occasionally, draped over her desk chair while they studied. She had not seen him in the light.

The ink started at his collarbone and came down — not finished, not yet, but present and definite and clearly the beginning of something much larger, the lines of it running down his left shoulder and across his chest with the specific and deliberate quality of work done by someone who knew what they wanted. It was dark against his skin in the gym light, and she could see that it had been done recently, the work still settling into itself, the edges still sharp.

He hadn't mentioned it.

She realized, with the particular clarity of someone assembling a retroactive picture, that she had been in his presence in the dark, in her bedroom, in all the contexts where a jacket stayed on, and had simply not seen it. She thought about all the times his arm had been around her and she had been looking at his face or out the window or at the ceiling and had not been looking at his arm, and found herself feeling the specific strangeness of realizing you had been close to something for a long time without seeing it.

"When did you—" she started.

"This summer," he said. He pulled the jacket back on, not quickly, not with the energy of someone covering something up, just with the practicality of someone who was done with the conversation and had moved on to whatever came next.

She held his gaze. "Does it hurt?"

He looked at her as if this were a question with an obvious answer, which she supposed it was, and she pressed her lips together and let it go.

"Show me," she said instead, because she had been thinking it since she walked through the door and saw the ring in the center of the room, and she was not someone who held back things she had already decided. "What you can do. Show me."

He looked at her for a moment. There was a quality to his stillness that she had learned — the beat between deciding something and acting on it, brief and barely perceptible.

Then he moved to the center of the ring and looked back at her with one hand resting on the rope. "Get up here," he told her.

She crossed to the apron and stepped up and ducked under the rope the way she had seen people do it, considerably less gracefully than she had imagined she would, and straightened, and found herself standing in the ring in a gym in Brooklyn at one-thirty in the morning in September in the clothes she had slept in, which was not a sentence she had ever anticipated constructing about her own life.

Sunwoo looked at her. She looked at him.

"Stance," he said.

"What?"

"Where you put your feet." He moved toward her, unhurried, and adjusted her feet with one hand at her hip and one at her ankle — easy and matter-of-fact, the way he touched her when he was focused, which was its own specific warmth, the kind that didn't announce itself. "Like this. Weight on the back foot."

She adjusted. He stepped back.

"Hands up," he continued.

She raised them. He looked at her form with the assessing attention he brought to everything operational and she could see him deciding things — what she needed to know, where to start, the specific and patient logic of someone who had taught this before and understood what the first things were.

"Now," he told her, moving to stand in front of her at a distance that was close but not crowding, "hit me."

She blinked. "Hit you."

"Yes."

"With my hands."

"Ideally," he confirmed.

"Sunwoo, I'm not going to—"

"You're not going to hurt me, Sol," he told her, with the specific flatness of someone stating a geological fact. "Hit me."

She looked at him — at the complete and unhurried calm of him, the particular, infuriating certainty — and she swung.

He caught her fist before it connected, his hand wrapping around hers with the complete ease of someone for whom this was an entirely reflexive action, and she felt the warmth of it and the specific frustration of it simultaneously. He looked at her arm.

"Your elbow," he pointed out, and turned it slightly, and the thing that happened in her arm when he did was, she could admit internally, not nothing. "Again."

She tried again.

He corrected.

She tried again.

He didn't correct that time, which she understood was the version of good that existed in Sunwoo Kim's vocabulary, and it landed in her chest with the particular warmth of something she hadn't known she wanted until she had it.

They stayed in the ring for a while — she couldn't have said how long, time moving differently in the specific focused state of learning something with your whole body. He was patient in the way he was patient about things he found genuinely worth being patient about, and she found she was not bad at this, which surprised her enough that she said so.

"You're not surprised," he told her, which was correct.

"I'm not," she agreed. "You've been watching me, haven't you? You already knew I'd be—"

"I had a sense," he said, with the composure of someone who had very much clocked this and was not going to be dramatic about it.

She shook her head, smiling despite herself, and looked at him across the center of the ring in the gym in Brooklyn at whatever hour it was now, his jacket back on and his hair slightly displaced from the work, and she thought about the solar eclipse on the helmet and the rebuilt motorcycle and the gym he had bought when he was fourteen and the way he had looked at Yein's hand around his finger and the small heart in the margin of a math worksheet from three years ago, and she thought that she had been finding the bottom of Kim Sunwoo for five years and she had not found it yet.

She thought she was beginning to understand that she never would.

And she thought, standing in the ring with the gym lights over them and the September night outside and the Castle impossibly far away, that this was the exact right place to be.

Maybe, she had worried that things were going to be ruined, that she would never be able to stand on her own two feet in a world that was so horrifyingly raw and vulgar compared to everything she had ever known. In reality, she had known many of these things all along, she had always known something was off about Sihyuk Kim, from the day that Jaehyun came into her bedroom with that newspaper. But none of that was Sunwoo's fault.

And none of that changed the fact that looking at him right now, she didn't ever see herself with anyone else.

Solar Lee was fifteen years old. She was completely certain.

Chapter Twenty Four

2010 || NEW YORK

October arrived the way October always did in New York — decisively, the warmth gone, the light changing from summer's flat brightness to something more angled and deliberate, the kind of light that made the city look like it was being photographed. The trees in the Castle's east path had begun their turn, amber and rust at the edges, and the morning air had that specific chill that required a jacket and made everything feel slightly more serious than it had a month ago.

Solar noticed it from the window of her AP Economics classroom and thought about the fact that she had not seen Sunwoo in four days.

This was not unusual, technically. The absences had been happening with more frequency since September — small ones, a day or two, the pattern she had identified and filed without examining too closely. He was preparing for something. She understood that without being told, the way she understood most things about him: by accumulation, by inference, by the specific quality of his silences when she asked how things were going and he said fine in the register that meant progressing as required. She trusted him and she understood and she was also, if she was being honest with herself, slightly afraid of what before the end of the year actually meant in practice.

She looked at the textbook open on her desk — derivatives, the mechanics of marginal change, the way a small shift in one variable produced calculable effects everywhere else — and thought that this was not entirely unrelated to the thing she was actually thinking about, and then she made herself focus, because AP Economics was not going to pass itself and the version of herself that existed in this classroom did not have the luxury of being distracted.

She had two tests that week. She had a Lee Industries preparatory session on Friday evening, where Mr. Park would sit across from her in her father's conference room and walk her through Q3 financial analysis frameworks with the patient efficiency of someone who had decided she was going to understand this material and was simply waiting for her to catch up to the decision. She had a young executives function on Saturday morning that she could not miss and did not particularly want to attend.

She wrote marginal cost = change in total cost / change in quantity in the margin of her notebook and kept going.

The lunch table was its usual configuration — Kevin had arrived first and claimed the largest portion of available bench space by the simple method of spreading his belongings across it and then looking confused when people asked him to move, which Chanhee had been audibly objecting to for approximately three minutes by the time Solar arrived. Jacob had quietly shifted Kevin's bag to make room without making it a confrontation, which was such a Jacob move that Solar smiled before she even sat down. Hyunjoon materialized from somewhere and dropped into the seat beside Chanhee with the easy familiarity of someone who had decided this table was his at some point in the past six months and had experienced no meaningful resistance.

The members were further along the table — Sangyeon with his lunch and his characteristic composed quiet, Hyunjae with the slightly post-training bonelessness he carried into the school day when sessions had run early, Eric at the periphery in the way he still sometimes occupied peripheries, present and watchful. Sunwoo was not there, which she had already noted, which she was already not making a thing of.

Changmin arrived slightly after her, with his jacket still on and a book under his arm — he had been in the library, she knew, because he was almost always in the library when he wasn't required to be somewhere else, which Chanhee found bewildering and Jacob found admirable and Kevin had once described as Changmin's whole thing with such affection that it was hard to argue with. He sat at the end of the table and settled in with the unhurried ease of someone who was always exactly as comfortable as he appeared to be.

Kevin immediately turned to him. "Changmin, if you could only eat one food for the rest of your life—"

"We're not doing this again," Chanhee interrupted flatly.

"We are absolutely doing this again," Kevin countered, pointing a fork at him.

"I would eat rice," Changmin said mildly, not looking up from the process of unpacking his lunch, "because you can prepare it approximately forty different ways and it pairs with most other foods, which technically makes the restriction more manageable."

Kevin stared at him. "That's — actually that's a really good answer."

"I know," Changmin confirmed, and opened his book.

Jacob was smiling. Chanhee had the expression of someone who had expected to be irritated and had been outmaneuvered. Solar was watching the table with the specific warmth she felt when they were all like this — easy and known, the ten years of them showing in the way they moved around each other without thought.

It was Changmin who brought it up, and he brought it up the way he brought everything up — quietly, in the space between one thing and the next, without preamble.

"Something happened last week," he mentioned, in the tone of someone sharing information rather than performing news. He was still looking at his book. "My mom got a call from the school's financial administration. An anonymous donation came through the Ji family educational fund." A brief pause. "Significant enough that my tuition is covered through graduation."

The table was quiet for a moment.

Kevin's fork lowered. Jacob looked up. Chanhee was very still in the specific way he was still when he was processing something carefully. Hyunjoon, at the far end, was looking at his lunch with an expression of studied neutrality that Solar clocked immediately and filed.

"Min," Jacob said, softly.

"She cried," Changmin remarked, still with that same measured evenness, and there was something underneath it — not performed composure, but the real and practiced kind, the kind that had been built over four years of carrying something heavy and deciding, every day, to carry it without letting it knock you over. "She didn't think I noticed. I made her tea." A small pause. "She accepted it."
