It was something that had no performance in it whatsoever — just the full and unmediated truth of Sunwoo Kim looking at Sihyuk Kim, and everything that fifteen years of that relationship had deposited between them, present and entire and stated without a single word.

"If you touch her again," Sunwoo said, and his voice was the same as always — flat, low, unhurried — "I won't stop."

He didn't wait for a response. He turned to Solar.

She was still against the wall, her hand at her throat. She was breathing. She was composed in the specific way she was composed when she was doing it deliberately, and her eyes on him were very steady and very wide and full of things she wasn't saying, and he crossed to her and took her face in both his hands and looked at her for a long moment, checking.

"Sol."

"I'm okay," she said, which was what she always said, and he knew it was true in the ways that counted and not entirely true in the others, and he held her face and looked at her and did not let go.

Behind him, he heard his father getting to his feet. He did not turn around. He kept his eyes on Solar's.

His father left the corridor without speaking. The sound of his footsteps faded. And then it was just the two of them in the dim east corridor with the ballroom noise at their backs and December outside the window, and she was looking at him and he was looking at her and neither of them said anything because there was nothing that needed saying that wasn't already being said by the fact of them, here, unchanged.

The corridor door opened.

"Solar — oh my God."

Chanhee moved fast, which was not something people expected from someone so carefully composed, but when it was Solar, Chanhee moved fast. He crossed to her in three steps and his hands were immediately at her face, her shoulders, tilting her chin to look at her throat, and his expression had abandoned all of its performance entirely.

"What happened, are you okay, what—" He was talking fast, which was also not Chanhee, and the combination of the speed and the unguardedness of him landed in Solar's chest in a way that made her eyes sting. "Who did this, your neck—"

"Hee," she said.

"Don't 'Hee' me, Solar, your throat is—"

"Chanhee." She reached up and put her hands over his, which were still at the sides of her face. He stopped. He looked at her. And then, with the complete and sudden certainty of someone who had run out of noise to put between himself and the feeling, he pulled her in.

She let him. She leaned into it — into him, into the specific and irreplaceable reality of her oldest friend, the person who had known her since before either of them had the language for knowing someone — and she felt, for the first time since the corridor, the thing she hadn't been letting herself feel. It pressed against her chest and she breathed through it and held on.

He held on too.

"You scared me," he muttered, into her hair. Not performing anything. Just saying the true thing.

"I know," she said. "I'm sorry."

He pulled back and looked at her face, and his own face was doing the thing it did when he had decided to stop being gentle about something. He looked at Sunwoo. Sunwoo looked back at him.

There was a moment. A long, specific, charged moment of Chanhee Choi and Sunwoo Kim looking at each other across a corridor over the head of the person they both loved most, and whatever passed between them had no words and didn't need them.

Chanhee looked back at Solar. "Okay," he said, which meant a great many things simultaneously.

The door opened again.

Jaehyun took in the corridor in the way he took in everything — immediately, completely, arriving at the correct conclusion in approximately two seconds. His eyes moved from Solar to Chanhee to Sunwoo to the wall where Sihyuk had been, and then back to Solar, and in his face was the thing that was always underneath the Jaehyun of it — the older brother, the one who had been watching and waiting and had arrived, now, to the thing he had been watching toward.

"Solar," he said, and his voice was controlled in the way it was controlled when he was managing something that wanted to be larger. He crossed to her and his hand went briefly to the back of her neck — the gesture he had been making since they were small, the one that meant I see you, I have you — and then he stepped back.

He looked at Sunwoo.

"Is he still in the building?" he asked, and the question was flat and direct and had the quality of someone asking because the answer affected a plan they were already forming.

"He left," Sunwoo said.

Jaehyun nodded, once. He looked at Solar again, and what she saw in his face then — underneath the hard-headedness, underneath the controlled directness of him — was the thing he never led with. Genuine fear. The displaced, retrospective terror of someone who had arrived after the worst of it and was looking at his sister's throat and understanding what he had arrived after.

"You could have—" he started, and didn't finish, which was not like him, which told her exactly how much the unfinished sentence contained.

"I know," she said, quietly. "But I didn't."

He held her gaze for a long moment. Then he exhaled, and his jaw set, and the Jaehyun he showed the world reassembled itself around him. "We're leaving," he said, to the corridor in general. "I'll handle the parents."

"Jae," Solar said.

"Solar," he returned, in a tone that was not a discussion, and she understood and did not argue.

Chanhee squeezed her hand once before he followed Jaehyun back toward the door, pausing at the threshold to give her one last look — the one that said I need to talk to you about everything that just happened and also I'm furious and also I love you — and then the door closed and it was just the two of them.

///

He came through her window late that night.

The Castle was quiet. Her family was home and her parents' voices had long since faded and Chaerin had knocked on her door at some point and Solar had told her she was fine and heard her sister stand in the hallway for a moment before going back to her room, and then it was just the dark and the December cold and the specific silence of the Lee mansion after midnight.

She heard him at the window and she was already turned in her chair by the time he climbed through.

He was still in the suit. The jacket was gone somewhere, the tie loosened, and he looked — not fine, not the composed neutral, but something that had been compressed through a very particular evening and had not yet fully settled. He crossed to her and didn't say anything, and she stood up, and his hands came up and held her face.

He looked at her throat. Something moved through his expression — very brief, very private, the thing that had no performance in it.

"I'm okay," she said, before he could ask.

He held her gaze. His thumbs were at her cheekbones and she could feel the warmth of his hands and the specific quality of his attention, the one she had been the only recipient of for years, and she looked back at him and didn't look away.

"I know," he nods, confidently, at first. And then, quietly, with the flat certainty he brought to the things he meant completely, "I will never let anyone touch you again, Sol. Not anyone. Not ever."

She looked at him. She thought about the corridor and his hands on his father and the if you touch her again, I won't stop, and she thought about five years and a park bench and a pencil heart and a motorcycle in the dark and a ring on her finger and all of the year that had just ended, the full accumulated weight of it, and she believed him.

She believed him the way she believed things she had already decided were true.

"I know," she told him.

He held her face for another moment and then drew her in, and she went, and he pressed his mouth to her forehead and held it there, and she closed her eyes and let the year settle around her.

She was fifteen years old.

She was not who she had been in January. She could feel the shape of the difference — the places where she had hardened, quietly, the things that had stopped being shocking and started being simply true, the knowledge she now carried that she would carry for the rest of her life. It sat in her differently now. Not lightly — it would never be light — but as something she had chosen, with her eyes open, every single time she had been given the choice.

She thought about Mirae in the hallway and the thing she hadn't felt that she would have felt six months ago. She thought about Chanhee's hands on her face in the corridor and Jaehyun's unfinished sentence and the ring on her finger catching the low light of the room. She thought about everything that was coming — the things she could see and the things she couldn't, the years between now and whatever waited at the end of them.

She thought that she was not afraid.

She thought that she was not the same.

She thought, with the specific certainty of someone who has already decided, that this was exactly right.

Outside the window, the Castle was cold and still and entirely unaware of itself. December sat on it like a held breath. And in the dark room, two people who had found each other when they were ten years old held on — the way they always had, the way they always would — and the year ended.



Chapter Thirty

2011 || NEW YORK

The secondary building had a smell she had come to know the way you came to know the smell of any place you spent enough time in — something between machine oil and old wood and the particular layered warmth of people living and working in close quarters. It wasn't unpleasant. It wasn't the Lee mansion, either, with its staff-maintained neutrality and the faint trace of her mother's preferred candles that had been burning in the same rooms for as long as she could remember. It was just itself. The secondary building was just itself, and she had stopped noticing the difference the way she had stopped noticing a lot of things that had once required deliberate adjustment.

She had her textbooks open on the table in the main room. This was not unusual anymore.

What was unusual — what had been unusual, once, and had ceased to be — was the fact that Hyunjae was on the couch three feet to her left with his feet up and an arm slung over his eyes, and that Sangyeon was at the far end of the same table running something on his laptop with the focused, unhurried attention he brought to all technical work, and that the sounds coming from the back room were the sounds of Sunwoo working on something mechanical. The specific, periodic sounds of tools and metal and the low hum of concentration that meant he was somewhere comfortable.

She knew the sounds. She knew the building. She knew, without having to check, exactly where everyone in it was at any given moment, and when she noticed she knew this, she sat with it for a moment and found she didn't have a particular feeling about it.

That was new too.

She turned a page and kept reading.

"You're wrong," Hyunjae said, without moving the arm from his eyes.

She looked up. "I haven't said anything."

"About the essay." He tilted his head slightly toward her textbook without looking at it. "Whatever you're writing in the margins. You've been making that sound for ten minutes."

She looked down. She had been making small notations in pencil, the kind she made when she was working through an argument and finding the gaps in it. "How do you know I'm wrong if you can't see what I'm writing?"

He shrugged, which was an impressive gesture for someone lying down. "You make a different sound when you're right."

She stared at him. Sangyeon, from the far end of the table, made a very small sound that was technically not a laugh but occupied adjacent territory.

"What does being right sound like?" she asked, because she couldn't help it.

Hyunjae finally moved the arm from his eyes and looked at her with the flat, direct attention she had gotten used to — the one that took everything in and gave very little back. "Less like you're arguing with the page," he said, and put his arm back.

She turned back to her textbook. The corner of her mouth was doing something she chose not to acknowledge.

This was one of the things about the secondary building that she had not anticipated when she started spending time here — not the guns or the operational conversations or the specific gravity that certain topics carried in this room, but the fact that these people were, in their various and entirely individual ways, genuinely funny. Not performing humor. Just possessed of it, the way some people were, the way it lived in them whether they meant it to or not. Hyunjae's dry bluntness. Sangyeon's absolute, unhurried precision that occasionally produced observations so accurate they landed as comedy without intending to. Eric's careful questions that sometimes revealed, in their careful-ness, that he had been thinking about something completely unrelated to the conversation for an extended period of time and had finally decided to ask about it.

And Hyunjoon, who was a category entirely his own.

She heard him before she saw him — the sound of the refrigerator opening, the particular Hyunjoon energy of someone who had decided that whatever he was doing was the most important thing currently happening in any room he entered. He came into the main room with a bowl of something in each hand, set one in front of Sangyeon without ceremony, and looked at Solar.

"Sol," he greeted her, with the specific warmth he reserved for her that she had come to understand was not performance — it was just that she had been placed in a particular category in Hyunjoon's mental architecture, and the category was people I genuinely like, and the warmth was the natural expression of that. He looked at her textbook. "Are you actually studying right now?"

"I have an exam."

"You always have an exam."

"I always have an exam because I take difficult classes."

He sat down across from her, pulling the bowl toward him, and looked at her with the particular combination of amusement and something more genuine that he sometimes wore when she said things he found specifically Solar. "You know," he remarked, picking up his spoon, "most girls who spend time here are not using it to do AP History."

"Most girls who spend time here," she returned, without looking up, "are not going to inherit one of the largest companies in New York."

A beat. She could feel the quality of the silence in the room shift — not dramatically, not with weight, just the small acknowledgment of something true having been said. She finished the sentence she was annotating.

"Fair," Hyunjoon conceded, and ate his food.

Sangyeon looked up from his laptop briefly — not at her, at the room in general, the way he sometimes did when he was cross-referencing something internal. Then he looked back at his screen and didn't say anything, which from Sangyeon meant the same as agreement.

She was on her third page when Sunwoo appeared in the doorway.

She knew he had come in before she saw him — she had developed, over the course of the past year, a specific and entirely involuntary awareness of his proximity that operated below the level of conscious attention. It was like something in the room changed temperature. She looked up.

He was leaning against the doorframe with his arms loosely crossed, jacket on, watching her. There was machine oil on his hands and something that looked like it might be grease at the edge of his jaw and he looked — comfortable, in the specific and total way he looked comfortable in his own spaces, the version of him that didn't perform anything for anyone. He looked at her with those dark eyes and the corner of his mouth moved.

"How long have you been here?" he asked.

"An hour."

"You didn't come find me."

"You were working." She tilted her head at the room around them, at the members, at the textbooks and the bowl Hyunjoon was working through and the laptop Sangyeon was focused on. "I was fine."

Something in his expression moved — very brief, very private, the specific thing that happened when she said something that landed in him in a particular way. He pushed off the doorframe and crossed to her and sat beside her, and she moved her textbook to make room with the automatic ease of people who had been making room for each other for a long time, and he looked at the page she was on and then looked at her margins.

"You're wrong about the third point," he said.

She stared at him. Hyunjae, from the couch, made a sound that was definitively a laugh this time and then returned to silence.

"That's—" she started.

"The primary source interpretation," Sunwoo said, calmly. "You're reading intent as evidence. That's not the same thing."

She looked at her notation. She looked at the text. She looked at her notation again, and felt, with a specific and entirely unfair clarity, that he was correct.

"I hate this," she informed him.

"I know," he returned, and picked up her pencil and made a small correction in the margin, and handed it back, and the warmth of his arm against hers was constant and unhurried and she had stopped cataloguing it the way she had once catalogued everything, which was its own kind of information about what this year had made of her.

Eric came in from the east door sometime after that, stamping cold from his boots, and he looked at the room and did the brief environmental assessment he always did before fully committing to an entrance — Solar at the table, Sunwoo beside her, Hyunjoon with his bowl, Sangyeon working, Hyunjae on the couch — and he nodded to himself like this configuration made complete sense.

"Solar," he greeted her, with the seriousness of someone delivering important information. "You're here again."

"I'm here again," she confirmed.

"Third time this week."

"Fourth," Hyunjoon corrected, without looking up.

Eric absorbed this. He looked at Solar. "Do you live here now?"

"She has her own room at the Lee mansion," Sunwoo said flatly.

"I know that," Eric returned, in the careful tone of someone clarifying a misunderstanding, "I'm just saying, statistically—"

"Eric," Sangyeon said, from the far end of the table, with the unhurried precision of someone redirecting a natural disaster, "sit down."

Eric sat down. He was quiet for approximately forty-five seconds before he began to pick at the edge of the table in the methodical, restless way he had when he was thinking about something and hadn't decided whether to say it yet. Solar watched this in her peripheral vision and waited.

"Is the exam tomorrow?" he asked finally.

She looked at him. "Yes."

"And you're ready?"

"I'm always ready."

He considered this. Then: "What's the exam on?"

She looked at him for a moment, and then at the secondary building around her — at Hyunjoon eating, Sangyeon working, Hyunjae pretending to sleep, Sunwoo's arm against hers, the tools visible through the back room door, the particular occupied quiet of a space that had become simply normal — and she thought about the version of herself that had stood in this same room six months ago and looked at the guns on the table and made herself not react.

She thought about how long ago that felt.

"The Cold War," she told Eric.

He nodded, like this was useful information. "I know stuff about that," he offered.

"You absolutely do not," Hyunjae said, from the couch.

"I know things," Eric insisted.

"Name one thing."

A pause. Eric's expression took on the particular quality of someone who had made a claim they were now trying to support with available resources. "The wall," he ventured.

Hyunjae was quiet for a moment. "Which wall."

"The...Cold War wall."

"The Berlin Wall," Sangyeon said, without looking up from his laptop.

"That one," Eric confirmed, with the confidence of someone who had been right all along. "I knew about that one."

Solar pressed her lips together. Sunwoo, beside her, was looking at the table with the expression he wore when he had decided something was her fault, which it wasn't, but which she understood as his version of sharing the moment with her — the specific and private humor that lived between them in rooms where everyone else could see it but only she was supposed to know what it was.

She looked back at her textbook.

She was sixteen years old. She was sitting in the secondary building of the Kim property on a Tuesday in January doing AP History in the company of people who carried weapons in their jacket pockets and who had, collectively, seen things that the rest of the world kept carefully out of sight. She was wearing the promise ring. She had a curfew she was going to break by approximately forty minutes and she was already composing the Chanhee text in her head.

She was, she realized, entirely at ease.

She had not always been. She remembered the first time she had stood in this room — the deliberate composure, the careful management of her own reactions, the guns on the table that she had looked at and filed and told herself to accept. She remembered the specific effort of it. The weight of choosing this, day after day, with full knowledge of what the choice entailed.

The weight was still there. She was not naive enough to think it had disappeared. She knew exactly what this room was and what the people in it did and what she had become by choosing to be here. She carried that knowledge the way she carried everything now — not lightly, but without flinching.

She just no longer had to think about not flinching.

Sunwoo's hand found hers under the table, easy and unhurried, and she let him take it and turned the page, and the secondary building held them all in its occupied quiet, and outside the January Castle was cold and still and 2011 had only just begun.


Chapter Thirty One

2011 || NEW YORK






The thing about Upper East Side High was that it had a specific kind of memory.


Not institutional memory — not the kind that lived in the plaques along the main corridor or the trophy cases outside the gymnasium or the framed photographs of graduating classes stretching back to 1962, all those earnest faces pressed together in rows, smiling the particular smile of people who believed they were standing at the beginning of something. That kind of memory was decorative. It didn't move. What Solar meant was the other kind — the living kind, the kind that breathed through the student body like weather, passed from one hallway conversation to the next with the speed and enthusiasm of people who had nothing more urgent to do with their attention.


Upper East Side High remembered everything. And in January of 2011, what it remembered most specifically was this: Kim Sunwoo and Solar Lee were together.


Not new information, technically. The school had been aware, in the vague and speculative way of institutions that trafficked in awareness, for the better part of the previous year. But there was a difference between aware and certain, and somewhere between December and now, the distinction had collapsed entirely. She couldn't have told you what changed. There had been no announcement, no visible shift, no single moment she could point to and say — there, that was when everyone knew. It had simply settled, the way facts eventually settled, into the texture of the place.


The effect was difficult to describe precisely.


It was in the way the hallway moved around her now. Not parting — it was more subtle than that, more like a slight and involuntary adjustment, the specific recalibration of people who had already calculated where she was and had arranged themselves accordingly. It was in the quality of the looks she received, which had changed from the frank, unguarded curiosity of the previous year into something more careful. More considered. Like people were trying to figure out what, exactly, they were looking at — the girl they had always known, or whoever she had become.


She was both. That was the thing none of them had worked out yet.


She noticed it most on mornings when she arrived alone — when the Bugatti had pulled up to the curb and let her out and pulled away again, and she walked into the building with her bag on her shoulder and her expression set to the pleasant neutrality she had worn at Castle events since she was old enough to attend them. In those moments, she was acutely and precisely aware of the quality of the attention she was moving through. Not afraid of it. Not flattered by it. Just aware, in the way she was aware of most things now — clear-eyed, cataloguing, entirely unhurried.


She had learned that from him.


She was at her locker when it happened.


It was nothing. That was the important part — the thing worth noting, the thing that told the whole story in the space of four seconds. A boy from her AP Economics class came around the corner at speed, backpack swinging, not looking, the universal body language of someone already running three minutes late and choosing optimism over realism. He caught her locker door with his shoulder, and the impact sent the textbook she had just picked up skidding out of her hands and onto the floor between them.


He stopped.


She looked at the textbook. She looked at him.


He looked like he was briefly reconsidering every decision that had led to this moment. His mouth opened. "I'm — I'm so sorry," he managed, voice pitched slightly higher than was natural for him, already bending to retrieve it before she could, holding it out with both hands like an offering, like the specific body language of someone who was not just apologizing for a dropped textbook. "Really, I'm so—"


"It's fine," she told him, taking it from him. She meant it — it was fine, it was a textbook, it had taken approximately two seconds. But the apology had been the kind that came from somewhere deeper than the collision warranted, and she knew it, and he knew she knew it, and the knowledge of that moved briefly and visibly across his face before he nodded and turned and walked away at a pace that was slightly faster than the one he had arrived at.


She tucked the textbook under her arm.


From three feet to her left, where he had come to stand at some point in the previous thirty seconds with his jacket on and his expression set to its default blankness, Sunwoo watched the boy go. It was a specific kind of watching — not a threat, nothing so overt as that. Just the flat, unhurried attention of someone who noticed everything and had reached a conclusion about what he'd noticed and was not particularly moved by it. The boy did not look back. Solar suspected he could feel the attention on the back of his neck and had decided, correctly, that looking back was not in his best interest.


Sunwoo looked at her.


She looked back at him. "Don't," she instructed.


"I didn't do anything."


"You were going to."


The corner of his mouth moved. Not quite a smile — never quite a smile, not where anyone else could see it — but the specific, slight displacement of expression that meant he was amused and was choosing not to perform it. He fell into step beside her as she started down the hall, and she felt the shift in the hallway the way she always felt it when they were together — the adjustment, the recalibration, amplified. People looked and then looked away and then looked again. She didn't watch them do it. She didn't need to.


She could feel it.


She could also feel the specific warmth of him at her shoulder — not touching, there was a distance between them that existed because they were in a hallway in a school in the middle of a Tuesday morning — but present. Constant. The particular nearness of him that she had stopped actively noticing because it had stopped being something that required notice. It was just the shape of things. He was there, and she moved through the world accordingly, and it had become as unremarkable and as necessary as breathing.


The whispers reached her in third period.


She heard them before she processed them — the particular hiss and murmur that her presence sometimes produced in rooms she entered, the way it traveled a half-second ahead of full awareness. She was walking into AP Literature with Chanhee, his shoulder against hers the way it always was in hallways, the comfortable and automatic press of twelve years of friendship, when she caught the tail end of it — two girls near the window, heads tilted together, voices dropped to the specific register that wasn't actually quiet.


— and he walked her to every class last Thursday, did you see—


— I heard they've been together since last year—


— she's different now, don't you think? Like, she looks—


Chanhee heard it too. She knew because of the brief, contained quality of his inhale — not outrage, not discomfort, just the noticing of it, the same noticing she was doing. He didn't say anything. He found their seats toward the middle of the room and sat and uncapped his pen and looked at the board, and she sat beside him and did the same, and neither of them acknowledged the whispering because there was nothing to acknowledge. It was weather. You didn't discuss the weather with the weather.


"You have that face," Chanhee murmured, not looking at her, pen already moving across the top of his page with the practiced ease of someone who had been taking meticulous notes since the fifth grade and had no intention of stopping.


"I don't have a face," she returned, equally quiet.


He gave her a look so brief it barely qualified as a look, and so expressive it didn't need to be longer. She pressed her lips together. He looked back at the board.


"They're not wrong," he observed, after a moment. His voice had taken on the particular register he used when he was saying something he had thought about for longer than the conversation implied.


She glanced at him. "About what."


"That you're different." He paused, wrote something down. "You are."


The teacher walked in and the room settled and Solar looked at the board and turned the words over in the back of her mind the way she turned things over when she wasn't ready to examine them directly. Different. She knew it was true. She had known it for months in the way you knew things that had happened too gradually to track — the knowledge arriving fully formed one day, the process of its arrival entirely invisible. She was not the person she had been in January of last year. She couldn't have said precisely where one person ended and the other began.


She thought about that for the length of the period, and then the bell rang, and she gathered her things, and Sunwoo was in the hallway outside the door when she came out, which was not a coincidence and they both knew it.


She had once found this alarming — the uncanny, precise frequency with which he appeared. She had catalogued it, analyzed it, tried to determine whether it was surveillance or instinct or something that simply defied rational category. She had concluded, eventually, that the answer was the third option, and had stopped cataloguing.


"How was it," he directed at her, falling into step like he had simply been paused and she had pressed play.


"Illuminating," she returned.


He waited, which was a Sunwoo technique she had long since identified — the specific patience of someone who knew more would come if he simply didn't fill the space.


"Chanhee thinks I'm different," she offered.


"Chanhee's right."


She looked at him sideways. He was looking ahead, expression unchanged — not blank in the way it was around people he didn't trust, but the settled, unhurried neutral that meant he was simply present and not performing anything for her benefit. She had learned to read the difference. The architecture of his face had become, over the course of the past year, something she knew with the same unconscious fluency she knew the layout of the Lee mansion — not because she had studied it, but because she had been paying attention without meaning to, for long enough that the knowledge had simply become part of her.


"Is that a good thing," she asked.


He was quiet for a moment. Not evasive — considering, which was different, which was the particular quality of Sunwoo taking something seriously rather than brushing it aside. "You're more yourself," he said finally. "Than you were."


She thought about that.


She thought about the girl who had stood in the secondary building for the first time and looked at the guns on the table and made herself breathe through it. The girl who had stood at the edge of an east park bench in the dark and decided something without fully understanding what she was deciding. The girl who had been performing composure for so long she had sometimes, in moments of exhaustion, confused the performance for the thing itself.


She didn't confuse them anymore.


"Yeah," she agreed, quietly.


They walked in silence for a moment — the comfortable kind, the kind that had taken on its own weight and texture over the course of a year and a half of accumulation, the kind that meant neither of them needed to fill it. The hallway moved around them the way it moved around them. People looked. Nobody said anything.


She was aware, at the edge of her attention, of the exact distance between his hand and hers.


"You were going to say something," she observed. "Earlier. About the boy."


"No."


"You were."


He was quiet for a beat too long to be convincing. "I wasn't going to say anything."


"You had the face."


He looked at her. She looked back at him, and the expression on her face was the specific one she wore when she had already won an argument and was being generous enough not to announce it. Something moved through his expression — brief, private, the thing that happened when she caught him and he knew she knew it, which happened more often than either of them fully acknowledged.


"He should watch where he's going," Sunwoo said, which was not the nothing he had claimed and they both knew it.


She laughed — small, real, the kind that arrived before she could compose herself around it. She heard it come out of her and felt, in the half-second afterward, the specific and uncomplicated rightness of it, of being here, of this being the shape of her Tuesday morning. The hallway and the whispers and the careful adjustments of people who had correctly understood something about the weight of the thing walking beside her.


She didn't mind being known, she realized. Or at least — she didn't mind being known this way. By these people, in this hallway, as his. The word had once felt like a boundary, a thing she was being pressed into. It didn't feel that way anymore. She had chosen it, specifically and repeatedly, with full knowledge of what the choice entailed, and she would choose it again without being asked.


The thought settled in her chest, warm and certain, with the particular gravity of things that had stopped being revelations and started simply being true.


Sunwoo's hand brushed hers as they walked — brief, easy, the lightest possible contact, the kind that could be accidental if either of them wanted to claim it was. He didn't claim it was. She didn't either.


She turned the corner toward her next class, and he turned it with her, and the school arranged itself around them the way it always did, and she was sixteen years old and entirely, uncomplicatedly herself, and it was only January.


There was still so much of the year left.


///


The secondary building at four in the afternoon had a different quality from the secondary building in the evening.


In the evening it was louder, more inhabited — Hyunjoon's voice carrying from whatever room he was in, Eric finding reasons to be present in every room simultaneously, the low sounds of people moving through a space they had claimed as theirs. In the afternoon there was a quieter version of the same thing. Softer at the edges.


She had her calculus spread across the main table when Sunwoo came and sat beside her, and she moved her things without looking up the way she always did, and he looked at what she was working on and said nothing, which meant he was thinking about something.


She let him think.


He did that sometimes — arrived in her proximity when something was sitting in him that hadn't found its shape yet, and stayed close without explaining why, and she had learned that the correct response was simply to be there. To keep doing whatever she was doing. To let the silence be the thing it was, which was not empty, which was not nothing, which was in its own way a form of conversation they had been having since they were ten years old without ever naming it.


Outside, the January light was going the way January light went in New York — early, the sky pulling toward grey-blue at the edges, the particular cold quality of late afternoon in a city that ran out of warmth before it ran out of day. Inside, the secondary building was lit and occupied and itself.


"Can I ask you something," she ventured, eventually. Still looking at her calculus.


He looked at her. She could feel it.


"Have you ever thought about what this looks like," she continued, carefully, "from the outside."


A pause. "What specifically."


She set her pencil down. She turned the question over, trying to find the right shape for it. "Us," she said simply, and then, because simplicity wasn't quite right, "all of it. The school, and — everyone knowing, and—" She stopped. Started again. "Chanhee said I was different. The way he said it wasn't bad. But it made me think about what I look like to people who don't have the whole picture."


Sunwoo was quiet for a moment. Then he said, with the particular evenness of someone choosing their words the way he always chose them — deliberately, unhurried, meaning what he said: "I don't think about the outside very much."


"I know you don't."


"You do."


"I grew up in a house that runs on what the outside thinks," she answered, which was simply true. "It's hard to turn off."


He absorbed this. She watched him absorb it, the way she watched him process things — the slight stillness, the quality of his attention turning inward for a moment before returning. "Does it bother you," he asked. "What they think."


She considered that honestly. The hallway this morning. The whispers in AP Literature. The boy with both hands extended, holding her textbook like a peace offering. The specific way the school had rearranged itself around the fact of her.


"No," she said, and found that she meant it. "That's the part that surprises me, actually. It doesn't."


Something shifted in him — she felt it more than saw it, the slight adjustment in the quality of the air between them. He reached over and picked up her pencil from where she had set it down, turned it in his fingers once, set it back down closer to her hand. It was nothing. It was completely nothing. It was also the most Sunwoo thing he had done all day, and she understood it the way she understood all the small languages of him — as a response, as an answer, as the thing he produced instead of the words he didn't have.


She picked up the pencil.


She looked at her calculus.


She was, she thought, entirely at ease.


Outside the January Castle was cold and going dark, and inside the secondary building was warm and occupied and theirs, and the year had only just started, and for the first time in as long as she could remember — there was nowhere else she would rather have been.



Chapter Thirty Two

2011 || NEW YORK






The name came from Eric.


Which was, Sunwoo thought, the most unlikely possible source — and also, in retrospect, entirely logical, because Eric had spent the better part of the past year developing a specific and somewhat alarming talent for absorbing information from adjacent spaces without appearing to be paying attention to anything at all. He brought it up in the secondary building on a Wednesday afternoon, with the careful, measured delivery he still brought to things he had been thinking about for a while before he said them. He waited until the room had settled into its particular working quiet — Hyunjae cleaning a gun at the table, Sangyeon running something on his laptop, Hyunjoon somewhere in the back — and then he said, with no preamble:


"There's a kid."


Nobody looked up immediately. In this room, that sentence could mean a great many things across a considerable range of urgency, and the room had learned to wait for the second sentence before reacting.


"At the Hartley House," Eric continued. "The one that was in the news last fall. They said the building was in bad shape but they kept it open anyway." A pause. He was looking at the table rather than at any of them, which was the sign that he had thought about this long enough to have opinions he wasn't entirely sure he was supposed to have. "I heard from someone that there's a kid there that's — smart. Like, the kind of smart that's wasted in a place like that."


Sangyeon looked up from his laptop. Hyunjae set down the cloth he was using and looked at Eric. The room had shifted to the particular quality it shifted to when someone had said something that mattered.


Sunwoo, from his position at the far end of the table, looked at Eric steadily. "Who told you."


"Someone who knows someone," Eric returned, which in this world was a complete and entirely sufficient answer. "He's seventeen. Been there three years."


Sunwoo was quiet for a moment. He was running the calculation the way he ran all calculations — efficiently, without drama, mapping the variables in the specific order they needed to be mapped. He thought about what he had said in the gym in Brooklyn, the first night, to four boys in a room that hadn't become what it was yet: there's going to be more of us. He thought about the kind of person this operation needed — the kind of person a communications role needed, specifically, someone who could interface with the world outside their walls with clarity and control and the precise management of information. He had been thinking about it for a while. He had been waiting for the right name.


"I'll look into it," he said.


It was not a dismissal. Everyone in the room understood the difference.


///


He went on a Thursday, in the Bugatti, alone.


He had told Solar the night before — not because he owed anyone his itinerary, but because he had developed, over the course of the past year, the specific and entirely voluntary habit of telling her where he was going when it was something that might take longer than expected. He wasn't sure when this had started. He suspected it had something to do with the fact that she never asked him to, which somehow made it easier to do.


"What kind of place is it," she had asked, from her desk chair, looking at him with the particular quality of attention she brought to things she was trying to understand rather than judge.


"The kind that shouldn't still be open," he told her.


She held that. She knew what he meant — not operationally, not in the ways that would matter to a mission report, but bad in the human way that she understood he registered even when he didn't perform registering it. She said nothing else, which was its own kind of answer — the specific Solar Lee response that meant she understood and was filing it away in the place where she kept the things about his world that she had chosen to make room for.


The Hartley House was in the Bronx, forty minutes north through traffic that Sunwoo moved through with the patient efficiency he brought to anything that couldn't be hurried. He had already done what he needed to do before arriving — two calls, the right people, the kind of preliminary work that meant the visit itself would be clean. The connections had been set up. The paperwork, such as it was, had been handled through channels he didn't need to explain to anyone. He pulled into the block and sat in the car for a moment, looking at the building.


It was bad in the way he had expected it to be bad — the specific, accumulated bad of long neglect, nothing recent or sudden about it. The kind of building that had been declining slowly enough that each year's deterioration seemed manageable until suddenly the whole thing was simply what it was: peeling paint along the upper windows, a front step cracked down the middle and held together by optimism, the particular grey of a structure that had stopped being maintained and had simply become resigned to its own condition. A notice board by the front door had one corner free of its mounting. Nobody had fixed it.


Sunwoo Kim got out of the car.


The interior was worse. Not dangerous, not immediately — just inhabited in the specific way that places were inhabited when the people living in them had been given very little reason to believe that things would improve. The hallway was clean enough but bare, the kind of bare that wasn't minimalism, it was absence. A woman at the front desk looked up when he entered and registered the Bugatti idling at the curb outside the window behind him, and her expression moved through several adjustments in rapid succession before it arrived at carefully professional.


He gave his name. The paperwork had preceded him, as he had arranged. She made a call.


He waited, hands in his jacket pockets, looking at the hallway with the unhurried attention he brought to every new space. He was reading it the way he read all spaces — exits, sightlines, the movement of the people in it. Not for any operational reason. Just because it was the way he moved through the world and he had long since stopped trying to do otherwise.


The boy who appeared at the end of the hallway looked nothing like what the space around him suggested he should.


He was tall — nearly Sunwoo's height, which Sunwoo noted and filed — with the particular, unperformed composure of someone who had learned to be still in a situation that would have made other people visibly nervous. He looked at Sunwoo the way Sunwoo looked at things: completely. Without hurry. Taking inventory without appearing to. His eyes moved from Sunwoo's face to the window where the Bugatti was visible at the curb and then back to Sunwoo's face, and the small recalibration that followed was the only sign that he was processing what he was seeing.


He stopped a few feet away. He waited.


Sunwoo looked at him for a moment. "You were told someone was coming," he confirmed.


"I was told someone was coming," Younghoon Kim agreed. His voice was even, clear, giving nothing away — not suspicion, not eagerness, just the careful calibration of someone who had learned to offer the minimum until he knew what he was dealing with. "I wasn't told much else."


"That was intentional." Sunwoo studied him. "Younghoon Kim."


"Yes."


"How long have you been here."


A brief pause. Not hesitation — more like the specific pause of someone who had the answer immediately but was deciding how much of it to give. "Three years."


Sunwoo nodded, once. He looked at the hallway behind Younghoon — at the bare walls, the cracked linoleum, the particular quality of a place that had been asking very little of the people inside it for a very long time. He looked back at Younghoon, who was watching him with those level, waiting eyes.


"Is there anything here you need to take with you," Sunwoo asked.


Something moved in Younghoon's expression — very controlled, very brief, the thing that happened when a person had been holding themselves together for a long time and something finally touched the place where the holding was happening. He managed it quickly and completely. His composure settled back into place like water finding its level.


"Not much," he replied.


"Then let's go."


///


The heir pressure, Solar had decided, had a particular texture in the morning.


It arrived before she was fully awake — the specific weight of the day's obligations settling across her chest with the same reliability as the alarm, as the light through the curtains, as the sound of the house coming to life around her. By the time she was downstairs it was fully present, familiar enough that she no longer startled at it. She just carried it.


This particular Thursday it came with a folder.


Her father had left it on the kitchen table beside her usual breakfast setting — a cream-colored folder with the Lee Industries letterhead embossed at the top right corner, her name written in his handwriting across the front. Not Sol. Her full name: Solar Lee. Which was the version her father used when he meant business rather than daughter, and she had learned, over the years, to distinguish between the two.


She sat down. She ate her breakfast. She opened the folder.


It was a summary document — a condensed briefing on one of the company's current acquisition processes, the kind of thing that crossed the desks of senior staff. Not her desk. Not yet. But the fact of it here, at her breakfast setting, said something that her father had not bothered to say out loud, because Jaesang Lee rarely said out loud the things he communicated most clearly. She was sixteen years old and he was asking her to read acquisition briefings over breakfast, and the ask was so matter-of-fact, so entirely embedded in the routine of the morning, that the only possible response was to simply do it.


She did it.


She read it twice — once for comprehension, once for the things underneath the comprehension, the way she had been trained to read any document that mattered. She finished her coffee. She closed the folder and set it to the side and looked at the kitchen window, at the January Castle outside it, at the pale sky above the rooflines of the houses she had looked at her whole life.


She thought, not for the first time, that the Castle was the kind of place that made a specific kind of person — and that being made into that person was not always the same as being the person you already were.


She picked up her bag, tucked the folder under her arm, and went to school.


///


The secondary building that evening held the particular quiet of a room waiting for something.


Hyunjoon knew before Sunwoo said anything — he had a specific and nearly infallible instinct for when the configuration of a room was about to change, and he was watching the door with the warm, unhurried attention of someone who had already made up his mind about something before the door opened. Sangyeon had his laptop open but was not looking at it. Hyunjae had his arms crossed and was doing the particular ceiling-study that meant he was paying attention to the room. Eric was very studiously not looking at the door, which in Eric's specific body language meant he was absolutely looking at the door.


When it opened, Younghoon came through it first.


He stopped just inside the threshold and looked at the room — all of it, all of them, with the same complete and methodical attention he had turned on Sunwoo in the Hartley hallway, and the room looked back at him. The quality of the assessment was mutual and unhurried and honest, the way these things were between people who were being considered for something real rather than something performative.


Sunwoo came in behind him and closed the door.


"This is Younghoon," he said, which in this room was neither introduction nor endorsement — it was simply information, offered plainly, in the register Sunwoo used for things that were already decided.


Hyunjoon was the first to move, because Hyunjoon was always the first to move — he unfolded from his chair with the easy, warm confidence of someone who had decided to welcome something before he had finished evaluating it, which was a very specific Hyunjoon quality, and he crossed to Younghoon and looked at him with those bright, assessing eyes for exactly one moment before something in his expression settled into approval.


"Hyunjoon," he offered, by way of introduction. "You hungry? I made too much."


Younghoon looked at him. Something in his face moved — not quite surprise, but the particular adjustment of someone who had been prepared for something and gotten something different. "I could eat," he said.


Hyunjoon nodded, pleased, and moved toward the kitchen. Sangyeon looked up from his laptop and studied Younghoon for a moment with the particular quality of Sangyeon's assessments — thorough, without warmth but not unkind, the evaluation of someone deciding whether a variable was reliable. Younghoon met it without looking away.


Eric, from his chair, cleared his throat. "I'm Eric," he offered, in the careful tone he used when he was trying to make a good impression on something he felt personally responsible for, which he was, technically. "I heard about you."


Younghoon looked at him. "I gathered," he returned, dry and even, and something in the room shifted fractionally — the slight, collective adjustment of people who had just heard someone new say something that landed correctly in the specific register of this space.


Sunwoo sat. He looked at Younghoon Kim standing in the middle of the secondary building and felt, with the same quiet operational certainty he felt about things that were right, that this was going to work.


///


She was at her desk when her mother appeared in the doorframe.


Not late yet — early evening, the kind of hour that still had the texture of day in it, the Castle not yet fully dark outside her window. Solar had her calculus open, a half-finished cup of tea going cold beside her elbow, the acquisition folder from breakfast filed carefully into her bag where she would not have to look at it until she had decided what she thought about it.


She heard the footsteps first — measured, deliberate, the specific lightness of her mother choosing to move quietly rather than simply being quiet, which were different things and Solar had long since learned to hear the difference. She turned in her chair.


Solji Lee stood in the open doorframe with her hands folded in front of her, composed in the way she was always composed, every inch the woman who ran the Lee household and the Lee family's public face without visible effort. She looked at Solar the way she occasionally looked at her when she thought Solar was occupied and wouldn't notice — the specific look of a woman trying to find something in her daughter's face that her daughter had not offered.


Solar looked back at her and said nothing. She gave her the room.


"How are you sleeping," her mother asked, softly. The question was ordinary. The asking of it, at this hour, from the doorframe rather than from inside the room, was not.


"Fine," Solar said.


"School is going well."


"Yes."


Her mother's eyes moved — briefly, almost imperceptibly — across the room. The desk. The folder-shaped outline in the bag. The tea gone cold, which meant she had been sitting here long enough to forget about it, which meant she had been thinking about something. Solji Lee noticed all of it, and said none of it.


"You're handling everything alright," she murmured. Not a question, not entirely. The particular register her mother used for the things she was trying to find the edges of without pressing.


Solar held her gaze. "I am."


A pause. There was something in her mother's expression in that pause — something underneath the composure, something searching and almost uncertain, the look of a woman trying to locate a door in a wall she had spent years helping to build. It lasted only a moment. Then Solji blinked, and it was gone, seamless and complete, and she was simply her mother again — collected, warm at the edges, present in the careful way she was always present.


"Good," she said, quietly. "You look well, Solar."


She held Solar's gaze for one more beat — long enough to mean something, short enough to be deniable — and then she stepped back from the doorframe and her footsteps retreated down the hall, measured and light, and Solar was alone again.


She turned back to her desk. She looked at her calculus without reading it.


Her mother had not said a word about a boy. She had not needed to.


///


It was past ten when the cameras looped.


She knew the sequence by now — the particular shift in the security light outside her window, the brief and barely perceptible flicker that meant the footage was cycling back. She turned in her chair before the first tap on the glass.


Sunwoo Kim was on the ledge outside her window, jacket on, the January night dark and cold behind him. He looked at her through the glass with the expression that was not quite a smile and contained everything — the particular look that was for her specifically and only, the one that lived underneath the composed exterior and emerged in the spaces where no one else was watching.


She pushed the window open.


"You could text," she told him, though she made no effort to sound like she meant it.


"I was in the neighborhood," he returned, dry and flat, which was Sunwoo Kim's version of a joke — the specific humor of someone pointing at the obvious with a completely straight face. The Castle was the neighborhood. He lived three houses down. He was, technically, always in the neighborhood.


The corner of her mouth moved before she could stop it. She stepped back to let him in.


He climbed through with the unhurried economy of someone who had done this enough times that the logistics had long since fallen beneath his active attention — one hand on the frame, one leg over the sill, the rest of him following without incident — and landed on her floor without a sound. He straightened. He looked at her.


She looked back at him.


He was different at the end of a day that had cost him something — not visibly, not in any way the rest of the world would have caught. But she knew the particular settling quality of him when he arrived somewhere he could stop performing the absence of weight, and she saw it now, and she said nothing about it, because saying something about it was the fastest way to make him close it back up.


"How was it," she said simply, pulling her knees up under her on the desk chair.


He moved to her bed and sat on the edge of it — the particular Sunwoo version of settling in, which was not quite relaxed but was the closest thing to it he did in most spaces. He looked at his hands for a moment. "The building was bad," he said.


"But he's okay," she said. Not a question.


"He's okay." He looked up at her. "He'll be good." He said it the way he said the things he was certain about — without elaboration, without qualifying. Just the fact of it, placed plainly.


She felt the warmth of it move through her. She got up from the desk chair and crossed to him and sat beside him on the edge of the bed, the way she had been sitting beside him in one space or another for over a year now, and he turned slightly toward her and she looked back, and the distance between them was the kind that existed by mutual, understood awareness rather than by accident.


His hand found hers on the mattress. Easy. Unhurried. Not reaching, not asking — just arriving, the way his hand always arrived where hers was, as though the distance between them had simply been closing all evening and this was where it ended up.


"You did a good thing," she told him, quiet.


He looked at her for a moment. He wasn't going to respond to that directly — she knew that, the same way she knew most things about him, through the accumulated fluency of paying attention for long enough that the knowledge had simply become part of her. The landing was the response. They both knew it. He just looked at her with those dark eyes and the face that was hers in the specific way nothing else was hers, and she felt the full weight of the year behind them and the year ahead and everything she already knew was coming, and the fact that she was going to walk into all of it anyway.


She leaned her head against his shoulder.


He didn't say anything.


He didn't have to.


Outside, the January Castle was cold and still beneath a sky so dark between the stars it looked like something you could fall into. Inside, the Lee mansion held its expensive, quiet warmth around them. Solar Lee and Sunwoo Kim sat together at the edge of her bed with their hands linked between them, and the night did what nights did — it held them, briefly, in the space between one thing and the next.


This was the peak of them. Neither of them knew it yet.




Chapter Thirty Three

2011 || NEW YORK






The job was supposed to be clean.


Collection run — mid-level, nothing Sunwoo hadn't done a version of a dozen times. A supplier in Mott Haven who had been skimming off distribution numbers for the better part of three months, small enough increments that it had taken Sangyeon two weeks of cross-referencing to catch it, large enough in aggregate that Sihyuk Kim had noticed the gap and made known, in his particular and specific way, that he expected the gap to close. The message had been delivered. The response had been insufficient. Tonight was the next step.


Sunwoo had taken Hyunjae and Eric — Hyunjae for the close work, Eric for the car and the logistics — leaving Sangyeon and Hyunjoon at the secondary building and Younghoon, three weeks in, running his first communications assignment from a position two blocks north with a radio and Sangyeon's instruction in his ear. The operation had a clean entry and a clean exit and approximately four variables that could complicate either. He had mapped all four. He was not concerned.


The problem with mapped variables was that they assumed a closed system.


They were on the return route — job completed, the supplier's revised understanding of his obligations now comprehensively established — moving south on Willis Avenue with the quiet that followed completed work, when Sunwoo registered the change in the road ahead. He processed it in pieces, the way his brain processed things that required immediate recalculation: the blue and red of police lights, multiple sources, not moving. Positioned. Three patrol cars arranged across the gas station at the corner in the specific geometry of containment rather than response. Not arriving at a situation. Already there.


Then the shot.


A single crack, the flash preceding the sound by half a second, originating from the far side of the gas station forecourt. And then movement — a figure breaking from the near corner of the building at a dead sprint, running south along the avenue toward them, and even at this distance Sunwoo could register two things simultaneously: the gun in the runner's hand, and the fact that he was young.


The patrol cars began to move.


In the passenger seat, Hyunjae had gone very still in the specific way he went still when he was waiting for a call. From the back, Eric was already leaning forward between the seats, watching the figure close the distance with the focused, tightly wound attention of someone who had not yet decided what was happening.


Sunwoo looked at the boy running toward them. He looked at the police cars. He looked at the road ahead — the geometry of it, the angles, the specific and narrow window of time in which any decision made now would still be a decision rather than a consequence.


He pulled over.


"Sunwoo," Hyunjae warned, flat.


"I see him."


He rolled the window down.


The boy reached them in the next five seconds — breathing hard, head down, the gun still in his hand — and pulled up short when he registered the car idling at the curb. For one very brief moment he and Sunwoo looked at each other through the open window. The boy's eyes were quick and sharp and doing the same rapid calculation Sunwoo's were, which was itself a piece of information worth filing.


"Get in," Sunwoo instructed.


No explanation. No guarantee. Just the two words, flat and certain, in the tone of someone who had already assessed the situation and made a decision about it and was not going to repeat himself.


The boy got in.


He pulled the rear door shut and Eric pressed himself against the opposite window with the practiced, instinctive spatial awareness of someone who had learned that being adjacent to an unknown quantity with a gun required immediate and non-negotiable personal distance. The boy didn't look at him. He was watching through the back window at the police cars, chest heaving, gun still in his hand — not raised, not aimed, just held, the grip of someone who had not yet decided whether the situation still required it.


Sunwoo pulled out.


He drove the way he always drove when driving needed to not be interesting — at exactly the speed of traffic, nothing sudden, nothing that warranted a second look from anyone positioned at a distance with a radio. He took two rights and a left, putting three blocks and a direction change between them and the gas station, then drove for another four minutes in a straight line before turning into a side street and cutting the engine.


The car went quiet.


From the back seat came the sound of controlled breathing — the boy bringing it down, systematically, the specific effort of someone who knew how to manage a body that had been running hard and needed to stop. Sunwoo watched it in the rearview mirror. It took about forty seconds. Then the breathing leveled, and the boy looked up and met his eyes in the glass.


"You can put that down," Sunwoo directed, with a slight motion of his eyes toward the gun.


A pause. The boy looked at the weapon in his own hand like he had briefly forgotten it was there — which Sunwoo doubted — and set it on the seat beside him. He looked back up at the mirror.


"Who are you," he pressed, directing it at Sunwoo.


Hyunjae, in the passenger seat, turned his head and looked at the boy with the particular expression he wore when someone had said something he found either impressive or inadvisable. He hadn't determined which yet.


"Nobody you knew twenty minutes ago," Sunwoo returned. "Who are you."


A beat. The boy had the specific quality of someone doing a fast and honest assessment — not stalling, not performing — and arriving at the conclusion that the information cost him nothing he wasn't already past protecting. "Haknyeon," he answered. "Ju Haknyeon."


Sunwoo Kim looked at him steadily. "What was that back there."


"What it looked like."


"Which was."


Ju Haknyeon held his gaze in the mirror with a directness that most people didn't produce in situations like this one — not defiant, not performing toughness, just straightforward in the way of someone who had decided honesty was the more useful currency here. "How I make my living," he replied. "First time I'd been caught. Almost."


Almost, because he hadn't been caught. Because Sunwoo had pulled over.


Eric, from his corner of the back seat, was doing the particular quiet thing he did when he was paying very close attention to something and didn't want to interrupt it. Hyunjae had turned back to face forward. Sunwoo looked at Haknyeon in the mirror for a long moment — at the steadiness of him, the complete absence of panic now that the running was over, the level and waiting eyes of a person who understood exactly what he had just accepted and wasn't going to pretend otherwise.


He thought about the gas station. About the way Haknyeon had run — not blindly, but with direction, constantly recalibrating, aware of the geometry around him even in motion. About the gun, which he had not raised in the car when he had every reason to, and had set down when asked without making a production of it.


He thought about what the enforcement side of his operation actually needed.


"How old are you," Sunwoo pressed.


"Seventeen."


"Where do you live."


A flicker in Haknyeon's expression — not discomfort, but the slight pause of someone deciding how much the answer revealed about the rest of the picture. "Wherever," he returned, which was its own kind of answer.


Sunwoo nodded, once. He looked at the street outside the windshield — dark and quiet, the kind of Bronx side street that minded its own business — and then back at the mirror.


"I have a proposition," he offered.


Haknyeon looked at him. Something shifted in his expression — not surprise, because Sunwoo suspected surprise was not something Ju Haknyeon produced in response to things he had half-expected. More like the quiet, honest acknowledgment of a person who had already run the math and was waiting to hear the number confirmed.


"I'm listening," he returned.


///


The cameras looped at half past ten.


She had been at her desk for an hour, calculus open, not particularly moving through it, when the security light outside her window did its familiar flicker and she turned in her chair before she'd even made the conscious decision to. She already knew what she was going to find.


Sunwoo Kim was on the ledge, jacket on, the January dark behind him. He looked at her through the glass with that expression — the one that only existed here, only for her, the one that was somewhere between I know exactly what I'm doing and something much warmer underneath it that he would never say out loud and didn't need to.


She pushed the window open.


"Long night," she observed, leaning against the frame, taking him in — the set of his jaw, the particular quality of his stillness. Still wired from the job, she could tell. Not rattled. Just awake in the way he was awake after something had happened.


"Eventful," he returned, and climbed through, landing without a sound, straightening to his full height. He looked around her room briefly the way he always did — the sweep of it, automatic, cataloguing — and then he looked at her.


"Tell me," she prompted, dropping back into her desk chair and pulling her knees up, watching him.


He moved to her bed and sat on the edge of it, elbows on his knees, and told her — the operation, the return route, the gas station and the police cars and a boy running south on Willis Avenue with a gun in his hand and the particular, fast calculation he had made in the span of about four seconds.


When he finished, she was quiet for a moment, just looking at him.


"You pulled over," she said finally, her voice carrying the specific tone she used when something had surprised her in a way she didn't want to fully admit.


"After," he corrected, with the flat precision of someone drawing a meaningful distinction.


"Still." She tilted her head. "Most people don't pull over."


"Most people aren't me," he returned, without a trace of irony, which was the Sunwoo Kim version of making a joke, and she felt the corner of her mouth pull before she could stop it.


"No," she conceded. "They're really not."


He watched her smile happen with the look he got sometimes when she did something he liked and wasn't going to tell her — the slight shift, the dark eyes holding a fraction warmer than they would for anyone else. She had learned to catch it. He had learned that she caught it. Neither of them said anything about it.


"So now you have seven," she ponders aloud, looking at him.


"Now I have seven."


She was quiet for a moment. She thought about a gym in Brooklyn and five boys and there's going to be more of us, and she thought about Younghoon arriving in the secondary building three weeks ago and settling in like he had always been there, and now this — a boy on a dark avenue who had looked at Sunwoo through a car window and gotten in.


"You always know," she said softly. Not a question.


He looked at her across the room, and something in his expression shifted in the specific way it shifted when she said something that landed. He didn't answer immediately — he almost never answered the things that mattered immediately, he let them sit and she had learned to let them sit with him.


Then, quiet: "I know what I'm looking for."


She held his gaze. The lamp on her desk cast the room in warm yellow and the Castle was silent outside and he was looking at her with those dark eyes and she felt, not for the first time and not for the last, the specific and total gravity of him — the particular weight of being the one person in the world that Kim Sunwoo let in completely.


She got up from the chair.


She crossed to where he was sitting on the edge of the bed and sat beside him, close, the way they sat together now — not the careful distance of two people figuring out what they were to each other, but the easy, settled closeness of people who had already decided and stopped keeping track. His arm came around her without ceremony, unhurried, like it had simply been on its way there all evening.


"Princess," he said, quietly, into her hair.


She looked up at him. He was already looking at her, and the expression on his face was the unguarded one — the smile that almost wasn't a smile, the warmth he kept sealed for her specifically — and she thought that of all the versions of Sunwoo Kim she had ever known, this one was the only one that was entirely, uncomplicatedly real.


"Hi," she returned, which was a ridiculous thing to say to someone who had been sitting next to her for the past ten minutes, and the corner of his mouth moved properly this time.


"Hi," he echoed, in the dry, deadpan tone that was his version of indulging her.


She settled into him, and he held on, and the night outside was cold and still and entirely unaware of itself, and inside the Lee mansion it was warm and quiet and theirs.


She was not afraid of any of it.


She was not the same girl who would have been.




Chapter Thirty Four

2011 || NEW YORK




Eric Sohn brought it up on a Thursday, which was either good timing or deliberate, and with Eric it was genuinely difficult to tell which.


He waited until the secondary building had settled into its after-hours quiet — operations done for the day, Sangyeon at the table with his laptop, Hyunjae cleaning a gun with the methodical patience he brought to the task, Younghoon and Haknyeon on opposite ends of the couch still learning the specific rhythms of the room. Hyunjoon was in the kitchen making something that smelled better than it had any right to. Sunwoo was at the far end of the table, going through the week's numbers, when Eric set a piece of paper on the table in front of him.


Sunwoo looked at it without picking it up. Then he looked at Eric.


"It's a proposal," Eric clarified, with the specific careful delivery he used when he had been thinking about something for a long time and had finally decided to say it. He pulled out the chair across from Sunwoo and sat, which meant he was serious. "For a party. Here."


A brief silence settled over the room. Not uncomfortable — the room had developed its own specific vocabulary of silences, and this one was the kind that meant people were paying attention without performing it.


"Here," Sunwoo repeated, flatly.


"Here," Eric confirmed. He pointed at the paper, which Sunwoo had still not picked up. "The numbers are on there. I looked into it. If we invite the right people — people from school, people who are already adjacent to what we do — and we make product available, we move more volume in one night than we do in a week of standard distribution." He paused. "People pay more when they're having fun. That's just a fact."


Hyunjoon emerged from the kitchen doorway at this point, holding a bowl, looking at Eric with the bright and immediately interested expression of someone who had heard just enough to be invested. "A party," he echoed.


"A party," Eric confirmed, with slightly more confidence now that he had an audience beyond Sunwoo.


"I love a party," Hyunjoon announced, to nobody in particular.


"You would," Hyunjae said, from the couch, not looking up from the gun.


Sunwoo finally picked up the paper. He read it the way he read everything — thoroughly, in one pass, not going back. Eric had done the work: guest profile, product projections, a rough headcount, a note about keeping the profile low enough that it didn't draw attention from outside the building. It was careful. It was methodical. It was, underneath the obvious excitement driving it, a genuinely sound business argument.


He set it down. He looked at Eric across the table.


"Sangyeon," he said.


"Numbers check out," Sangyeon replied, without being asked a question, because this was how Sangyeon operated — he had already run it, probably while Eric was still pitching.


Sunwoo was quiet for a moment. He looked at Eric, who was holding himself very still in the practiced way of someone who had learned that filling silences with Sunwoo Kim was never the right call.


"Friday," Sunwoo said finally.


Eric blinked. "...yeah?"


"Keep the headcount under thirty. You run the door, nobody comes in you didn't personally clear." He picked up his pen and went back to the numbers in front of him, which was the Sunwoo Kim version of a meeting being over. "Don't break anything."


Eric looked at the paper. He looked at Sunwoo. He looked at Hyunjoon, who was already grinning at him from the kitchen doorway with the blinding warmth of someone who had decided this was going to be a great development, and the specific thing that happened in Eric's expression in that moment — the flicker of something loosening, something that had been held carefully in place for a long time beginning to ease — was too brief for most people in the room to catch.


Younghoon caught it. He looked back at his book without comment.


"Sol's coming," Sunwoo added, not looking up.


"Obviously," Hyunjoon called from the kitchen, entirely unbothered.


///


She found out the way she found out most things about his world now — he told her, without ceremony, the way you told someone something that concerned them because of course it concerned them. They were in her room, Wednesday evening, her AP Chemistry spread across the desk and him on her bed with one of her novels he had picked up and was approximately forty pages into and would not admit he was enjoying.


"There's a party Friday," he remarked, still looking at the book.


She turned in her chair. "At the secondary building?"


"Eric's idea. Smart one, actually."


She considered that. She thought about the secondary building transformed into something other than what it usually was — the table with the laptops and the guns, the specific working quiet of it, and then people, music, the thing it would become with a crowd in it. She tried to picture it and found she couldn't quite, which told her something about how completely she had absorbed the building's current identity.


"Am I coming?" she asked.


He looked up from the book. He looked at her with the expression that was doing something at its edges — the one she had learned to read as his version of finding something obvious. "Yes," he said, as though this required no further explanation, which it didn't.


She turned back to her chemistry. She pressed her lips together to keep from smiling at his tone, which she thought she managed, though from the quality of the silence behind her she suspected he knew anyway.


"You have that competition paper due Monday," he noted, going back to the book.


"I know."


"You haven't started it."


"I know," she repeated, more pointedly.


A pause. "I'm just saying."


"You're not just saying anything, you're telling me to start it."


"Start the paper, Sol."


She picked up her chemistry pencil and pointed it at him without turning around. "The book is yours to borrow, not to use as a platform for unsolicited academic advice."


"Page forty-seven has a mistake in the margin," he returned, completely unruffled. "Whoever annotated this got the third argument wrong."


She spun in her chair. "That's my annotation."


The corner of his mouth moved. Not quite a smile. Absolutely a smile.
