She turned back to her desk and opened her competition paper document with more force than strictly necessary, and from the bed she heard the smallest sound that was not technically a laugh but lived in its exact neighborhood, and she decided to let him have it because she was, despite everything, in an extremely good mood about Friday.


///


The secondary building on a Friday night was a different creature entirely.


She heard it before she saw it — the bass reaching through the walls and into the cold February air, low and present, the kind of sound that changed the temperature of a space just by existing in it. The building's exterior looked the same from the outside but something was different about it, some quality of aliveness that it didn't carry on an ordinary evening.


Sunwoo pushed the door open and the sound hit them properly.


The main room had been rearranged — the work table pushed back, the furniture reconfigured into something that suggested gathering rather than operating, the lights lowered to the specific warm dimness that made everything look like it was happening inside a secret. There were already people here, maybe two dozen, the headcount Eric had promised, and the room carried the particular charged energy of people who had been let into something and knew it.


She stepped inside and felt it immediately — the shift in the room when people registered who had just walked in. Not the Castle shift, not the careful social recalibration of people managing their proximity to a powerful family. This was different. This was the specific ripple of a room that already knew who Kim Sunwoo was and had just clocked who was beside him, and the information was being processed and redistributed through the crowd with the quiet speed of a current moving through water.


Her phone buzzed in her pocket before she'd fully taken the room in.


jae: where are you


She typed back quickly, stepping further into the room.


sol: out


jae: out where


sol: out out


jae: Solar.


She almost smiled at the period. Jaehyun only punctuated when he meant it.


sol: i'm fine jae i promise, don't tell anyone ok?


jae: i'm not a snitch, just please be careful


That was the whole of it. No follow-up, no lecture. She tucked her phone away and felt something warm move through her chest at the specific economy of him — her brother who never said more than he needed to and always said exactly enough.


She had already texted Chanhee from the Bugatti.


solsol: hee cover for me tonight i'm staying out


heehee: SOLAR


heehee: it's a friday night what are you DOING


solsol: relax i'm with sunwoo


heehee: oh so that makes it BETTER


heehee: fine FINE i'll tell your mother you're at mine


heehee: you owe me approximately one million favors


solsol: love you best friend :)


heehee: i hate you so much


She recognized some of them from school. Not the student council type, not the ones who competed for valedictorian and attended every required event with their parents' social ambitions wearing their faces. These were the other ones — the ones who existed at the periphery of Upper East Side High's official culture and had always been more interesting for it. A few of them met her eyes and quickly looked away. One girl she vaguely knew from AP Art History gave her a nod that was either respect or wariness and was probably both.


Sunwoo's hand found the small of her back.


It was automatic — not a gesture, not a statement, just the natural placement of his hand where it belonged when they were in a room together and he wanted her to know he was paying attention. She felt the warmth of it through her jacket and moved into it slightly, not enough to perform anything, just enough to close the distance, and his hand pressed in a little more and she exhaled.


"You good," he said against her ear, low enough that only she heard it.


"Getting there," she admitted, equally quiet.


She could feel him not smiling, which was its own thing entirely.


Hyunjoon materialized from somewhere to their left with the supernatural social instinct he brought to every room he entered, appearing at her side as though he had simply been there all along. He was holding two drinks and he offered her one with the warm, conspiratorial energy of someone who had been looking forward to this specific moment.


"Sol," he greeted her, with that particular Hyunjoon brightness. "First party. This is a big night for you."


"It's a party, Hyunjoon, not a milestone," she said, looking at the drink without taking it immediately. She wasn't sure what was in it. She wasn't sure she was ready to find out.


"It's absolutely a milestone," he countered, cheerfully, not missing the hesitation. He tilted the glass toward her slightly. "It's just a drink, Sol. Not a test."


She took it. She held it.


"You've been spending all this time at the secondary building doing homework and being responsible," he continued, undeterred, "you deserve to just exist in a room for once without an agenda." He looked at her with those assessing eyes and added, not unkindly, "You look like you're waiting for someone to grade you."


She opened her mouth. Closed it. Because he was, infuriatingly, not wrong.


"I'm fine," she said.


"You will be," he agreed, in the tone of someone who had already decided the outcome of the evening and was simply waiting for her to catch up to it. He disappeared back into the crowd with the easy, confident movement of someone entirely in his element, and she watched him go and felt, already, the slightest loosening of something in her chest.


She looked around the room properly.


This was not a Castle party. There was no choreography here, no social mathematics to calculate, no version of herself she was required to produce. The music was too loud for that. The lighting was wrong for it. The people here were not performing attendance at an obligation; they were just here, in the specific, uncomplicated way of people who had shown up because they wanted to be somewhere and this was where they wanted to be.


She wasn't sure she knew how to do that.


She was aware, at the edge of her attention, of the paper on her laptop at home. The competition deadline. The acquisition review her father had sent to her inbox that she had not yet opened. The specific and familiar weight of everything that existed outside this room, pressing against the walls of it.


She pulled out her phone. Just to check.


Sunwoo's hand closed around her wrist, gently, before she'd gotten the screen unlocked. She looked up at him. He was looking at her with that expression — not a lecture, not an instruction, just the dark, patient eyes and the slight tilt of his head that meant I see what you're doing.


"It'll all be there tomorrow," he said.


She held his gaze for a moment. He wasn't wrong. He was, in fact, entirely correct, and the paper would not write itself before Monday regardless of how many times she checked her phone between now and then. She let the screen go dark.


"You're infuriating," she told him.


"Mm," he agreed, unmoved, and took the phone from her hand and put it in his jacket pocket, which was either extremely annoying or extremely comforting and she hadn't decided yet.


He handed her drink back. She took it.


///


An hour in, she was different.


She couldn't have said when it happened — the precise moment the composure she'd walked in wearing had started to soften around the edges. It had happened gradually, the way things happened when you stopped holding them. She had taken her first sip of the drink somewhere in the second half of the first hour, after holding it long enough that the ice had mostly melted. It tasted sharper than she expected and she made a face she immediately hoped no one saw. She took another sip anyway, more prepared this time, and it was less terrible.


She wasn't drunk. She wasn't anywhere close to drunk — she was sixteen and had never done this before and she was being careful about it in the specific, measured way she was careful about everything. But there was something, somewhere in the space between her first sip and her third, that was different. A slight loosening at the edges of the evening. The room feeling a degree warmer than it had. Hyunjoon helped more than the drink, appearing at her side at regular intervals with the warm, irreverent presence of someone who had appointed himself her guide to an unfamiliar country and was taking the job seriously.


"You're smiling," he observed at one point, with the satisfaction of someone collecting evidence.


"I'm always smiling," she returned.


"Not like that." He tilted his head. "That's a real one."


She looked at him. He looked back, and his expression did the thing it did sometimes — the warmth going deeper than charm, the genuine version of it surfacing for just a moment before he wrapped it back up in something easier to carry. He had a way of doing that, she had noticed. Hyunjoon Hur felt things completely and packaged them in whatever register was easiest for the room to receive.


She thought she understood him a little better every time they talked.


She noticed the other thing sometime in the second hour.


It wasn't obvious — it wasn't meant to be. But she had spent enough time in this world now to have developed a specific peripheral awareness of the things that happened in rooms where the gang operated, and what she caught at the edge of her vision was Hyunjoon, near the far wall, leaning toward someone she recognized vaguely from school and producing something from his jacket with the fluid, entirely casual ease of a person doing something routine. The other person took it. Money changed hands. The whole exchange lasted approximately six seconds and looked, to anyone not already watching for it, like two people having a conversation.


She looked away.


She filed it. She didn't ask about it. She was sixteen years old and she was standing in the secondary building at a party she had just started to enjoy and the thing she had just seen was simply the room being what the room was. She understood this. She had understood it for a long time.


She picked up her drink.


She was standing near the edge of the room, watching the crowd, when she became aware of a boy from school she didn't know well — junior, she thought, one of the peripheral ones — who had been looking at her for a little too long with the specific quality of attention that had a direction to it. Not aggressive. Just there. The kind of look that existed in a room like this between people who didn't know each other well enough for the looking to mean nothing.


She held his gaze for exactly one second and then looked away, which was a skill she had developed early and used often.


She didn't have to look to know what happened next.


She felt it — the shift in the air, the particular quality of a room adjusting around a change in its center of gravity. Sunwoo appeared at her side with the silent, unhurried certainty of someone who had always been getting here and had simply arrived, his arm coming around her waist in one smooth motion, pulling her in against him. His chin dropped to rest against her temple. Not looking at the boy. Not needing to. Just there — wholly, undeniably, entirely without ambiguity.


The boy found somewhere else to look.


"That was unnecessary," she informed him, though she made absolutely no effort to move away.


"Mm," he returned, against her hair, in the tone that meant he disagreed and had no intention of debating it.


She tilted her head up to look at him. He was already looking at her, the way he so often was — those dark eyes, the particular and total quality of his attention. Close enough that she could feel the warmth of him against her side, her back, everywhere he was touching her, which was most of her. She felt the slight tightening of his arm around her waist and leaned into it.


"You know everyone in this room already knows," she pointed out.


"Good," he said simply.


She laughed — small, real — and he pressed his mouth to her temple briefly, the lightest possible contact, warm and deliberate and entirely his, and she closed her eyes for one second and let herself simply be here, in this room that was nothing like any room she had grown up in, held by the one person in the world she had chosen with her eyes completely open.


The secondary building pulsed around them. The music was loud and the room was alive and the night outside was cold and none of it touched them where they stood.


///


The party wound down past midnight in the way parties wound down when they had been the right size — gradually, honestly, people finding their way out in ones and twos until the room had returned to something closer to its usual self, the building's specific character reasserting through the dissipating noise.


Solar was still there.


She was on the couch with Hyunjoon, who was in the generous, expansive phase of his evening where everything was funny and everyone was his favorite person, his arm slung across the couch back behind her, telling a story about something that had happened on a job in December that she was absolutely not supposed to find as funny as she did. Haknyeon was on the other end of the couch eating chips with the blunt, unhurried contentment of someone who had enjoyed the evening without making a production of it. Younghoon was at the table, tidying papers with the quiet efficiency that was simply Younghoon being Younghoon regardless of the hour. Eric, who had spent the entire night in near-constant motion — managing the door, the music, the flow of people, the product — was finally still, sitting on the floor with his back against the wall and his head tipped back, looking at the ceiling with the specific expression of someone experiencing the first real exhale they'd had all evening.


He looked pleased with himself. He had every right to be.


Sunwoo came and dropped onto the cushion beside her, the solid weight of him settling against her side, and she moved into him automatically — legs tucking up, head finding his shoulder, his arm coming around her without either of them having to think about it. The ease of it, the complete and unhurried fit of them in the same space, was something she had stopped cataloguing and simply inhabited.


"Good night," he observed, to the room.


"Great night," Hyunjoon corrected, with conviction.


"Profitable night," Sangyeon said from the table, without inflection, which was the highest possible endorsement in Sangyeon's register and everyone present knew it.


Eric tipped his head up from the wall. He looked at Sunwoo. Something passed between them — brief, acknowledgment in both directions — and Eric looked back at the ceiling with the small, private expression of someone who had been right about something and would not be making a large point of it.


Solar turned her face into Sunwoo's shoulder. She was tired in the specific, honest way she rarely got to be tired — not the exhausted, grit-your-teeth-through-it tired of midterms and company reviews and everything the week demanded of her, but the good kind. The kind that came from having been somewhere real.


"Did you have fun," Sunwoo said, quietly, against her hair. Not asking it the way someone asked who wasn't sure of the answer. Asking it the way someone asked when they already knew and just wanted to hear her say it.


She considered making him work for it. She decided against it.


"Yeah," she admitted, soft. "I did."


She felt the slight movement against her hair that she had learned to recognize as the version of his smile that he kept for moments like this one — the unguarded, private, entirely real one that only came out in the specific dark of rooms where no one was watching except the people who already knew all of it.


He pulled her a little closer.


She let him.


Outside, February was cold and honest and entirely indifferent. Inside, the secondary building settled into its end-of-night quiet around the seven of them, and Solar Lee was the furthest she had ever been from the version of herself that existed in acquisition briefings and competition papers and her father's handwriting across the front of a cream-colored folder.


She was not afraid of this version.


She thought she might be starting to love it.




Chapter Thirty Five

2011 || NEW YORK




The dress had been a decision.


Not an accident, not an impulse — a decision, made with full awareness of what she was doing, standing in the fitting room of a boutique on Madison Avenue on a Thursday afternoon with her credit card already out. She had looked at herself in the mirror for approximately ten seconds and thought about Eric's party and the girls she'd seen there and the specific, unbothered ease with which they had moved through a room she had been nervous to enter, and then she had bought the dress.


It was black. It was shorter than anything she owned. It had a neckline that would have made her mother's expression go very carefully neutral and her father's jaw tighten and Chanhee — Chanhee, who she had called from the dressing room — make a sound that was somewhere between impressed and deeply alarmed.


"You're wearing that," he had repeated, on the phone, in the tone of someone confirming information they weren't sure they had received correctly.


"I'm wearing that," she confirmed.


A pause. "Where exactly is this party."


"Don't ask."


"Solar—"


"Hee, are you going to cover for me or not."


A longer pause. The sound of him exhaling through his nose, which was the Chanhee version of losing an argument he had already decided to lose. "Fine. You're at my house. Again. You're always at my house."


"I love you," she told him warmly.


"I know," he returned, in the tone of someone who found this entirely insufficient compensation.


She hung up and bought the dress.


///


Sunwoo was at the side entrance of the Lee mansion at nine, the motorcycle idling, jacket on, looking at his phone. She came out through the door and his eyes lifted from the screen — automatic, the specific reflex of someone who had developed a precise and involuntary awareness of her proximity — and then they stayed.


He looked at her.


She looked back at him, with the expression she wore when she was pretending she hadn't noticed the effect she was having, which she absolutely had.


He said nothing for what felt like a slightly longer moment than usual. The motorcycle idled. The Castle was dark and quiet around them. His eyes moved over her once — unhurried, entirely unashamed about it — and then came back to her face, and something in his expression had shifted into the specific register that lived just underneath the composed exterior, the one that wasn't performing anything.


"You're going to be cold," he said finally, which was not what he had been thinking and they both knew it.


"I'll survive," she returned, and crossed to him.


He handed her the helmet without looking away from her face, which meant he was still processing, and she took it and put it on and swung onto the bike behind him. Her arms went around his waist and she felt the slight intake of breath that he didn't mean her to feel, and she pressed her cheek against the back of his jacket and smiled to herself in the dark and said nothing about it.


He pulled out. The Castle fell away behind them, the New York night opening up around them as they hit the street, and she held on and let the speed and the cold air exist around the warmth of him against her, and thought that she could spend the rest of her life on the back of this motorcycle and not want for anything.


His hand left the handlebar briefly to cover hers where they were locked around his waist — warm and certain, pressing in for one second before returning — and she tightened her arms and the city moved around them like water.


///


The warehouse was not on any map she had ever seen.


It was in a part of lower Manhattan that didn't advertise itself — industrial, unremarkable from the outside, the kind of building that existed specifically to be overlooked. No signage. No line. Just a door, and a man at it who looked at Sunwoo and stepped aside without a word, and then they were inside and the whole world changed.


The bass hit first — deep and physical, the kind of sound you felt in your chest before you heard it with your ears. Then the light, or rather the careful lack of it: low and amber and deliberately atmospheric, transforming the warehouse's raw bones into something that felt genuinely other, genuinely underground, the specific feeling of having been let into something most people didn't know existed. Bodies moved through the space with the loose, confident energy of people who belonged here — not young, not the Upper East Side school crowd, but older, more settled in themselves and in the particular world they occupied. There was money in the room, she could feel it, but it was a different kind of money from the Castle's — not displayed, not performing itself, just present in the way things were present when they didn't need to announce it.


She stood just inside the entrance and took it in.


Sunwoo's hand found the small of her back, warm and steady through the thin fabric of the dress, and she felt him lean in slightly, his mouth close to her ear. "Stay close until you've got your bearings."


"I'm fine," she returned.


"I know," he said, in the tone that meant he was going to keep his hand exactly where it was regardless, and she felt something warm move through her that had nothing to do with the ambient heat of the room.


The gang was already dispersed through the space — she spotted Hyunjoon near the far wall immediately, because Hyunjoon was always immediately spottable, and Eric beside him looking more at ease than she had ever seen him at the secondary building, and Haknyeon near the entrance with the flat, observant attention he brought to rooms he was still cataloguing. Sangyeon was somewhere she couldn't locate yet, which meant he was doing something deliberate, and Hyunjae was against the far wall with a drink, watching the room the way he watched all rooms — like a thing he was in the process of fully understanding.


Younghoon appeared at her right. "You look different," he observed, in the clear-spoken, non-loaded way he said most things.


"Thank you," she returned.


He seemed to consider whether it had been intended as a compliment. He decided it had. "You're welcome."


///


Hyunjoon reached her within approximately four minutes, which was faster than Eric's party and which she attributed to the fact that Hyunjoon in this environment was fully and completely in his element in a way that even the secondary building didn't quite capture. He moved through this room like he had built it himself, and his eyes found her the moment he arrived with the specific, bright warmth he reserved for her.


"Sol," he greeted her, looking her over with the frank and entirely affectionate appreciation he brought to things he found impressive. "Well. Look at you."


"Don't start," she warned him.


"I'm not starting anything, I'm simply observing—"


"Hyunjoon."


"—that you look absolutely devastating and I fully understand why Sunwoo has barely looked anywhere else since you walked in." He said this with such casual warmth, like a person making a comment about the weather, and then handed her a drink and steered her further into the room before she could formulate a response.


She took the drink. She thought about not taking the drink and then thought about the last party and the fact that she knew what she was doing now, relatively, and she took it.


The room was different from Eric's party in every way that mattered. The music was older, the crowd was older, the specific atmosphere of it was — darker at the edges, she thought, the way a room was darker when the people in it did things they didn't explain to anyone. But it wasn't frightening. She was with Sunwoo Kim, which meant the room had already sorted out how to treat her before she'd taken ten steps into it.


She noticed it — the way people registered them. Not with the school hallway recalibration, not with the polite Castle adjustment, but with something more direct and more honest: assessment, followed by conclusion, followed by the specific deference of people who understood exactly who they were looking at when they looked at Sunwoo and had adjusted their behavior accordingly. And she was beside him, unmistakably, and that meant something in this room that it meant nowhere else.


She stood a little straighter without meaning to.


Sunwoo reappeared at her shoulder. He had gone briefly to speak with someone — she had tracked him without appearing to track him, the way she did everything with him — and now he was back, and his hand went to her waist without preamble, drawing her back against his side with the comfortable, claiming ease of someone who had stopped asking permission for anything a long time ago.


"Having fun," he remarked, not really asking.


She tilted her face up to look at him. He was already looking down at her, and the expression on his face in the low amber light of the warehouse was the private one — the warm, unguarded version that belonged to her specifically and that she had never seen him produce for anyone else.


"Getting there," she told him.


His thumb moved against her waist, a slow arc through the thin fabric of the dress, and she felt it everywhere.


"Good," he said, and looked back at the room, and his arm stayed exactly where it was.


///


Hyunjoon found her again an hour in.


She was standing near the edge of the room nursing her drink — more comfortable now, the nerves of the first hour mostly dissolved — when he appeared at her side with the specific energy of someone who had been waiting for the right moment and had decided this was it. He looked her over with the same frank, warm appreciation as earlier, except this time he seemed to have more opinions prepared.


"Can I just say," he began, with the tone of a man about to deliver something important, "that you look absolutely—"


"Hyunjoon."


"—criminally good tonight," he continued, completely undeterred, tilting his head with the theatrical warmth he deployed when he wanted to be both sincere and irritating simultaneously. "I mean it, Sol, if Sunwoo had any sense at all he'd never let you leave the house looking like—"


"He does have sense," she informed him.


"Then he's exercising remarkable self-control," Hyunjoon remarked, genuinely impressed. "Remarkable. I on the other hand have no such—*"


He stopped.


She had seen it before he said anything — the specific shift in the air behind her, the change in the room's atmosphere that she had come to identify as precisely and only one thing. She didn't turn around. She waited.


Hyunjoon's eyes moved from her face to a point approximately six feet behind her and slightly to the left, and the dramatic warmth in his expression rearranged itself into something that was technically innocent and communicated absolutely nothing of the kind. He took a very deliberate sip of his drink.


Sunwoo's hand closed around her wrist.


Not hard — not anything close to hard — just certain, the specific grip of someone who had made a decision and was acting on it, and she was being turned and then she was facing him and he was looking at her with an expression that was, technically, entirely composed. Technically.


"Come here," he said, flatly, which was not a request.


"I was talking to Hyunjoon," she pointed out.


"You were done."


Behind her, Hyunjoon made a sound that was somewhere between a cough and a laugh and then very purposefully became interested in something happening across the room.


Sunwoo looked at her for one second — the dark eyes, the composed face, and underneath it the specific thing she had been watching build all evening, the thing that had been sitting in him since she walked out of the side entrance of the Lee mansion in that dress — and then his hand moved from her wrist to her jaw and he kissed her, right there, in the middle of the warehouse floor with the music and the crowd and all of it happening around them.


It was not a brief kiss. It was not a polite kiss. It was the specific, unhurried, entirely intentional kind of kiss that communicated several things simultaneously and left absolutely no room for ambiguity about any of them.


When he pulled back he looked at her, and his thumb stayed at her jaw, and he said nothing because he didn't need to.


She looked back at him. Her heart was doing something she was not going to acknowledge out loud. "You couldn't wait," she observed.


"No," he agreed, with the complete, unashamed bluntness of someone who had stopped managing what showed on his face approximately forty-five minutes ago and had no intention of starting again.


She exhaled. "Okay."


Something shifted at the edge of his expression — the warm thing, brief, entirely hers. His hand dropped from her jaw to her waist and stayed there, and they stood in the middle of the warehouse floor with the night humming around them, and she thought that she would remember this specific feeling for the rest of her life.


///


It was Hyunjae who pulled Sunwoo away eventually — something operational, quiet words exchanged, Sunwoo's hand leaving her waist with the specific reluctance of someone who had not finished what he was doing and intended to return. He looked at her before he went, the dark eyes and the unspoken instruction of stay where I can see you, and she gave him a look that said I know and he went.


She stood where she was for a moment, drink in hand, and let herself take the room in properly.


That was when she saw it.


Sunwoo was across the warehouse — far enough that she had to look, but close enough that she could see the quality of his expression and what it was doing. A man had come to him, older, the specific manner of someone with a position in this world and the comfort that came with it. There was conversation — brief, efficient, the language of business between people who had done business before. The man produced something. Small, from the inside of his jacket. He held it out toward Sunwoo with the casual familiarity of someone extending a formality — try the product, assess it, that's how this works.


She watched Sunwoo look at it.


She watched the hesitation.


It was nothing. It was less than a second, a fraction of a beat between the offer and the response, invisible to anyone who hadn't spent years learning the specific texture of Sunwoo Kim in a room. But she had spent years learning it, and she saw it — the slight, contained stillness of someone making a calculation they hadn't planned to make — and then his hand came up and he took it, and the transaction completed, and his face went back to the composed, expressionless register he wore for rooms like this one.


She looked away.


She took a sip of her drink and looked at the lights and thought about it, and then she filed it in the place where she kept the things that needed time before they became questions.


Not yet.


///


He found her fifteen minutes later, when the transaction was done and the older man had moved on, and whatever had been in the thing he'd taken had begun to do what it did — she could see it in the slight loosening of him, something at the edges of the composure, a warmth that was a degree more than his usual. He looked at her and the warmth directed itself entirely at her, which was different from the warmth of a room and more like something with a direction to it.


He didn't say anything. He walked to her and took the drink from her hand and set it on the nearest surface and his hands went to her waist and he walked her backward one step until she was against the wall, and the noise of the room was somewhere behind him and there was just this — his face, close, the amber light, the warm weight of his hands and the way he was looking at her like she was the only fixed point in the room.


She looked up at him. "Hi," she said.


"Hi," he returned, and his eyes moved over her face, her neck, the neckline of the dress, back to her face — unhurried, unashamed about the looking — and the heat of it moved across her skin without his hands having to do anything.


"You've been staring at me all night," she told him.


"Yeah," he said, simply, with the flat directness of someone not even slightly interested in denying it, and she felt that land somewhere in her chest in a way she didn't entirely have words for.


His thumb pressed in at her waist. "The dress."


"What about it," she returned.


"You did that on purpose."


She looked at him with complete and total innocence, which worked on approximately nobody who knew her and certainly not on him. "I don't know what you mean."


He held her gaze for one second — the dark eyes, the composed face doing the thing it did when she was testing his patience and he was enjoying it — and then his mouth was at her jaw, the side of her throat, warm and deliberate, and she tipped her head back slightly and let him and her hands found the front of his jacket.


"Sunwoo," she said, not as a protest.


"Mm," he returned, against her neck, which was not a response so much as an acknowledgment that he had heard her and had no plans to stop.


She pulled him back by the lapels of his jacket and he came, and when she kissed him this time it was different from the first — less composed, more honest, the accumulated weight of the whole evening in it. He kissed her back the same way, his hands at her waist pressing in, and she thought distantly that the warehouse and the crowd and the amber light were all still happening somewhere but she genuinely couldn't locate them.


When they finally broke apart he stayed close, his forehead dropping briefly against hers, his breath just slightly uneven.


"I know you're mine," he said, quiet and flat and entirely certain, against her mouth. "So does everyone else in this room."


She looked at him. He looked back at her. There was nothing performed about any of it — not the possessiveness, not the warmth, not the specific dark quality of his eyes in the low light of the warehouse, and she thought that she could spend every night of her life in a room like this, in exactly this position, and it would never stop being the only place she wanted to be.


"Good," she said, simply.


Something in his expression shifted — brief, private, the warmest version of him — and he kissed her once more, shorter this time but no less deliberate, and pulled back and looked at her.


///


The rest of the night happened in that register — the two of them moving through the room with the loose, certain warmth of people who have already said everything and don't need to say it again. He kept her close. His hand never left her for long — her waist, her hand, the small of her back, his arm across her shoulders when they were still. She stopped noticing each individual touch and started noticing the aggregate of them, the constant specific presence of him against her like a current that never stopped.


Hyunjoon caught her eye at some point and gave her a look of elaborate, performative neutrality that meant he had been watching and had opinions.


She raised an eyebrow at him.


He shrugged, sipped his drink, and looked elsewhere, absolutely minding his own business.


Eric, from across the room, was loosening in the way that was becoming his signature — something in him unlocking in party environments that the secondary building's working hours kept careful and contained. He was laughing at something someone had said, his head back, and Solar thought she could see the beginning of the person he was going to become in the specific unguarded brightness of it.


She filed that away too.


There was a lot to file tonight.


///


They left past midnight, back through the unremarkable door and into the cold of lower Manhattan, the city quiet around them after the bass and heat of the warehouse. She was warm despite the dress, warm from the night and from the drink and from the specific residual warmth of being near him for four hours in a room full of strangers who couldn't touch them.


He handed her the helmet and she put it on and they looked at each other in the streetlight for a second — her in the black dress with the helmet on, which was objectively funny, and him in the jacket looking at her with the specific expression he'd been wearing all night — and then she got on the bike and her arms went around him and he pulled out into the city.


She held on.


The skyline moved around them, the lights of Manhattan doing what they did at this hour — less performance, more truth, the city in the specific honest version of itself that only existed when most people had gone to sleep. She pressed her face against his back and thought about the warehouse and the amber light and the hesitation she had seen on his face and filed, and the weight of his hands and the warmth of his mouth and the particular sound of you're going to be the end of me, princess, delivered like a fact.


She thought about all of it, separately and together.


She was sixteen years old. She was on the back of a motorcycle at midnight going nowhere she was supposed to be, holding onto the person she loved most in the world, and the thing she had seen tonight — the hesitation, the thing he had taken, the slight loosening it had produced — sat in her chest not as fear but as something quieter than that.


I hope you never find out. The words he had said to her in 2010, about something else, in a completely different context, surfaced without warning.


She held on tighter.


///


Her room was dark when he came through the window.


She was already there — she had come up through the side entrance while he looped the cameras, and she was sitting on the edge of her bed still in the dress when he landed on her floor and straightened and found her in the low light.


He looked at her. She looked at him.


"You're thinking about something," he determined, which was not a question.


"I'm always thinking about something," she returned.


He crossed the room to her and stood in front of her, close, and she tilted her face up to look at him and he looked back down with those eyes that didn't miss anything, reading her the way he always read her — thoroughly, without hurry, arriving at whatever conclusion he was arriving at without announcing it.


His hand came up and moved the hair from her face, tucking it back, his fingers trailing at her jaw afterward. Warm and deliberate, the way he touched her when he wasn't performing anything for anyone.


"Are you going to tell me what it is," he asked.


She considered him — the composed face, the dark eyes, the specific quality of a person who had asked a question already knowing the answer might not come tonight and having decided that was acceptable.


"Not tonight," she told him honestly.


He held her gaze for a moment. He nodded — once, unhurried, the acknowledgment of someone who trusted the timeline even when he didn't have the contents. Then he sat beside her on the bed and she turned toward him and his hand found her face again, properly this time, tilting her up, and he kissed her with the same certainty he'd had all night, the warmth and the weight of it, and she kissed him back and let the question she was carrying sit quietly in its place, patient, waiting for the right moment.


It would come.


For now there was just this — his hands and her face and the dark of her room and the Castle asleep outside, and the specific and entire feeling of being exactly where she was supposed to be, with exactly who she was supposed to be with, in the specific and uncomplicated way of two people who were each other's and had stopped needing to say it.


She was not afraid of anything he was.


Not yet.





Chapter Thirty Six

2011 || NEW YORK






The door to her father's office was heavier than any other door in the Lee mansion.


She had thought this since she was small — had noticed it every time she was called in, the particular resistance of it, the way it required a specific and deliberate push to open. She had decided, years ago, that it was intentional. Everything about Jaesang Lee's office was intentional. The dark mahogany walls. The curtains that swallowed the light. The desk positioned to put maximum distance between him and whoever sat across from it. The room was a statement, and the statement was: you are here because I summoned you, and you will leave when I permit it.


Solar pushed the door open and went inside.


He was at his desk. He didn't look up immediately — he never did, it was the specific power move of a man who had learned that making people wait was its own form of communication — and she crossed the room and sat in the chair across from him and folded her hands in her lap and waited. The clock on the mantle ticked. Through the thick walls of the mansion, the house made its ambient sounds — staff in the kitchen, the distant low murmur of the television in the sitting room where Chaerin and Juyeon had been when she passed them on the way down the hall. Jaehyun had been at his desk. She had felt his eyes on her back as she descended.


Her father set down his pen and looked at her.


"Your review," he began, without preamble, and slid a document across the desk toward her. She recognized it — the quarterly analysis she had prepared for the company's current acquisition project, the one that had taken her the better part of two weeks alongside her AP coursework and the tutoring sessions and everything else. She had been proud of it. She had thought it was good.


She looked at it now, and at his expression, and understood immediately that she had been wrong about that.


"This is insufficient," he told her, in the flat, certain tone he used for things he had already concluded and was not interested in debating. "The projections in section three don't account for the competitor landscape. The risk assessment is surface-level. This is the quality of work I would expect from someone at entry level, Solar, not from the person who is going to inherit this company."


She kept her face where she wanted it. "I can revise it," she answered, evenly.


"You should not have needed to revise it," he returned. "You should have gotten it right."


The clock ticked.


She was sixteen years old. She was enrolled in four AP courses, two college-level business seminars, and a weekly tutoring program that met on Monday and Thursday mornings before school. She had written the analysis after a week in which she had slept an average of five hours a night. She knew, with complete and quiet certainty, that the analysis was not entry-level. She also knew that pointing this out was not something she was going to do.


"I understand," she said.


"Do you?" His eyes stayed on her, the flat inventory-taking look that didn't warm or soften regardless of the content of the conversation. "Because I'm not sure you do. What I see in this work is someone going through the motions. Checking boxes. That is not what this company needs from its future."


She breathed, slowly, through the particular controlled calm she had been building since she was old enough to sit in this chair. The words landed the way they always landed — precisely, each one placed. He knew exactly how to do this. He had probably always known. He was, in every measurable way, exceptional at what he did, and what he did right now was take the thing she had worked hardest on and reduce it to insufficient in a single word.


"Furthermore," he continued, "your economics tutor informs me your last session was below your usual standard. You were distracted."


She said nothing.


"I don't know what is occupying your attention, Solar, and frankly I am not particularly interested in knowing. What I am interested in is results. You are the eldest. You are the one I have chosen to carry this forward. That is not a gift — it is an obligation, and I expect you to honor it." He picked up his pen again. The motion was a dismissal and they both knew it. "You will revise the analysis and have it to me by the end of the week. Properly this time."


"Yes, sir," she said.


She stood. She crossed the room. She pushed open the heavy door and walked out into the hall and pulled it shut behind her with the particular controlled precision of someone who was not going to let anything show.


///


Outside the office door, in the hallway, there were three of them.


Jaehyun was against the wall, arms crossed, jaw set, with the expression she had learned to read as the one he wore when he was angry about something and had decided that acting on it would make things worse. He looked at her when she came out and the anger was visible in his eyes even though his face was doing its composed thing.


Chaerin was beside him, arms across her chest, her own jaw tight, green eyes sharp and reading the situation with the specific, instinctive intelligence she had been demonstrating since before she had words for it. She was twelve years old and she already understood the weight of a closed door.


Juyeon was slightly behind them both, not sure what to do with his hands, the discomfort on his face the specific kind that came from hearing something through a wall that you couldn't un-hear.


None of them said anything.


Solar looked at them — at their three faces, at Jaehyun's fury held carefully in place, at Chaerin's tightness, at Juyeon's discomfort — and she felt something move through her that she kept off her face entirely. It was the thing she always reminded herself of in these moments: this is why. This was the reason she sat in that room and kept her hands folded and said yes, sir and revised documents that were not insufficient. So that he would never look at them the way he looked at her. So that Chaerin would never have to sit in that chair and hear that word applied to her best work. So that Juyeon, who was fifteen and warm and goofy and had no architecture for rooms like that one, would never have to build any.


She was the buffer. She had chosen to be. She would choose it again.


"I'm fine," she told them, before anyone could ask.


Jaehyun looked at her for a long moment with those eyes that didn't believe her and weren't going to say so. Then he pushed off the wall and went back to his room without a word, which from Jaehyun meant he was going somewhere to be angry privately rather than in the hallway where it would make things complicated.


Chaerin was watching her with the particular look she got when she wanted to say something and was deciding whether to say it. She decided against it — unusual for Chaerin, which meant whatever she had almost said was something real. She disappeared back toward the sitting room instead, quieter than she'd been on the way out.


Juyeon lingered for a second. He looked at Solar with his brow furrowed and his mouth doing the thing it did when he was genuinely worried and didn't have the words for it, and then he said, quietly: "He's wrong, you know."


She looked at him.


"About the work," he clarified, shuffling slightly. "I heard him say it wasn't good enough. He's wrong."


She held his gaze for a moment. Then she reached out and squeezed his arm once, brief and certain, and let go. "Go finish your show," she told him, not unkindly.


He went.


///


Her room was quiet.


She sat at her desk and she did not open a single textbook. She did not open the revision document. She did not do anything except sit there with her hands flat on the desk surface and look at the wall and let herself, in the privacy of her own room with no one to perform anything for, be exactly as tired as she was.


The tiredness was a specific and particular kind. Not the tiredness of a bad night's sleep or a long week — the tiredness of carrying something heavy for a long time in a way that didn't allow for setting it down. She had been carrying it for years. She was good at it. She would keep being good at it. She knew all of this.


It didn't make her less tired.


Her phone buzzed on the desk.


She looked at it.


sunwoo: come out tonight


She stared at the text. She hadn't told him about the office — she hadn't told anyone — but there it was, three words, the way he communicated when he already knew what he needed to know and had decided accordingly. He always knew. She had stopped being surprised by it a long time ago.


She picked up the phone.


sol: where


sunwoo: I'll come get you


She looked at the revision document she was supposed to finish by the end of the week. She looked at the textbooks she hadn't opened. She looked at the wall, and the ceiling, and the clock, and then she looked back at the phone.


sol: okay


She put the phone down and went to get changed.


///


The party was somewhere in lower Manhattan again — a different building from the last time but the same world, the same underground quality of it, the bass reaching through the walls before the door opened. She had dressed better this time, knowing what she was walking into, and she felt it as soon as they were inside — the loosening that came from a room that wanted nothing from her. No analysis. No projections. No section three.


She was good at parties now, or at least better than she'd been. The nervousness of the first night at Eric's felt like a different person ago. She moved through the room with the growing confidence of someone who had stopped cataloguing the rules and started simply being in the space, and Sunwoo was beside her, his hand at her back, present in the way he was always present when they were here — watchful, close, the particular possessive ease of someone who had claimed something and was entirely at peace with the claiming.


They had been there maybe an hour when she saw it.


Not offered to her — just existing in the room, the way it always existed in rooms like this. Someone a few feet away, passing something small between them. She watched it happen with the same cataloguing attention she brought to everything she was still learning, and then she looked at Sunwoo beside her.


He was talking to Sangyeon — something low and operational, the two of them doing their particular efficient exchange of information. She waited until there was a natural break, and then she turned to him and said, simply, without dropping her voice or making anything of it:


"I want to try something."


He stopped. He looked at her.


The quality of the look shifted immediately — away from whatever he had been discussing and entirely onto her, the specific and complete focus he brought to her specifically. He read her face the way he always read it, thoroughly, arriving at the conclusion fast because he always arrived at conclusions fast and because she wasn't hiding anything. She meant what she'd said. She had meant it for a while.


Something moved in his expression. The surprise was brief — just a fraction of a beat, the specific adjustment of someone who hadn't quite expected this and was recalibrating — and then it settled into something warmer, something that was, unmistakably, pleased. Not in a performed way. Just Sunwoo Kim being pleased about something and not managing it off his face in time.


"Yeah?" he said.


"Yeah."


He held her gaze for a moment. "You sure."


"Sunwoo." She looked at him directly. "Yes."


He looked at her for one more second — reading her, the way he always read her, making sure — and then he nodded, once, and his hand found the small of her back and he steered her toward Hyunjoon, who had the specific, unhurried ease of someone entirely in his element and who looked between the two of them and read the situation immediately.


"Sol wants to try something," Sunwoo told him, in the flat, informational tone he used for things that were already decided.


Hyunjoon looked at her. The warmth in his expression was genuine and specific, the particular Hyunjoon warmth of someone who genuinely liked her and was glad about something on her behalf. He produced something small — a tablet, pale and unremarkable, the kind of thing that existed in this world the way everything existed in this world: casually, without announcement.


He set it in her palm.


"Start small," Sunwoo told her, which was the closest he was going to get to a disclaimer, and she could hear underneath it the thing he wasn't saying — I've got you, I'm watching, nothing's going to happen to you — which was just Sunwoo, the way he said things without saying them.


She looked at it for a moment. She thought about the office, and the clock, and the weight of the document she was supposed to revise. She thought about insufficient and the flat certainty with which it had been delivered.


She took it.


///


It didn't happen immediately. That was the first thing she noticed — there was a stretch of time where everything felt exactly the same, and she was aware of the waiting of it, the particular self-consciousness of expecting something. She stayed close to Sunwoo. He stayed close to her. He didn't make anything of the watching he was doing — he was just there, present, his arm around her, talking to whoever came to talk, carrying the evening with the easy authority he brought to all spaces he inhabited.


And then, gradually, it began.


It wasn't a wall or a wave or anything she could have described precisely. It was more like — loosening. Like something that had been held very tightly for a very long time had simply, without drama, let go. The warmth started in her chest and moved outward, slow and total, and the edges of the room softened in a way that wasn't frightening at all. The music had texture she hadn't heard before. The air felt warmer.


She looked at Sunwoo.


He was already looking at her — he had been watching, she understood, with the specific quiet attention of someone monitoring something they cared about. He saw whatever was on her face and something shifted in his expression, the warmth going deeper, and he pulled her closer with the arm he had around her and dropped his mouth to her temple.


"There it is," he murmured, against her hair.


She laughed.


It came out of her before she could manage it — real and unguarded, the specific laugh that arrived without warning, the kind she produced in his room at midnight and at the Choi mansion when Mrs. Choi was being delightful and nowhere else. He made a sound against her hair that was the closest thing to a laugh he produced in rooms where other people could hear it.


"How do you feel," he asked.


She thought about it. She tried to find the right word for the specific and total quality of how she felt, for the fact that the document didn't exist right now and the office didn't exist and the clock didn't exist and all that was real was this — the music and the warmth and his arm around her and the particular sensation of not being required to be anything for anyone.


"Free," she said.


He was quiet for a moment. She felt the slight shift of him — something in the way he held her changing, something private moving through him that she didn't need to see to know was there.


"Good," he said. Just that. Just the one word, the way he said the things that mattered most — plainly, without decoration.


She leaned into him and let the warmth do what it was doing and felt, for the first time in what felt like years, like she could breathe all the way down.


It was not a responsible feeling. She understood that, distantly, the way you understood things that were true but not currently relevant. It was the specific and dangerous thing about it — not the warmth, not the loosening, but the simple and total fact of relief. She had been carrying the weight for so long that she hadn't noticed how heavy it was until it lifted.


She wanted to feel this again.


She did not say that out loud. She just held onto him and let the night happen around them and filed the thought in the quiet place where she kept the things she was still deciding about.


///


They didn't go back to the Castle after.


Sunwoo drove them to the waterfront — not the Castle's manicured grounds, but actual waterfront, lower Manhattan, the kind of place that existed for people who needed the dark of the river and the lights of the opposite bank and the specific honesty of open air at this hour. He parked and they walked, and she was still warm from the evening, still loose in a way she hadn't been in months, and the cold of the February night was present but manageable against it.


She was quiet for a while. He was quiet too, which was natural — they had always known how to do this, the walking without talking, and it didn't require filling. She had her hands in the pockets of his jacket, which she had claimed at some point during the party without asking, and he had let her take it with the specific resignation of someone who had never once successfully objected to anything she decided she was going to do.


"You knew," she said eventually, watching the lights on the water. "Before you texted me. You knew something had happened."


"Mm," he confirmed, which was not an explanation and was entirely sufficient.


"How."


He was quiet for a moment, his hands in his own pockets now, his breath visible in the cold air. "You had been at the desk for two hours and hadn't moved," he said. "Sangyeon has the camera feeds."


She turned her head to look at him. He looked back, with that expression — the composed face and the dark eyes and the specific private warmth that lived underneath all of it, the version of him that was entirely, uncomplicatedly real.


"You watched the cameras," she said, flatly.


"Sangyeon watched the cameras."


"At your request."


"He watches all the cameras."


She stared at him. The corner of his mouth moved. She turned back to the water because she was not going to smile at that and let him know it had worked, which it had, completely.


They walked for a while longer. The city was doing its middle-of-the-night thing — quieter than it ever fully got, the specific honest version of itself. She thought about the river and the warmth that had faded now to something softer, just a residual ease in her chest, the sense of having been somewhere free for a few hours and carrying a little of it back.


She thought about the document on her desk.


She thought, also, about the tablet in her palm and the way the weight had lifted, and the word she had said — free — and the specific and total way she had meant it.


"Sunwoo," she said.


"Mm."


"It won't become a thing," she told him. Not a question. Not quite a promise. More like a statement of intent, offered to both of them.


He was quiet for a moment. "No," he agreed, in the flat, certain tone of someone who believed what he was saying completely.


They both believed it.


That was the thing about the beginning of things — you always did.


She tucked herself against his side, and his arm came around her, and they stood at the waterfront in the February dark with the city at their backs and the river in front of them and the night holding them in its cold and honest quiet.


She was sixteen years old.


She felt, right now, like nothing in the world could touch her.


That was the problem.




Chapter Thirty Seven

2011 || NEW YORK






The Foundation Dinner was in its third year, and it had grown.


That was the word Solji Lee's Foundation board kept using — grown — with the particular satisfaction of people who had built something from the ground up and were now watching it acquire its own gravity. The guest list had expanded from forty to nearly seventy. The venue had moved from Carnegie Hall's private dining space to the larger room on the floor above it, the one with the floor-to-ceiling windows that looked out over 57th Street. Three of New York's most prominent philanthropic foundations had sent representatives. Two of the city's wealthiest private arts donors were at the table immediately to the left of Solar's.


She knew this because she had studied the seating chart before they arrived.


This was new — the studying of seating charts, the specific preparation of someone who was no longer simply attending an event but operating within one. Her father had handed it to her in the car on the way over, with the particular matter-of-fact delivery he used for things he had decided were her responsibility now. Know the room before you're in it. She had memorized it by the time they pulled up to the curb, which he had noticed and which had produced one of his almost-approvals — the slight, brief nod that passed for pride in Jaesang Lee's register.


She was sixteen years old. She was sitting at a table with people whose names appeared on the sides of museum buildings, and she was the one her father introduced first.


"My daughter Solar," he told them, with the specific casual certainty of someone laying long-term groundwork in a room full of people who understood exactly what that sentence meant. "She'll be managing certain aspects of our philanthropic portfolio within the next few years. Getting her acquainted early."


The people he introduced her to responded the way they always responded — warmly, appraisingly, looking at her with the particular interest of adults who were recalibrating their understanding of a person they had assumed was simply a child present at a grown-up event. She smiled and shook hands and said the right things and moved through the room the way she had been trained to move through rooms — with composure, with presence, with the specific and deliberate ease of someone who knew exactly where she was and what was being asked of her.


Underneath the ease, her mind kept drifting.


Not visibly. Not in any way that would register to anyone watching her. She was too good at this for it to show. But in the space behind the composed face and the correct responses, the thought kept surfacing — quiet, persistent, the specific pull of something she had already decided she wanted. The warmth. The loosening. The particular feeling of free that she had carried home from the waterfront and had been turning over in the days since, like something she kept picking up to examine and setting back down and picking up again.


She shook another hand. She said the right thing. She smiled the Foundation Dinner smile.


The thought surfaced again and she filed it back under.


Not here.


///


The week after the dinner was the week of the revised acquisition analysis.


She completed it in three sessions — Tuesday evening, Thursday morning before school, and Friday after the tutoring session, by which point she had been working on it for so long that the words had stopped being words and had become shapes she was arranging in the correct order. She handed it to her father on Saturday morning across his desk and watched him read it with the practiced patience of someone who had learned that showing any anxiety in this room only made it worse.


He read it for a long time.


"Better," he said, when he set it down. Not good. Not excellent. Just the single comparative, offered and then retracted back into the silence before she could do anything with it.


"Thank you," she said, which was the correct response.


She went back upstairs and sat at her desk and thought about the word better and what it cost to earn it and what it bought her. She thought about the tutoring session and the Foundation Dinner and the company review and the AP coursework and the specific, accumulated weight of all of it sitting across her shoulders in the way it always sat across her shoulders, permanent and growing and hers to carry.


She thought about the waterfront.


She thought about weightless.


She opened her textbook.


She stared at it for forty minutes without reading a word.


///


Hyunjoon found her on Monday, which was either coincidence or the opposite of coincidence, and with Hyunjoon it was genuinely difficult to tell.


She was at her locker between second and third period when she heard him before she saw him — the specific quality of his presence registering in the hallway like a change in atmospheric pressure, warm and slightly inevitable. He appeared at her elbow with the ease of someone who had always been there and simply hadn't announced himself yet, leaning against the locker beside hers with his chains catching the light and that expression on his face — the bright, assessing one, the one that meant he had already decided something and was just waiting for the conversation to catch up to him.


"Sol," he greeted her, with the specific warmth he reserved for her exclusively, dropping into it like pulling on something comfortable. "You look exhausted, hon."


"Good morning to you too," she returned, not looking up from her locker.


"I mean it affectionately." He tilted his head. "The Foundation Dinner, right? You had that thing."


She looked at him sideways. "How do you know about the Foundation Dinner."


"I know everything," he told her, with complete sincerity, and then immediately: "Sangyeon watches the calendars. Anyway." He shifted against the locker, his chains clinking softly. "That's not actually what I came to ask you."


She pulled out her AP Economics textbook and waited. With Hyunjoon, you waited. He arrived at things in his own time and there was no use trying to rush him.


He was quiet for a moment — the specific Hyunjoon quiet that wasn't actually quiet, that was full of him running something through his head with that sharp, instinctive read of his that never announced itself but was always operating. Then he looked at her properly, dropping the performance of ease for just a second, his eyes doing the real thing.


"How'd you feel?" he asked. "The other night. After."


She closed her locker. She looked at him, and she thought about being careful, and then she thought about better and the forty minutes of not-reading and the specific accumulated weight of everything that had been sitting on her shoulders since before she could remember, and she decided she didn't want to be careful.


"Weightless," she said.


Something moved through his expression — not surprise, not triumph, just the warm, knowing acknowledgment of someone who had been waiting for exactly that word and found it significant. He looked at her for a moment with those eyes that saw more than he announced.


"Yeah," he said softly. Then, his voice going somewhere more genuine: "That's what I thought. You've been carrying a lot, Sol. Anyone can see it."


"I'm fine," she said, automatic.


"Mm." He made the sound of someone who did not believe this in the slightest and was too fond of her to pretend otherwise. He looked at the hallway around them — the morning traffic of Upper East Side High moving past, lockers and conversations and people who knew nothing — and then back at her. "Can I ask you something?"


"You're going to regardless."


"Accurate." He smiled — wide, immediate, rearranging his whole face into something brilliant and slightly dangerous. "Did you want to again?"


She held his gaze. He held hers back, completely steady, the question sitting between them with the specific weight of something that had been placed there deliberately.


She had already done the calculation. She had done it Saturday evening at her desk with the textbook open in front of her and her eyes not moving. She had thought about asking Sunwoo and she had thought about what he would say — not with cruelty, not with judgment, just with the flat and complete certainty of someone who had already decided, and she didn't want to have that conversation. She knew how it ended.


Hyunjoon was right here.


"Yeah," she said. "I did."


He nodded, like this was the most reasonable thing he had heard all morning. No lecture. No hesitation. Just Hyunjoon being Hyunjoon — warm and matter-of-fact and genuinely, unhurriedly on her side. He reached into his jacket pocket with the practiced nonchalance of someone for whom this was simply the ordinary texture of a Monday morning.


"Lucky for you," he said lightly, "I've always got something for my favorite person."


Something small moved between them — palm to palm, easy, no ceremony.


"I'm not your favorite person," she pointed out.


"You're absolutely my favorite person," he returned, without missing a beat. "Don't tell Sunwoo, he gets sensitive."


Despite herself, she almost laughed. "He does not get sensitive."


"He absolutely gets sensitive," Hyunjoon told her, with the conviction of someone who had witnessed this firsthand and found it delightful. His expression shifted then — briefly, just a fraction, the warmth going a layer deeper. "Hey." He caught her eyes. "You good, Sol? Like actually."


She looked at him. At the genuine question behind the performance, the real Hyunjoon who surfaced occasionally in these specific moments and was always startling in how much he actually saw.


"Getting there," she said honestly.


He looked at her for one more moment. Then the warmth came back up to the surface and he gave her shoulder the easy, affectionate squeeze he always gave her shoulder and pushed off the locker.


"That's my girl," he declared, already moving. "You know where to find me."


He was halfway down the hallway before she had processed the transaction, his chains catching the light as he went, and she stood at her locker for a moment with what she had in her hand and the weight on her shoulders and thought about nothing in particular.


The bell rang. She put it in her bag and went to third period.


///


At the secondary building that evening, Hyunjoon set the day's earnings on the table in front of Sunwoo with the routine efficiency of a Monday — except the number was not what a Monday produced.


Sunwoo looked at it.


He looked at it the way he looked at numbers that didn't add up — not with suspicion, not with heat, just with the specific, calibrating attention of someone running the arithmetic and finding the result requires examination. Distribution on a school day ran a predictable pattern. He knew the school population, knew the usual volume, knew the value of each transaction in the network down to the specific. This number was outside the pattern. Significantly.


He looked up at Hyunjoon.


Hyunjoon was at the refrigerator, looking for something to eat, his back to the room, with the particular quality of someone who was comfortable in his own body in a space he had been comfortable in for a long time and who was not performing anything for anyone.


Sunwoo looked at the back of his head.


Hyunjoon opened the refrigerator, considered its contents, closed it again, and turned around. He met Sunwoo's eyes across the room with the direct, unhurried quality he always brought to things — not evasive, not guilty, just present, returning the look with the specific equanimity of someone who had done something he intended to do and was not in the business of explaining himself unless directly asked.


Sunwoo looked at the number again.


He thought about high-value clients. He thought about the specific distribution of wealth in the school they attended — the Castle's children, the names he knew, the credit cards that didn't have limits. He thought about who in that building had both the access and the money to produce a transaction like this on a Monday morning.


He thought, briefly, about a waterfront and a word he hadn't heard but somehow knew.


He filed it.


He didn't ask. Not yet. The calculation wasn't complete and Sunwoo Kim did not act on incomplete calculations — he waited until all the variables were present, until the picture was whole, until he knew with the specific certainty he required before he said anything that mattered.


He picked up the money and put it away.


"Good day," he said, flatly.


"Always," Hyunjoon confirmed, and went back to looking for something to eat.


///


Her room was quiet that night.


She had come home to the usual Monday's worth of obligations — the revision she still owed for AP Literature, the company reading her father had sent to her inbox, the tutoring prep for Thursday. She had put her bag down and sat at her desk and opened the laptop and looked at the company reading for a while without reading it.


The cursor blinked.


She thought about the Foundation Dinner and the seating chart memorized in the back of a car. She thought about better, offered plain and immediately retracted. She thought about the tutoring session and the AP coursework and the specific, accumulated weight of it — always there, always hers, never fully setting down.


She reached into her bag.


She looked at what was in her hand for a moment. She was not thinking about anything in particular — not the consequences, not the future, not the 2010 evening when Sunwoo had sat beside her and told her he hoped she'd never find out what this felt like. She had thought about that. She had turned it over, examined it, and arrived, with the specific and complete confidence of a person who had never developed a dependency on anything in their life, at the conclusion that she was different. That she had more self-control than he did, than most people did, that she understood the risk and could manage it, that she would know when to stop.


She believed this completely.


She took it.


///


The weight lifted.


It was exactly as she remembered — the slow, total loosening, the warmth moving through her chest and outward, the specific and entire feeling of a burden she had been carrying so long she had stopped noticing its shape simply being gone. The company reading was still on her screen. The cursor was still blinking. None of it was real in the specific way that things that required something from her were real.


She was just here. In her room, in the amber light of her desk lamp, with the Castle quiet outside her window and the whole complicated world of Lee Industries and AP coursework and Foundation Dinners and revised analyses existing somewhere outside the warmth, somewhere she could see it but didn't have to be in it.


Better, her father had said.


She thought about the word and felt nothing about it at all, which was the point.


She leaned back in her chair and looked at the ceiling and let the warmth be what it was — uncomplicated, total, hers — and thought that this was manageable. She could do this the same way she managed everything else: carefully, deliberately, on her own terms. A specific occasion. A controlled thing. Not a pattern, not a dependency, just a tool. Just this.


She was in control of this.


She was in control of everything.


The cursor blinked on the company reading and she didn't look at it, and the night held her in its warmth, and she did not think about Sunwoo Kim sitting beside her in 2010 saying I hope you never find out in the quiet, resigned voice of someone who already knew too well what it felt like to believe exactly what she was believing right now.


She was asleep before midnight for the first time in weeks.


She slept well.
